End-to-end manufacturing of a vaccine/biologic product (modality-agnostic) — Deep Research Report

English edition. This is a faithful rendering of the Korean report. It preserves the same evidence ledger, source links, confidence markers, and unresolved questions.

Purpose: Comprehensive end-to-end learning resource: how a biologic/vaccine is made and released, from starting materials through regulatory inspection.
Map version: 1 · Axis: process/lifecycle · Leaves: 36 · As of: 2026-07-10

Evidence: 812 claims (solid 536 / weak 276) · 381 unique sources. ⚠️ = weak evidence (lower confidence or blog/forum source).

1. Starting materials & inputs

The biological, molecular, and raw-material inputs a process consumes before unit operations begin.

1.1 Expression systems & cell line engineering

Purpose and Role: Top-level decisions that are difficult to reverse

Expression system selection and producer cell line engineering are the fundamental steps at the forefront of the entire bioprocess, and determine “in which host and how to produce the target product (protein/gene).” The starting point is the sequence/structure of the desired product, post-translational modification (PTM) requirements, expression construct (vector), and candidate host cell line, and the output of this step is a high-producing, genetically stable single clone (cell bank for research). This clone is then handed over to the MCB/WCB establishment phase (node ​​1.2). This step is important because the decisions made here — host, selection system, gene integration method, lead clone — are fundamental decisions that are “difficult to reverse,” effectively setting the upper limits of potency, quality, and stability for the entire subsequent cultivation (2.x) and purification (3.x) processes. However, whether this concept is valid depends on the product type. It is most directly applied to mAbs and recombinant proteins, but the concept of producer cell lines itself is not applicable to cell-independent processes such as mRNA (see differences by product type below).

Principle and flow: From host selection to cell line development

This step is largely divided into four parts: (1) host selection, (2) cell line development (CLD), (3) determination of gene integration method, and (4) host engineering.

Host selection.The first decision is which host to make, and the axes are the origin of the protein (prokaryotic/eukaryotic), need for PTM (especially glycosylation), number of disulfide bonds, size, and required yield. The core principle is that “no one system fits all”, with each host having clear trade-offs of strengths and weaknesses (Source). E. coli is fast and cheap, but it does not provide most PTMs, including glycosylation, and is unsuitable for complex proteins with many disulfide bonds (Source). The yeast Pichia pastoris offers high yields and accurate folding, and more than 70 commercial products have already been derived from this host, but it has limitations in that hyper-mannose-type N-glycosylation may cause antigenicity compared to mammalian systems (Source). Insect cell BEVS (baculovirus expression) has strengths in expressing multiprotein complexes and membrane proteins of up to 17 subunits, but virus production takes more than 3 weeks and leaves behind potentially immunogenic glycans such as α1,3-fucose, which are not present in mammals (Source). Among mammalian hosts, HEK293 is derived from human embryonic kidney cells transformed with adenovirus type 5 DNA, and has derived lineages such as 293T (SV40 T-antigen), 293F (suspension-adapted), and 293E/6E (EBNA-1 episomal replication) (Source). The adenovirus E1A/B genes possessed by HEK293 provide a helper function in viral vector production, and have higher γ-carboxylation (glutamic acid) and tyrosine sulfation abilities than CHO, making them advantageous for proteins that require such modifications (Source). Nevertheless, the industry standard is occupied by CHO, which is reported to produce approximately 84% of recently approved mAbs due to its human-like glycosylation, g/L titer, suspension culture suitability, viral safety, and accumulated regulatory history ⚠️(Source).

Cell line development (CLD).Once the host is determined, the expression vector is transfected into the cells, then selection pressure is applied to enrich the cells containing the target gene, and single clones with stable high expression are isolated through single cell cloning (limiting dilution or microfluidics/FACS sorting) ⚠️(Source). The standard selection methods in CHO are two metabolic selection systems. The DHFR (dihydrofolate reductase) system is used with the auxotroph host CHO-DG44/DUXB11 and amplifies the target gene using an MTX (methotrexate) concentration gradient, and the GS (glutamine synthetase) system applies selection pressure with MSX together with GS-deficient CHO-K1 ⚠️(Source). This is followed by stable pool formation → single cell cloning → high-production clone screening through minipool, well imaging, ambr minibioreactor, etc. → lead clone selection.Gene integration method. Much of the homogeneity and stability depends on where and how the target gene is inserted into the genome. In traditional random integration, the integration location is random, causing position effects, inter-clonal variation, and expression instability. An alternative to this is target integration. Recombinase-mediated cassette exchange (RMCE) integrates the gene of interest into a conserved locus, creating a genetically identical and homogeneous expression pool, and is reported to shorten the development timeline by approximately 50% compared to random integration ⚠️ (Source). Transposase-based approaches are also widely used, with commercial systems such as Sleeping Beauty, PiggyBac, and Leap-In co-transfecting transposon vectors and transposase mRNA to generate stable cell pools with higher integration homogeneity (Source). In addition, methods of applying CRISPR/Cas9 knock-in to the safe-harbor/landing-pad locus are also being developed.Host engineering. Lastly, the host itself is improved to increase potency and quality. Representative examples include apoptosis resistance (Bcl-xL overexpression, BAK/BAX knockout), secretion/UPR improvement (XBP1s), and glycoengineering that strengthens ADCC by inducing afucosylation through FUT8 knockout. However, there is a significant gap in the actual effect size of such cell engineering between vendor claims and academic meta-analyses, as seen in “Issues” below.

Key parameters and materials

The combination of host and selection has been solidified into several standardized sets in practice. CHO-K1 is paired with the GS system (selection pressure with MSX), CHO-DG44·DUXB11 is paired with the DHFR system (amplification with MTX) ⚠️(Source), HEK293 and its derivatives (293T/293F/293E·6E), Sf9/Sf21 insect cells (BEVS), Pichia pastoris, etc. are selected depending on the purpose (Source). In terms of tools and equipment, transfection (electroporation, PEI/lipofection), single cell distribution and whole-well imaging platform (Berkeley Lights/Bruker Beacon optofluidic, Advanced Instruments Solentim·ClonePix, iotaSciences), FACS, ambr microbioreactor, qPCR/NGS for copy number and integration location, and STR profiling for identity confirmation are used. Typical calculation ranges include the mAb lead clone titer typically being 3 to 8 g/L (8 to 12 g/L also reported for high-expression platforms), and the target integration pool is said to be around 6 g/L. The overall CLD turnaround time from lead clone to research bank is typically 3-6 months, and the targeted integration platform claims to reduce this to a “6-month IND timeline” and “screening of dozens of clones.” The starting point for this step is an expression construct/vector + host cell line + selection reagent, and the output of this step is a high-producing, stable clone with documented clonality and a research cell bank (RCB).

Quality and regulation: clonality, genetic stability, construct characterization

The regulatory focus at this stage is “is this cell line truly derived from a single cell (clonality)” and “is it genetically stable throughout manufacturing?” ICH Q5D and the FDA require that the produced cell substrate be cloned from a single cell precursor, and the FDA recommends documenting the probability of clonality (>99.99% in the literature) by two rounds of limit dilution cloning at a low seeding density (<0.5 cells/well) or day-0 imaging (Source). The important regulatory philosophy here is that this is not absolute proof but high probability assurance — the goal is to document a “high enough probability” since perfect proof is impossible.

In terms of expression constructs and genetic stability, ICH Q5B covers transgene copy number, integration site, and sequence integrity analysis, and ICH Q5D requires verification that the cell line is genetically stable beyond the number of generations used for manufacturing (including end-of-production/EOP cells). In practice, the level of requirement varies depending on the development stage. In the IND stage, qPCR-based copy number estimation is usually sufficient, and the NGS integrated location mapping required by Q5B is usually deferred to the BLA stage ⚠️ (Source). Identity is confirmed through STR profiling in MCB. Host/clone selection defines downstream CQAs such as glycosylation (afucosylation → ADCC), charge variants, aggregation, and host cell protein (HCP) profile, but what is tested and managed directly in this section is clonality, genetic stability, copy number, identity, and sterility/foreign agent preparation, and the actual CQA testing is performed downstream. Relevant guidances are ICH Q5A(R2) (viral safety), Q5B, Q5D, and Q5E.

Differences by product type: Points at which this stage changes form or disappears

This step is “most directly applicable” only to mAbs/recombinant proteins, where CHO (GS/DHFR) is the standard and cell line development is the core activity. In viral vectors/CGT, HEK293 is the standard (possessing an adenovirus helper gene), but the nature of its activity varies. In early clinical trials, transient transfection (AAV 3-plasmid, lenti 4-plasmid) is the de facto standard because it is fast, but has limitations in scalability, reproducibility, and cost, so stable producer/packaging cell lines are developed in parallel for commercial manufacturing — in other words, “cell line engineering” morphs into packaging cell line construction ⚠️(Source). In mRNA-LNP, this step is virtually disrupted and replaced. Producer cell lines do not exist and are replaced by cell-independent enzymatic in vitro transcription (IVT) and template DNA (linearized plasmid), and host cells are used for plasmid amplification E. coli, the role moves to Section 1.3. In fact, AAV relies on triple transfection of HEK293 culture, while mRNA is E. The form of this step varies fundamentally depending on the product type, such as amplifying plasmid DNA by coli fermentation and performing enzymatic IVT ⚠️(Source). In classical vaccines, “cell substrate selection” is key, such as fertilized egg, Vero·MDCK cell substrate, and insect cell VLP, which includes substrates outside the five hosts (CHO/E.coli/yeast/HEK293/insect) specified in this section.

Practices and changes

The current standard is the CHO GS/DHFR combination combined with limiting dilution or imaging-based cloning. Random integration is still widely used, but there is a rapid shift to targeted integration. Changes in the past three years include the commercialization of target integration platforms (WuXi Biologics WuXia 4.0 “TrueSite TI”, Asimov “CHO Edge”, Lonza GS piggyBac, etc.), putting homogeneity, stability, and timeline reduction at the forefront, CRISPR knock-in and hyperactive transposons, automation of clonality documentation based on whole-well imaging, and ML-based vector and clone design. It is spreading. In particular, CLD automation provides monoclonal culture and clonality documentation validated by whole-well imaging and AI analysis (e.g. Advanced Instruments Solentim, iotaSciences), and is reported to enable screening of thousands of clones when combined with CRISPR/transposase ⚠️ (Source).

However, this optimistic narrative has clear limits. There is a clear disconnect between the potency and stability claims made by vendors — for example, WuXi WuXia 4.0 claims an average mAb titer exceeding 8.0 g/L, more than 99% of clones are stable in 60 generations, and a 6-month IND timeline ⚠️(Source) — and the peer-reviewed evidence. According to a systematic review covering CHO cell engineering (2011–2024, 164 articles, 466 targets), the most common method was overexpression (63%), the median reported productivity improvement was only 2.1-fold, and no significant improvement was observed over time (Source). In addition, the fundamental resolution of inter-clonal variation and genetic instability is incomplete, and the lack of maturity of difficult-to-express proteins and stable producer cell lines for CGT still remain as challenges.

Issue:There are three points where the sources conflict with each other in this section. First, regarding how targeted integration/cell engineering increases productivity, the vendor (WuXi WuXia 4.0) suggests an average of >8 g/L, 99%/60 generations stable, and 6-month IND, while a peer-reviewed systematic review (164 articles) reports a median engineering improvement of 2.1 times and no clear improvement over time. Because vendor benchmarks tend to be best practices/marketing and academic meta-analyses reflect central trends across a variety of conditions, single vendor numbers should not be treated as established facts. Second, when it comes to whether transient transfection or stable producer cell lines are superior in viral vector production, the former is the de facto standard for early clinical trials due to speed and flexibility, but the latter is more suitable for commercial high-dose in terms of scalability, reproducibility, and cost — that is, it is a trade-off between speed (clinical entry) versus robustness and scalability (commercialization), so the answer varies depending on the program stage. Third, regarding whether two rounds of cloning are necessary to prove clonality, the FDA traditionally recommends two rounds of limiting dilution (<0.5 cells/well), but the industry claims that a single round is sufficient assurance with day-0 whole-well imaging and high-probability statistics. Practical standards are moving as the regulatory perspective that assurance is not absolute proof meets the advancement of imaging hardware.Unresolved questions: (1) Quantitative calculation of the probability of clonality (Poisson-based, accurate probability value of a single round of imaging) could not be confirmed to the verification level due to access to the original text of the primary source (403). (2) Quantitative data on the incidence of genetic instability (e.g., transgene silencing frequency) for each specific host.

1.2 Cell banking & characterization (MCB/WCB)

Why you need it — purpose and role

Cell banking is the step of taking as input the highly productive and stable single clone selected and established in the previous step (node 1.1), that is, the research cell bank (RCB), and “freeze-fixing” it in a two-tier (Master/Working) frozen cell inventory system to provide homogeneous and reproducible biological starting materials for the entire commercial life of the product. The outputs of this step are threefold — a fully characterized and qualified Master Cell Bank (MCB), a Working Cell Bank (WCB) derived from the MCB that serves as the inoculum for each GMP production batch, and end-of-production cell (EOPC) data supporting the genetic stability of both banks. In the end-to-end flow, this step is the final gateway to the “starting material (Part 1)” and the point at which the cell vial is handed over to the upstream process culture (Part 2).

Why do we have two tiers, Master and Working? The core logic is summarized in three points. The idea is to protect a single, irreplaceable MCB from routine consumption, expand a WCB from a vial of that MCB to supply the actual production inoculum, and staged testing in each tier to save costs while ensuring the quality assurance of a “well-characterized bank” (Source). ⚠️ The guidance that governs this two-tier approach is ICH Q5D. This structure is most directly applied to monoclonal antibodies (mAb) and recombinant proteins, but in autologous cell and gene therapy (CGT) or mRNA, the form is disrupted or replaced by another (see “Modification by Product Type” below).

How It Works — Establishment, Storage, and Qualification Flow

From RCB to MCB.Typically, entry into the first clinical trial (FiH) is the trigger for GMP MCB manufacturing (Source). A single, homogeneous cell suspension is created from the selected clones, then aseptically dispensed into hundreds of vials (1 to 2 mL each) and frozen by controlled-rate freezing in the presence of a cryoprotectant such as DMSO. This filling operation is performed in a Grade A/ISO 5 sterile environment.MCB to WCB. Each WCB is made by thawing a single vial of MCB, expanding the cells in a defined series of passages, and then cryopreserving them (Source).⚠️ The resulting 100-300 vials of WCB serve as routine starting material for GMP production, and subsequent routine production involves thawing one vial of WCB as the inoculum each time — MCB is only consumed when creating a new WCB (Source).⚠️

Storage.Cell vials must be kept below −130°C (recommended −150°C) to prevent ice crystal formation inside the cells, and storage in vapor-phase liquid nitrogen is recommended (Source).⚠️ Connect continuous temperature monitoring and proven 24/7 alarms here. Furthermore, to spread the risk of freezer failure and disaster, it is best practice to store cell bank inventory in at least two geographically separate locations (and separate freezers) (Source).⚠️ This “split banking” is insurance that ensures that unique clones will not disappear from the face of the earth even if one location is completely destroyed.Test stratification and bank qualification. MCBs receive the most extensive testing (full characterization), WCBs qualify with an abbreviated panel, and EOPCs cultured to the limit of production cell age are separately harvested to determine genetic stability and adventitious agents (Source).⚠️ Qualification of new or supplemental WCBs is usually sufficient for growth and viability assessments (and small-scale product quality assessments when necessary), and full-scale Re-characterization is not required. However, if the WCB exceeds the reported in vitro cell age limit (LIVCA) or if there are questions about the clonality and stability of the MCB, a commercial-scale process confirmation batch may be required (Source).

Scale, materials, timeline — key parameters

Let’s get a sense of scale. MCB is typically manufactured in 200 to 500 vials (1 to 2 mL each, 5 to 20 It gets bigger — In the case of Vero cells, 500 to 1,000 vials of WCB can be easily made from one vial of MCB, and the WCB requirement for a large vaccine factory is 100 to 200 ampoules per year, so in theory, one MCB can cover hundreds of thousands of ampoules or hundreds of years’ supply (Source).⚠️

In terms of timeline, it takes approximately 4-6 months to obtain a fully characterized MCB (Source). ⚠️ The longest part of this period is the testing phase (approximately 12-20 weeks) rather than the actual vial filling (approximately 1 week), so the lead time for cell banking is effectively determined by “how long the characterization testing takes.” Required equipment and materials include a controlled-rate freezer, gas-phase LN2 tank with monitoring, DMSO cryoprotectant, GMP medium, Grade A filling line, and STR, isoelectric point, and cariotyping equipment for characterization and qPCR, NGS, and TEM (electron microscopy).

Quality and regulation — what to test and what to prove

The regulatory frameworks governing this phase are ICH Q5D (Derivation/Characterization of Cell Substrates—Fundamentals of Tier 2 Banking), Q5A(R2) (Viral Safety/Adventitious Agents), Q5B (Expression Construct/Genetic Stability), Q5E (Equivalence after Modifications), and 21 CFR 610.18 in the United States. 21 CFR 610.18 applies to cultures, including cell banks, and requires cell banks to be stored under conditions with proven long-term stability (e.g., liquid nitrogen, ultra-low temperature freezers) and routine QC for MCBs, WCBs, and production cell substrates (Source).⚠️

The purpose of MCB characterization is to confirm the identity, purity, genetic stability, and tumorigenicity of the cell line, and to ensure safety from foreign agents such as bacteria, fungi, mycoplasma, mycobacteria, and viruses (Source). ⚠️ Specifically, MCB receives the broadest panel, including identity (STR profiling, isoelectric enzyme analysis, and cariotyping); It encompasses purity (sterility, mycoplasma), virus safety (instructor cell line inoculation in vitro test, in vivo inoculation, TEM, reverse transcriptase (RT) activity, species-specific PCR), and genetic characterization (transgene sequence, copy number, integration site) (Source).⚠️ On the other hand, WCB only receives abbreviated panels, which include abbreviated identity, sterility, mycoplasma, in It includes in vitro virus testing and confirmation of productivity and growth characteristics (Source). ⚠️ For reference, in the case of mycoplasma testing, USP <63> is Ph.Eur. Since it specifies a more stringent tolerance range than 2.6.7, it is firmly confirmed that the judgment standard varies depending on which pharmacopoeia is followed (Source).

Demonstration of genetic stability is the regulatory core of this stage. Regulators have established LIVCA for commercially manufactured MCBs and WCBs, requiring them to demonstrate that the maximum cell age actually used in production does not affect product quality and process consistency throughout culture expansion and manufacturing (Source). The ICH Q5D text specifies that when assessing stability during production culture, at least two time points should be tested—cells with minimal passage and cells cultured beyond the cell age limit for production—this is the two-time point principle comparing production cells (MCB) and EOPC (Source). Additionally, since ICH Q5A(R1), each WCB has been required to undergo one cycle analysis using LIVCA cells (Source).

In practice, the biggest risk of failure at this stage is sterility failure during filling — even a single microbial contamination invalidates the entire bank (Source). ⚠️ Sterility is a non-negotiable requirement, as hundreds of vials containing a unique clone can be thrown away entirely after a single contamination. Meanwhile, the weaknesses most often pointed out by developers are insufficient raw material CoA and documentation, as well as generating data to convincingly prove the monoclonality of bank cells. In fact, this is reported to be the most difficult part for developers in cell banking (Source).

Variations by Product Type — This is where this step takes shape.

This section applies most directly to mAbs/recombinant proteins — the two-layer CHO-based MCB/WCB is the industry standard. Cell-based vaccines use the same two-tier structure as continuous cell line banks such as Vero, MDCK, and EB66, but the safety burden is high due to the tumorigenicity/oncogenicity and foreign agent testing unique to continuous cell lines and are much more extensive, and as seen earlier, one MCB is large enough to supply hundreds of thousands of ampoules (Source).⚠️

In viral vector/allogeneic CGT, a separate viral seed-lot (node 1.3) is required in addition to MCB/WCB of HEK293 or stable producer/packaging cell line, resulting in a double banking structure of “cytosubstrate bank + vector seed”. In autologous CGT, the MCB concept itself virtually collapses. Unlike homologous processes that start from cell bank vials with known quality and predictable process behavior, the starting material in the autologous process is derived from patients, so each batch is different and has great variability (Source). ⚠️ Autologous products are manufactured in parallel scale-out for each patient without a stock bank. Finally, in mRNA-LNP, there is no producer cell bank at all — the equivalent of the “cell bank” is transferred to the MCB/WCB of the E. coli host for plasmid amplification (node ​​1.3), and the actual mRNA synthesis (IVT) is cell-independent, displacing this step entirely.

Current practices and recent changes

Current standard practices are summarized as GMP two-tier banking with FiH triggering, MCB full characterization + WCB abbreviated qualification, meteorological LN2 dual location storage, and CDMO/laboratory outsourcing of characterization and adventitious agent testing. The biggest change over the past three years has been the modernization of virus safety testing methods. ICH Q5A(R2) updated the virus screening test method to include PCR and NGS. The key point is that while PCR is a targeted method that detects a known sequence, NGS is a hypothesis-free approach that does not require prior knowledge of the sequence (Source). More specifically, NGS was introduced as a risk-based alternative to conventional in vivo, in vitro, and PCR tests in ICH Q5A(R2) in November 2023, and was adopted by the FDA (January 2024) and EMA (June 2024), and non-targeted NGS can replace in vivo infectivity tests by detecting a wide range of unknown and unexpected viruses. Presented (Source).⚠️ In addition, the introduction of rapid microbiological method (RMM) and single-use aseptic dispensing and automatic freezing are improving banking throughput and contamination risk.

The remaining limitations are also clear. The data requirements for replenishment WCBs are identified as problematic even on solid grounds; neither the US nor the EU guidance specifically sets out the data requirements applicable to the manufacture of replenishment WCBs (Source). In this regulatory vacuum, health authorities (HAs) increasingly began requiring comparability to be demonstrated with at least one commercial-scale lot (Source). Conclusive proof of monoclonality still remains a fundamental challenge (Source), and verification and standardization of NGS are still incomplete (Ph.Eur. 2.6.41 High Throughput Sequencing chapter is in draft stage). Self-CGT is structurally subject to reproducibility risk due to the absence of a bank.

Issue:There are three points where the sources diverge. First, regarding Can NGS completely replace conventional in vivo foreign virus testing, regulatory documents (ICH Q5A(R2), FDA, EMA) officially recognize non-targeted NGS as an in vivo alternative, but practical verification and standardization are still maturing (Ph.Eur. 2.6.41 draft) and adoption is dependent on risk assessment for each product and context of use, so full replacement is not automatically guaranteed — time lag between regulatory “acceptance” and actual review practices. It’s a problem. Second, in terms of what level of data is needed for supplementary WCB, the glottal guidance is read as sufficient for growth and viability evaluation, but HA increasingly requires comparative equivalence of commercial-scale lots in practice, resulting in a discrepancy between the documented minimum requirements and actual approval practice. Third, regarding how to sufficiently prove monoclonality, the traditional approach (documenting marginal dilution cloning history and probability) and NGS-based clonality evaluation coexist, but clonality is not an absolute proof but a high-probability guarantee, so consensus on which combination of evidence is “sufficient” is still in progress.Unresolved questions: (1) Because the ICH Q5D original PDF is not parsed into binary, the verbatim definition of MCB/WCB (“aliquot of a single pool …”) is obtained only from secondary sources and is not confirmed to the level of verbatim citation of the regulatory text (however, the genetic stability 2-point principle is secured from the EMA Q5D original text). (2) Quantitative data on the incidence of genetic instability (e.g. transgene silencing, copy number drift) by host and cell line, and accurate cell number and animal model requirements for tumorigenicity testing were not secured. (3) The minimum number of regulatory bank vials is not a fixed number, but is based on business demand and varies from source to source.

1.3 Nucleic-acid & viral seed starting materials

Role and Scope — “Non-cellular” starting materials

While the previous node 1.2 dealt with cell substrates (master/working cell banks, MCB/WCB), this section deals with non-cell-line starting materials, which exist alongside them but have a completely different nature. There are three main branches. (a) GMP-grade plasmid DNA (pDNA), (b) DNA template for in-vitro transcription (IVT) used for mRNA production, and (c) virus master/working seed-lot system for vaccine production. Location on the process map is the highest, ‘foundational’ level, establishing the molecular-level inputs consumed before any unit process begins. Purified pDNA is transferred to triple transfection of viral vectors (node ​​9.2) or as DNA vaccine raw material, linearized IVT template is transferred to mRNA manufacturing (node ​​9.1), and qualified viral seeds are transferred to classical vaccine USP (node ​​9.3).

What you must understand here is that product modality dependence is extremely large. In the mAb/recombinant protein process, the starting material is a cell bank, so this section virtually stays on the periphery. In contrast, in mRNA, viral vector/cell gene therapy (CGT), and classical vaccines, the molecule defined by this section is the ‘heart’ of the process. In other words, even if the word “starting material” is the same, its weight is completely different depending on the product being made.

How it works — a three-pronged mechanism

(A) GMP plasmid DNA manufacturing.The big picture is a chain that starts with microbial fermentation and ends with purification. The plasmid manufacturing workflow for IVT templates was designed using engineered E. coli culture → cell harvest → alkaline lysis and neutralization → clarification → chromatographic operation to remove host cell proteins, lipids, gDNA, and process-derived impurities (Source). If we solve the principle, E carrying a plasmid. coli is amplified by high-density fed-batch fermentation, the cells are destroyed with NaOH/SDS, the genomic DNA is denatured through alkali lysis, neutralized with potassium acetate, cell debris is removed by centrifugation/depth/microfiltration, and then usually 2 to 3 orthogonal chromatographies (anion exchange AEX, hydrophobic interaction HIC, size exclusion SEC, etc.) are used to identify the host. Removes RNA, gDNA, protein, and endotoxin. The ultimate goal of this purification is to maximize the concentration of the supercoiled (covalently closed circular CCC) form, as this form is expected to have the highest titer and transfection efficiency (Source).⚠️(B) IVT template preparation. Rather than using the purified plasmid as is, it must be cut downstream of the poly(A) tail with a restriction enzyme and linearized to become a run-off transcription template for T7 RNA polymerase. In this case, Type IIS restriction enzymes (e.g. BspQI) are preferred because they cut ‘outside’ the recognition sequence and preserve the integrity of the poly(A) tail without a scar (Source). ⚠️ Proper linearization can be confirmed by checking that a single dominant linear species is present and that there are no residual supercoils or open-circular forms. Verify (Source). This step is important because the ratio of supercoiled to open-loop conformations determines the concentration of initiation-competent template, which determines the initial IVT kinetics and thus the titer trajectory and time to plateau (Source). Additionally, the formation of double-stranded RNA (dsRNA), which threatens downstream mRNA quality, depends not only on RNA polymerase/IVT conditions but also on the quality of the linearization template (Source). If the starting plasmid is poorly purified, the remaining impurities can be transcribed by T7 RNAP as an alternative template to increase dsRNA (Source), and in fact, linearized plasmids purified by chromatography (CIMmultus C4 HLD monolith column) produce dsRNA below the 1% threshold without additional polishing. lowered (Source).(C) Virus seed-lot system. This is a two-step system whose logic is parallel to the cell bank (MCB/WCB) but applied to the ‘virus itself’. A master seed lot (MSL) is established from well-characterized parent seeds, which are propagated under defined conditions to establish a working seed lot (WSL), and then production batches are initiated. The formal definition of a WSL is “a microbial culture derived from a master seed lot to specific passages and used in one or more production batches” (Source). The number of passages is strictly limited to prevent genetic drift and reversion to virulence, and viral vaccine production is usually not carried out beyond 5 passages from the master seed lot (Source).

Key parameters and ingredients — what to measure and what to put in

Materials and equipment for pDNA are E of the endA⁻/recA⁻ series. coli host (e.g. DH5α), fed-batch fermentation, alkaline lysis reagent (NaOH/SDS, potassium acetate for neutralization), AEX/HIC/SEC resin, and UF/DF (ultrafiltration/diafiltration). Quality characteristics (CQA) include supercoiling ratio (%CCC, homogeneity), concentration (A260 UV), A260/A280 ratio, residual host gDNA and RNA, host cell protein (HCP), endotoxin, bioburden/sterility, and identity (full-length sequencing/restriction enzyme mapping). Among these, residual gDNA is tested by qPCR. Quantify (Source).⚠️ Grades are divided into three levels: research → high-quality → GMP (see quality and regulation section below).

Getting a sense of scale is key to understanding this verse. Supplying 1 billion doses of an mRNA vaccine may require the production of more than 1 kg of DNA (Source), and the mass yield of the IVT reaction is typically 10 to 40 µg RNA per 1 µg of template based on laboratory standards, and 100 to 200 µg RNA per 1 µg of template with optimized commercial kits, approximately 20 to 30 times that of the template. (Source).⚠️ The key management values for the IVT template are linearization completeness, residual superhelix, and dsRNA 1% threshold. On the virus seed side, the virus strain, cell substrate for growth, titer, and number of passages (≤5) are the main variables, and especially in the early stages of development, the sample itself is subject to volume constraints (see below).

Quality and regulation — areas where standards are not yet set in stone

The most important regulatory framework is USP General Chapter <1040> (‘Quality Considerations for Plasmid DNA as a Starting Material for Cell and Gene Therapies’), which at the draft stage in November 2023 defines plasmids as ‘starting materials’ for CGT and excludes vaccines, direct therapeutics, and drug substances from the scope. Above all, the draft itself “An established quality standard for plasmids for CGT does not yet exist. “, he admitted (Source). In other words, regulatory standards in this area are in the process of being formed.

This gap in standards is reflected in the grading system. pDNA is divided into three grades: research, ‘high-quality’, which is an intermediate grade that is close to GMP but does not completely reach it, and GMP (Source).⚠️ The problem is that this ‘high-quality’ grade has no universal standard, so each supplier defines it differently with its own standards (Source).⚠️ (The fields in this section are It is stated that the exact numerical acceptance criteria such as the lower limit of supercoil percentage, endotoxin EU/mg, and residual gDNA ng/mg have not been confirmed, so this part is left as an open question below.)The quality logic for virus seeds is virtually isomorphic to that of cell banks. The characterization logic of the master/working virus seed stock (MVSS/WVSS) is equivalent to that of the cell bank, but adjustments are made to suit the biological properties of the virus-based product (Source). However, there is one practical limitation unique to virus seeds: sample availability—high titer seed stocks can be volume limited early in development (Source). In addition, seed tests include identity/sequence, foreign agent/sterility/mycoplasma, genetic stability/virulence reversion, and phenotype (neurovirulence of live vaccines, etc.), and with ICH Q5A(R2) (revised in 2023), NGS is being accepted as a replacement for in vivo animal testing in testing for foreign viruses.

Differences by product type — same node, different weight

For mAb/recombinant proteins, this section is almost non-applicable. The starting material is a producer cell bank (1.2), and pDNA appears only briefly in transient transfections, which are rare in commercial use. In mRNA-LNP, on the contrary, this is the core of the cutting process. Since the linearized pDNA is the IVT template, in effect the ‘template DNA’ is the starting material, and the quality of the pDNA directly determines the quality of the mRNA — the dsRNA example seen above clearly shows this direct connection. In viral vector/CGT, plasmids (2-3 constructs for AAV, 3-4 constructs for lentivirus) serve as the starting material for temporary transfection, and mass demand in the g to kg range creates a supply bottleneck. Interestingly, in the early stages, even in the production of GMP vectors such as AAV, there is a practice that plasmids do not necessarily have to be GMP and high-quality grades are widely used (Source). ⚠️ In classic vaccines, the viral seed-lot system (MSL/WSL) is the main starting material and pDNA is not involved. One thing to make clear is that in the case of DNA vaccines, the plasmid is not the starting material but the active drug product itself, so it is outside the scope of USP <1040>.

Practices and recent changes — bottlenecks and attempts to circumvent them

One sentence that runs through this section is “pDNA manufacturing capacity is a structural bottleneck”. The evidence is multi-layered. GMP-grade pDNA E. in large stainless steel bioreactors. coli fermentation, a process that is inherently slow, expensive, limited in capacity, and prone to batch failure (Source). In the aftermath, it was even assessed that more than half of the world’s pDNA manufacturing capacity may be required for a single COVID-19 mRNA vaccine (Source), and contract manufacturers (CDMOs) that can supply GMP-grade pDNA have accumulated long waiting lists and significant backlogs (Source). Practical lead times are also long, so GMP-grade custom pDNA often needs to be ordered at least a year in advance (Source).⚠️

The response in recent years has roughly been in three directions. The first is expansion of manufacturing capacity, and the second is cell-free/enzymatically synthesized DNA to bypass the fermentation itself. Touchlight’s doggybone DNA (dbDNA), a representative example, is a cell-free enzymatic plasmid alternative without bacterial sequences and antibiotic resistance genes, and is known to have secured the world’s first cell-free DNA GMP license in 2025 following FDA Drug Master File acceptance in 2022 (Source). ⚠️ Third is the start of standardization, which includes the previously seen USP <1040> draft (2023) and seed. This includes ICH Q5A(R2), which accepts NGS for characterization. However, there are also clear unresolved issues - lack of harmonization of unified high-quality grades, unverification of kg-level scale-up of synthetic DNA, control of residual dsRNA, and long-term verification of genetic stability and restoration of virulence of seeds.

Issue

The data diverges at three points. First, does the plasmid used in viral vector GMP production itself have to be GMP?On the one hand, even in the production of GMP vectors such as AAV (especially in the early stages), there is a practice that plasmids do not necessarily have to be GMP and high-quality is sufficient (Source),⚠️ On the other hand, regulatory and industry standards require Phase 3/commercial starting material plasmids to be GMP and USP <1040> attempts to standardize this. This is a gray area of ​​the phase-appropriate approach, a trade-off between the speed and cost of early clinical trials and late-stage regulatory requirements. Second, does cell-free/enzymatic synthetic DNA actually solve the bottleneck? Vendors (Touchlight, etc.) claim that it does, but plasmids are still the overwhelming standard and the kg-level commercial scale-up and regulatory history of synthetic DNA has not yet been verified, so it is strongly in the nature of vendor marketing — the performance of a single vendor should not be treated as a settled fact. Third, can the ‘high-quality (GMP-like)’ grade itself be trusted? In practice, it is widely used as an initial clinical starting material without the lead time and cost of GMP, but there is no universal standard, so definitions differ from supplier to supplier, making comparison and audit difficult, and USP <1040> explicitly acknowledges the absence of the standard. It can be seen that the market has temporarily filled the gap in regulatory standards.Unresolved Questions: (1) The exact numerical acceptance criteria for pDNA CQA (lower supercoil % limit, endotoxin EU/mg, residual gDNA ng/mg, etc.) are set forth in USP <1040> Draft·Ph. Eur. 5.14 The original figures could not be confirmed (secondary sources only list items). (2) Specific upper passage limits for each virus type (yellow fever/measles/influenza, etc.) and quantitative standards for specific tests for each seed (neurovirulence/viscerotropism) are not confirmed in the original text of the WHO TRS. (3) The GMP scale actual ratio of grams pDNA per gram mRNA (depending on reaction scale and yield variables) was only approximated by the laboratory protocol value (approximately 20 to 30 times).

1.4 Raw materials, media/buffers & supplier qualification

Role and Scope — The top-level gateway to everything the process consumes.

If we compare biopharmaceutical manufacturing to a long digestive system, this section is the entrance. This is the basic step to control ‘all material inputs consumed by the process’ excluding the cell substrate itself (1.1~1.2) and nucleic acid/virus seed (1.3), and specifically includes cell culture media components, process buffers, chromatography resins, single-use consumables, and process aids that come into contact with the product or intermediate (Source)⚠️. The starting point is the required specifications (grade, purity, animal origin) and candidate suppliers for each substance, and the output of this step is a ‘qualified lot’ and an ‘approved supplier list’ according to risk. Afterwards, all unit processes including upstream processes, downstream processes, and finished products consume this output as is. In other words, the material quality and lot-to-lot variation specified in this section virtually determine the reproducibility of downstream CQAs such as titer, impurities, and glycosylation, and are also the first line of defense to prevent the introduction of animal-derived hazards (TSE/BSE, foreign agents).

How it works — 7-step risk-based qualification

Qualification recognition roughly flows in the following order: ‘Substance definition/specification → risk-based classification → supplier selection/qualification → qualification testing → approval/shipment → continuous monitoring → change management’. First, the purpose, required grade, purity, and animal origin of each raw material are specified, and specifications and CoA (Certificate of Analysis) requirements are confirmed. Excipients should be of pharmacopeia grade or multi-compendial grade whenever possible.

The second step, risk stratification, is the intellectual focus of this section. Following the ICH Q9 thinking, substances are graded by likelihood × impact, that is, RPN, and media, animal-derived ingredients, resins, growth factors, and antibiotics are classified as the highest risk (RPN 16–25) group (Source)⚠️. In the cell and gene therapy (CGT) area, the 4-tier system of USP <1043> is used in addition. This system starts with Tier 1 (substances intended to be used as approved drugs, biological products, or medical devices, lowest risk), increases in risk to Tier 2 (ancillary material use), Tier 3 (research use not intended to manufacture biological products), and Tier 4 (produced for industrial or research use and may contain hazardous impurities), and the test scope is mapped to these tiers (Source).

Third, select and qualify suppliers. EU GMP Chapter 5 (5.27) requires the selection, qualification, approval, and maintenance of starting material suppliers, as well as their purchase and receipt, to be documented as part of the quality system (Source). The framework of practice is a written quality agreement, which sets specifications, change notification, audit rights, and CAPA responsibilities. In particular, it is essential to have a mechanism for suppliers to agree in advance before implementing changes that will affect GMP status or production and testing parameters, and the quality contract must clearly distinguish which changes are ‘subject to notification’ and which changes are ‘subject to prior approval’ (Source). Audits must be performed with an appropriate period and scope to completely and clearly evaluate GMP, and the repetition cycle is determined by the quality risk management (QRM) process (Source). The re-qualification cycle commonly used in the industry is on-site audit every 1–2 years for high risk, document audit every 2–3 years for medium risk, and CoA verification every 3–5 years for low risk (Source)⚠️, but as pointed out in the unresolved questions below, it should be noted that this figure is not a value fixed by regulation, but a customary value that varies depending on the company’s QRM.

Animal-derived substances are particularly difficult in the fourth qualification test stage. They require BSE/TSE country of origin certification, exotic virus panel testing, mycoplasma testing (PCR+culture), gamma irradiation or viral inactivation, on-site supplier audits (which are virtually mandatory), and whole lot traceability back to the animal source (Source)⚠️. The reason these layers of control are necessary is fundamentally because “it is very difficult to ensure that any animal-derived material does not contain any infectious agents” (Source). Moreover, even if the company does not use animal ingredients, there remains a risk of carryover of contaminants during the supplier’s processing stage. EMA’s TSE minimization guidance (EMA/410/01) also requires special caution when using materials derived from TSE-susceptible animals such as cattle, sheep, and goats, and ideally recommends using materials derived from non-animal or TSE-non-susceptible animals (Source).

In the final stage, approval, shipment, and monitoring, CoA review, incoming testing (identity, purity, sterility, endotoxin), trending, and the aforementioned re-qualification cycle are followed, and change management closes this cycle.

Key parameters and materials — media, trace elements, resin

Among the material categories, media are the most complex. Cell culture media alone contains 60–80 individual ingredients, each with its own unique supply chain, manufacturing process, and lot variation, with amino acids, vitamins, and trace metals accounting for most of the observed variation (Source)⚠️. Precise management of pH and osmolarity is essential for medium production, and the control parameters for each batch include weight, sealing integrity, charging, temperature, tank volume, fill pump %, input sequence, mixing time, impeller speed, dwell time, total production volume, and sterility assurance.

Trace elements are two-faced. At the target concentration, it has a positive effect on protein quality attributes such as cell growth, biotherapeutic efficacy, and glycosylation, but if impurities from incoming raw materials are introduced, the concentration may fall outside the acceptable range, which signals the need for tighter control for medium consistency (Source).Chromatography resins, especially Protein A resins, are materials that have dramatically different economic feasibility. They are expensive, costing thousands of dollars per liter, and although proven column lifetimes can support 100–200 cycles in clinical production, they are severely underutilized in practice, often being discarded after only 3–20 cycles (Source)⚠️. One of the physical backgrounds for this underutilization is the feed dependence of resin aging. A 50-cycle lifespan study confirmed that resins cycled in feeds with higher HCP/histone concentrations showed greater capacity loss (Source). In other words, the impurity load from upstream erodes the lifespan of the downstream resin.

Quality/Regulation — Changes in raw materials are CQA

The proposition that this clause regulates downstream CQA is not an abstract claim but a proven fact. There are reports that small amounts of metals, such as zinc, can affect the glycosylation pattern of mAb and directly reflect drug efficacy/efficacy (Source)⚠️, and more importantly, it was confirmed in a primary study that even for the same CHO clone, titers and N-glycan profiles were statistically significantly different in different media — in one medium (EX-CELL 325) Many glycans, including G0F·G1F·G2·G2F·G2S2, were significantly different (Source). In the same context, even if the specifications are the same, if the supplier is different, the cell response, growth pattern, and experimental results may vary due to the different media composition, so specifications alone do not guarantee fungibility (Source). This is why media should be treated not as a simple ‘consumable’ but as a substance that defines CQA.

Governing standards overlap in several layers. Upstream raw material, GMP, and quality risk management principles are governed by ICH Q7/Q9/Q10/Q11, and supplier management and consignment activities are governed by EU GMP Chapters 5·7 and Annex 16. Animal-borne risks are defined in EMA/410/01(rev.3) and Ph. Eur. 5.2.8, material grades and tests are in accordance with USP <1043>, <1024>, <1083> and Ph. Eur. 5.2.12, ISO 20399 is responsible. Among these, Ph. Eur. 5.2.12 specifies biological activity, identity, impurities (including residual HCP/DNA), mycoplasma, virus contamination, and water quality as quality standards for biological raw materials for cell and gene therapy production, and, if not specified, requires sterile or terminally sterilized production under sterile conditions (Source)⚠️.

Differences by product type

This section applies most directly to mAb/recombinant proteins — CD media, buffer, Protein A resin, and single-use are the key raw materials, and how to manage media lot variation to control glycosylation is the central axis. When we move to CGT (cell/gene therapy), the terminology begins to diverge (US ‘ancillary materials’ vs EU ‘raw materials’; see issues below). In CGT, the proportion of high-risk biological substances such as cytokines, growth factors, enzymes, and transfection reagents is large, and crucially, many CGT products do not undergo final sterilization, so securing the sterility of raw materials and robust sterility testing becomes much more important than mAb manufacturing (Source)⚠️. In mRNA-LNP, high-purity chemical and enzyme raw materials such as enzymes (RNA polymerase, capping enzyme), nucleotides, cap analogs, and ionized lipids instead of media and cell substrates, as well as their supplier qualifications, are the dominant concerns. In classical vaccines, there is a history of using fertilized eggs and animal-derived ingredients, so the pressure to control TSE and foreign agents and to convert to animal-derived-free is especially high. Overall, the proportion of ‘animal-derived hazards’ and ‘high-risk biological raw materials’ increases depending on the product type.

On the excipient side, a multi-pharmacopoeia strategy is a practical tip. Ph. Eur. Standardizing a method typically also satisfies USP–NF·JP requirements because it applies the most stringent acceptance criteria, resulting in easier multi-country regulatory submissions (Source)⚠️.

Practices and recent changes

The current standard is risk-based (ICH Q9) stratification + supplier audit + CoA/receipt testing + requalification cycle, and the transition from serum/animal-derived media to chemically defined (CD)/animal-derived-free (AOF) media has been extensive for safety and reproducibility. However, there is a hidden paradox in this transition that will be discussed in a later issue.

Supply chain vulnerabilities have been a hot topic in recent years. U.S. drug shortages in 2024 will be the highest since ASHP began tracking them in 2001, and single-source items like reagents are still common, meaning manufacturing can turn into a “big problem in a day/week” (Source). The bottleneck wasn’t just APIs — during COVID, less-noticed consumables like glass, syringes, cardboard, and label backing paper were turning out to be real supply bottlenecks (Source). In response to this, as of 2025, companies are increasing safety stocks and adopting dual sourcing for key raw materials. In fact, there is an observation that those that relied on a single source (e.g., a single Indian API producer) suffered production disruptions, while those with dual sources from the EU and Asia absorbed the shock (Source)⚠️. In addition, FMEA-based critical material selection, regionalization, government-level supply chain initiatives, and strengthening spectral-based incoming identity and trace element characterization such as NIR are the trends of the past three years.

Issue

Issue:Sources diverge on three points. First, the direction of the net effect of CD/AOF media on process ‘consistency’. From a regulatory and safety perspective, CD medium is a standard advancement that increases definition by eliminating the risk of intrinsic fluctuations, TSE, and extraneous agents in serum. However, on the other side, it is pointed out that trace raw material fluctuations, which serum was masked by thousands of components, are revealed in CD medium and their impact is amplified at high productivity, making the process more sensitive to trace fluctuations (Source)⚠️. It is a trade-off with different conclusions depending on whether ‘consistency’ is viewed as safety/justice or robustness against small changes. Second, in the actual number of reuse cycles of resin, there is a wide gap between the physical capacity of the proven column life (100–200 cycles) and conservative disposal practices in practice (3–20 cycles), and aging variation due to feed composition and concerns about cross-contamination of multiple items lead to conservative disposal. Third, the identity of the US ‘ancillary material’ and EU ‘raw material’ terms. Although they are used interchangeably in practice, ‘ancillary’ implies the premise that it does not remain in the final product, so the conceptual framing may be different and classification and testing expectations may be misaligned, which becomes a practical risk when submitting to multiple countries.Unresolved Questions: - Quantitative range of trace element control (permissible upper/lower limit ppb/ppm) and size of CQA impact for each individual element — The primary data is behind the subscription application note, so the figures at the verification level cannot be confirmed. - The original text of the ISO 20399 and BioPharm ‘Quality Standard’ article is blocked with 403, and the specific provisions and terms of ISO 20399 can only be checked at the secondary snippet level. - The requalification audit cycle (high 1–2 years/middle 2–3 years/low 3–5 years) is only an industry-wide value and is not a fixed number by regulation, and varies depending on the company’s QRM (regulatory binding force of the figure is not confirmed).

2. Upstream processing (USP)

Growth of the producer organism and generation of the crude product-containing harvest (Drug Substance precursor).

2.1 Cell culture & fermentation

The Heart of Production: What Do These Steps Do?

Cell culture and fermentation are the ‘biological production engines’ of the upstream process (USP). One Working Cell Bank (WCB) vial qualified in the previous step (node ​​1.2) is thawed and received as an inoculum, the production cells or microorganisms are multiplied in large quantities, the target product (recombinant protein/antibody or virus/vector) is expressed and accumulated, and the crude culture (harvest) with the product dissolved is passed on to the next harvest and purification step (node ​​2.3). The starting point is ‘WCB vial + medium + gas’, and the output is ‘culture broth, a precursor to raw drug substance (DS)’.

This step is crucial to the overall process for two reasons. First, titer — how much product is extracted per reactor volume is the primary driver of process economics. Second, product quality is imprinted here — many key quality attributes (CQAs), such as glycosylation, charge variants, aggregation and fragmentation, are effectively determined by culture conditions. Practice is broadly divided into two categories. One is mammalian cell culture (CHO-based mAb/recombinant protein), which has the highest CQA sensitivity, and the other is microbial fermentation (E. coli/yeast), which makes non-glycosylated proteins quickly and inexpensively. For reference, this section (biology, medium, culture mode, metabolic control) and node 2.2 (bioreactor engineering, scale-up) are complementary pairs that view the same reactor from different perspectives.

How it works: From seed train to harvest

Seed train.Cultivation begins with thawing one vial of WCB. The volume and number of cells are expanded step by step (N-x, N-2, N-1) by sequentially going through shake flask → rocking bag → seed bioreactor to reach the inoculum density required by the production bioreactor. This expansion process itself sets the starting point for the subsequent production period.Three types of culture modes. Methods for producing products are broadly divided into three.

  • Batch is the simplest mode in which all nutrients are already present at the time of inoculation and there is no additional supply thereafter. When nutrients are used up, growth reaches its limit and ends early, resulting in low titer (Source).⚠️
  • fed-batch is the industry standard and mainstay of mAb production today. Concentrated feed is distributed and supplied at various times during the culture to prevent nutrient deficiency, thereby extending the culture period and increasing final productivity, and the product continues to accumulate in the reactor (Source). ⚠️ The culture is usually cultured for 12 to 21 days, the highest viable cell density (VCD) is about 5 to 25 × 10⁶ cells/mL, and the titer is about 3 to 8 g/L. It’s at the same level (Source).⚠️
  • Perfusion is a method of continuously injecting fresh medium and continuously extracting only the cell-free harvest while retaining cells in the reactor with a cell retention device such as ATF/TFF (Source). ⚠️ It can reach ultra-high density (VCD 30~100×10⁶ cells/mL) while operating in steady-state. It usually maintains a much higher cell density than fed-batch diets (5~25×10⁶) (Source). ⚠️ There is also a quality advantage in that the product is less exposed to protease or cell lysate due to the shorter residence time.Process intensification.The strategy that has recently attracted the most attention is to operate the N-1 seed bioreactor through perfusion to create a high-density inoculum and inoculate it into an ‘intensified fed-batch diet’. In this way, the initial inoculation density of the production bioreactor can be increased to ≥2 The logic of this intensification is clear, because the leverage to increase upstream volumetric productivity is ultimately threefold: (1) increased viable cell density, (2) improved cell-specific productivity, and (3) increased bioreactor utilization (Source). According to vendor estimates, N-1 flow-through can reach densities approximately 20 times higher than traditional batch N-1, increasing volumetric productivity by 2 to 10 times and reducing upstream costs by 20 to 60 percent without new equipment or large capital investments (Source).⚠️Medium/feed design. Chemical Definition (CD) The basal medium supplies amino acids, glucose, vitamins, trace elements, and lipids, and the concentrated feed replenishes the exhausted nutrients. The supply method is divided into bolus and continuous supply, and the supply amount is adjusted dynamically based on the glucose setpoint and optimized using design of experiment (DoE) and response surface method (RSM). Antibody glycosylation, such as fucosylation, galactosylation, and mannosylation, can be fine-tuned by selecting the medium composition and CHO host cell line, and a case of increasing the maximum cell concentration by 4 times and IgG titer by 8 times (up to 5.8 g/L) according to the fed-batch strategy has been reported (Source).⚠️

Metabolic control.Glucose and glutamine are the main carbon and nitrogen sources, but lactate and ammonia produced when they are metabolized inhibit growth. Therefore, by manipulating the temperature, pH, osmotic pressure, and chemical inducer levels, a ‘metabolic shift’ is induced to consume lactic acid (Source). ⚠️ In particular, temperature shift, which lowers the temperature from 37°C to 30-33°C when moving from the growth phase to the production phase, is a key manipulation to increase cell-specific productivity (qP) and viability.Microbial fermentation. E. coli and yeast are cultured in a high-density fed-batch manner. E. coli is the main host for recombinant proteins in the pharmaceutical industry because it is easy to culture, inexpensive media, and capable of high titers. However, many of the products accumulate in cells as inclusion bodies, requiring downstream refolding, and expression is usually induced by IPTG (Source). ⚠️ Glycerol, commonly used as a carbon source, has a lower cellular uptake rate than glucose, allowing cultivation at a high specific growth rate without accumulating toxic by-products. DO-stat fed-batch diet controls dissolved oxygen at a level of 20% (Source).⚠️ Microbial fermentation is characterized by a short incubation period of several days, inexpensive medium, and no saccharification.

Key process variables and materials

The critical process parameters (CPPs) governing cultivation and their optimal ranges are generally well documented. Temperature shifts from 37°C during the growth period to 30-33°C during the production period. pH is controlled at approximately 6.8 to 7.2, but sialylation of mAb increases as pH increases, reaching a maximum around pH 7.2 (Source). Dissolved carbon dioxide (pCO2) is optimal at about 10%, and if it goes higher than this, both cell growth and sialylation are inhibited, and there is a significant interaction effect between process pH and pCO2 that changes the charge variant/N-glycosylation pattern (Source). On the other hand, dissolved oxygen (pO2) had the smallest effect on cell physiology and product quality compared to pH·pCO2 (Source). In addition, osmotic pressure, agitation/shear, feed rate, glucose setpoint, and inoculation density are managed together.

It has also been demonstrated how far it is possible to push perfusion to its extreme. In CHO perfusion cultures combining high perfusion rates with a delayed temperature drop on day 6, the titer reached 16.19 g/L, the relative monomer content was maintained at 97.6%, and the highest VCD reached 63.0 × 10⁶ cells/mL on day 6 (Source). In addition, continuous perfusion, which flows the medium without interruption, was able to maintain cell density, titer, and quality at a higher level than intermittent perfusion (Source).

In terms of materials and equipment, consumables include WCB vials, chemical well base medium and concentrated feed, glucose, glutamine (GlutaMAX), antifoam, base (NaOH/bicarbonate), and CO2/O2 gas, and the reactor includes a stirred tank (STR) type bioreactor (single-use SU or stainless steel SS), cell support device for perfusion (ATF/TFF), and seed train (shake flask, locking). Back-seed bioreactor) is used. Here, PAT probes such as Raman, capacitance (biomass), and DO/pH are combined. The duration is about 12 to 21 days for fed-batch feeding, several weeks to several months for perfusion, and several days for microbial fermentation.

Quality and regulation: where CQA makes its mark

The cultivation stage is the ‘origin’ of many CQAs. At this stage, glycosylation (galactosylation·sialylation·fucosylation·high-mannose), charge variants (deamidation, C-terminal lysine), aggregation/monomer ratio, and clipping/fragmentation are determined, and the initial accumulation of process-derived impurities (HCP/residual DNA) resulting from cell lysis also begins here.

Much of how CPP is connected to CQA has been identified in primary literature. Process pH is positively correlated with sialylation (maximum around pH 7.2), and also affects the presence of mid-chain lysine residues and the deamidation rate (Source). Elevated pCO2 inhibits growth and sialylation and interacts with pH to change charge variant and N-glycosylation patterns (Source). The effect of pO2 is the smallest compared to these (Source).

How quality differs depending on the culture mode can also be confirmed through direct comparison (head-to-head). Perfusion cultures reduce intra-lot heterogeneity due to shorter residence times and reduced extracellular exposure (Source). In fact, the LDH measurement results showed that the exposure of the product to cell lysate was 7 to 10 times greater than that of fed-batch perfusion (Source). The resulting perfusion product had a higher proportion of neutral species, higher purity, and less half-antibody, which was due to reduced reactor residence time and extracellular exposure (Source). However, there is one important caveat — in the same comparison, glycosylation was similar in both modes (Source). In other words, the quality benefits of perfusion are focused on degradative properties (clipping/charge variation) that are sensitive to retention time, and do not necessarily apply to glycation, which is dependent on cellular metabolism.From a regulatory perspective, the frameworks that govern this phase are ICH Q8/Q11 (QbD, design space, and control strategy), Q6B (specification), Q5A (R2) (adventitious virus safety—culture is the epicenter of contamination risk), Q13 (continuous manufacturing), and the FDA PAT guidance. In particular, for continuous/once-through processes, ICH Q13 covers batch definition, residence time distribution (RTD) characterization, diversion of nonconforming products, and steady-state design space, and is required to describe management strategy, sensor network structure, and steady-state criteria when submitting regulations (Source). Since the cultivation stage is the source of several CQAs, the management strategy is centered on CMC city (3.2.S.2.2/2.4), monitors bioburden, mycoplasma, titers, and metabolites in-process, and closed/single-use systems and contamination control strategies (CCS) are applied.

Variations depending on product type

The intensity of application of this section varies greatly depending on the product type. This section is most directly applicable to mAb/recombinant proteins, where fed-batch/perfusion of CHO mammalian cells is the standard and has the highest sensitivity to CQAs, including glycosylation. Non-glycosylated proteins (insulin, etc.) are made through E. coli/yeast fermentation and are faster and cheaper, but require downstream refolding due to inclusion body formation and do not have the ability to saccharify.Vaccines change form. Egg-based (influenza) proliferates the virus in fertilized eggs rather than cell culture, and cell-based (Vero·MDCK·EB66) infects cells with the virus to obtain the ‘virus itself’ rather than ‘secreted protein’ as a product, so the goal is reversed (the recombinant antigen method is similar to the protein route). In viral vector/cell gene therapy (CGT), cultured cells are transiently transfected or vectors are produced using stable producer cell lines. The product is a virus, harvest includes cell lysis, and autologous CGT goes for scale-out rather than scale-up — that is, there is a culture step, but the goal (protein secretion → virus production) is reversed. Lastly, in mRNA-LNP, there is no cell culture or fermentation involved in product production. Since mRNA is synthesized by cell-free IVT, and the only ‘fermentation’ is upstream E. coli fermentation (node ​​1.3), which produces the pDNA template, this section is subject to disruption/displacement in the mRNA (cross-reference to node 9.1).

Current practices and recent changes

The current standard is the CHO platform fed-batch method using a chemically defined medium, and titers of 3 to 8 g/L for 12 to 21 days of culture have become routine, which is a result of about a 10-fold improvement over the past 20 years. There are three major changes over the past three years. First, process intensification — N-1 perfusion seeds were used to create a high-density inoculum, doubling the titer in intensive fed-batch (Source), adoption of perfusion and continuous processes was expanded, and ICH Q13 (2023) provided the regulatory framework for continuous manufacturing (Source). Second, PAT·Real-Time Control — In mature continuous PAT architectures, inline Raman-predicted glucose values ​​drive a PID controller to automatically adjust the glucose feed pump, making Raman the preferred inline technology for continuous metabolite monitoring in perfusion bioreactors (Source). Here, capacitance-based biomass sensing, soft sensors, mechanistic theory/hybrid model, and digital twin (Bioprocessing 4.0) are added.

The limits are also clear. Perfusion is hindered by complexity, cost, robustness burden, and large-scale medium consumption. There is scale dependence where CQA changes due to pCO2 accumulation during scale-up, and dynamic real-time control of glycosylation is still a challenge. Media lot volatility and connection/integration with continuous downstream processes also remain challenges, and the overall industry is still dominated by batch/fed-batch systems, so the adoption of continuous process is slow.

Issue

The sources diverge at three points. (1) Will perfusion become the next-generation standard replacing fed-batch?On the one hand, perfusion/N-1 intensification provides 2 to 10 times volumetric productivity, 20 to 60% reduction in upstream COGS, ultra-high density and excellent quality uniformity, and ICH Q13 opens the regulatory door. On the other hand, the incentive to switch is weak because the complexity of perfusion, media consumption, robustness, and cost are high, and the concentration of fed-batch diets can reach the 16 g/L range — adoption is dependent on product characteristics (stable/labile) and facility strategy. (2) Does perfusion improve overall quality? Perfusion is superior in many indicators such as clipping↓·charge variant uniformity↑·half-antibody↓, but the two modes of glycation were similar in the same head-to-head — residence time-dependent degradation and cell metabolism-dependent formulas have different mechanisms and cannot be generalized as ‘perfusion = superior quality’. (3) Should pCO2 be designated as a critical process variable (CPP)? Scale-up research supports criticality on the grounds that pCO2 inhibits growth and sialylation and interacts with pH, ​​but some working white papers believe that product-specific risk assessment is more appropriate than universal designation because criticality is dependent on the product, scale, and management strategy context.Unresolved Questions: ① The primary quantitative basis for detailed economic feasibility (equipment footprint, capital, COGS reduction rate) for each culture mode was not fully confirmed from free, accessible sources, and only the vendor (Repligen) estimate (20-60% cost reduction) was secured. ② Comparison of detailed parameters and saccharification ability of yeast (Pichia/Saccharomyces) fermentation, and quantitative magnitude of sugar chain shift (e.g., sialylation % change range) according to specific CPP changes were only partially secured. ③ The quantitative relationship between pCO2 accumulation/mixing/shear effects due to scale-up is under the jurisdiction of node 2.2 (bioreactor/scale-up), and only the CQA connection was confirmed in this section.

2.2 Bioreactor operation & scale-up

Role: The heart of the upstream process

The bioreactor is a unit operation that is the heart of the upstream process (USP). The production cell line prepared in the previous step (2.1) is mass-cultured in a controlled environment to produce a crude harvest containing the target product (antibody, recombinant protein, virus particle, etc.). The starting point is a verified inoculum, that is, N-1 culture, which is the last step of the seed train, and medium/feed, and the result of this step is a culture solution mixed with cells and cell debris, which becomes the raw material for downstream processes (clarification and purification). The tasks addressed in this section are two-pronged. One is operation — to stably supply the physical and chemical environment (dissolved oxygen, pH, temperature, shear, nutrient and metabolite homogeneity) required by cells at a laboratory scale, and the other is scale-up — to maintain the environment experienced by cells as equal as possible when transferring a process developed at a few liters to a commercial scale of thousands to tens of thousands of liters. Scale-up is a central activity in technology transfer and process characterization, and is particularly important from an end-to-end perspective because any misalignments propagate directly to downstream yields and quality attributes of the final product (CQA, e.g. glycosylation profile).

How it works: The fundamental dilemma of agitation and aeration and scale-up

The mainstream equipment is the stirred tank (STR). The impeller circulates the liquid to ensure homogeneity, and the sparger at the bottom injects bubbles to supply oxygen. Oxygen transfer is governed by the mass transfer equation OTR = k

La It’s awesome (Source).

The fundamental dilemma of scale-up is that it is physically impossible to simultaneously keep constant the various characteristic parameters that define the cellular environment when scaling up: (1) power per unit volume, P/V, (2) impeller tip speed, (3) kLa, (4) Reynolds number Re, and (5) mixing time. If you fix one thing, the rest will inevitably change ⚠️(Source). For example, if you scale up while keeping P/V constant, the impeller rotation speed decreases, but the terminal speed, circulation time, and kLa actually increase ⚠️(Source). In practice, the practice is to take P/V, the most widely used standard in the industry, as a starting point ⚠️(Source), check kLa as a secondary verification standard, and then adjust it repeatedly. (These three sentences are approximate practical descriptions based on technology blogs, so the level of confidence is low.)

As the scale increases, physical phenomena that were not visible at the bench scale become more prominent. First, the mixing time is prolonged, creating a nutrient/pH gradient between the feed injection point and the other side. If mixing takes tens of seconds, cells near the injection point momentarily experience much higher glucose concentrations. Second, the hydrostatic pressure at the bottom of the tall tank increases the partial pressure of dissolved CO2 above the bench scale, leading to accumulation of pCO2, which causes growth inhibition and changes in glycation (Source). Third, increasing agitation and sparging to meet increased oxygen demand increases shear damage. The key insight here is that the primary cause of damage to mammalian cells in agitated/sparging bioreactors is not the impeller, but interfacial shear when bubbles burst from the liquid surface (Source). Therefore, mitigation strategies also focus on promoting surface aeration, separating control oxygen spargers from CO2, air, and nitrogen ballast spargers, using microporous sintered spargers, and adding shear protection agents such as Pluronic F-68.

Since verification cannot be done by replicating a commercial scale reactor, scale-up verification is performed using a scale-down model. For example, a scaled-down model of a 2,000 L production reactor is typically a 10-15 L smaller reactor that operates to simulate the kLa, mixing time, and pH/DO control dynamics of the larger vessel (Source), and process characterization and abnormal variable studies are conducted in this scaled-down model.

Key parameters and materials

Key process parameters (CPPs) to be controlled during operation are dissolved oxygen (DO, typically 30-50% air saturation), pH (usually 6.8-7.2, controlled by CO2/base), temperature (approximately 36.5-37°C for mammals), agitation speed, sparging gas flow rate (vvm), and pCO2, whose accumulation must be managed. Among the more reliable quantitative standards for scale-up, typical P/V ranges from 1 to 5 W/m³ for mammalian cell cultures, 3 to 10 W/m³ for microbial fermentations, and commercial-scale reactors operate at P/V below 50 W/m³ to reduce adverse effects on cell health (Source). Impeller terminal speed is usually recommended to be maintained in the range of 0.6 to 1.8 m/s to avoid adverse effects on cell growth (Source). To get a feel for the oxygen transfer ability, when using pure oxygen as a sparging gas, a density of about 4×10⁶ cells/mL can be supported under the condition of kLa=1(1/h) ⚠️(Source). In addition, as shear-related thresholds, energy dissipation rate (EDR) of about 10⁴ W/m³ is used as a sub-lethal standard for most mammalian cells, bubble entrance velocity (GEV) is harmful when exceeding 60 m/s in CHO, and bubble wake eddy length is recommended to be maintained above 20 μm (same BPI data).

In terms of equipment, STR is the mainstream, and impellers (Rushton/Pitchblade/Marine), spargers (open pipes/drill holes/microporous sintering), DO·pH·temperature·pressure·mass flow meters are the basics, and recently, PAT sensors such as Raman and capacitance (live cell density) probes have been added. Container types are divided into reusable stainless steel (SS) and single-use bioreactor (SUB). The starting point is N-1 inoculum (which significantly increases cell density when enriched), growth medium, feed, antifoam agent, and shear protectant. The result of this step is a culture medium containing cells, and in the case of fed-batch mAb, the harvest titer reaches the level of several g/L. (Many of the detailed figures cited here come from education/technology blogs or vendor data, and it should be taken into account that they are approximate values that vary greatly depending on the process and equipment.)

Quality and Regulation

The items managed and tested at the bioreactor stage are sterility, absence of mycoplasma and foreign agents, cell viability, density, titer, and the effect of this stage on product CQA (glycation profile, charge variant, aggregate, etc.). In particular, pCO2 accumulation and pH gradients that occur during scale-up can change saccharification and charge variants, making them key targets for process characterization.
