The regulatory framework involves ICH Q8 (QbD, CPP/CQA·design space), Q11 (process development), Q5A(R2) (2023 revision, virus safety), and Q13 (continuous manufacturing).A significant change in terms of GMP concerns single-use systems. The 2022 revised EU GMP Annex 1 significantly expands single-use system requirements, requiring manufacturers to demonstrate that all product-contact single-use materials are compatible with the drug product and that extractables/liquables (E&L) risks have been systematically assessed (Source). In the United States, USP <665>, the extract test standard for plastic parts and systems for pharmaceutical manufacturing, has been enforced since May 2026 (Source). As a result, single-use bioreactors must characterize compounds that can migrate to the product from all plastics they come in contact with (bags, tubing, connectors, sensors, gaskets). Sterility assurance methods also vary depending on the type. SS requires CIP/SIP (cleaning/sterilizing in place) and cleaning validation, while SUB eliminates the burden of cleaning validation through gamma-ray pre-sterilization and manages integrity, particle, and E&L risks.

Differences by product type

For mAb/recombinant protein, fed-batch in a CHO suspension culture stirred tank is the modern standard, and maintenance of a homogeneous environment and P/V·kLa scale-up are well-established maturation platforms. Since the product is secreted throughout the culture, homogeneous conditions are aimed from start to finish.Virus/vector (CGT) is fundamentally different from this. By using a lot of attachment-dependent cells (Vero, HEK293, etc.), a microcarrier method that combines the advantages of suspension and attachment culture or a fixed bed/packed bed (e.g. iCELLis) bioreactor is used. Vero cells have been cultured in microcarriers in bioreactors since the 1960s and are now expanded to 6,000 L, achieving volumetric virus titers 100 times higher than Nunc Cell Factory or roller bottle monolayer cultures (Source). In addition, the virus vaccine process uses different parameter conditions (pH, temperature, DO, MOI) in the growth stage to first grow cells and the subsequent stage to infect and replicate the virus, which is in sharp contrast to the traditional recombinant protein process in which the product is secreted homogeneously with growth (Source). The shear sensitivity is also high, so hydrodynamic shear in the microcarrier can cause cell lesion effects, so large-scale process development takes a long time, and the scale is relatively small, so disposable STR/fixed layers are preferred.mRNA-LNP is a completely cell-free upstream process, and uses an enzyme-based in vitro transcription (IVT) reactor instead of a cell culture bioreactor. In this case, most of the traditional kLa, shear, and scale-up logic does not apply, and the reaction volume is much smaller. Classical vaccines (e.g., egg-culture influenza) use embryonated eggs or cell substrates, but a transition to cell culture-based vaccines such as MDCK suspension cells is in progress.

Practices and recent changes

The current standards are clear. mAb is dominated by CHO floating fed-batch stirred tank, and scale-up relies on the quantitative standard of ‘P/V start + kLa verification’. The main movements of recent years have been four-pronged.

First, process intensification. The N-1 perfusion seedtrain is the most common starting point, inoculating the production reactor at a density 5 to 10 times higher to shorten the production period. The reported performance is, depending on the case, an 8-fold increase in titer, or an average 85% titer increase and 132% space-time yield increase compared to conventional low-density inoculated fed-batch diet ⚠️(Source). However, these figures are specific case reports, so generalization requires caution.

Second, the spread of disposable conversion and hybrid continuous process. Third, PAT·digitization, a representative example is in-line monitoring based on Raman spectroscopy. In fact, Raman spectroscopy, combined with in-line information, was used as input to a glucose controller to control the glucose mass fraction of the fermentation, achieving process control quality similar to a statistical model approach with much less calibration effort ⚠️ (Source). Here, the flow of using soft sensors, digital twins, and CFD for scale-up/scale-down model design is added. Fourth, with regulatory harmonization, the Contamination Control Strategy (CCS) of ICH Q13, Q5A(R2), and Annex 1 governs the adoption of intensified and continuous processes and single-use.

The limitations are also clear. Disposable cannot replace large-capacity SS due to physical scale limitations. Currently, the largest commercially available SUB is around 4,000-6,000 L, while SS vessels typically reach over 25,000 L ⚠️ (Source). The cost structure also changes depending on scale and frequency. The capital cost of a single-use plant (e.g. 4×2,000 L SUB) is approximately $50-100M, which is significantly lower than the $200-500M of a SS plant (4×10,000 L) ⚠️(Source) However, as the number of annual batches increases (approximately 100-200 batches/year or more), the cumulative cost of non-depreciable disposable consumables increases. Beyond the capital savings of SS, SS has an advantage in total cost ⚠️(Source). Supply chain vulnerabilities were also revealed, with biocontainer lead times increasing from 4-6 weeks to 6-12 months in 2021-2022 as multiple vaccine manufacturers simultaneously increased demand during the COVID-19 pandemic ⚠️ (Source). These cost and supply chain figures should be read with the understanding that many of them are approximations based on technology blogs.

Issue:There are two differences of opinion on this section. The first is surrounding the correct baseline for scale-up. The engineering parameter-oriented perspective believes that equivalence across scales can be ensured by verifying kLa using P/V as a starting point (bioprocesstools, BPI). On the other hand, the ‘biology-first’ perspective points out that simply matching engineering parameters or equipment geometry does not necessarily guarantee biologically equivalent processes or consistent operational results (Source). This is because, at large scales, the gas-liquid mass transfer mechanism and flow regime change and local gradients become prominent, weakening the bench similarity assumption. Rather than being exclusive, the two positions converge in the reinterpretation that ‘parameters are not goals but indicators that describe cell needs,’ but the emphasis in practice is different. Second is the total cost of ownership of disposable vs. stainless steel. Disposable is advantageous in terms of capital cost, construction period, contamination risk, and flexibility (especially for clinical trials, high variety, and new modalities), and SS is advantageous in terms of total cost in blockbuster commercial production with high deployment frequency and large capacity (>10k L), and the 25k L class scale cannot be replaced by disposable. The optimal solution varies depending on scale, frequency, and product type.Open Questions: Quantitative performance benchmarks between specific vendor SUB models (rigorous independent comparison data not available); Quantitative scale-up rules for non-stirred reactors such as wave/rocking type, airlift, and fixed bed (only partially secured).

2.3 Harvest & clarification

Boundary unit manipulation connecting upstream and downstream

Harvest & clarification is a ‘boundary unit operation’ that connects cell culture/fermentation (node 2.1) and purification (downstream process, DSP). What the previous step delivers is a crude culture broth mixed with cells, cell debris, colloids, and lysates, and what this step produces is only a liquid containing the product, that is, harvested cell culture fluid (HCCF). This clarification liquid must have particles, turbidity, and bioburden low enough to pass directly through a capture chromatography (node ​​3.1, typically Protein A) column and a 0.2 µm sterilization filter.

This gateway determines two main things. The first is yield. Since the product can be caught in the cell cake or filter and lost, harvesting and clarification are the first steps that determine whether the product is recovered or discarded. The second is downstream burden. The quality of clarification (turbidity, residual cell debris, initial host cell protein (HCP)/host DNA load) determines subsequent chromatography column life, filter area, and difficulty in removing impurities. Conceptually, this step is a phase transition point that changes ‘particle-filled biological suspension’ into ‘homogeneous purified feedstock’, and it is a sensitive GMP gateway from a bioburden perspective because it involves open/semi-open operations to transfer culture media and replace filters.

Principle and flow: Multi-stage clarification that reduces turbidity in steps

The core framework is a multi-stage structure. Let’s get rid of the big things first. It proceeds in the following order: ‘First clarification (removal of whole cells and large debris) → 2nd clarification (protection of downstream filter by removal of colloidal and submicron debris) → 0.2 µm bioburden reduction filtration’, and the turbidity decreases stepwise as each stage passes. Typically, bioreactor harvest exceeds 2000 NTU, but drops to 20–100 NTU after the first filter, less than 10 NTU after the second filter, and less than 1 NTU after the 0.2 µm sterilization filter (Source). ⚠️

Centrifugation is a standard primary separation method on large scales (approximately 1000-2000 L or more). A continuous disk-stack centrifuge stacks conical disks closely to reduce the distance that cells must settle in the thin channel between disks, sediments and discharges cells, and continuously extracts a supernatant containing the product. The centrifugal acceleration of industrial disk-stacks is typically in the range of 5,000–15,000 g (cell harvest itself is usually lower, ~1,500 g to reduce cell damage), and scale-up is performed on the principle of keeping the ratio of the flow rate Q and the sigma factor Σ, which represents the sedimentation area, constant Q/Σ (Source). ⚠️ However, the actual flow within the disk-stack is not ideal, so a correction coefficient must be applied when predicting performance across scales using sigma theory. While the coefficient of laboratory centrifugation, which assumes two-phase flow, is 1.0, the disk-stack coefficient is about 0.4, and in fact, 0.27–0.31 was measured in the pilot equipment LAPX-404 and 0.55 in BTPX-305 (Source). Since colloids still remain in the centrifugal supernatant, it must be polished with a depth filter.Depth filtration uses a graded-density fiber matrix made of cellulose, diatomaceous earth (DE), and positively charged resin to collect particles through two mechanisms: ‘sieving (size exclusion)’ and ‘adsorption (charge)’. Primary depth filters have a nominal pore size of 2–10 µm (e.g., D0HC, C0HC grades) to remove whole cells and large debris, while secondary depth filters have a nominal pore size of 0.1–1 µm (e.g., ⚠️ In small and single-use processes, the entire clarification may be performed using only a depth filter without centrifugation.

If the product is intracellular (e.g. AAV, inclusion bodies, pDNA), clarification is preceded by cell lysis. Cells are broken by chemical lysis (e.g. Triton In addition, there are a series of intensified and alternative technologies such as continuous harvesting through cell retention devices (ATF/TFF) for perfusion, tangential flow depth filtration (TFDF), flocculation, single-use fluidized bed centrifugation, and acoustic sedimentation.

Key parameters and materials: How to determine the filter area

The size (area) of the depth filter is calculated after measuring throughput (L/m²) per unit area through a small-scale experiment until the filter is clogged and the pressure reaches the end point (usually 1 bar/15 psi). Area A (m²) is the batch volume divided by (throughput ⚠️ The operating flux is usually 50–150 LMH, and the manufacturing standard is about 100 LMH, but lowering it to 100 LMH instead of 250 LMH and flowing it slowly increases the filter capacity by 20–40% (Source). ⚠️Capacity greatly depends on the harvest status. In CHO mAb harvest under standard conditions (survival rate exceeding 80%), it is 80–150 L/m² for the first time and 100–250 L/m² for the second time, but at high density (20–40 It rapidly decreases (Source). ⚠️ This difference in ‘clarification difficulty’ is also evident in actual measurements. When processing a harvest that is difficult to clarify with the same

The key risk on the centrifugal side is shear in the feed zone. It must be managed so as not to damage cells. LDH released is used as an indicator of cell damage, and the Kolmogorov eddy length is kept below 22 µm with the goal of preventing cell damage (Source). Materials and equipment include disk-stack centrifuge (stainless steel or single-use sealed type), depth filter cartridge/pod (cellulose, diatomaceous earth, positively charged resin; e.g. Millistak+ D0HC/C0HC (primary), TFF/ATF·TFDF membrane and nuclease (Benzonase) are used. The challenge of the input broth has expanded, with modern culture processes reaching well beyond the traditional 20×10/mL for CHO and over 150×10/mL for cells such as PER.C6, and the high solids content of these harvests (≤40%) exposes the limitations of both centrifugation and filtration techniques (Source). ⚠️

Quality and Regulation: Proactively Lowering Initial Impurity Loads

There are three quality and safety attributes that depend on this step. (1) clarity/turbidity and residual particles (manage to avoid clogging downstream columns and 0.2 µm filters), (2) bioburden/endotoxins (this is an open operation point, so microbial contamination control is important), and (3) initial load of process-derived impurities (HCP/host DNA). The core cause and effect is as follows. High shear and cell lysis in the centrifugal feed zone releases submicron particles and intracellular components such as host DNA, HCP, and protease, increasing the downstream impurity burden and the risk of product degradation. So shear minimization becomes part of the quality strategy (Source).

In the opposite direction, positively charged depth filters or coagulation adsorb and precipitate DNA and HCP, preemptively lowering the downstream burden. Treatment of high-density harvests (20–30 While DNA was reduced by 90%, HCP did not change significantly, and product recovery and quality were not affected for the three mAbs (Source). In the case of pDADMAC aggregation, it is reported that throughput was increased by up to 5 times in a process without centrifugation, DNA in Protein A eluate was lowered by 1.5–3.1 log, and HCP was reduced by up to 12.7 times (evaluated at harvest below 24 × 10⁶ cells/mL) ([Source] (https://www.bioprocessintl.com/filtration/flocculation-and-filtration-improving-mab-harvest-clarification-efficiency-from-high-density-cell-cultures)). ⚠️

From a regulatory perspective, harvesting and fining are not specified in a single ICH document but are integrated within the control strategy. ICH Q8/Q11 provides a framework for setting turbidity, yield, and bioburden as process controls, ICH Q5A(R2) From a virus safety perspective, harvest becomes a representative hold/sample point for adventitious virus testing, and ICH Q13, which addresses continuous manufacturing, requires steady state, residence time distribution (RTD), batch definition, and nonconformance branching for continuous and integrated clarification. Added to this are filter integrity testing practices (PDA TR26/TR45, etc.) and extractables and leachables from single-use depth filters (E&L, USP <665>/<1665>). Typical failure modes are early pressure rise and breakthrough due to filter fouling/clogging, submicron particle generation and downstream filter fouling due to centrifugal excessive shear, and yield loss caused by negative charge viruses binding to positive charge filters.

Differences by product type: What you ‘take’ changes

The standard framework in this section applies most directly to secreted mAb/recombinant proteins. The CHO culture medium is clarified by disk-stack centrifugation and two-stage depth filtration, the cells are discarded, and the supernatant containing the product is taken. In fact, it is confirmed by primary literature that the standard approach for mammalian cell culture broth clarification is ‘a combination of centrifugation and depth filtration’, and in high-density cultures (exceeding 30

In microbial fermentation (E. coli/yeast) the goal is reversed. If the product is inside the cells, such as inclusion bodies or periplasm, the cells are ‘concentrated’ by centrifugation/MF-TFF to take the cake, which is biomass containing the product, and the cells are disrupted by high-pressure homogenization, and then the inclusion bodies are recovered by centrifugation again. In other words, the solid content is taken, not the supernatant.

In plasmid DNA (pDNA) production, E. coli is harvested by centrifugation or microfiltration TFF (MF-TFF) and then subjected to alkaline lysis. Using 0.1–0.5 N NaOH (pH ~ 12) and 0.1–0.2% SDS, the detergent dissolves the cell wall and the alkaline environment denatures the genomic DNA while leaving the supercoiled plasmid intact, and then the lysate, which is highly viscous, sensitive to shear, and contains many suspended aggregates, is specially purified (Source). ⚠️ This is a fundamentally different challenge from mAb harvest, which takes the supernatant as is.

In mRNA-LNP (Node 9.1), the product (mRNA) is synthesized through cell-free in vitro transcription (IVT), so there is no cell harvesting or purification itself. This section is disrupted and replaced, and only the upstream pDNA template requires purification.In Viral Vector/CGT (Node 9.2), strategies vary depending on the charge and location of the product. Since AAV is not secreted from cells, it must be released through cell lysis (50 mM TRIS, 0.1% Triton L/m²)(Source). On the other hand, lentivirus is secreted, so it clarifies like a protein, but it is highly shear sensitive, so open-channel TFF and low shear are required. In particular, the charge of the filter is a problem. Negatively charged lentiviruses are poorly recovered in positively charged depth filters (low yield even without clogging), while adenoviruses are recovered well even in positively charged filters containing diatomaceous earth, and for influenza, 1.5 M NaCl high salt washing loosens the virus-filter bond, increasing the yield by about two times on average (Source).Classic Antivirus (Node 9.3) is also forked. For egg-based influenza, the allantoic fluid is first purified by low-speed zonal continuous centrifugation at approximately 4,000–5,000 g. The recovery rate is usually around 70%, and the allantoic fluid contains approximately 45 µg of hemagglutinin antigen per egg (Source). ⚠️ In cell-based production, 2–5 µm pore membranes are used in TFF mode to retain cells while simultaneously harvesting virus particles by passing them through the membrane using depth filtration, with TFDF being applied as an alternative to centrifugation (Source). ⚠️

Current practices and recent changes

The current standards are clear. Large-scale use is ‘continuous disk-stack centrifugation + two-stage depth filtration + 0.2 µm filtration’, and small-scale/single use is ‘depth filter-only filtration train’. This combination has not had any major skeletal changes since the early 2000s. The problem is that as upstream intensification increases high density (exceeding 20–30

The recent trend is three-pronged. First, with intensification/continuation, perfusion continuous harvest (ATF/TFF cell maintenance) and TFDF single device implement continuous clarification and are integrated with continuous DSP (ICH Q13 regulatory framework), and in the previous AAV case, TFDF showed the highest recovery rate for perfusion continuous harvest and lysate clarification (Source). Second, through coagulation/sedimentation pretreatment, the positively charged/stimulus-responsive polymer increases filter capacity several times and preemptively removes DNA/HCP (Source). Third, with new cell separator, targeting the ‘low biomass loading capacity and low product recovery rate’ bottleneck of existing stainless steel disk-stack centrifugation and single-use depth filtration, the single-use fluidized bed centrifugation system can process 20×10⁶ cells/mL culture at more than 350 L/h and purify high-density culture. There is (Source) (single-use closed centrifugation, same flow as acoustic sedimentation). ⚠️ However, the industry is still dominated by the centrifugal + depth filtration deployment method, and the adoption of alternative technologies is dependent on product, scale, and facility strategy.

Major suppliers include Alfa Laval (CultureOne), GEA (kytero), Sartorius (kSep fluidized bed), Pneumatic Scale Angelus (UniFuge) for centrifuges, MilliporeSigma (Millistak+, Clarisolve), Cytiva, Sartorius, 3M/Pall for depth filters, and Repligen (XCell ATF, TFDF)·Cytiva, etc.

Issue: Point of conflicting evidence

Issue:There are three points where the sources directly confront each other. First, Should we use centrifugation for large-scale clarification or should we go for single-use depth filtration alone? One side sees disk-stack centrifugation as a virtually essential primary separation in very large-scale, high-solids harvest that reduces the large area and consumables bottleneck of depth filters, while the other side sees centrifugation as having a large capital cost and installation space, and releasing submicron particles/hcDNA/HCP through the front end of the feed zone, thereby reducing the size of the depth filter downstream. Since it can actually increase the burden, it is believed that closed, flexible, low-capital, single-use depth filtration alone (or in conjunction with aggregation) is better for small and medium-sized businesses. There is no universal solution as the choice is strongly dependent on scale, solids content, facility strategy, and shear sensitivity. Second, Does upstream intensification improve the overall process? It is true that perfusion/intensification greatly increases upstream volumetric productivity, but the high-density harvest creates new bottlenecks in the clarification stage, such as rapid reduction in filter capacity, shear, and recovery loss, so the upstream benefits can be offset downstream—the net benefit depends on the maturity of the clarification technology (fluidized bed centrifugation, TFDF, and coagulation). Third, Will a depth filter with positive charge (containing diatomaceous earth) be used for virus harvest? Positively charged filters are advantageous for DNA/HCP adsorption and high colloid holding capacity and have good recovery of adenoviruses, but negative charge viruses (lenti/influenza) bind to them and greatly reduce recovery, so it is necessary to respond by product type, such as filters without inorganic filtration aids, open TFF, and high-salt washing. Although filter charge is an advantage for adsorption of mAb impurities, it is a cause of loss for charged virus products, and the optimal selection is reversed depending on the charge and properties of the product.Unresolved Questions: - The primary evidence on the quantitative economic feasibility (equipment footprint, capital, COGS reduction rate) of centrifugation vs. depth filtration alone vs. new type (fluidized bed/TFDF) was not fully confirmed as only abstracts and snippets were obtained through paid blocking (Wiley bit.28475, ScienceDirect technology/economic review). - Detailed process selection for pDNA alkaline lysate clarification (standard and yield of suspension separation vs. depth filter vs. centrifugation) and detailed parameters for inclusion body recovery were only partially confirmed based on search snippets due to failure to access vendor documentation. - Precise capacity and yield values ​​for specific depth filter grades and coagulants are dependent on product and harvest conditions, so only representative ranges are presented (individual process quantitative values ​​overlap with node 3.1/7.1 jurisdiction).

3. Downstream processing (DSP)

Purification of the clarified harvest into purified bulk Drug Substance.

3.1 Capture & purification chromatography

The first step to refinement: what to do and why to do it

Capture & purification chromatography is the first purification gateway in downstream processing (DSP) and is a unit operation series that contains two conceptual roles with different purposes within a single node. It receives the clarification harvest (HCCF) handed over by the previous node (2.3 Harvest/Clarification) as input, and uses the difference in affinity of each component for the stationary phase to separate the product from all kinds of impurities — host cell protein (HCP), host DNA, aggregates/high molecular weight species (HMW), fragments/low molecular weight species (LMW), charge variants, viruses, and leaching ligands shed by the chromatograph itself. The output flows to node 3.2 (virus inactivation/filtration) and node 3.3 (polishing/concentration/buffer exchange UF/DF).

The first role is capture. It selectively captures only products from a large-volume, low-concentration, high-impurity supply to reduce volume and achieve initial purification and concentration at once. In monoclonal antibodies (mAb), a single Protein A affinity step is the ‘platform anchor’ that takes on this position. Affinity chromatography removes over 90% of HCPs from the fining harvest, reducing HCPs from the typical 10^5 ppm level to the 2000–10000 ppm range (Source). ⚠️ The second role is polishing, which removes residual impurities (aggregates, residual HCP·DNA, leached Protein A, and charge variants) and viruses remaining in the capture eluate to approximate the final specifications.

The standard framework of the mAb industry platform is a three-chromatography train that connects these two roles: ‘Protein A capture (bind-elute) → low pH virus inactivation (node ​​3.2) → cation exchange (CEX) bind-elute polishing → anion exchange (AEX) flow-through polishing’. How clean the capture eluate is at this stage largely determines the subsequent polishing burden, virus safety margin (node ​​3.2), final bulk drug substance specifications (node ​​3.3), and cost of manufacturing (COGS). In fact, the Protein A step is often considered the ‘most expensive single step’ in the platform process due to the large capital investment to purchase chromatography media (Source).

How it works: Two operating modes and purification mechanisms for each mode

Chromatography operation is largely divided into two modes. bind-elute is a method in which the product binds to the stationary phase and impurities pass through, then the conditions are changed to elute and recover the product. flow-through is the exact opposite; the product passes through directly and only impurities are captured and remain in the stationary phase. Since which component is captured changes, the same ion exchange medium is used for completely different purposes depending on the operation mode.Capture—Protein A affinity.Ligand from recombinant Staphylococcus aureus Protein A (engineered for alkali resistance) binds very specifically to the Fc region of IgG. The operation consists of one cycle: Equilibration (neutral pH) → Loading (flow of HCCF to combine products) → Washing (HCP/impurities removal) → Low pH elution (pH 3–4, acetic/citric acid) → CIP (wash in place, NaOH) → Regeneration/re-equilibration. The key in loading is to bind the product to the inner surface of the bead, and for this, the residence time must be sufficiently long. For example, MabSelect SuRe LX resin shows a dynamic binding capacity (DBC) ≥60 g/L for multi- and monoclonal IgG, but requires a long residence time exceeding 6 minutes to bind to the internal surface (Source). Protein A is placed at the center of the platform because it achieves 2–3 log HCP removal and 95%+ purity with just a single affinity step.Polishing modes. After capture, several ionic/hydrophobic based modes are applied depending on the nature of the remaining impurities. - CEX bind-elute (cation exchange).At acidic pH, the product binds with a positive charge and is eluted with a salt gradient. In this process, charge variants, residual HCP, and leached Protein A·HMW are separated. - AEX flow-through (anion exchange).The product passes through in the neutral vicinity and negatively charged impurities (HCP, DNA, endotoxin, virus) are combined. Charge-based partitioning contributes a viral log reduction value (LRV) of 3–5, making it a key chromatographic step in viral clearance strategies (Source). ⚠️ - HIC (hydrophobic interaction) removes aggregates and HCPs through high-salt loading and low-salt elution, and mixed-mode/multimodal aims at both salt resistance and removal of aggregates and HCPs through ionic + hydrophobic multiple actions.Intensive flow-through platform.Recently, an approach has emerged to reduce the number of columns and media volume by combining all three columns in pass-through mode. The integrated all-flow-through polishing platform consisting of activated carbon + flow-through CEX + AEX can replace the existing chromatography platform, and showed excellent impurity removal and mAb yield exceeding 80% even at high mAb loading, and achieved DNA clearance exceeding 3 log10 for both mAbs in the activated carbon step (Source). Here, flow-through mode cation exchange selectively filters stronger residual aggregates from monomeric mAb to achieve ~95% monomer recovery, with a loading capacity 15–50 times higher than traditional bind-elute cation exchange (approximately 30–100 mg mAb/mL resin) (Source). In other words, the core logic of intensification is that if the same medium is run through flow-through, a much larger amount can be processed while only specific impurities can be selected and removed.Continuous/multi-column capture and convective media. Once loading reaches breakthrough, a single column cannot load any more, causing the ligand to sit idle. However, when 2–4 columns are cyclically loaded with PCC (cyclic countercurrent) or SMB (simulated moving bed), the excess of breakthrough from one column is captured by the next column, greatly increasing ligand utilization and productivity. Meanwhile, convective media such as membrane and monolith transfer substances through convection rather than diffusion, which is advantageous for high-flow flow-through polishing and capturing large particles (viruses), and is easy to use as a single device.

Key parameters and materials

Operating parameters for Protein A capture.

Typically managed are dynamic binding capacity DBC ≥60–80 g/L (approaching internal surface over 3–6 min residence time), elution pH 3–4 (low pH aggregation risk), CIP 0.1–0.5 M NaOH (contact approx. 15 min), reuse 100–300 cycles, and leaching ligand 5–15 ppm level. Performance is evaluated by DBC (g/L), recovery rate (%), HCP removal (log), ligand leakage (ppm), and resin lifetime (cycle/time). The cleaning durability of resin is economical, and the data of next-generation resin clearly shows this. The Purolite DurA Cycle/Praesto Jetted series produces a capacity of 80 g/L (65 g/L at a 3-minute residence time) for typical antibody purification and maintains more than 90% of the initial capacity even after 300 hours of exposure to 0.1 M NaOH, demonstrating multiple cycle reusability (Source). However, the more intense the cleaning, the more expensive it is. Mab

Select PrismA has no significant decrease in DBC in repeated washings with 0.3 M NaOH, but DBC decreases by 28% in 1.0 M NaOH. Conversely, washing with too weak 50 mM NaOH accumulates a binding capacity loss of 1–2% for each cycle, leading to an advanced breakthrough curve and reduced mass transfer after 100 cycles (Source). ⚠️ Amsphere A3, another alkaline-stable resin, is reported to maintain more than 90% of DBC up to 300 cycles with 0.1 M NaOH (15 minutes of contact) and withstand at least 100 cycles with 0.5 M NaOH (Source). ⚠️

Ion exchange parameters.The traditional loading of CEX bind-elute is about 30–100 mg/mL, and as seen previously, flow-through CEX is capable of loadings 15–50 times higher than that (Source). AEX flow-through targets LRV 3–5, and in either case, pH, conductivity (ionic strength), salt gradient, and loading density are the key variables.Equipment and materials. Chromatography resin (ligand bound to agarose or polymethacrylate base matrix), columns (from single-use pre-packed to stainless steel packed bed with a diameter exceeding 1 m), chromatography skid (ÄKTA, etc.) equipped with UV, pH, and conductivity in-line sensors, equilibration, washing, elution, and CIP buffer, membrane/monolith module, and activated carbon for aggregate removal are used.Cost (economic parameter) — note the variation by source. According to various secondary and blog data, Protein A resin costs approximately $8,000–15,000 per pack L, accounting for 30–50% of downstream costs and 15–25% of total COGS, and for economic efficiency, it is reused for 100–200 cycles, amortizing the cost per batch to $800–3,000. presented (Source). ⚠️ In addition, Protein A resin is about 50% more expensive than traditional chromatography media using non-protein ligands, and the resin cost for a large column with a diameter of more than 1 m can exceed $1 million, so for economic reasons, reuse for 50–300 cycles is required (Source). ⚠️ However, since these cost figures vary widely depending on the source and conditions, and a significant portion are representative of blogs and secondary data, it is safer to read them with a sense of the number of digits rather than accepting the exact $/L or COGS proportion as definitive (the primary quantitative cost model in terms of coverage is not secured due to paid blocking).Input/Output Specifications. The starting point is a low-turbidity product-containing clarifier (HCCF), and the output of this step is a stream that has been polished through capture eluate (purity 95%+, HCP 2–3 log reduction) to reduce HCP to ng/mg levels and residual DNA to very low levels. In the case of AAV, the parameters are completely changed, such as Capto AVB/CaptureSelect AAVX affinity capture followed by elution with 50mM citrate + 500mM NaCl + 350mM L-arginine pH 3.5 (Source).

Quality and Regulation

This step directly influences several key quality attributes (CQAs).

Process-derived impurities (HCP/host DNA).Protein A capture removes over 90% of HCP, lowering the 10^5 ppm level to 2000–10000 ppm (Source), ⚠️ Afterwards, CEX/AEX/HIC/mixed-mode polishing finishes to the ng/mg level. Residual DNA is handled by AEX flow-through and activated carbon, which exhibits DNA clearance exceeding 3 log10 for both mAbs (Source).Leaching Protein A (ligand). The capture eluate contains ligands at a level of 5–15 ppm, and it is reported that even resins as high as 10–15 ppm, such as MabSelect SuRe LX, are removed in subsequent ion exchange (Source). Due to concerns about antigenicity and safety, subsequent cation and anion exchange leached Protein A is controlled to less than 1 ppm, and in the final formulation, it is usually controlled to less than 10 ppm (Source). ⚠️

Viral safety (directly related to node 3.2).AEX flow-through contributes LRV 3–5 with charge-based distribution, and ICH Q5A(R2) requires at least two orthogonal clearance steps acting as independent mechanisms (Source). ⚠️ Therefore, capture/polish chromatography, together with the dedicated virus removal step in node 3.2, is jointly responsible for creating a cumulative virus safety margin.Agglomerates and failure modes. Low pH elution can cause aggregation at the expense of purity, so mild-pH eluting ligands are being developed as a countermeasure. Typical failure modes are (a) breakthrough due to excessive loading → product loss and impurities passing through, (b) resin fouling → DBC decline, increased ligand leakage, and lower elution purity, (c) low pH elution → aggregate formation, (d) loading and conductivity escape from AEX flow-through → virus and HCP passing.Governing standards. ICH Q5A(R2) (verification of viral safety and chromatographic clearance), Q8/Q11 (control strategy for setting loading density, residence time, pH, conductivity, and elution pool criteria as key process variables CPP), Q13 (retention time distribution, steady state, and batch definition of continuous chromatography), and Q6B (specification) form the framework. Resin ‘reuse’ is managed through cleaning verification, resin lifetime study, and continuous process verification (CPV), which are directly reflected in the manufacturing process items of regulatory submission documents.

Variations depending on product type

The most striking feature of this clause is that when the product modality changes, the ‘capture ligand’ is replaced entirely.

  • Recombinant protein containing mAb·Fc — This section applies most directly. The 3-chromatography platform of Protein A capture (bind-elute, low pH elution) followed by CEX bind-elute + AEX flow-through polishing is the standard framework. Recombinant proteins without Fc use CEX/mixed-mode capture instead of Protein A, but for His-tag proteins, capture is replaced by immobilized metal affinity (IMAC).
  • mRNA-LNP (Node 9.1) — There is neither cell culture nor Protein A, but oligo(dT) affinity capture ‘parallel displacement’ takes place. The ligand binds to the polyA tail of mRNA and washes away polyA-free DNA, NTPs, enzymes, and buffer. However, double-stranded RNA (dsRNA) generated during in-vitro transcription cannot be removed with oligo(dT) alone, so ion pair reverse phase (RP), cellulose-based chromatography, or anion exchange polishing steps are added (Source). ⚠️ In other words, in mRNA, chromatography, far from collapsing, becomes central to purification (only subsequent LNP encapsulation is not chromatography).
  • Viral vector/cell gene therapy (node ​​9.2) — AAV is captured with large-pore affinity resin (CaptureSelect AAVX pan-serotype, Capto AVB, etc. by serotype) and eluted with low pH (e.g. pH 3.5, citrate/NaCl/arginine) (Source). The critical discrimination step, which has no counterpart in mAb, is empty/full capsid separation by anion exchange, in which the difference in surface charge depending on the presence or absence of the genome is divided by a shallow salt gradient. The reason AEX is preferred for this separation is its scalability, and binding ionic strength 15–75 mM·pH 7.5–9.0·resin chemistry (POROS ⚠️
  • Classic Vaccine (Node 9.3) — Traditional density gradient ultracentrifugation is being replaced by IEX·mixed-mode·monolith chromatography. Convective monolith/membrane chromatography is preferred because large viral particles are excluded from the pores of packed-bed resin.

Practices and recent changes

Current standard.In mAb, the method combining resin reuse with a three-column deployment platform of Protein A capture → low pH inactivation → CEX bind-elute → AEX flow-through is dominant, and this framework itself has not changed significantly since the 2000s. The main limitations are Protein A’s high capital and consumable costs, capacity burden for rising upstream titers (5–10 g/L+), aggregation in low pH elution, resin fouling/lifetime, and ligand leakage.Recent trends. There have been roughly five changes over the past few years. First, continuous and integrated capture. With the commercialization of continuous chromatography systems such as ChromaCon CaptureSMB, Cytiva PCC, Sartorius BioSMB, and Novasep SMCC, three-column PCC has been widely adopted for continuous capture, achieving mAb recovery of 83–92%, reducing buffer consumption to less than half of the batch, and yielding 1.0–1.5 g mAb per mL of resin in 24 hours. ICH published the Q13 2022 guide for continuous biomanufacturing, providing a regulatory framework (Source). ⚠️ This advantage is not limited to mAb; there is also a case where cyclic countercurrent (PCC) chromatography in AAV achieved productivity about 3 times that of a single column (6.6×10^14 vs. 2.3×10^14 TP mL⁻¹ h⁻¹) thanks to material accumulation in the loading zone and parallel operation (Source).

Second, it is a flow that reduces the number of columns and media volume through intensive flow-through polishing such as activated carbon + flow-through CEX/AEX (Source). Third, the spread of convection media. Due to the nature of AEX’s flow-through operation, membrane chromatography has steadily encroached on the area of ​​traditional AEX columns, greatly increasing manufacturing speed and productivity, and in one mAb polishing comparison, monolith was superior to membrane and packed bed in both IgG recovery and impurity removal (Source). ⚠️ Fourth, the next-generation alkaline-stable high DBC Protein A resin (MabSelect PrismA, Amsphere A3, DurA Cycle Jetted, etc.) is a flow that withstands strong NaOH CIP and provides 150–300 cycles and improved mass transfer and mild-pH elution (Source). ⚠️ Fifth, advancement of column control through mechanism modeling, QbD, PAT, and real-time shipment testing.

Remaining tension.Synthetic Protein A mimetic and mixed-mode resin, which are mentioned as candidates for Protein A replacement, have low selectivity and affinity, and selectivity varies between the two antibodies, so even similar products require process modifications (Source). ⚠️ Ultimately, capacity vs. cost balance at high titers, robustness of AAV empty/full separation, capture of large mRNA structures, and maturity of process control and verification of continuous chromatography remain as outstanding issues in this section.

Issue

Issue 1 — Is Protein A the dominant cost bottleneck?One side sees Protein A resin as the ‘most expensive single step’, costing $8,000–15,000/L for media, >$1M for large columns, 50% more expensive than traditional media, and accounting for 30–50% of downstream costs and 15–25% of total COGS (Source). ⚠️ On the other hand, they point out that if the cost per batch is amortized by reusing high-density resin over 150 cycles, Protein A may only account for about 3% of the total manufacturing cost (Source). Depending on whether the ‘cost’ is viewed as the initial capital/column cost or the cost per batch amortized over the life of the resin, the conclusion is opposite, and the resin lifetime (number of cycles) is the determining variable in this calculation and is strongly dependent on the reuse/cleaning verification strategy.Issue 2 — Should we maintain mAb capture with Protein A or move it to a non-affinity/intensity platform? Protein A is a platform anchor that provides purity of 95%+ and HCP 2–3 log in a single step and is the de facto standard in terms of validation, robustness, and regulatory familiarity. On the other hand, synthetic mimetics, mixed-mode, activated carbon + flow-through intensive platforms are emerging as alternatives due to cost, capacity, and low pH aggregation, but the alternatives have low selectivity and affinity and the selectivity of each antibody is different, so the burden of redevelopment of the process for each product is high ([Source] (https://www.bioprocessintl.com/chromatography/revisiting-protein-a-chromatography)). ⚠️ The optimal choice is dependent on product properties (Fc presence, potency, stability) and facility strategy, so there is no universal solution.Issue 3 — Should I use a batch resin column for polishing/AEX or a convective/continuous multi-column? Packed-bed resin columns are a mature standard in terms of verification, robustness, and capacity predictability, and are advantageous for bind-elute high-selectivity separation. However, due to the nature of AEX’s flow-through operation, membrane/monolith convective media encroaches on the packed-bed with high-flow, single-use, and large-particle processing (Source), and ⚠️ PCC/SMB multi-column doubles ligand utilization and productivity, but process control and verification maturity are still key. Adoption will depend on scale, product modality and sequencing strategy (ICH Q13).Unresolved questions: (1) The complete CIP cycle-DBC curve of certain resins such as MabSelect SuRe/PrismA was partially confirmed only through secondary data and snippets as direct citation could not be secured in the Cytiva primary PDF (binary corruption). (2) The exact $/L·COGS proportion of Protein A cost is highly dependent on conditions and sources, so it is presented only in the blog/secondary representative range (primary quantitative cost model is blocked for a fee). (3) Detailed process parameters of mRNA oligo(dT) capture/dsRNA removal and quantitative yield of vaccine monolith polishing were secured only at the vendor/second snippet level (overlapping nodes 9.1/9.3 and jurisdiction).

3.2 Viral clearance (inactivation & filtration)

Purpose and role: ‘Virus safety assurance’ series distributed throughout the downstream process

Viral clearance is not a single unit operation, but a series of ‘virus safety assurance’ unit operations scattered throughout the downstream process (DSP). Apart from purifying the product, the virus safety of the final drug product is ensured by inactivating or physically removing the endogenous viruses originating from the cell line itself and adventitious viruses that may enter through raw materials, media, or manipulation. In terms of the process flow, this section receives the stream delivered by capture/purification chromatography (node ​​3.1), combines low pH/solvent/detergent inactivation, virus filtration (nanofiltration), and distribution removal by chromatography, and then passes it to polishing/UF/DF (node ​​3.3). In other words, the starting point is ‘a stream in progress of purification’, and the output is ‘a stream with a verified virus safety margin’.

There are three concepts that run through this series. The first is the three-tier defense strategy. Virus safety is not proven by any one test, but is a complementary combination of three pillars: ① careful selection and testing of starting materials (prevention), ② testing whether the materials during the process are free from foreign agents (detection), and ③ verification that the manufacturing and purification process can remove and inactivate potential foreign and endogenous viruses (removal/inactivation). Guaranteed (Source). ⚠️ What this section is responsible for is the third pillar, that is, the part that demonstrates the removal and inactivation ability of the process.

The second is orthogonality. There are at least two steps that operate through different physical and chemical mechanisms, so that even viruses that are resistant to one mechanism become susceptible to the other mechanism. For example, low pH inactivation only captures viruses with a lipid envelope and is almost powerless against parvoviruses without an envelope, but 20nm nanofiltration works by size exclusion, so it filters out regardless of the presence or absence of an envelope (Source). ⚠️ Third is cumulative LRV (log reduction value). The safety margin for the entire process is calculated by summing the log reduction values of each orthogonal step. In the mAb/CHO platform, a well-designed strategy reaches a cumulative LRV of approximately 12–18 logs depending on the virus/model, typically targeting ≥12 logs for the retrovirus model X-MuLV and ≥6 logs for the small non-enveloped model mouse minute virus (MMV). (Source). ⚠️ The deliverables of this section—a batch of orthogonal steps and a validated LRV—are the basis for regulatory submission (Section 3.2.S Viral Safety) and batch release, with ICH Q5A(R2) being the governing guideline.

Principle and flow: Inactivation and removal are arranged orthogonally

1) Low-pH inactivation (low-pH hold).If the product pool is maintained at acidic pH for a certain period of time following Protein A low-pH elution (node 3.1), the envelope/capsid protein of viruses (retro-herpes) with a lipid envelope is irreversibly denatured. As a representative parameter, 60 minutes at pH 3.5 gives >4 LRV against enveloped viruses, but is virtually helpless against non-enveloped parvoviruses with 0–1 LRV (Source). ⚠️ The higher the temperature (approximately above 15°C), the faster the inactivation occurs. The advantage of the mAb platform is that Protein A elution is already low pH, so this inactivation is naturally ‘built’ into the process.2) Solvent/detergent (S/D) inactivation. This method was developed in response to the HIV contamination of plasma products in the 1980s. A detergent (formerly Triton Eliminate it (Source). The reaction occurs virtually immediately (within minutes) and gives >4 LRV to enveloped viruses, but, like low pH, does not capture non-enveloped viruses at all.3) Virus filtration (nanofiltration). Unlike the previous two inactivations that depend on chemical and surface properties, virus filtration is an absolute mechanism that operates only through size exclusion, so it filters only by size regardless of the presence or absence of an envelope. The ‘parvovirus grade’ 20 nm filter provides broad removal (>4 LRV), even the smallest viruses (MMV approx. 18–24 nm), while the ‘retrovirus grade’ 35–50 nm filter targets larger viruses (X-MuLV approx. 80–130 nm) (Source). ⚠️ Filtration performance is managed by total virus load (total challenge), flux/pressure attenuation, and integrity test after use.4) Chromatography contribution. When anion exchange (AEX) is operated in flow-through mode, the virus binds and remains on the membrane and resin due to charge-based distribution, usually contributing 3–5 LRV (Source). ⚠️ This contribution comes from a unit operation that is physically the same as the polishing of node 3.1, and also partially adds CEX·mixed-mode.5) Validation—Spiking study. Since the actual virus cannot be shed in the actual process, validation is performed by adding a high-titer virus stock to the load in a scale-down model qualified to represent the actual process and calculating LRV=log (input/output) as the infectivity titer ratio of inflow to outflow. These spiking studies are always performed at laboratory scale, and the scale models must be qualified to demonstrate comparability with the actual process (Source). ⚠️ It is a regulatory GLP and is performed by an external specialized research organization (CRO), and ICH Q5A(R2) requires panel evaluation consisting of relevant, specific, and non-specific model viruses. CHO/mAb standard panels include X-MuLV (retro, endogenous RVLP model), MMV/MVM (parvo, worst-case non-enveloped), PRV (large envelope), and Reo-3 (non-enveloped), and the LRV of each orthogonal step is added to create a cumulative safety margin.6) Continuous/intensive process. When batch-type low pH holding tanks are replaced with coiled/tubular plug flow reactors, retention time distribution (RTD) and minimum incubation time assurance become key to verification. Q5A(R2) allows clearance studies using a batch-based reduced model for continuous chromatographic unit operations as an option (Source). ⚠️ However, it should be noted that the quantitative parameters of continuous low pH inactivation (tubular reactor RTD, minimum incubation time) were obtained only at a qualitative level because the original source was blocked in this research.

Key parameters and materials

Low pH inactivation. pH 3.4–3.7 (usually 3.5–3.6), retention time ≥30–60 minutes, temperature ≥15°C (higher temperatures are faster), and gives >4 LRV to enveloped viruses and 0–1 LRV to non-enveloped viruses under representative conditions of pH 3.5·60 minutes (Source). ⚠️ CPP (key process variables) are pH, time, and temperature. (For reference, the ‘≥15°C·≥30 minutes·pH≤3.6·5 log’ quantification commonly used in the industry is not a specific primary text but a value derived from a search synthesis, so here, ‘pH 3.5, 60 minutes, >4 LRV’ from the bioprocesstools blog was used as a representative quotation.)

Solvent/Detergent (S/D).Based on ASTM E3042-16, Triton It is 0 (Source).Virus filtration. The parvo grade 20nm filter excludes sizes down to the smallest MMV (18–24nm) and gives >4 LRV regardless of envelope/non-envelope, while the retro grade uses 35–50nm. Control variables are total viral load, flux/pressure attenuation, throughput (g/m²), and post-use integrity test (Source). ⚠️

Model viruses (spiking panel).Combination of X-MuLV (retro, 80–130 nm), MMV/MVM (parvo, 18–24 nm, worst-case), PRV (large envelope), and Reo-3 (non-enveloped). In particular, the parvo filter operates by size exclusion, so the smallest type, MMV, is the worst-case that is most likely to pass through. Based on this, there is a strong scientific basis for evaluating the removal ability of the parvo filter with a single worst-case model virus (Source).Cumulative LRV target and safety margin determination. X-MuLV ≥12, MMV ≥6, typically 12–18 logs (Source) ⚠️, Q5A(R2) for CHO-derived retroviral adoptive particles (RVLP) has a safety margin of <10⁻⁴ particles/dose. Allowed (Source).Equipment/Materials. Low pH inactivation tank (or continuous tubular reactor), S/D mixing skid, single-use virus filtration cartridge for each size class, GLP virus stock and scale model column/filter for spiking, and infectivity titer measurement (TCID50/plaque) and qPCR are used. The starting point is the elution/polishing pool of node 3.1, and the output of this step is the stream with the verification LRV of each orthogonal step and the data for 3.2.S submission.

Quality and Regulatory: ICH Q5A(R2) is the governing guideline

The highest level guideline is ICH Q5A(R2) (Virus safety assessment of cell line-derived bioproducts; adopted in November 2023, implemented in June 2024), and supplementally refer to Q6B (specification), Q13 (clearance verification during continuous manufacturing), regional pharmacopoeia, and CHMP/FDA guidelines. Regarded as the largest revision since the 1997/99 edition, R2’s key changes are in five areas.

First, scope expansion. VLP/subunits (baculo/HSV/adeno-producing systems), nanoparticle vaccines, and viral vectors such as AAV are now explicitly addressed in the new Annex 7—which incorporates new modalities not covered in previous editions (Source). However, genetically engineered viral vectors and vector-derived products (virus vaccines, gene therapy products, etc.) are included only to the extent that ‘virus clearance is possible and there is no adverse effect on the product’ (Source). ⚠️

Second, it is an in-vivo replacement for NGS (next generation sequencing). R2 reduces animal testing by recommending the use of NGS as a replacement for in-vivo animal testing, such as hamster (HAP), mouse (MAP), and rat (RAP) antibody production testing, which is also in line with the 3R principle (Source).

Third, prior knowledge (PrK)/platform access and continuous manufacturing response. R2 allows clearance studies using a batch-based reduced model for chromatographic unit operations in continuous manufacturing, and provides an example of applying PrK to clearance step evaluation, allowing room to replace some of the redundant verification for each product (Source). ⚠️

Fourth, risk-based mitigation. For RVLP of well-characterized CHO cell lines, a safety margin of <10⁻⁴ particles/dose is allowed, which is a relaxation of the previously implied threshold of <10⁻⁶, and parvovirus is accepted as a worst-case model instead of larger viruses in filter validation (Source).

The CQAs controlled by this section include residual infectious viruses (safety CQAs), CHO endogenous RVLP, and foreign viruses (MMV, etc.) that may originate from raw materials or media. The main failure modes are lack of inactivation due to deviation of pH/time/temperature of low pH, survival of enveloped virus due to insufficient detergent concentration of S/D, breakthrough due to destruction of the integrity of the virus filter and attenuation of flux, passage due to deviation of loading and conductivity of AEX flow-through, and invalidity of verification due to non-representativeness of the reduced model. A major regulatory issue is the withdrawal of the detergent itself—Triton The introduction of an alternative tax system involves revalidation of virus inactivation and global change approval.

One thing to note. In this research, direct citation of the primary text PDF (FDA/ICH) of Q5A(R2) could not be secured due to file damage and access blocking, and the revised content was replaced by cross-checking multiple secondary data. Key facts are consistently confirmed in multiple secondary sources.

Differences by product type: Strategy flips when ‘the product is a virus’

mAb/recombinant protein (CHO/mammalian).This section is the most directly applicable standard practice. The orthogonal three-step process of Protein A low-pH elution followed by low-pH hold inactivation + AEX flow-through removal + 20nm parvo-grade nanofiltration is standard, and both endogenous CHO RVLP and exogenous MMV are verified by X-MuLV·MMV spiking. Derived from microorganisms (E. coli), the risk of mammalian viruses is low and requirements are reduced. mRNA-LNP (Node 9.1) is a cell-free IVT (in-vitro transcription) production, so the risk of mammalian viruses is fundamentally low, so the proportion of traditional virus clearance tends to decrease and be replaced by raw materials (enzymes, nucleotides) and facility management.Viral Vector/CGT (Node 9.2)—Key twist. Here, the premise of the orthogonal strategy breaks down because ‘the product is the virus.’ The heat, low pH hold, and detergent exposure used in mAbs have little effect on non-enveloped viral vectors that share characteristics with AAV (Source). Moreover, since AAV itself belongs to the parvovirus family, if a 20nm parvo filter developed in response to MVM contamination is used, the AAV product itself will be filtered out, resulting in a sharp drop in yield. Conversely, if a 35–70nm large filter is used, a dilemma arises in that AAV will pass but small foreign viruses will not be filtered out (Source). ⚠️ Therefore, the center of gravity of safety moves to the axis of ‘prevention and detection’ (selection and testing of raw materials and cell substrates, confirmation of virus-free media).Nonetheless, ‘possible clearance’ is still substantiated. In one AAV case study, detergent lysis treatment gave almost immediate inactivation of the enveloped contaminating virus ⚠️ However, these figures are based on a single case study, so generalization requires caution. Lentivirus (enveloped) is not suitable for chemical inactivation because detergent inactivation itself destroys the product (enveloped virus), and instead, a compromise is used to achieve model virus reduction while maintaining >90% yield with 35·50nm large nanofilters (Source). ⚠️ In classical vaccines and plasma fractionation (node ​​9.3), formalin/BPL treatment of inactivated vaccines overlaps with clearance, and S/D inactivation of plasma products corresponds to the historical prototype.

In cases like these where clearance is virtually impossible, safety is ensured by the remaining two pillars of preventive control and virus testing and characterization (Source). ⚠️

Practices and changes

The current standard is stable. In mAb, the dominant method is to combine X-MuLV·MMV spiking GLP verification with low pH hold (linked to Protein A elution) + AEX flow-through + 20nm nanofiltration orthogonal three-stage arrangement, and this framework itself has been maintained for a long time. There are five major changes over the past three years. First, ICH Q5A(R2) (adopted in 2023 and implemented in 2024) reorganized the verification burden by introducing in-vivo replacement of NGS, PrK/platform approach, continuous manufacturing response, incorporation of viral vectors/new modalities, and risk-based mitigation all at once (Source). Second, the retirement of the Triton X-100. Due to the REACH SVHC designation, its use in the EU will be banned from December 22, 2023 (Source), and conversion to alternative detergents and re-validation are in progress—Of the six pharmacopeia-listed nonionic detergents evaluated, PEG 9 Lauryl Ether (L9) completely inactivated 1% of xenotropic murine leukemia virus to below the detection limit within 1 minute; PEG 6 Caprylic/Capric Glycerides (G767) achieved >4.0 LRV in 1 minute at 1% (Source).

Third, it is a continuous and connected process. Continuous inactivation, which replaces batch low pH tanks with tubular/coiled plug flow reactors, and clearance verification of PCC continuous capture (allowing batch downscale models) are maturing (Source). ⚠️ Fourth, it is a flow-through connection that combines single-use nanofiltration with intensive polishing. Fifth, this is a flow that simplifies parvo filter verification with a single worst-case model virus (MMV) (Source).

Limitations and unresolved points are also clear. Structural challenges remain such as proving that the reduced model is representative of the actual process, ensuring RTD/minimum incubation time for continuous inactivation and maturity of real-time verification, inactivation robustness and impurity profile of alternative detergents, and above all, the orthogonal strategy is not established as ‘product = virus’ in viral vectors, leading to increased dependence on prevention and detection.

Key players and tools

Representative virus filtration/inactivation technology vendors include Merck/MilliporeSigma (Viresolve Pro/NFP parvofilter, S/D reagent), Sartorius (Virosart HC/CPV nanofilter), Asahi Kasei (Planova 15N/20N/35N — originator of hollow fiber nanofilter), and Pall/Cytiva (nanofiltration/AEX membrane). In terms of alternative detergents, Croda Pharma is talking about a next-generation Triton Charles River, Texcell, WuXi, and Vironova/Nucleus are the main performing organizations for viral clearance verification (GLP CRO). The key standards and references are ICH Q5A(R2) (2023), and ICH Q6B·Q13, PDA Technical Report (viral clearance), and practical reviews by BioProcess International and BioPharm International are used as introductory materials.

Issue:There are three points where the sources diverge. (1) Is a single worst-case model virus (MMV) sufficient for filter verification? On the one hand, since the Parvo filter operates by size exclusion, the smallest MMV is the worst-case that is easiest to pass, and using it as a single model can simplify research and reduce costs, and Q5A(R2) also accepts this. On the other hand, there is a reason why traditional verification requires a related + specific + non-specific multivirus panel (X-MuLV, MMV, PRV, Reo-3) - viruses with different chemical and surface properties in addition to size behave differently in different steps such as inactivation and chromatography, so if a single virus represents the entire process, mechanisms are missed. If you look only at ‘size exclusion of one filter’, the former is correct, but if you look at ‘orthogonal clearance of the entire process’, the latter is necessary, a trade-off of simplification and cost vs. mechanism coverage and conservatism. (2) Is it possible to actually verify clearance for viral vectors? There is a view that safety is shifting to prevention and detection as the orthogonal strategy collapses as ‘the product is a virus’, and a view that partial clearance limited to contaminant viruses (XMuLV >4.67 for detergent lysis, MVM accumulation ~9.07 for chromatography) is proven - is the contaminant enveloped or non-enveloped, how much is the product, size, and chemistry? Conclusions vary depending on what is different. (3) Does the risk-based mitigation of Q5A(R2) reduce the burden while maintaining safety? The position that reducing animal testing and duplicate verification with accumulated platform knowledge and high-sensitivity NGS is scientifically justified and consistent with the 3Rs, and the safety implications of NGS method verification and RVLP threshold relaxation (<10⁻⁶→<10⁻⁴), as mitigation is largely dependent on regulatory discretion and prior consultation, still require a case-by-case judgment. There is a conflicting position that it is necessary.

Unresolved questions: (1) Direct citation of ICH Q5A(R2) primary text (FDA/ICH PDF) not secured due to file damage or access block—Revisions have been replaced by cross-checking multiple secondary data, and key facts are consistently confirmed. (2) The quantitative verification parameters of continuous low pH inactivation (tubular plug flow reactor, RTD, minimum incubation time) were only secured at a qualitative level due to blocking access to the original source. (3) AAV clearance LRV figures are based on a single case study, so generalization is limited, and the quantitative LRV contribution of the standard spiking panel (PRV·Reo-3) was mentioned only at the level of domain knowledge.

3.3 Polishing, concentration & buffer exchange (UF/DF)

At the final stage of the purification process (DSP), the product takes its final form, called “bulk drug substance (DS).” This section takes over the pool from the previous step, capture/polish chromatography (node ​​3.1) and the stream that has undergone virus inactivation/filtration (node ​​3.2), and does two things. First, final polishing is to reduce residual impurities (aggregate HMW, residual HCP/host DNA, leached Protein A, and charge variants) to below shipping specifications, and second, ultrafiltration/diafiltration (UF/DF) is to concentrate the product to the target concentration and completely exchange it with formulation buffer. The bulk DS completed in this way is handed over to formulation/filling (node ​​4) and CMC documentation (node ​​8.1).

The reason why this step is so crucial is because of its location. UF/DF is usually the last unit operation in the antibody (mAb) process line just before the end of filling, and the key quality characteristics (CQA) of the protein concentration and excipient concentration of the final product are actually determined only in this unit operation (Source). ⚠️ This proposition that “the concentration is determined only here” is somewhat less certain as it is based on vendor data (secondary), but it is based on the Gibbs-Donnan effect, which we will see later, etc. Many of the design considerations in this section all assume this proposition.

Principle and flow 1 — Final polishing: Why lower the agglomerates “in advance”?

Final polishing is the step that concludes the polishing chromatography mode described above in node 3.1. Aggregates (HMW) and charge variants are removed by cation exchange (CEX) bind-elute, hydrophobic interaction (HIC), and mixed-mode resins, and residual HCP, DNA, and viruses are filtered out by anion exchange (AEX) flow-through mode (logarithmic reduction value LRV 3~5). At manufacturing scale, preparative SEC (size exclusion) is used only rarely due to low throughput. There is a nuance that must be noted here. UF/DF membranes themselves do not “remove” aggregates — the MWCO of the membrane holds both monomers and aggregates together. Rather, the subsequent high concentration/shear environment can “generate” aggregates, so the aggregates must be lowered in advance during the polishing step. (The detailed operating parameters of polishing chromatography are under the jurisdiction of node 3.1 and will not be studied again in this section.)

Principle and flow 2 — UF/DF is tangential flow filtration (TFF)

UF/DF is physically implemented as tangential flow filtration (TFF). When the feed solution flows tangentially (cross-flow) through a semi-permeable membrane, water and low-molecular-weight solutes escape into the permeate through pressure-driven convection, and the products remain in the retentate. The MWCO of the membrane is chosen to be 3 to 5 times smaller than the target protein (e.g., a 30 kDa membrane for a 150 kDa mAb), which allows only small molecules to pass through while clearly retaining the product (Source).⚠️ Ultrafiltration (UF) is an operation that removes water to concentrate the product, and diafiltration (DF) continuously replenishes new agent buffer at the rate at which the permeate flows out. This is an operation that replaces the entire buffer with a “constant-volume” state.

The physical bottleneck in this process is “concentration polarization.” During ultrafiltration, partially or completely retained solutes are convected and accumulated on the membrane surface to form a polarized boundary layer (gel layer), and the permeation flux drops even if the transmembrane pressure (TMP) is kept constant — especially at the end of concentration, this gel layer thickens, causing both membrane overpressure and flux decrease (Source). ⚠️ So in practice, the flux-TMP curve is Operates at approximately 0.5 to 1.5 bar at protein UF, near the “knee” (Source). ⚠️ Increasing the cross-flow rate sweeps away membrane surface polarization and increases flux, but the trade-off is that it passes through the feed pump more frequently, increasing the risk of shear damage and agglomeration.

Principle and flow 3 — Mathematics of diafiltration and Gibbs-Donnan correction

How much buffer replacement has progressed is calculated by the diavolume (DV). The residual concentration of the freely permeating solute decreases exponentially along C/C0 = exp(−N·σ) (N is the diavolume and σ is the sieving coefficient), and the residual decreases by approximately e times (2.72 times) for each diavolume. As a practical standard, approximately 99.3% of 5 DV, 99.9% of 7 DV, and 99.99% of 9 DV are removed (Source). ⚠️ Usually, the buffer is first replaced with DF at a medium concentration, and then finally raised to the target concentration with UF (100-200 mg/mL for high-concentration mAb for subcutaneous administration). early).However, charged excipients do not follow this neat exponential law. This is because of the Gibbs-Donnan effect. When diafiltration reaches equilibrium, a difference in concentration of charged excipients and buffer components occurs between the DF solution and the retentate. Due to the Donnan effect, the concentration of charged components in the retentate containing the product is substantially lower than that in the DF buffer (Source). In other words, excipients with opposite charges to the protein are partially held by the protein due to electrostatic attraction, and as a result, the final pH and excipient concentration of high-concentration DS deviate from the DF buffer values. The size is surprisingly large: to remove 99.995% of negatively charged excipients for a positively charged mAb, 43 diavolumes are needed at a concentration of 120 g/L, while 17 diavolumes are sufficient at 55 g/L, a 2.5-fold difference depending on concentration (Source). When recalculated to reflect this effect, the optimal diafiltration concentration is much lower, around 57 g/L rather than the hydraulic optimum point (120 g/L) (Source). Therefore, a robust UF/DF design is determined by correcting the diavolume number and final concentration by the Donnan effect.

Principle and flow 4 — Single-pass TFF (SPTFF)

Traditional batch UF recirculates the product into the membrane module several times until the target concentration is reached. Single-pass TFF (SPTFF), in contrast, passes the feed liquid through the membrane module only once and operates in steady state (Source). Because there is no recirculation, in-line concentration (typically 10 to 20 times) is possible immediately after chromatography, shear is low because there is no pump recirculation, and it supports continuous/connected processes (ICH Q13). After completion of operation, bulk DS is filtered to reduce bioburden (0.2 µm), placed in single-use bags or storage containers, and frozen.

Key parameters and materials

The nominal MWCO of the membrane is 30 kDa for mAb, 10 kDa for small recombinant protein, and 100 kDa for AAV·mRNA-LNP depending on the product group. The main membrane material is low-adsorption regenerated cellulose (e.g. Ultracel/Hydrosart) or PES (polyethersulfone), and the format is divided into flat cassette (Pellicon, Sartocon, Centramate, Kvick) and hollow fiber. Typical operating parameters are TMP 0.5-1.5 bar (‘knee’), average flux 20-60 LMH (mAb), membrane loading 50-150 L/m², and diavolume 5-10 times (Source). ⚠️ In terms of equipment, supply pump, holding pressure control valve, inline A TFF skid with sensors (pressure, flow, conductivity, UV, and sometimes refractive index), a recirculating tank/bag, and a DF buffer supply line are required. Performance is evaluated by flux (LMH), final concentration (mg/mL), recovery (%), system hold-up/flush recovery, processing time, and flocculation increase (ΔHMW).

As a quantitative example, the Donnan effect seen earlier results in a concentration dependence of 43 diavolumes at 120 g/L and 17 diavolumes at 55 g/L (Source). In the case of AAV, in an experiment in which the clarified lysate was pre-concentrated with SPTFF before affinity chromatography, a bench-scale concentration of about 11 times and a yield of 89% was obtained with a 100 kDa regenerated cellulose/PES cassette. In the pilot, a yield exceeding 99% was maintained while single-pass concentration of 10 L to 1 L ([Source] (https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC12699131/)). To be honest, it should be taken into account that practical figures such as TMP ‘knee’ of 0.5 to 1.5 bar, flux of 20 to 60 LMH, and MWCO of 3 to 5 times are representative ranges obtained from blogs (secondary) and search snippets, as primary vendor pages such as Merck, Sigma, and Cytiva failed to secure direct quotations due to rendering and timeouts.

Quality and Regulation — Bulk DS Definition and Shipping Specifications

There are three CQAs that are confirmed and managed at this stage. First, the final protein concentration, formulation buffer composition, pH, and excipient concentration are determined only in UF/DF, and charged excipients deviate from the DF buffer value due to Gibbs-Donnan/volume exclusion, so correction design is essential (Source). Second, with the agglomerate (HMW), the value lowered in polishing is managed so as not to “increase” in UF/DF (high concentration, shear, and low flux polarization cause agglomeration). Third, as for the final impurity level, UF/DF serves as the final purification and dilution gateway to reduce residual HCP, host DNA, leached Protein A, endotoxin, and bioburden below specifications.

The output, bulk DS, is defined as “purified active material prior to formulation.” In other words, it is a purified bulk that has completed downstream processing (chromatography/UF/DF), before being placed in the final container/closure system, is not claimed to be sterile (sterility is secured at the DP stage), and is usually stored in a frozen (or refrigerated) state (Source). ⚠️ The shipping specifications for bulk DS are identity, purity/impurities, and potency/activity (as applicable). time), concentration, and bulk bioburden/endotoxin management (Source).⚠️ These two DS definition/specification items were obtained from the CMC consulting site (secondary), and ICH Q6B original text comparison was not performed.

In terms of regulatory framework, this section is governed by ICH Q6B (specification, test, and acceptance criteria), Q5C (stability of drug substance — freezing hold time and storage conditions), Q11 (manufacturing and control strategy), Q8/Q11 (TMP, cross-flow, diavolume number, final concentration, and processing time managed by CPP), Q13 (continuous/connected UF/DF), and on paper, CTD. It is reflected in 3.2.S.2.2 (manufacturing process) and 3.2.S.4 (raw drug substance management). Typical failure modes are over-concentration (→ viscosity, pressure surge, aggregation, membrane damage), insufficient diavolume (→ old buffer residue), Donnan non-correction (→ DS pH, deviation from excipient specifications), membrane fouling (→ flux decrease, yield loss), and pump shear (→ aggregation, particles).

Differences by product type

This section applies most directly to m

Abs and recombinant proteins — exchanging and concentrating the formulation buffer with cassette TFF (high concentration for subcutaneous administration) after final AEX/CEX polishing is the standard framework, and UF/DF is the final unit operation.

In mRNA-LNP (node ​​9.1), UF/DF appears twice and is the central operation for preparation completion. (a) Before making LNPs, the mRNA stock solution itself is concentrated with TFF. (b) After forming LNPs by micromixing, the mRNA-LNPs in the water/ethanol mixture are purified with TFF to remove ethanol, exchanged with a preparation buffer, and concentrated to the administration target concentration (Source).⚠️ At this time, it is advantageous to preserve the size of the LNPs by first removing the ethanol before changing the pH, and placing it in a diafiltration medium. PEG-lipid supplementation is required to maintain particle size (membrane 100 kDa), and it should be noted that these details are based on the journal abstract/secondary snippet level.

In viral vector/cell/gene therapy (node ​​9.2), concentration and buffer exchange (based on membrane 100 kDa/particle) are performed with TFF, but hollow fiber has traditionally been considered advantageous for shearing because it is a large, fragile particle. However, batch recirculating UF can cause AAV aggregation due to high shear rates, so SPTFF has emerged as an alternative (Source). Please note that empty/full capsid separation is the responsibility of chromatography (node ​​3.1), not UF/DF. In classical vaccines (Node 9.3), TFF is used for virus/antigen concentration and buffer exchange, but large virus particles require larger nominal MWCO/opening membranes and diafiltration is used to remove sucrose and salts.

Practices and recent changes

The current standard is batch recirculating TFF using regenerated cellulose flat cassettes. Flux-TMP is operated at the ‘knee’, and after an intermediate concentration of DF (5 to 10 diavolume), the concentration is raised to the target with final UF, and then finished with bioburden reduction filtration and freezing. Although it is a mature and proven method, it has clear limitations. The maximum protein concentration achievable in high-concentration mAb ultrafiltration is directly related to the pressure drop within the cassette and the solution viscosity that governs backfiltration (Source),⚠️ plus shear/flocculation from pump recirculation, membrane fouling, Gibbs-Donnan excipient/pH drift, system hold-up yield loss, and long processing times.

There have been four changes over the past three years. The first is the spread of single-pass TFF (SPTFF). By in-line concentrating (10 to 20 times) in a steady state without circulation, reducing tanks and shear, and connecting directly after chromatography, it supports the continuous/connected process of ICH Q13 adopted in 2022 (Source). The vibrating SPTFF stably concentrated a 20 g/L protein solution to 100 g/L in a single cassette for over 8 hours without aggregation, achieved a 20-fold single-pass concentration factor at a feed flux of 17.2 LMH, and dropped the TMP from 114 kPa to 6 kPa by vibration when fouling occurred (Source).
