In mRNA, a case was reported where a 10-fold enrichment factor was provided without loss of integrity during 12 hours of continuous operation at low TMP (Source). ⚠️ Above all, the intensive process that introduced single-pass pre-concentration in AAV had an overall productivity of 8.5 times higher than that of the 500 L batch process, the affinity column loading time was reduced from 350 minutes to 70 minutes, and the manufacturing cost was approx. It was 9.1% lower (Source) — showing that the benefits of SPTFF extend beyond simple shear savings to process economics.

The remaining three branches are (2) mechanism/machine learning model and in silico DS pH/excipient prediction to predict Donnan/volume exclusion of high concentration UF/DF, (3) development material and time reduction through small-sized high-throughput screening such as ambr crossflow, and (4) RTRT that determines the buffer exchange end point in real time with in-line conductivity/UV/refractive index PAT. The equipment landscape is formed by Merck/MilliporeSigma (Pellicon), Sartorius (Sartocon·ambr crossflow), Cytiva (Kvick·hollow fiber), Repligen (Spectrum hollow fiber·TangenX), and Pall (Centramate·Cadence SPTFF). Unresolved issues remain including robust handling of ultra-high concentrations (subcutaneous mAb), Donnan prediction accuracy, process control and validation maturity of continuous UF/DF, membrane fouling control, and membrane selection by product type (large particles).

Issue:There are three points in this section where opinions differ between sources. First, is the hollow fiber in TFF of shear-sensitive large particles (such as AAV) inherently softer than a flat cassette? Conventionally, open channels of hollow fiber have been recommended for AAV, lentivirus, and living cells due to low shear, but recent views point out the assumption that hollow fiber is inherently softer than cassettes as an error and believe that choosing a format based on shear sensitivity alone can harm process efficiency and manufacturing economics (Source). ⚠️ Format selection should be judged by fluid dynamics, yield, and scale, and there is no general solution. Second, it is a trade-off whether to go with batch recycling TFF, which is mature in verification and robustness for enrichment and buffer exchange, or SPTFF, which reduces shear and time and opens serialization. In SPTFF, process control and verification maturity are still key, so adoption is dependent on scale, product modality, and serialization strategy. Third, where to look at the “optimal” concentration for high-concentration diafiltration — looking at hydraulics (flux/volume efficiency) alone, a high concentration (120 g/L) is advantageous, but when the Donnan correction is reflected, the diavolume rapidly increases (43 vs 17) and the optimal concentration is calculated to be much lower at 57 g/L, so the conclusion is more than twice as different depending on the definition of “optimal.”

Unresolved Questions: - Detailed operating parameters (resin, pH, conductivity, yield) of final polishing chromatography are not re-studied in this section and are delegated to node 3.1 — there is a large overlap in jurisdiction with 3.1. - Direct quotation from Merck/Sigma/Cytiva primary vendor page due to rendering/timeout not secured — TMP ‘knee’/flux/MWCO figures are presented only as blog/snippet representative range (partially confirmed). - Quantitative parameters of large containers/cryovessels for bulk DS freeze-thaw and quantitative yield/membrane selection of vaccine TFF are secured only at the snippet level (node ​​4.4/9.3 and remit overlap).

4. Drug Product — formulation, fill-finish & packaging

Conversion of bulk Drug Substance into the final sterile, packaged, distributable Drug Product (CTD 3.2.P).

4.1 Formulation development

Purpose and role: Steps to convert bulk into “administrable drug”Formulation development is the step of determining the composition of buffer, pH, excipients and physical form (liquid vs. freeze-dried) that will protect the molecules from aggregation and degradation in order to change the bulk drug substance (DS) from the purification process into a stable and administrable drug product (DP). In the E2E flow, this section receives the upstream tablet/UF-DF bulk DS and target product characteristics (TPP: administration route IV/SC, target concentration, storage/distribution conditions) as input, outputs an optimized formulation composition and stability data package, and passes it to aseptic filling/finishing (node ​​4.2) and container closure system/CCIT (node ​​4.3). Additionally, the mRNA-LNP (9.1) and Viral Vector/CGT (9.2) sections depend on the output of this section.

It is necessary to point out a common misunderstanding here. Formulation development is not a pure downstream activity that begins only after purification, but is treated as an activity that must be performed in parallel with process development (Source). ⚠️ The developability of the molecule should be evaluated at an early stage, so that waste of returning candidate materials when aggregation and viscosity problems are revealed late can be reduced.

Principle and flow: Identification of decomposition path → Selection of solution environment/form → Verification of stability

The logical framework of formulation development is the flow of “identify the decomposition path of the molecule → select the solution environment (buffer/pH/excipient) and physical form (liquid/lyophilized) that inhibits the path → verify with stability data.”

The first step is pre-formulation/development evaluation. Thermal stability (Tm, DSC/nanoDSF), colloidal stability (coagulation temperature Tagg, diffusion interaction coefficient kD, secondary virial coefficient B22), pH and buffer solution screening are performed, and major degradation pathways such as aggregation, fragmentation, deamidation/isomerization, and oxidation are identified through forced degradation. Forced decomposition is a development-stage test that intentionally attacks molecules, subjecting it to harsh conditions such as relative humidity above 75%, wide pH, oxidation, photolysis, and stirring/heat combination (Source).⚠️ The purpose of intentionally causing decomposition is to establish a stability-indicating analysis method that can detect the decomposition.

Second, buffer and pH selection. The principle is to control electrostatic attraction, aggregation, and viscosity by selecting a pH that deviates from the isoelectric point (pI) of the protein. For monoclonal antibodies (mAb), histidine buffer has become the standard because it has adequate buffering capacity in the pH range of 5.5 to 6.5, which is often optimal for antibody stability (Source). In addition, acetic acid, citric acid, and phosphate buffer solutions are screened as candidates.

Third, it is a combination of the six major categories of excipients. Buffers, salts, amino acids (arginine, histidine, methionine), polyols/disaccharides (sucrose, trehalose), surfactants (polysorbate 20/80), and antioxidants belong to these categories, and each category protects molecules through different physicochemical mechanisms. Sugars such as sucrose and trehalose are representative ingredients that stabilize proteins during freeze-drying and storage through a preferential exclusion mechanism (Source). Arginine is a multifunctional excipient that prevents aggregation by inhibiting electrostatic protein-protein interactions and suppressing conformational changes in addition to preferential exclusion (Source). Polysorbate, a surfactant, stabilizes proteins by preventing adsorption to the container surface and air-liquid interface, where proteins are prone to unfolding and aggregation, and is the most widely used surfactant in biological pharmaceuticals (Source). To this, antioxidants such as methionine, which scavenges reactive oxygen species (ROS), and salts, which shield electric charges, are added.

Fourth, multifactor optimization. Because the interaction cannot be captured by changing these many variables one by one, in practice, the interaction of pH × buffer × excipient × concentration is simultaneously evaluated through Design of Experiments (DoE) and high-throughput (HT) automated screening is used together.

Fifth, liquid vs. freeze-dried crystal. The general principle is clear. If the molecule has low stability in the liquid phase, a freeze-dried formulation is selected (Source).⚠️ Freeze-drying forms a glassy matrix with lio/cryoprotectants such as sucrose and trehalose and is performed in a cycle of freezing → primary drying (removing ice by sublimation) → secondary drying (removing residual moisture), and the reconstitution time is one. It becomes a critical quality attribute (CQA).

Lastly, there is stability verification. The expiration date and final formulation are confirmed through SEC/MALS (monomer/aggregation), IEX/LC-MS (charge mutation/oxidation), light shielding/flow imaging (subvisible particle), and efficacy tests under real-time (2~8°C), acceleration (25°C/40°C), and stress (freeze/thaw/stirring/light) conditions.

Key parameters and materials

Representative parameters and properties subject to management include pH (typically 5.5 to 6.5 for mAb), ionic strength/salt concentration, protein concentration, target viscosity, buffer concentration (generally 10 to 25 mM), surfactant concentration (polysorbate 20/80 approximately 0.01 to 0.1% w/v), osmotic pressure (isotonic pressure around 300 mOsm/kg), and heat/colloid. There are indicators (Tm, Tagg, kD, B22). In particular, subcutaneous (SC) formulations for self-administration usually require a protein concentration of 100 to 150 mg/mL or more, and the rapid increase in viscosity in this area is a fundamental challenge. In fact, antibodies show excellent biophysical properties under diluted conditions (e.g., <10 mg/mL), but when concentrated beyond 100 mg/mL, their viscoelasticity changes dramatically. In the literature, a viscosity exceeding 20 cP at pH 5.2 > 100 mg/mL is classified as high viscosity ([Source] (https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC10939158/)). To ensure injectability, the target viscosity is usually set to less than 20 cP.

Main materials and reagents include histidine, acetic acid, citric acid, and phosphate buffer solutions, sucrose and trehalose as lioprotectants, arginine for viscosity reduction and aggregation inhibition and antioxidant methionine, surfactant polysorbate 20/80 (ultra-refined grade, low peroxide content), and NaCl for isotonic and charge shielding. Measurement equipment includes DSC/nanoDSF for Tm measurement, DLS for particle size/kD, SEC-MALS for aggregation/monomer, IEX/icIEF/LC-MS for charge mutation/oxidation, optical shielding (LO)/flow imaging (FI) for subvisible particles, viscometer, and HT robotic/microfluidic screening platform. In summary, the starting point is bulk DS + TPP, and the output of this step is the formulation composition (concentration, buffer, pH, list of excipients) and stability data package.

Quality attributes and regulations

Representative CQAs managed in formulation development include high molecular weight aggregates (HMW, SEC/MALS), low molecular weight fragments (LMW), charged variants (deamidation, isomerization, C-terminal lysine, etc., IEX/icIEF/LC-MS), oxidation (Met/Trp), subvisible/visible particles (USP <787>/<788>/<789>, <790>), Potency, pH, osmotic pressure, content, appearance, and for freeze-dried products, residual moisture and reconstitution time. The stability indicator analysis method is established through the aforementioned forced decomposition, and 5 to 20% decomposition is usually targeted to verify the chromatographic analysis method.

At the center of the regulatory axis is ICH Q5C. This guidance believes that a biopharmaceutical stability program should not simply follow time and temperature, but should be mechanism-aware, attribute-centric and aware of degradation mechanisms and quality attributes (Source). The reference point for the expiration date also varies depending on the product type. For most liquid mAbs, long-term storage at 2~8℃ is the standard for determining the expiration date, and for concentrates or gene therapy intermediates, frozen storage at −20℃/−70℃ is the standard (Source).

The regulatory landscape has recently been integrated and modernized. The new ICH Q1 integrated draft, which unites the existing Q5C and Q1A(R2), seeks to encompass stability requirements for synthetic APIs, biologics, vaccines, gene/cell therapy, conjugates, ATMPs, drug-device complex preparations, and enclosed diluents in one framework (Source).⚠️ In addition, Q6B in specifications/test items, Q8 in QbD-based design space, Q5A(R2) of virus safety, regional pharmacopeia (USP, Ph.Eur.) and FDA/EMA review, and CTD 3.2.P structure are applied together. Container closure integrity (USP <1207>) is addressed in adjacent node 4.3.

Differences by product type

In mAb/recombinant protein, the so-called “platform formulation” of histidine (or acetic acid/citric acid) buffer + polysorbate + sugar/amino acid combination is standard (Source). In high-concentration SC formulations, viscosity and self-association are the key challenges, and priority is given to the liquid phase, but if it is unstable, go to freeze-drying.

In mRNA-LNP, the concept of “excipient” is different. In fact, the four lipids (ionized lipid/DSPC/cholesterol/PEG-lipid) themselves are the framework of the formulation, to which Tris buffer and sugar are added. In terms of composition, the ratio (N/P) of ionized lipid to mRNA is usually maintained at about 6:1, and the pKa of ionized lipid is in the range of 6.0 to 7.0 (Source). ⚠️ A characteristic of manufacturing is pH switching. To aid encapsulation, particles are formed at low pH (~4.0) and then quickly adjusted to neutral (~7.4) for storage, and Tris buffer is widely used due to its low pH fluctuation during freezing and freeze-drying (Source).⚠️ Management focus also shifts from protein aggregation to lipid oxidation, mRNA hydrolysis, particle size, and encapsulation efficiency. Storage conditions are stringent, and many commercial mRNA vaccines require ultra-cold storage at −60 to −90°C, which limits distribution logistics (Source). ⚠️ Promising measurements are emerging from the freeze-drying approach to solve this limitation. mRNA-LNPs freeze-dried with 8% sucrose maintained a HEK-293T transduction efficiency of 98% even after 12 weeks at 4°C (Source).

In viral vector (AAV)/CGT, the key is to prevent capsid aggregation, surface adsorption, and freeze-thaw damage. In practice, to prevent loss of potency due to surface adsorption, PBS + 0.001% Pluronic F68 (nonionic surfactant poloxamer 188) is used, and glycerol is commonly added as a cryopreservation agent (Source). 2.5 to 10% sucrose may be added here. Meanwhile, autologous cell therapy products are frozen and stored in a cryopreservation solution containing DMSO, so the formulation concept itself is replaced by “cryopreservation,” and in classical vaccines, aluminum adjuvant adsorption becomes the center of the formulation.

Practices and changes

The current standard is a platform formulation approach combining HT automated screening + DoE + QbD, exemplified by the histidine/polysorbate/sucrose combination of mAb (Source). The movement over the past three years can be seen in four ways. First, as the demand for high-concentration SC formulations (≥150 mg/mL, viscosity <20 cP) for self-administration grows, viscosity reduction strategies using multifunctional excipients such as arginine and co-processed excipients from partner companies, and machine learning viscosity prediction based on hundreds of mAb actual measurement datasets are emerging (reported about 30% viscosity reduction at 200 mg/mL IgG1; Source). Second, it is a super-refined polysorbate that suppresses subvisible particles originating from oxidation and peroxides. Third, research to reduce dependence on ultra-low temperature cold chains through mRNA-LNP freeze-drying is rapidly increasing (Source). Fourth, from a regulatory perspective, revision of the integrated draft of ICH Q1 series and Q5C, Q5A(R2) is in progress (Source).⚠️

It is also worth looking at the major supply chains. Large suppliers of excipients include Merck KGaA, BASF, Roquette, Evonik, and Croda, and high-purity sugar and amino acids include Pfanstiehl. Representative technologies include Croda’s Super Refined polysorbate, which removes impurities through a proprietary purification process to ensure performance consistency between batches, and ultra-refined grade surfactants improve long-term stability (Source). ⚠️ In the high-concentration formulation platform/CDMO, WuXi Biologics (WuXiHigh), Lonza, and Catalent are active.However, there are clear limitations that remain unresolved. These are problems with viscosity, injectability, and manufacturability at high concentrations, particle formation due to enzymatic/oxidative decomposition of polysorbate, lack of room temperature stability of mRNA-LNP, and inaccuracy of prediction models that extrapolate low concentration measurements to ultra-high concentrations.

Issue

Issue:In this section, the data diverges on three points. (1) Basic choice of liquid vs. freeze-drying. On the one hand, freeze-drying is considered to be advantageous from a stability perspective because it significantly extends the shelf life by removing moisture and is resistant to temperature fluctuations and light (preferred for unstable molecules). On the other hand, freeze-drying is time-consuming and expensive, and there is a burden of reconstitution and risk of loss of activity, so as long as stability is ensured, liquid is considered better in terms of patient convenience and manufacturing costs. This is a compromise between stability gains and manufacturing/administration convenience/cost, and the conclusion varies depending on the intrinsic stability of the molecule and TPP. (2) Standard storage form of mRNA-LNP. While ultra-low temperatures of −60 to −90°C are the de facto standard for commercial mRNA vaccines, freeze-dried mRNA-LNP maintains encapsulation and transduction efficiency at 4°C for more than 12 weeks, showing potential as a cold chain replacement. This is a time gap between commercial performance and the latest research; freeze-drying is an emerging approach that has not yet become a commercial standard. (3) Resolution path to the high-concentration mAb viscosity problem. There is a conflict between the view that viscosity is a result of electrostatic and hydrophobic self-association resulting from sequence and structure, so it must be fundamentally solved through protein engineering (sequence optimization), and the view that lowering it with formulation additives such as arginine and multifunctional excipients is a realistic solution. The difference is whether the intervention point is the molecular design stage or the formulation stage, and in a situation where the candidate material is already fixed, there is no choice but to rely on the formulation approach.

Open Questions:Specific dosage form dosing tables for specific commercial products (e.g., commercial mAbs/vaccines) are only partially covered within the scope of this section, and engineering design and control details of the freeze-drying cycle (freezing rate, annealing, temperature-pressure profile of primary/secondary drying) are covered in greater depth in adjacent node 4.2 (Aseptic Filling/Finishing).

4.2 Aseptic fill-finish, sterilization & lyophilization

Role and scope — the end point of manufacturing

Aseptic fill-finish is the final step in manufacturing, where the formulated bulk drug substance (Drug Substance) from the previous step (Node 4.1, formulation development) is filled and sealed in the primary container in a sterile state, turning it into a final drug product (CTD 3.2.P). The starting point comes in two ways. One is a formulated bulk DS, and the other is a primary container and stopper (vial, stopper, prefilled syringe (PFS), cartridge) that has been cleaned, sterilized, and deheated, or a ready-to-use (RTU) member that has already been sterilized. After receiving these, sterile filtration → Grade A aseptic filling → stoppering → (optional) freeze-drying → capping/crimping → 100% visual inspection is performed to produce a sealed sterile finished drug product, which is then passed on to container closure integrity testing (CCIT, node 4.3) and cold chain/distribution (node ​​4.4).

The general principle that governs this step is that “sterility cannot be granted by testing the finished product; it must be built in during the process.” Therefore, regulation and design efforts are focused on this section of the entire manufacturing process with the highest risk of contamination. In fact, the standard flow of aseptic fill-finish of biological drugs is to remove microorganisms through 0.2 µm sterile filtration, then aseptically fill in an ISO 5/Grade A environment, cap and seal, and finish with an external inspection. Vials, PFS, cartridges, and ampoules are representative primary containers (Source). For heat-resistant products, terminal sterilization (sterilization in a final sealed state) is a priority, but most biological drugs are weak to heat, so aseptic processing has no choice but to be selected.

How It Works — Five Step Flow

The entire process is organized into five steps: ‘Determination of sterilization method → Sterilization of components and products → Grade A aseptic filling and sealing → Freeze-drying (if necessary) → Verification of sterility’.

(1) Determination of sterilization strategy.Regulations require that terminal sterilization, which is highly reproducible and less susceptible to post-sterilization contamination, be given priority “to the extent possible.” However, the moist heat cycle is designed with an F0 lethal value rather than a fixed temperature of 121℃, and F0 ≈ 12-15 minutes is commonly used as the so-called overkill benchmark, and heat-resistant spores Geobacillus stearothermophilus are used as biological indicators (BI) for verification (Source). However, heat-sensitive molecules such as proteins, mRNA, and viral vectors cannot withstand this cycle, so aseptic processing followed by sterilization filtration is virtually the only route.

(2) Absence/Product sterilization.After washing, the vial and stopper undergo dry heat deheating or moist heat sterilization, and the product is filtered through a sterilization grade filter to remove microorganisms. Annex 1 specifies that products that cannot be terminally sterilized must be filtered using a sterilization grade filter with a nominal pore size of 0.22 µm or less (Source). Furthermore, PUPSIT (Pre-Use Post-Sterilization Integrity Test), which sterilizes filters immediately before filtration and verifies integrity before use, is a basic expectation, but alternative approaches are allowed when justified by a thorough risk assessment (Source). By using the RTU sterile nest member, on-site cleaning and sterilization can be omitted.(3) Aseptic filling. The exposed product is protected and filled with unidirectional airflow in the ISO 5/Grade A critical area. The concept of First Air is key here, which refers to the flow of filtered air that must be uninterrupted before reaching the exposed product (Source). In all Grade A sections of open isolators and RABS, unidirectional airflow (UDAF) is mandatory and wind speed is controlled to 0.45 m/s ±20% (Source). Filling is quantified based on volume or weight using peristaltic pump, rotating piston, and time-pressure methods, and is managed by weight in-process inspection (IPC), followed by stoppering and capping/crimping. Capping and crimping are also considered part of the aseptic process.(4) Freeze drying (optional). The vials in a partially stoppering state are loaded into the freeze dryer and undergo three stages. First, in the freezing step, the formulation is completely frozen below Tg’ (glass transition temperature), and then the ice crystals are rearranged through annealing — that is, holding the formulation at a certain temperature above Tg’ for a certain period of time — to reduce subsequent drying time. In GMP production, controlled nucleation (pressure-drop method) is adopted (Source). In the first drying, ice is sublimated under vacuum, and the basic principle is to preserve the cake structure by maintaining the product temperature several degrees Celsius lower than the collapse temperature Tc. If the shelf temperature is raised by 1 degree Celsius, the first drying time is shortened by about 13% (Source). In the final secondary drying, the remaining bound water is desorbed but kept below the Tg of the matrix, and then completely stoppered and capped under vacuum or inert gas in the chamber.(5) Assurance of sterility. Worst-case conditions are verified by simulating media fill/APS. Initial qualification requires three consecutive passing badges covering all shifts, and regular re-verification is usually performed twice a year by line, shift, and process (Source). Each operator maintains aseptic processing qualifications by participating in media filling initially and at least once a year (Source). Cultivation is carried out in two stages: 7 days at 20-25℃, then 7 days at 30-35℃, for a total of 14 days or more to induce fungal and bacterial growth respectively (Source). ⚠️ Here, environmental monitoring (suspended, settled, surface microorganisms, non-biological particles), worker monitoring, and gowning qualification are carried out in parallel, and at the end, 100% external inspection is performed to inspect particles and bacteria. Filter out defects.

Key parameters and materials

Cleanliness level and microbial limits.The critical area for aseptic filling is Grade A, requiring no growth, and the background grade varies depending on the type of barrier. The background of RABS for aseptic processing must be at least Grade B, the background of open isolators must be at least Grade C, and the background of closed isolators must be at least Grade D (Source). The UDAF wind speed standard for Grade A is the previously mentioned 0.45 m/s ±20% (Source).Sterile filtration and heat sterilization. Sterile filtration combines PUPSIT and post-use integrity test (bubble point/diffusion flow rate) on a sterilization grade filter of 0.22 µm or less (Source). The reference point for heat sterilization is moist heat at 121℃ class F0 ≈ 12~15 minutes and SAL 10^-6 (Source), and the standard cycle for dry heat deheating is 250℃/30 minutes, which reduces endotoxin by more than 3-log (1000 times), and the sterilization itself has SAL 10^-6. Demand (Source). In other words, it should be noted that ‘sterilization’ and ‘deheating’ are separate processes with different goals.Freeze drying parameters. For primary drying, cake uniformity is optimal at a medium chamber pressure, specifically, the uniformity is best in the range of 100 to 150 mTorr (Source). Secondary drying is performed at 20-40°C, 60-150 mTorr, and 3-12 hours while maintaining below the matrix Tg (Source). Sugars are used as stabilizers, and sugars such as sucrose and trehalose are the most effective, and it is reported that stability improves as the molar ratio of stabilizer to protein increases (Source).⚠️

Badge charging passing criteria.The goal of badge charging is always zero contamination. In particular, if the batch is less than 5000 units, it is subject to revalidation if even one contaminated unit is found (Source). The culture protocol is a two-step process of 7 days at 20-25℃ + 7 days at 30-35℃ as described above for a total of 14 days (Source).⚠️

Input-output organization.The starting point is the formulated bulk DS and the sterile primary container/closure (or RTU nest), and the output of this step is a sealed sterile drug product — a liquid vial/PFS/cartridge, or a lyophilized cake.

Quality/Regulatory Frame

Representative CQAs managed at this stage include sterility (USP <71> sterility test, SAL 10^-6 guarantee), bacterial endotoxin (USP <85>/BET), container closure integrity (CCI, details node 4.3), filling volume/weight, visible/invisible particles (USP <787>/<788>/<790>), content/efficacy, and in the case of freeze-dried products. These are residual moisture, reconstitution time, and cake appearance (Source). The management and testing method that supports this consists of sterilization filtration integrity (PUPSIT + after use), verification of F0 and endotoxin reduction of heat sterilization and depyrogenation, passing standards for media filling, environmental and worker monitoring and trending, gowning and intervention qualifications, and 100% external inspection.

At the center of the governing regulations and standards is EU GMP Annex 1. It was revised in 2022 and took effect on August 25, 2023, and only the provisions related to loading and unloading freeze dryers were postponed to August 25, 2024. In addition, FDA’s ‘Sterile Drug Products Produced by Aseptic Processing’ (2004), PIC/S PE 009 Annex 1, ISO 14644 (clean room classification), USP multiple chapters (<71>/<85>/<788>/<790>/<1207>/<1211>/<1228>), Ph.Eur., CTD 3.2.P applies. The high-level integrated requirement that runs through Annex 1 is the Contamination Control Strategy (CCS), which requires covering the entire manufacturing chain from raw materials to components and packaging materials (Source).

Differences by product type

Even for the same aseptic filling, practices vary depending on the product type. mAb/recombinant proteins are filled as standard aseptics, liquid vials/PFS/cartridges are used as standard, and unstable molecules are freeze-dried. Platformized RABS/isolator charging is already mature.mRNA-LNP is aseptically filled with a lipid nanoparticle suspension under Grade A. Since the particle size is around 80-100 nm, the particles cannot be filtered out through sterilization filtration, so terminal sterilization is not possible and instead relies on sterilization filtration and bioburden management. Cryogenic liquids are the current commercial standard, but freeze-drying is emerging to reduce cold chain burden.Viral vectors (AAV·lenti) have similar fillings, but cell-based products cannot be sterilized and filtered, so they rely heavily on closed systems and disposable assemblies. In autologous cell therapy (CGT), the nature of fill-finish completely changes. ‘Filling/sealing’ is replaced by small cryovial/bag dispensing and DMSO cryopreservation, and the batch is scale-out for one patient, so ultra-small and multi-type filling is required, so the flexibility of the gloveless robot isolator is especially attractive. Classical vaccines are commonly available in multi-dose vials and large batches, live vaccines are freeze-dried, and terminal sterilization routes are possible for some components or adjuvants.

Practices and changes

The current standard is semi-automatic aseptic filling performed under Grade A UDAF inside a RABS or isolator, plus media filling verification and environmental and operator monitoring. The center of gravity has shifted over the past three years, and the biggest driver is the implementation of Annex 1 in 2023. The revised Annex 1 requires documented contamination control strategies (CCS) and emphasizes First Air/UDAF. According to industry commentary, the revisions require manufacturers to have RABS as a minimum for new product approvals and isolators are becoming the ‘new normal’ (Source).⚠️

The second trend is the rise of gloveless robotic isolators. The logic behind this approach is clear — if you can separate workers from the production process, you remove the number one factor in drug contamination from the equation (Source). Approximately 95% of contact points are eliminated by eliminating intervention points such as conveyors, vibrating balls, and star wheels, and handling risks and glass-to-glass contact particles are reduced because the robot only handles pre-sterilized nested RTU members rather than containers and closures individually (Source). Thirdly, the spread of RTU freeze-drying nest members has unified the handling of vials, PFS, and cartridges, reducing changeover and making it easier to respond to CGT and personalized small batches. Fourthly, there is a movement to quantify freeze-drying cycle design and transfer through primary drying mechanism modeling, design space, continuous freeze-drying, and digital twin.

There are still clear limitations that remain. The risk-versus-benefit debate of PUPSIT, scale-up and transfer reproducibility of the freeze-drying cycle, glove integrity, particle management in expensive small batches, and limitations in statistical sensitivity of media filling acceptance criteria are considered unresolved issues.

Issue: Point of conflicting evidence

In this section, there are three issues that directly clash between regulatory philosophy and practice.

PUPSIT mandatory debate.EU/Annex 1 basically requires integrity testing before use to detect damage during sterilization, transportation, and installation or flaw masking during filtration. On the other hand, the US industry and regulations take the opposite stance, and believe that by forcing PUPSIT to manipulate the sterilized filtrate side, it violates the highest principle of sterility, ‘Do not touch the sterilized filtrate side’, and actually increases the risk of contamination (Source). The essence of the conflict lies in how each region and regulatory philosophy weighs the net benefit between the theoretical benefit of detecting defects and the risk of contamination caused by actual manipulation.Isolator vs RABS. Annex 1 promotes isolators that provide a higher sterility guarantee through automatic biological decontamination and complete separation of workers, but RABS has low introduction and conversion costs and is flexible for multiple products and frequent changeovers, while isolators have a long preparation time due to the VHP biological decontamination cycle. It is a compromise between the level of sterility assurance and capital cost, flexibility, and operation rate, and the conclusion varies depending on the product portfolio (single large quantity vs. small quantity of various types) and regulatory expectations.Terminal sterilization vs. aseptic process. Annex 1 applies regulatory pressure to prioritize terminal sterilization, which has superior SAL and reproducibility, as much as possible, but most proteins, mRNA-LNPs, and cell products cannot withstand moist heat and radiation, so sterilization filtration + aseptic processing is virtually the only route. Regulatory preference for the highest assurance method conflicts with the physical tolerance limits of the molecule, and product stability data ultimately determines the choice.Unresolved Questions: The detailed steps of gowning (sterile gowning, qualification, and re-qualification) and the gown qualification practical protocol only cover high-level requirements (worker badge-filling qualifications) and are shallow on specific procedures; Quantitative comparison of accuracy, shear, and product suitability of specific charging methods (peristaltic pump vs. rotating piston vs. time-pressure) is only outlined; Container Closure Integrity (CCI) test method details are intentionally transferred to adjacent node 4.3.

4.3 Container closure system & CCIT

Purpose and role: Maintaining sterility is not a test but a barrier

The finished drug product that has passed aseptic filling/finishing (node 4.2) must now be “contained and protected.” The container closure system (CCS), the core object of this step, refers to the entire combination of the primary container and closure. Taking a vial as an example, the vial body, elastomer stopper, and aluminum seal with or without a flip-off button form one CCS, and the stopper is deformed by crimping to seal the vial-stopper interface (Source). In addition to vials, prefilled syringes (PFS), cartridges, BFS/FFS fusing containers, and ampoules are all included in the CCS format.

This section has two interconnected missions. One is to select and qualify CCS as a sterile barrier and a member that is chemically compatible with the product and can withstand filling, freeze-drying, and administration functions, and the other is to demonstrate the physical integrity of the barrier through Container Closure Integrity Testing (CCIT) throughout the entire life cycle from development to verification, commercialization, and stability. The principle that goes through here is the same as node 4.2. Sterility is not confirmed by final testing but is maintained by a barrier, and 4.3 quantitatively guarantees the integrity of that barrier. Because of this principle, CCS qualification must be performed with absent options and platforms that will actually be used in the final product formulation, and appropriate CCI evaluation requires a comprehensive understanding of the CCS as a whole (Source). The output of this step (qualified CCS specifications and CCI data) is the premise for maintaining integrity under temperature and transport stress in the cold chain and distribution (node ​​4.4) and the basis for 3.2.P packaging and stability in the CMC document (node ​​8.1).

Operation method: From member selection to proof of life cycle completeness

The overall flow continues as “CCS member selection and compatibility → capping/sealing qualifications → CCIT method selection (determinism first) → correlation with MALL → life cycle CCI verification.”Selection of materials and sealing qualifications.Materials are selected based on product compatibility (extract/eluate, pH, adsorption), function (glide force/resealability), and sterilization resistance. Crimping compresses the stopper to provide residual seal force (RSF), which is inherently time-dependent and decreases exponentially over time due to the elastomer’s Compression Stress Relaxation (CSR), eventually flattening out (Source). More important is the effect of temperature. The reduction in sealing force due to low temperature is much greater than this time-dependent relaxation and has a markedly detrimental effect on sealing performance (Source). So regulators view capping as part of the aseptic process and require data to qualify the correlation between stopper height (seating) and good CCI (Source). ⚠️

The crossroads of determinism and probability.CCIT methods are largely divided into two series. While deterministic leak testing is based on physical phenomena in which leak events to be detected and measured follow a predictable chain of events, probabilistic methods produce qualitative results by relying on a chain of random events described by a probability distribution (Source). Because of this structural difference, the FDA and industry clearly prefer deterministic methods, which give quantitative, reproducible, and objective results, over probabilistic methods (Source). It is explicitly pointed out that the dye ingress test, a representative probabilistic method, is not sufficiently verified, reliable, and sensitive to verify overall packaging integrity, and the results are variable and subjective (Source).

The principles and detection capabilities of representative determinism techniques are as follows. Vacuum attenuation calculates leakage by changing the vacuum of the sealed chamber, follows ASTM F2338·FDA consensus standard, and detects defects up to 0.002 cc/min (Source). Among high voltage leak detection (HVLD), the microcurrent method detects pinholes, microcracks, and seal defects at the µm level and is applied to a wide range of liquid products, from ultra-low conductivity water for injection (WFI) to protein products (Source). Laser headspace analysis measures the gas composition in a sealed container using laser absorption spectroscopy, and headspace changes indicate integrity damage, and is characterized by being non-destructive (Source). In addition, helium leak mass spectrometry (highest sensitivity based on tracer gas) and mass extraction belong to the determinism series.

MALL connects sterility.The decisive basis for preferring the deterministic method is specified in the standard. USP <1207>, revised in 2016, stipulates that “deterministic methods with the ability to detect leaks of the size of the product’s Maximum Allowable Leakage Limit (MALL) are preferred to establish the inherent integrity of the container closure system” (Source). Physical leakage values ​​are translated into microbial integrity via this MALL. The acceptable MALL criticality for rigid containers (where headspace maintenance is not critical) is approximately 6 ⚠️

Life cycle CCI.During the development phase, the sensitivity and detection limits of the method are established with defective positive controls made from laser drills and micropipes, and the CCI is demonstrated at the time of validation and commercial release and stability. In this life cycle approach, requirements vary depending on the sealing method. While EU GMP Annex 1 requires 100% CCIT by batch for fusion-sealed containers (BFS cards/bottles, glass/plastic ampoules) (Source), if there is a solid management strategy for mechanically sealed containers such as vials/PFS, 100% testing upon release is not necessary and can be replaced by applying verified CCIT within the stability program to confirm shelf life integrity. There is (Source). ⚠️

Key parameters and materials

Container/closure types vary from glass (Type I borosilicate) vial/cartridge/PFS, molded vs. tubing glass, COP/COC plastic, BFS polyethylene/polypropylene, and ampoule, and the stopper side has a bromo/chlorobutyl elastomer stopper (ETFE/PTFE coating/laminate) and an aluminum crimp seal as standard. The core of the sealing parameter is the RSF, which we saw earlier, which decreases exponentially with time and rapidly at low temperatures, and is managed by correlation data from the stopper height/seating sensor and CCI (Source). In terms of detection performance, vacuum attenuation (approximately 0.002 cc/min), microcurrent HVLD (unit: µm), and laser headspace (non-destructive, in-line possible) have different strengths (Source).Materials risk is an area that this section deals particularly heavily with. First, there is glass delamination. The lamellae (flakes) that come off as the inner surface of the glass erodes are small and difficult to detect through visual inspection, and if injected directly into a patient, they can cause serious harm to the vascular system such as emboli and thrombosis (Source). An increase in glass ions (Si·Al·B), especially an increase in Si concentration, is an indicator of decreased durability or the onset of delamination and is tested by USP <1660> (evaluation of inner surface durability of glass containers) (Source). Second, it is silicone oil. It is widely used for lubricating syringe barrels and vial stoppers, but if the product is in direct contact for a long period of time, it may dissolve and affect product purity and efficacy, so precise analysis and detection are important (Source). ⚠️ Third, it is tungsten. Soluble tungsten introduced during glass syringe manufacturing has been shown to be a strong inducer of protein aggregation and may pose an immunogenic risk in patients receiving mAb chronically (Source). ⚠️

Cryogenic parameters form a separate axis in cell and gene therapy. Cell therapy is stored below -130℃ to maintain cell structure integrity, and genetically modified cell therapies such as CAR-T and TCR often require temperatures below -150℃, and liquid nitrogen tanks and dewars are maintained at -135 to -196℃ (Source). ⚠️ The problem is the glass transition temperature of the elastomer (Tg approximately -50℃). Below this temperature, the stopper becomes a glassy solid, which can cause the stopper to shrink and lose elasticity during deep cold storage, resulting in loss of CCI in the vial product (Source). ⚠️

Quality and Regulation: USP <1207> Framework and 2023 Annex 1

CQAs subject to management include container closure integrity (maintenance of sterile barrier), visible and invisible particles (USP <787>/<788>/<790>), extractables and leachables (E&L), elastomer functional synthesis (fragmentation, resealability, sealing, injection power), glass inner durability/delamination, and headspace (oxygen, moisture, pressure). CCIT gives priority to deterministic methods and sets aside stochastic methods such as dye penetration and microbial immersion due to lack of sensitivity and reproducibility. After establishing the detection limit and MALL correlation as a positive control in the development stage, CCI is verified under a stability program and transportation and distribution stress.

The framework of dominant regulations and standards has two axes. One is USP <1207> (and inspection technique selection <1207.1>, leak testing technology <1207.2>, and microbial-based testing <1207.3>), which is the basis of the determinism/MALL framework (Source). Here, the new USP <382>, which deals with the functional synthesis of elastomers, heralds an important change. This standard, which will be implemented on December 1, 2025, shifts the paradigm from the method in which suppliers historically tested members individually (USP <381>) to a method in which manufacturers holistically evaluate members after assembling them into a finished packaging and delivery system (Source). This means that the responsibility for CCS qualification shifts from the absentee supplier to the manufacturer.

The other axis is EU GMP Annex 1 (revised in 2022, effective 2023-08-25). As seen earlier, fusion-sealed containers are subject to 100% CCIT for each batch (Source), and mechanically sealed containers do not require 100% release if the management strategy is solid (Source). ⚠️ A particularly notable change is that visual inspection is no longer recognized as an acceptable integrity testing method (Source). Here, capping/sealing process verification and stopper height-CCI correlation requirements (Source) ⚠️, and USP <660>/<1660>·ASTM F2338 regarding glass delamination work together.

Variants by product type

Although the CCIT method itself (determinism first) is common between modalities, material risks and testing requirements vary depending on container type and temperature stress.

For mAb/recombinant proteins, standard vials (stopper/crimp seal) or PFS/cartridge are standard. Preference for PFS is increasing due to the convenience of self-/assisted administration, but as there are many interfaces with barrels, plungers, tip caps, and needle shields, sterility assurance is more complicated, and the risk of material-protein interactions such as silicone oil, tungsten, and glass delamination increases. Therefore, silicone-less/PTFE-coated stoppers and low-tungsten barrels are used as countermeasures (Source). ⚠️ As cryogenic liquid is the commercial standard for mRNA-LNP, the key is to reduce the stopper sealing ability at low temperatures and maintain CCI, and when converted to freeze-drying, it reverts to vial CCS.In viral vector/autologous cell therapy (CGT), fill-finish is replaced by small cryovials/bag dispensing, and the storage temperature is deep-freezing at -130 to -196°C (Source). ⚠️ In this temperature range, the stopper shrinks and vitrifies below the elastomer Tg (approximately -50℃), making the standard glass vial-stopper system prone to losing CCI (Source). ⚠️ Furthermore, if gases such as CO2 and N2 flow into and accumulate in the headspace during deep cold storage, overpressure may occur after the container regains its integrity by return to temperature, which is a challenge unique to this format (Source). ⚠️ For this reason, closed vials with proven deep cooling (e.g. AT-Closed Vial) and dedicated cryobags and elastomers are selected. For classic vaccines, multi-dose vials (antibacterial preservatives) and large batches are common, and fusion-sealed formats such as BFS and glass ampoules are subject to 100% CCIT for each batch in Annex Phase 1 ([Source] (https://www.wilco.com/use-cases/eu-gmp-annex-1-influence-on-cci-testing-of-bfs-containers/)).

Practices and changes

The current standard is to establish inherent integrity with deterministic CCIT under the life cycle approach of USP <1207>, demonstrate CCI in stability and transport, and inspect 100% of fusion-sealed containers for each batch. The movement of the past three years can be summarized in five directions. First, the transition from probabilism to determinism was regulated. Following the 2016 USP <1207> revision, which specified deterministic methods with MALL detection ability as preferred (Source), the 2023 Annex 1 accelerated this by excluding visual inspection and requiring 100% verified CCIT/fusion sealing (Source). Second, with USP <382> (effective 2025-12-01), CCS qualification practice is being reorganized as responsibility for elastomer functional synthesis is shifted from individual testing by absentee suppliers to holistic evaluation of the “final filling system” by the manufacturer (Source). Third, with the widespread use of non-destructive deterministic techniques (vacuum attenuation, laser headspace, microcurrent HVLD), emission testing is moving from sample to full (100% in-line) testing. Fourth, as deep-cooled CCI has become a verification issue due to stopper shrinkage and Tg problems in cryogenic storage of mRNA and CGT, the development of deep-cooled verification closed containers, dedicated elastomers, and low-temperature CCIT is in progress. Fifth, materials such as silicone-less/cross-linked coating stoppers, delamination-resistant glass (internal treatment/COP), and low-tungsten PFS continue to be reduced.

The remaining limitations are clear. These are the burden of justifying the MALL correlation that translates physical leakage values ​​into microbial integrity for each product, the applicability of deterministic methods in specific formulations such as high viscosity, suspension, and low conductivity liquids, CCI reproducibility in deep cooling and restorative cycles, PFS multi-interface sterility assurance, and 100% CCIT capacity and cost due to exclusion of visual inspection.

Issue

Issue 1 — Does deterministic CCIT, which measures physical leakage, actually guarantee microbial integrity?USP <1207>·FDA·Annex 1 considers that quantitative, reproducible, and objective deterministic methods have replaced and improved stochastic microbial and dye penetration tests. However, the counterargument points out that only the microbial penetration challenge directly measures the result directly related to safety (sterility), and that deterministic physical leakage values ​​are translated into sterility only through “correlation” with the probability of bacterial penetration (e.g. Kirsch limit), so product-specific MALL correlation justification is necessary. The translation step intervenes between the surrogate indicator (physical leakage rate) and the actual safety result (exclusion of microorganisms), and judgments differ as to how much of the reliability and justification burden should be imposed on the correlation.Issue 2 — Should in-line 100% CCIT be required for all sterile products? One side believes that Annex 1 mandates 100% batch-specific fusion sealing, and that 100% inspection has become technically possible with the commercialization of non-destructive determinism techniques. On the other hand, for mechanical seals (vials, PFS), if the management strategy is solid, 100% emission is unnecessary and statistical CCIT within the stability program is sufficient, and 100% inspection has a large burden of capacity, cost, and product exposure. Differences in the failure risk profile of sealing methods (fusion vs. mechanical) are at the root of this confrontation.Issue 3 — Whether to maintain or remove the silicone oil of PFS. Silicone oil is essential for transmission functions such as plunger glide force and injection force as barrel and stopper lubrication, and has a long commercial track record. On the other hand, silicone oil dissolves to create subvisible particles and increases the risk of protein aggregation and immunogenicity, so siliconeless/PTFE-coated/crosslink-coated stoppers are preferred for sensitive biopharmaceuticals. Because the same member causes both function (lubrication) and risk (agglomeration/particles), the trade-off between product sensitivity and delivery device requirements varies.

Unresolved Questions

  • Integrated comparison table comparing quantitative sensitivity and detection limits of specific deterministic methods (vacuum attenuation vs. HVLD vs. helium leak vs. laser headspace) by dosage form and container. Individual figures were obtained, but the integrated comparison was only superficially covered.
  • Details of the primary package extraction and leachable (E&L) test protocol (USP <1663>/<1664>, controlled extraction·AET). Since it overlaps with adjacent node 5.3 (single-use E&L), it was only briefly covered.
  • PFS multi-interface (tip cap, plunger) sterility assurance and quantitative practice of CCI combined with auto-injector and delivery device. Only the outline was covered.

4.4 Cold chain, storage & distribution (GDP)

Roles and Positions — The last step that adds no value but can be destructive.

The cold chain, storage, and distribution (GDP, Good Distribution Practice) node is the stage where aseptic filling is completed and the final drug product (DP) contained in the container closure system (node 4.3) is physically moved and stored from the manufacturing site to the final point of use (pharmacy, hospital, patient) by controlling temperature, quality, and traceability to ensure that it does not exceed the storage conditions specified on the label. From the perspective of E2E flow, this section receives three inputs from upstream. (a) Finished DP subject to release, (b) Label storage conditions and expiration date calculated by the stability program (ICH Q1A/Q5C, node 6.5), and (c) Serialization unique identifier assigned to each unit. This section delivers product to the point of use via a validated, GDP-compliant pathway and is downstream from batch release (node ​​7.6) and regulatory oversight (node ​​8).The essential characteristic of this step is that although it does not add new physical value to the product, it can instantly destroy the value built up by all previous processes. Therefore, the key output is not the product itself, but rather a ‘temperature and tracking data record that proves that integrity has been maintained’ and a disposition decision to determine whether to ‘use, quarantine, or dispose of’ the product when a temperature deviation occurs. Here, it is necessary to distinguish between two terms that are often confused in practice. Validation is an activity that confirms whether the storage chamber actually produces the expected results during transport, and qualification is an activity that proves that the system, transport container, or organization meets a specific purpose (Source). ⚠️

How it works — the cycle from defining conditions to disposing of deviations

The mechanism of this section is clear when understood as a circular loop. It is a repetition of ‘define requirements using product stability data → confirm the suitability of the storage, packaging, and transportation system that maintains and verifies the conditions → detect deviations through real-time monitoring → dispose of based on stability data in case of deviation.’

(1) Establishment of requirements.The storage temperature and expiration date written on the label are not arbitrarily determined but are derived from ICH stability tests (real-time, accelerated, stress). This stability data defines the ‘temperature and time budget’ that the product can handle, and becomes the basis for all subsequent judgments of deviation.(2) Storage. GDP warehouse identifies temperature distribution and hot/cold spots inside the facility through temperature mapping, then stores products in constant temperature facilities equipped with automatic monitoring and alarms.(3) Packaging system. Temperature maintenance methods are largely divided into passive and active. Passive packaging combines insulation materials such as vacuum insulation panels (VIP) and expanded polystyrene (EPS) with phase change material (PCM), gel packs, and dry ice to maintain the temperature for a specified holding time (usually 96 to 120 hours) without external power. Active containers precisely control long-distance and large-capacity cargo through compressor-based cooling. Cryogenic logistics of cell and gene therapy (CGT) is a separate category and uses a dry vapor dry cipher that absorbs liquid nitrogen into a porous wall material.(4) Shipping container verification and transport lane querying. Confirming the eligibility of the packaging itself is an activity that proves through rigorous testing that the required temperature range for the worst-case summer/winter ambient profile can be maintained throughout the entire transport section (Source). ⚠️ In practice, it is divided into thermal chamber testing (OQ) and actual delivery (PQ). Furthermore, the activity of systematically evaluating the temperature performance of a specific transportation route, that is, the ‘lane’ itself, is called lane querying (Source). ⚠️ This is to prove that the route maintains temperature under real conditions such as air, land, transshipment, waiting time, and airport operation.(5) Monitoring. Calibrated data loggers and real-time GPS/temperature sensors record temperature at least every 24 hours (or continuously) and sound an alarm when thresholds are exceeded.(6) Disposal of deviation. Temperature deviation is divided into ‘spike’, which recovers briefly, and ‘plateau’, which lasts for a long time. The cumulative heat load of refrigerated and room temperature products is summarized by the Mean Kinetic Temperature (MKT). MKT is an indicator that integrates the thermal challenge received by the product over various temperatures into one weighted average temperature based on the Arrhenius equation (Source). If the basis for the decision is insufficient, the wholesale authorization holder must seek advice from the marketing authorization holder (MAH) of the drug in question since there is no data to evaluate the impact of the product on its own (Source).(7) Traceability. Each unit is tracked throughout the distribution network with a serialized unique identifier, and in the case of CGT, chain-of-identity/chain-of-custody is added to this.

The power and limitations of MKT — When to use it and when not to use it

MKT is the most important concept in this section, so it is worth mentioning separately. Regulatory guidance broadly supports the use of MKT to evaluate deviations from storage conditions for refrigerated and room temperature products (Source). However, MKT has clear application boundaries. First, MKT cannot be applied to frozen products (Source). Second, MKT does not reflect irreversible quality defects caused by freeze-thaw cycles, physical damage mechanisms, or instantaneous extreme temperature spikes (Source). Third, MKT should not be used to cover up poor temperature management of storage and transportation facilities after the fact, and should only be applied when management is good but intermittent deviations occur (Source). In short, since MKT is an index that assumes chemical and cumulative thermal decomposition, it should always be kept in mind that it may be unsuitable for biological drugs where physical and irreversible damage such as freezing and agglomeration are dominant.

Key parameters and materials — temperature hierarchy and consumables

The framework that runs through this section is the hierarchical structure of storage and transportation temperatures. As a standard, USP defines controlled room temperature (CRT) as 20~25℃, allowable deviation as 15~30℃, and controlled cold storage is usually considered as 2~8℃ (Source). The temperature hierarchy of practice extends downward from here. Refrigeration of 2 to 8°C is the standard for most biological drugs such as mAbs and vaccines, followed by freezing (approximately −15 to −50°C), ultra-cold (−60 to −90°C), and cryogenic (≤−150°C).Key parameters include label storage conditions, expiration date, holding time of manual packaging (typically 96 to 120 hours), summer/winter worst-case ambient profile, MKT value, cumulative deviation time and stability budget, monitoring cycle (more than 24 hours or continuous), and alarm threshold. In terms of materials and consumables, PCM, gel pack, dry ice (maintained at −78.5°C during sublimation, no power required, but classified as IATA dangerous goods), liquid nitrogen, VIP/EPS insulation, and verified shipping boxes are used. Measurements and equipment include digital data loggers (DDL), real-time GPS, temperature, shock, tilt, and light sensors, constant temperature warehouses, freezers, ultra-low temperature chambers, and active refrigerated containers, and calibration is performed regularly based on international standards such as NIST. (Many of the detailed figures, such as dry ice temperature, retention time range, and insulation type, are practical values described in the node summary field, and values without individual source citations should be read as reference values rather than established benchmarks.)

Quality and regulation — ‘preservation’, not ‘transformation’

The quality attributes managed at this stage are different from other processes. This is because the key is not to change something, but to ‘preserve’ the quality that has already been completed. Specifically, compliance with temperature history, prevention of loss of potency, prevention of cohesion, decomposition, and freeze damage, container integrity (no damage or leakage), and traceability and authenticity (prevention of counterfeiting) are subject to management.

The USP <1079> series, the dominant standard in the United States, provides a framework for learners to understand. <1079> describes a risk-based approach and risk mitigation strategy for the storage and transportation of finished pharmaceutical products, and applies to organizations and individuals involved in storage and transportation (Source). Among them, <1079.2> specifically deals with how MKT is applied to temperature deviation evaluation of room temperature (CRT) and controlled refrigeration (CCT) products (Source). Its companion <1118> complements the <1079> guidelines by focusing on qualification and verification of monitoring equipment (Source). In Europe, the corresponding standards are the EU GDP Guideline (2013/C 343/01) and EU GMP Annex 15, and internationally, WHO TRS 961 Annex 9 and WHO PQS are the corresponding standards (these are a list of governing standards organized in the node summary field, without individual source citations), and the stability basis is specified by ICH Q1A(R2)/Q5C. GDP is a downstream quality system that succeeds GMP and is subject to inspection by the FDA, EMA, and member state regulatory agencies.

On the traceability axis, serialization is at the core of regulation. The U.S. DSCSA requires serialization (unique identification) through serial numbers assigned to individual drug packaging/units, and each unit is linked to NDC, lot number, and expiration date (Source). It is important to note that this serialization axis is a physically separate topic from temperature management and strongly overlaps with regulation (node ​​8).

Differences by product type — extends down the temperature hierarchy

Interestingly, this section does not show a collapse in requirements as modalities become more complex, but rather moves toward expanding down the temperature hierarchy and strengthening traceability requirements.

mAb·Recombinant Protein:Most are refrigerated at 2~8℃ as standard and are vulnerable to freezing. Because freezing causes agglomeration and particle formation, ‘Do not freeze’ warnings are common. The proven manual 2~8℃ sipper is the main product, and bulk undiluted solution is transported by freezing at −20/−70℃.mRNA-LNP: This is a representative example of the popular introduction of the third temperature category, ultra-low temperature. The fundamental reason why commercial mRNA vaccines require temperatures of −80°C (Pfizer) or −20°C (Moderna) and rely on dry ice logistics is that the formulation is chemically fragile. The lack of heat stability of mRNA vaccines stems from the fragile nature of the synthetic mRNA itself, which is susceptible to hydrolysis above pH 6 (Source). In addition, the components (DSPC and ionized lipids) of LNP, the delivery chain, are also vulnerable to temperature- and pH-dependent hydrolysis (Source). Therefore, the dependence on cryogenic cold chain is the greatest among all modalities.Viral vector·CGT: Autologous cell therapy is the most vulnerable in terms of logistics because it is a patient-derived, non-substitutable, and hyper-personalized product. The cryogenic sipper maintains temperatures below −150°C for up to 10 days through dry vapor without free liquid nitrogen and meets IATA non-hazardous material standards (Source). ⚠️ Additionally, this cryogenic CGT delivery tracks not only temperature but also direction (tilt), impact, and location in close real-time (Source)⚠️ — This is because identity and storage continuity are essential as it is an irreplaceable product. AAV vectors are typically treated as −60 to −80°C or 2 to 8°C (based on the node summary field).Classical vaccines: The WHO EPI cold chain (2-8℃) is traditional, but aluminum adjuvant vaccines are rather damaged by freezing, so ‘freeze avoidance’ is key, and WHO allows limited room temperature exposure as a controlled temperature chain (CTC) for some products (based on the node summary field).

Practices and changes — real-time, serialization, and thermal stabilization

Current standard practice is the same cycle as described so far. Label conditions defined by stability data are maintained through verified passive (PCM/VIP) or active packaging and transport lane querying, monitored with data loggers, and any deviations are disposed of based on MKT/stability data. Changes over the past three years can be organized into four categories.

First, with the spread of IoT real-time monitoring and cloud cold chain software, there is a transition from post-download method to real-time alarm and intervention. Second, serialization and traceability have been strengthened. DSCSA’s final deadline for full supply chain compliance was originally November 27, 2023, but the FDA delayed enforcement of the electronic interoperability system by one year until November 27, 2024 (Source). Third, with the commercialization of mRNA and CGT, cryogenic and cryogenic infrastructure such as −80℃/LN2 dry vapor logistics network has grown rapidly. Fourth, thermal stabilization research is in progress to reduce dependence on cryogenic temperatures. In fact, freeze-dried RNA-containing LNPs maintained their biophysical properties for 21 months at 4°C and 8 months at room temperature, showing the potential to become an alternative to ultra-low temperature cold chains (Source).

Meanwhile, learners should be aware that vendor marketing figures distributed in practice (e.g., claims that verification sippers reduce deviations by up to 90%) should be treated only as reference values ​​and not established facts.

Issue

There are several points in this section where sources and practice diverge. First, the role and reliability of MKT in disposition of deviations.On the one hand, MKT is believed to be a defensible data indicator based on Arrhenius and broadly supports deviation evaluation of refrigerated and room temperature products and GDP compliance. On the other hand, they warn that MKT cannot handle frozen products, freeze-thaw, or instantaneous spikes, and should not be misused to cover up poor temperature management. These two are not actually a contradiction, but a matter of ‘scope of application’, and the key point is that if the scope is misunderstood, there is a risk of releasing damaged products. Second, it is the standard temperature condition for distribution of mRNA-LNP. Commercial performance shows that ultra-low temperatures of −80°C (Pfizer)/−20°C (Moderna) are the de facto standard, but research on freeze-dried LNPs suggests that they can be distributed without ultra-low temperatures with a stability of 4°C for 21 months. This is the difference between commercial performance and emerging research, and thermal stabilization has not yet become a commercial standard. Third, the choice of passive vs. active packaging. Passive packaging is simple and low-cost as it can withstand 96 to 120 hours without power, but active containers are expensive and have a large power and operating burden in exchange for precise control over long distances, large volumes, and long periods of time. The conclusion varies depending on the trade-off between route length, cargo volume, required precision, and cost/carbon footprint.

Unresolved Questions

  • The quantitative calculation procedure for the specific stability budget (allowable cumulative deviation time and temperature) for each product depends on the stability program (node 6.5), and is covered only at the conceptual level in this section.
  • Many of the quantitative maintenance time, cost, and carbon footprint benchmarks for active/passive packaging are vendor data, so they cannot be presented as fixed numbers and are only indicated as reference values.
  • Details of last-mile/CTC (control temperature chain) operation in low- and middle-income countries and actual quantification of disposal rates were only partially covered.

5. Facilities, contamination control & enabling technology

Cross-cutting physical plant and process-architecture layer that every unit operation runs inside.

5.1 Facilities, utilities & cleanrooms

The physical foundation that supports all processes

If you imagine biopharmaceutical manufacturing as a single building, individual tasks such as upstream processing (cell culture), downstream processing (purification), finished product filling, and QC testing are activities that occur in several ‘rooms’ within it. The facilities, utilities, and clean rooms covered by 5.1 are the rooms themselves and the plumbing, air, and water supplied to them. In other words, this section deals not with one specific unit process, but with the infrastructure layer consumed by all other unit processes. As inputs, it receives raw water (potable water at the level of drinking water), electric power, source steam, and compressed air, and as outputs are (1) water of a specified grade - purified water and water for injection (WFI), (2) pure (clean) steam used for sterilization, (3) process gases such as nitrogen and oxygen, (4) clean air classified and maintained by grade (Grade A~D), and (5) this facility and utility. Creates a qualification status that demonstrates documented ‘suitability for intended use’. This layer is a prerequisite for virtually all GMP activities, including contamination control strategy (CCS), single-use systems, aseptic filling/freeze-drying, cell culture/purification, and from a regulatory perspective, verification and qualification of water purification, storage, and distribution systems are treated as the basis of GMP and an essential subject of inspection (EMA Water Quality Guidelines).

How it works — water, steam, gas, air

GMP water system.Water is a cascade structure in which raw water is pretreated (water softening, activated carbon, microfilter) and then purified first to produce purified water, which then produces WFI. The key is the method of WFI production, with EU GMP Annex 1 stipulating that WFI “must be produced by distillation or a purification process equivalent to distillation, which may include reverse osmosis (RO) in combination with appropriate techniques such as electrodeionization (EDI), ultrafiltration (UF) or nanofiltration” (Annex 1). That is, not only the traditional multiple effect (MED)/vapor compression (VC) distillation, but also the RO+EDI+UF combination is recognized. The produced water is placed in a storage tank and constantly circulated in a circulation loop to eliminate stagnation. Annex 1 cites “continuous circulation at temperatures exceeding 70°C” as an example of a method that minimizes the risk of microbial growth (Annex 1). The piping maintains turbulent flow, excludes dead legs, and is sloped to completely drain the pipe to suppress biofilm formation. Because water quality is difficult to capture system-wide status through individual sampling alone, Annex 1 requires WFI systems to include continuous monitoring, such as total organic carbon (TOC) and conductivity, stating that this is a better indicator of overall system performance than discrete sampling (Annex 1).Pure (clean) steam. Purified feed water is evaporated with a pure steam generator and used directly as a sterilizer. The condensate from this steam must meet the quality of the WFI monograph of the relevant pharmacopoeia, and non-condensable gas, dryness, and superheat are subject to management (Annex 1).Process gases. To ensure that gases in direct contact with the product or primary container surface do not become a source of contamination, Annex 1 requires that gases used in aseptic processing be filtered at the point of use with a sterilization grade filter of nominal 0.22 µm or less (Annex 1). Filter integrity testing and backflow prevention in the vacuum/pressure system are managed together.HVAC and clean room. The air logic is summarized as ‘push in clean air and keep dirty air out.’ By supplying air filtered by HEP filters, high-class rooms are maintained at a higher pressure than the adjacent low-class background, and Annex 1 recommends a pressure difference of at least 10 Pa (guideline value) between adjacent rooms of different classes (Annex 1). The most dangerous critical areas are protected by unidirectional airflow, which should provide a uniform wind speed of 0.36 to 0.54 m/s (guideline value) at the working location (Annex 1). Another management item is how quickly cleanliness is restored when it is disturbed by work. Annex 1 sets the ‘cleanup’ period to return to the particle state ‘at rest’ to be less than 20 minutes (guideline value), and this value must be determined and documented in the procedure for room qualification (Annex 1).Cleanroom classes and classification. Annex 1 defines four classes of cleanrooms/areas from A to D for the manufacture of sterile pharmaceutical products (Annex 1). Grade A is a critical area where high-risk operations such as aseptic processing lines, filling areas, open operations, and aseptic connections are performed, and is usually protected by a unidirectional airflow workstation within a RABS or isolator (Annex 1). Grade B is the background cleanroom of Grade A, while Grades C and D are responsible for less critical steps. The actual classification of the classes refers to the ISO 14644 series (Annex 1), and total particles ≥0.5 µm and ≥5 µm are measured both ‘at rest’ (equipment installation/HVAC operation, unmanned) and ‘in operation’ (maximum personnel operation or simulation).

Key parameters and materials

Water quality standards.The dividing line between purified water and WFI is clear. Both waters must meet conductivity ≤1.3 µS/cm (25°C) and TOC ≤500 ppb, but WFI must also meet bacterial endotoxin ≤0.25 EU/mL, and this endotoxin standard is the key indicator that differentiates WFI from purified water (Lab Manager).⚠️ (These precise values are not from the original Pharmacopoeia monograph, but from the secondary Since it is based on a summary, the strength of evidence is relatively low.)

Cleanroom particle limits (Annex 1 Table 1, ≥0.5µm/m³).Grades A and B are the same at 3,520/m³ at rest, but differ in operation — A must remain at 3,520, while B is allowed up to 352,000 (Annex 1). Next, Grade C is set at rest 352,000 / in operation 3,520,000, and Grade D is set at rest 3,520,000 / in operation by risk assessment (Annex 1). These grades are also commonly referred to as ISO 14644 classes, and according to one industry summary, Grade A/B corresponds to ISO 5, Grade C corresponds to ISO 7, and Grade D corresponds to ISO 8 (ACH Engineering).⚠️

Clean room microbial limit (Table 2, upon qualification confirmation).Grade A requires no growth on any of the air, settling plate, and contact plate, and Grade B is 10 CFU/m³ in air and 5 CFU on the contact plate (Annex 1). Going down to lower grades, the limits are relaxed, so that Grade C becomes 100 air / 50 sinking plate / 25 contact plate, and Grade D becomes 200 / 100 / 50 (Annex 1).HVAC operation/maintenance parameters. In addition to the minimum 10 Pa pressure difference between adjacent classes, 0.36 to 0.54 m/s unidirectional wind speed, and cleanup <20 minutes as previously seen, there are also limits on how often the room is re-verified. The maximum re-qualification cycle in Annex 1 is 6 months for Grades A and B and 12 months for Grades C and D (Annex 1). However, the quantitative standard for ‘number of ventilation per hour (ACH)’ commonly sought by practitioners is not specified in a universal table in Annex 1, but is determined by CCS and risk assessment of each space (FDA aseptic process guidance only provides examples such as a minimum of 20 ACH in ISO 8 support area).Core equipment/measurements and materials. The water/steam system includes a multi-effect/steam compression still, RO/EDI/UF skid, PW/WFI storage tank with hydrophobic bacteria-blocking vent filter, circulation distribution loop, and pure steam generator. The air system includes an air handler (AHU), HEPA filter, isolator/RABS surrounding the critical area, suspended particle counter, differential pressure gauge, sedimentation/contact plate, TOC and conductivity online sensors are deployed. Which grade of water is used at which stage is determined based on risk based on the nature of the finished product and the stage of use. WFI is used for parenteral and biopharmaceuticals, and purified water is used for oral products.

Quality and regulation — the two annexes are the framework

The regulatory framework for this section is the two annexes of EU GMP. Annex 1 (2022 document, effective 2023-08-25) addresses clean room classification (particle Table 1, microorganism Table 2) in §4 and utilities (water, pure steam, gas) in §6. The penetrating principle is risk proportionality, which states that “the nature and degree of control applied to a utility system should be proportional to the risk that the utility poses to product quality” — direct contact with the product, such as cleaning/rinsing water or sterilizing gas/steam, is treated as high risk (Annex 1).Annex 15 (effective 2015-10-01) specifies how to prove that this equipment and utility is ‘properly installed, operated and performed.’ Specifications are defined as User Requirements Specifications (URS), which serve as reference points throughout the verification life cycle and at this stage instill the essential elements of quality into the design to reduce GMP risks to an acceptable level (Annex 15).
