Qualification then proceeds in three stages — Installation Qualification (IQ), which verifies in writing that facilities, systems and equipment, as installed/modified, conform to the approved design and manufacturer recommendations (Annex 15); Operational Qualification (OQ) verifies that they operate as intended throughout the expected operating range (Annex 15); and performance Qualification (PQ) verifies that the product performs effectively and reproducibly based on approved process methods and product specifications (Annex 15). The quality of utilities such as steam, water, air, and other gases is also checked at this IQ/OQ/PQ stage immediately after installation (Annex 15), and water systems in particular must be qualified and verified to maintain physical, chemical, and microbial control levels to reflect the effects of seasonal fluctuations (Annex 15). The scope and extent of all these decisions must be based on a justified and documented risk assessment of facilities, equipment, utilities, and processes under the quality risk management system, and the retrospective verification that was used in the past is no longer allowed (Annex 15).

Separate pharmacopeia and EMA standards overlap when selecting water grades. The European Pharmacopoeia (Ph.Eur.) has three classes: WFI, purified water, and extract preparation water, and WFI is required for products for parenteral administration and biopharmaceuticals (including hemofiltration, peritoneal dialysis fluid, etc.) (EMA water quality guideline). The critical turning point was 2017, when Ph. Eur.’s WFI monograph (0169) was revised, making it possible to produce WFI through a ‘distillation equivalent’ process that combines single/double pass reverse osmosis with EDI, UF, and nanofiltration in addition to distillation ([EMA Water Quality Guidelines] (https://www.ema.europa.eu/en/documents/scientific-guideline/guideline-quality-water-pharmaceutical-use_en.pdf)). In addition, the ISO 14644 series (particle classification, monitoring, HEPA integrity, etc.) is referenced for clean room testing, the FDA sterile process guidance and 21 CFR 211 in the United States, and ASTM E2500 and ISPE Baseline Guide 5 are referenced for risk-based commissioning and qualification (C&Q).

Changes by Modality — Containment vs. Protection

The facility, utility, and clean room floors generally do not discriminate between product types. WFI is the same WFI whether in a mAb factory or a vaccine factory. There are two points of divergence — the logic of ‘containment versus protection’ and the scale paradigm. The mAb/recombinant protein line consumes a large amount of WFI/buffer in large stainless steel or single-use equipment, and is dominated by the logic of protecting the product in a positive pressure clean room. Although mRNA-LNP requires WFI for buffering and dissolution, a fire/explosion proof area and low-temperature handling room for handling organic solvents such as ethanol are added, and the footprint is relatively small. The one with the most different results is viral vector/cell gene therapy (CGT), which deals with live vectors/genetically modified organisms, so it requires a negative pressure/containment cascade to protect workers and the environment, which can directly conflict with the ‘positive pressure’ logic of asepsis. Autologous CGT reorganizes the facility not by scale-up, but by scale-out, where multiple small sterile suites proliferate in parallel. This difference by product type is also reflected in water quality requirements. The EMA water quality guidelines specify the minimum water quality for each product type by requiring purified water for viral vectors that undergo a sterilization step and WFI for cell-based products that are not sterilized (EMA Water Quality Guidelines). Classical vaccines are egg-based and deal with live bacteria, so containment is important and a large amount of water and steam are consumed for fermentation and final sterilization. In short, the unit process itself does not disappear, but containment requirements (negative pressure) and scale-out of personalized product types reverse the direction of airflow in the clean room and increase the number of rooms.

Practices and recent changes

Today’s standards are still high-temperature distillation WFI + high-temperature circulation loop, ISO 14644-based cleanroom classification, and traditional IQ/OQ/PQ qualification. However, the movement in recent years is clear.

First, membraneization of ambient WFI. As seen earlier, the 2017 Ph.Eur. Since the 0169 amendment allowed non-distilled WFI (EMA Water Quality Guidelines), cold WFI has emerged as a means of decarbonization and cost reduction. According to the ISPE analysis, cold WFI consumes significantly less power than hot systems, using up to 50% less power than VC distillation and less than one-tenth of the total energy use per unit of production compared to ME distillation (ISPE). However, it is still at a pre-adoption stage, and the amount met by cold WFI membrane systems is estimated to be less than one-fifth of the new WFI demand (ISPE). ⚠️ Reliability of microorganism and biofilm control during room temperature storage and distribution is key.Second, CCS-centered reorganization of Annex 1 (2022). This is where the flow of proportioning utility controls to risk and specifying continuous monitoring of TOC and conductivity in the WFI comes from (Annex 1). Third, risk-based C&Q. ASTM E2500-25 specifies an approach to design and verify manufacturing systems for their intended use through scientific and risk-based decision-making based on product and process information, and its risk management principles are aligned with ICH Q9(R1) (ISPE). This is to lighten the batch IQ/OQ/PQ through a ‘verification’ approach centered on critical elements (CA/CDE) and subject matter experts (SME) and reduce repetitive field tests by utilizing FAT/SAT. Fourth, digitization is in progress, including paperless validation, utility continuous monitoring (water PAT), and digital twin.Issue:The biggest difference between sources in this section is the temperature management of the WFI. Annex 1 cites the proven high temperature practice of ‘continuous cycling above 70°C’ as an example of microbial control (Annex 1). On the other hand, ISPE and others have been pushing cold WFI (RO+EDI+UF+ozone) as a means of decarbonization and cost reduction since 2017, suggesting up to 50% power savings (ISPE), but practices have not yet come together regarding the reliability of controlling microorganisms and biofilms at room temperature (cold WFI meets only less than 1/5 of new demand). ⚠️ Moreover, the savings figures themselves vary depending on the source. It varies greatly at 30%, 50%, and 60~90%, so there is a large variation depending on the comparison standard (whether VC/ME/storage loop is included). The second tension lies in ventilation rates. FDA sterility guidance exemplifies normative values ​​such as a minimum of 20 ACH in an ISO 8 support area, but Annex 1 (2022) does not have a universal ACH table, but rather determines it based on CCS and risk assessment for each space (Annex 1), creating a gap between practice seeking quantitative standards and risk-based flexibility. The third is a collision of verification frames. Annex 15 stipulates the discrete steps and definitions of IQ, OQ, and PQ (Annex 15), while ASTM E2500-25·ISPE BG5 (2nd edition) attempts to replace and lighten blanket qualification with a ‘verification’ approach centered on critical elements (ISPE), focusing on regulatory acceptance of the two frames and terminology harmonization. Differences in interpretation remain.Unresolved Questions: (1) Specific number of ventilation times per hour (ACH) — Annex 1 does not specify a universal table but is determined by CCS and risk assessment, so quantitative evidence is limited other than the FDA example (ISO 8=20 ACH). (2) The detailed items and judgment criteria of the IQ/OQ/PQ test protocol for each facility type are different for each site and project, so generalized primary evidence is limited. (3) USP·Ph.Eur. The precision of water quality standards relies on secondary summaries (the full text of the original pharmacopoeial monograph is not available), so the strength of evidence is relatively low.

5.2 Contamination control strategy & environmental monitoring

Role and location: ‘Pollution management layer’ that operates on top of physical infrastructure

This section is a contamination control layer that operates ‘above’ the physical infrastructure of qualified clean rooms and utilities covered in the previous section (5.1). It consists of two complementary components. One is the Contamination Control Strategy (CCS) — a site-wide integrated strategy that combines design, procedures, technology, and organizational control and monitoring to prevent contamination from microorganisms, endotoxins, heat sources, and particles. The other is the Environmental Monitoring (EM) program — a measurement system that verifies and monitors whether the controls are actually operating. EU GMP Annex 1 defines CCS as “a planned set of controls for microorganisms, endotoxins/pyrogens, and particles, derived from current product and process understanding, to ensure process performance and product quality” (Source).

The core principle governed by this hierarchy is boiled down to one sentence — “Monitoring or testing alone does not guarantee sterility.” Annex 1 specifies the priorities for quality risk management (QRM) as first the proper design of facilities, equipment and processes, then the implementation of well-designed procedures, and finally the application of a monitoring system, with monitoring as the last element to ‘prove’ that the design and procedures have been implemented correctly and continue to operate as expected (Source). In other words, the starting point of this section is qualified cleanroom/utility (5.1), product/process knowledge, and CCS risk assessment, and the results of this stage are inputted into batch certification/shipment (§9.3) and PQS/regular management review as trend-analyzed EM/APS data, species identification results, and deviation investigation/CAPA. In terms of flow, this layer is a cross-cutting premise that supports aseptic finished product filling (4.2) and the overall low bioburden/aseptic process.

Principle and flow (A): How to build a CCS

Annex 1 defines CCS as a high-level, integrated activity rather than a ‘list’ of individual controls. A CCS must be implemented to define all critical control points throughout the entire facility and evaluate the effectiveness of all control and monitoring measures (design, procedure, technology, and organization), and must be actively reviewed and updated, and its effectiveness itself must be part of regular management reviews (Source). What is important here is the nature of a ‘living system that is actively reviewed,’ which is an axis that appears repeatedly in the later ‘issues’ and ‘practical status.’

The elements to be considered by CCS are listed in Annex 1 §2.5 as a total of 16 items — plant and process design (and documentation), facilities and equipment, personnel, utilities, raw material control (including in-process control), containers and closures, supplier approval, consignment activity management, process risk management, process verification, sterilization process verification, preventive maintenance, cleaning and disinfection, monitoring system (including feasibility assessment of introducing alternative methods), These are prevention mechanisms (trend analysis, investigation, root cause, CAPA) and continuous improvement (Source). The key is not to check these 16 individually, but to look at their interactions and vulnerabilities together and evaluate their ‘collective’ effectiveness.

Principle and flow (B): EM program design logic

Designing an EM program begins with risk assessment, not copying a set table. Annex 1 requires that sampling location, monitoring frequency, monitoring method, and culture conditions (time, temperature, aerobic/anaerobic) be established with a risk assessment based on process input, final product, facility, process criticality, and knowledge of formal microbiota (Source). And a crucial warning about how to read this program - the monitoring elements of live, non-viable, and APS have limited reliability when taken separately and should not be used alone as indicators of sterility; only when viewed together do they confirm the reliability of the system (design, verification, and operation) they monitor (Source). The reason for placing the three series of measurements in parallel is this complementarity.

Non-viable bacteria (total particle) monitoring.The Grade A area must continuously monitor particles ≥0.5 µm and ≥5 µm throughout the critical process period (including aseptic equipment assembly), capture all interventions, transient events, and system degradation with a flow rate of at least 28 L/min (1 ft³/min), and generate an alarm when the alarm level is exceeded (Source). Because brief pollution events can be missed at low flow rates, a lower flow limit and ‘continuity’ are specified together.Biological and personnel monitoring. Where aseptic operations are performed, frequent microbial monitoring should be performed using a combination of settle plates, active/volumetric air sampling, and gloves, gowns, and surfaces (swabs and contact plates), and the sampling method used should be justified within the CCS and demonstrated not to harm Grade A and B airflow patterns (Source). In Grade A, continuous live air monitoring (air sampling or settling plate) is performed throughout the critical process (Source). Personnel monitoring is determined by location, type, and frequency based on risk assessment based on activity and proximity to critical areas. At a minimum, gloves (or appropriate areas of gowns, if necessary) must be monitored after a critical intervention, and gloves and gowns must be monitored whenever leaving a Grade B clean room. If gloves are monitored after an intervention, outer gloves must be replaced before resuming activities (Source). The fact that humans are the largest source of pollution is the background to this tight personnel control.Limits, trending, and excess response. Both live bacteria and total particles have alert levels and action limits. In particular, microorganisms detected in Grade A and Grade B should be identified to the species level and their potential impact on product quality and overall control of each batch involved should be evaluated (Source). Trend analysis goes beyond simple limit exceedances and looks at increases in the number of action/alert limit exceedances, continuous alert exceedances, sporadic exceedances due to common causes, and changes in the type, number, and dominance of specific bacteria in microorganisms. In particular, special attention should be paid to the recovery of bacteria that indicate loss of control or deterioration of cleanliness, and bacteria that are difficult to control, such as spore-forming bacteria or mold (Source). Response procedures are also multi-layered—if action limits are exceeded, procedures should specify root cause investigation, product impact assessment, including batches produced between monitoring and reporting, and CAPA requirements; and if alert levels are exceeded, evaluation and follow-up actions should be specified (Source).APS (Aseptic Process Simulation/media fill). The effectiveness of aseptic operation control is periodically verified by APS, which replaces the product with a sterile nutrient medium. The goal of APS is zero growth, and if even one contaminated unit appears, it is treated as an APS failure (Source). (The detailed parameters of APS are continued in ‘Key Parameters’ below. For reference, APS is an element that borders 4.2, which deals with the charging process itself, and is treated here as a ‘verification component of the monitoring program.’)

Expedited methods.Annex 1 recommends considering the adoption of rapid and automated microbial monitoring methods to accelerate the detection of microbial contamination and reduce product risks, but specifies that such methods can only be introduced after verification of equivalence or superiority compared to existing methods (Source).

Key parameters and materials

Viral action limits (Table 6).Annex 1 Table 6 specifies absolute CFU action limits by grade. Grade A means no growth in all of the air, settling plate, contact plate, and glove, and any growth should lead to investigation (Source). Grade B is 10 CFU/m³ for volumetric air sampling, 5 CFU for settling plate (90mm/4h), 5 CFU for contact plate (55mm), and 5 CFU for glove (5 fingers on both hands) (Source). The point at which these values ​​are expressed as absolute counts is where they differ from the US USP approach in the ‘issue’ below.Total particle action limits (Table 5) and continuous monitoring parameters. For Grade A, the total particle action limits are ≥0.5µm 3,520/m³ and ≥5µm 29/m³ for both at rest and in operation, and for Grade B, the action limits are ≥0.5µm 3,520 (at rest)/352,000 (in operation). put (Source). Grade A continuous monitoring is based on monitoring ≥0.5µm and ≥5µm with a flow rate of at least 28 L/min (Source).APS Parameters. APS typically fills a minimum of 5,000 to 10,000 units; for small batches of less than 5,000 units, the number of APS containers must be at least equal to the production batch size (Source). Initial verification is conducted at least three consecutive successful tests, and regular re-verification is repeated every six months (twice a year) for each sterilization process, each filling line, and each shift, and each worker must participate in a successful APS at least once a year (Source).Gown eligibility. Compliance with sterile gowning procedures should be confirmed by reassessment at least once a year, using a combination of visual and microbiological assessments (monitoring locations such as glove fingers, forearms, chest, hood/face, etc.) (Source).See US practice — USP <1116> Contamination Recovery Rate (CRR). US practice looks at recovery ‘frequency’ instead of absolute CFU. USP <1116> defines Contamination Recovery Rate (CRR) as the percentage (%) of the medium plate showing microbial recovery, regardless of the number of CFU (Source). ⚠️ Recommended initial CRR is presented as isolator/closed RABS (ISO 5 or higher) <0.1%, ISO 5 <1%, ISO 6 <3%, ISO 7 <5%, ISO 8 <10%, evaluating the environment by frequency of recovery rather than absolute CFU value (Source). ⚠️ However, even in the CRR system, if a single sample exceeds 15 CFU in ISO 5, it is considered subject to investigation (Source). ⚠️ It should be noted that this CRR figure relies on secondary summaries (blog and journal citations) rather than the full text of the original USP chapter, so the strength of evidence is relatively low.Core equipment/materials. Tools used in practice include a positive displacement air sampler (impaction type), settling plate (TSA 90mm), contact plate (RODAC 55mm, containing neutralizer), swab and glove print, continuous non-viable bacteria particle counter, real-time live bacteria monitor (live fluorescent particle count), and dual-temperature incubator (bacteria 30–35°C, fungi). 20–25℃), TSA/SDA medium, MALDI-TOF MS·16S rRNA for species identification, and EM data management/trending software. (This list of equipment and materials is a comprehensive description of node research, and separate sources have not been secured for each item.)

Quality/Regulation

The properties that are actually monitored and controlled in this layer are live bacteria (air, surface, personnel CFU), total non-live bacteria particles (≥0.5·≥5µm), endotoxin/pyrogen and particle contaminants, and type and identification of microorganisms. In high-risk areas where direct contact with the product or open operation occurs, the intensity of control and monitoring is proportional to the risk.

The central axis of regulation is EU GMP Annex 1 ‘Manufacture of Sterile Medicinal Products’. It was published as a document dated 2022-08-22 and implemented on 2023-08-25 (Article 8.123 related to freeze-drying went into effect on 2024-08-25). §2 deals with QRM/CCS (16 elements), §7 deals with personnel/gown qualification, and §9 deals with environment/process monitoring, and Table 5 (total particles) and Table 6 (viable bacteria) action limits and APS. It contains requirements and includes CCS definitions in the annex glossary. Regarding limit notation, Annex 1 specifies that the limit is applied in CFU throughout the document, and adds that when using a new technology that presents results in a different way from CFU, the manufacturer must scientifically justify the applied limit and, if possible, correlate it with CFU (Table 6 Note 2) (Source). This proviso becomes a regulatory gateway to the introduction of real-time new technology.

Several standards are referenced together in Annex 1. The ISO 14644 series (-1 particle classification, -2 monitoring) is the standard for clean room qualification and monitoring, and in the United States Pharmacopoeia, there are USP <1116> (microbiological control and monitoring in a sterile process environment, CRR concept), <1113> (microorganism characterization and identification), and <1223> (verification of alternative microbial methods). For industry practice guidelines, refer to PDA Technical Report No.13 (revised in 2022) and TR13-2 for low bioburden facilities, and for US regulatory expectations, refer to FDA’s ‘Sterile Drug Products Produced by Aseptic Processing’ (2004). (The detailed version/year of this standard list is a comprehensive description of the node study.)

Differences by product type

The ‘framework’ of CCS·EM is applied equally regardless of product modality wherever sterility or low bioburden is important, but it differs in containment requirements and operation methods. mAb and recombinant proteins have a typical Grade A (B background) structure that combines raw drug bioburden control and aseptic finished product filling, and the burden of personnel intervention and monitoring is relatively low due to the large proportion of automated and closed processes. For mRNA-LNP, aseptic filling and bioburden/RNase control are the focus of EM, and an organic solvent handling area is added, but the contamination control logic itself is similar to that of protein. Viral vector and cell gene therapy (CGT) deals with live vectors and genetic variants, so containment (BSL-2 or higher) and aseptic ‘protection’ can conflict, and there are many open and manual operations, so microbial monitoring must be strengthened. Autologous CGT’s multiple small aseptic suites multiply EM points and personnel monitoring in parallel. In cases where live organisms or powders damage or pose a hazard to the particle counter, as in classical vaccines (egg/live cell-based), frequency/strategies should be adjusted and live cell monitoring increased to ensure environmental triage before and after exposure to the hazard. EM is still applicable for terminally sterilized products, but the criticality of its impact is different from that for aseptic processing. In short, the framework of EM/CCS is maintained, but containment (negative pressure), manual operation, and parallel suites reorganize and multiply monitoring points and personnel control. (This classification by product type is a synthesis of node research Annex 1 §9.19–9.20·§9.26, etc., and separate sources were not secured for each individual sentence.)

Practices and changes

The current standard of practice is culture-based EM (settlement plate, contact plate, and volumetric air sampling; 1 to 7 days for results) + fixed CFU alarm/action limits + manual trending. The biggest change over the past three years has come in regulation. The 2022 Annex 1 revision required a comprehensive and overarching pollution control strategy (CCS) for the first time, and CCS should be understood as an activity that analyzes interactions between elements and vulnerabilities and gaps, rather than listing existing documents (Source). ⚠️ This revision elevated CCS from ‘good practice’ to an essential system subject to inspection, and incorporated primary packaging such as vials, stoppers, and seals as a core element of CCS that directly affects sterility assurance (Source). ⚠️ The revised edition also places great emphasis on aseptic manufacturing, especially continuous environmental monitoring that monitors the number of microorganisms and particles in real time and immediately detects deviations in a Grade A environment (Source). ⚠️

The center of the technology trend is real-time continuous live bacterial monitoring. As §9.24 requires Grade A continuous live bacterial monitoring, devices that distinguish living and non-living particles in real time using biofluorescent particle counting (BFPC) are proliferating. Although eliminating the 1-7 day delay required by culture is attractive, BFPC does not provide species identification and CFU correlation/validation (see Table 6 Note 2 above) is still maturing, so the current standard of practice is to use it in parallel with culture methods rather than completely replacing them. In addition, automatic EM data collection and trending software, CRR-based trending, digitalization that saves badges and labor through low-flow continuous sampling, and expansion of adoption of rapid method (RMM) are in progress.

From a post-implementation inspection perspective (2024–2025), contamination control-related issues ranked high for many sterile manufacturers. The MHRA explicitly cited CCS deficiencies in its 2025-03 FQS Pharma UK inspection report, and the former Catalent Indiana facility acquired by Novo Nordisk received a 483 for failure to inspect extrinsic particles in mid-2025 (Source). ⚠️ The common lesson is clear — making the CCS a ‘one-off submission’ will result in increased scrutiny. Active operation and review are key, and this exposes the real gap between the intent of the regulatory text (a ‘living system’) and field implementation.

Issue

Fixed CFU action limit (EU) vs. contamination recovery rate (US).While Annex 1 Table 6 specifies the absolute CFU action limit for each grade (e.g. Grade B air 10 CFU/m³), USP <1116> considers individual CFU counts to be statistically unreliable in a low-contamination sterile environment and considers the contamination recovery rate (CRR), which is the ‘percentage of plates with recovery (%) regardless of the number of CFU’, as the main trend. It is recommended as an indicator. Both systems require virtually no to very rare retrieval at Grade A/ISO 5, but their philosophies for interpreting EM data (absolute limits versus retrieval frequency) differ.Culture-based EM vs. real-time continuous viable monitoring. Although §9.24 requires Grade A continuous viable monitoring and §9.28 recommends rapid methods, BFPC does not provide species identification and CFU correlation/validation (Table 6 Note 2) is still maturing. Vendors are pushing for real-time adoption, but for now the standard of practice is to parallel culture methods rather than completely replace them.Status of sedimentation plates. Although sedimentation plates have a lower recovery rate compared to active (volumetric displacement) air sampling and are criticized for being irregular because they are passive/sedimentation-based, Annex 1 Table 6 still defines sedimentation plates as a recognized viable bacteria monitoring method and also cites them as an example of Grade A and B continuous monitoring. It persists in regulatory text despite known limitations.’Living system’ vs. ‘one-time document’. CCS was intended to be a system that is actively reviewed and updated (§2.3), but in post-implementation inspections, it was pointed out that many manufacturers made CCS an output for submission (MHRA FQS Pharma, etc.), and a practical gap between regulatory intent and field implementation was observed.

Unresolved Questions

  • Specific values of formal sampling frequency by grade — Annex 1 does not have a universal frequency table but delegates it to risk assessment, so generalized primary quantitative evidence is limited.
  • Detailed statistical methods of trend analysis (control charts, statistical significance determination, etc.) — Annex 1 only lists trend types and does not specify statistical techniques, so the primary evidence is limited.
  • USP <1116> contamination recovery rate (CRR) figures rely on secondary summaries (blogs and journal citations), so the strength of evidence is relatively low compared to the full text of the original USP chapter.

5.3 Single-use systems & extractables/leachables

Role and Position: Fluid Path Architecture and Chemical Qualification Workstream

Single-Use Systems (SUS) and Extractables & Leachables (E&L) are not one independent process step, but a physical fluid path architecture in which all unit operations of upstream (USP), downstream (DSP), and fill-finish flow ‘in’ it, and a workstream that chemically verifies the polymer materials that make up the fluid path. SUS is a disposable assembly — bags, tubing, connectors, filters, sensors, disposable bioreactors — that is pre-sterilized with gamma rays or The actual work of this layer is organized into three categories. (1) Decide whether to use SUS or stainless steel at the beginning of facility and process design, (2) ensure the integrity and sterility of the selected assembly, and (3) prove through E&L qualification that the polymer does not leak and contaminate the process fluid and final product. In other words, the starting point of this section is the process fluid and contact conditions in contact with the polymer surface, and the result of this step is a ‘qualified fluid path’ complete with extractable data, leachable risk assessment, and integrity/sterility assurance.

The latest regulatory axis governing this entire hierarchy is USP <665> of the United States Pharmacopoeia. This chapter takes effect on May 1, 2026, and establishes requirements for plastic parts and systems used in products manufactured and sold in the United States (Source). Its counterpart, USP <1665>, contains a risk assessment framework but is non-binding informational guidance (Source).

Principle and flow (A): SUS vs. stainless steel decision and integrity assurance

The economic logic of introducing SUS starts from two axes: capital cost and cleaning validation. A single commercial-scale GMP stainless steel bioreactor system can have a capital expenditure (Cap

Ex) of $2 million to more than $10 million (Source). On the other hand, SUS uses a new disposable fluid path for each batch, so there is no need to clean or verify carryover, eliminating the burden of cleaning validation (Source). These characteristics lead to low initial investment, quick changeover, and flexibility, making SUS the default in high-mix, clinical, and flexible production.

However, SUS’s advantage lies in size and supply chain. Commercial single-use bioreactor platforms are only reliably supplied to approximately 2,000 L, while stainless steel has no practical upper scale limit (Source). Additionally, the overall production capacity of single-use facilities is structurally vulnerable, relying entirely on the availability of bags, tubing, connectors, and filters from a small number of qualified suppliers (Source). Therefore, in practice, stainless steel, which has a lower total cost of ownership (TCO), is used in very large-scale (approximately over 2,000 L) and single-product, high-operation facilities, and SUS is used in flexible production, and hybrid facilities that mix SUS in the upstream and stainless steel in the downstream are often adopted.

If SUS is selected, integrity testing must demonstrate that the assembly is leak-tight and sterile. Integrity can be assured at three stages of the product life cycle — component testing by the system manufacturer, assembly testing by the supplier/assembler, and point-of-use testing by the end user (Source). The size of defects that can be detected varies depending on the test method. Pressure decay/airflow-based methods detect defects larger than 10 µm in small flat bags and 100 µm or larger in large 3D systems, and the helium tracer gas method is the most sensitive, detecting defects larger than 2 µm (Source). The more sensitive a method is, the more likely it is to filter out smaller leaks, but also the greater the testing burden, so it is chosen according to the size and shape of the bag and the risk.

Principle and Flow (B): E&L Qualification Workstream

The starting point for E&L qualification is the fact that ‘extractables’ and ‘leachables’ are related but distinct concepts. Extractables are compounds released by materials under exaggerated experimental conditions such as aggressive solvents, high temperatures, and long periods of time, and leachables are compounds that actually transfer to drug substances (DS) or finished drugs (DP) under actual process conditions such as normal temperature, contact time, and solvent composition (Source). In other words, extractables are the worst-case list of what a material can ‘release’, and leachables are those that ‘actually enter the product’.

The regulatory workflow to address both follows the two-step approach defined by USP <665>/<1665>. First, an initial assessment is performed to determine whether testing is necessary and whether it is equivalent to a comparative product that has already been verified, and then a risk assessment is performed (Source). Risk assessment grades low, medium, and high risk by combining the chemical nature of the material, chemical nature of the process stream, contact time, temperature, surface area, proximity to the final product, downstream dilution/removal ability, and administration route/dose, and determines the intensity of the standardized extraction study according to the grade.

Compounds from extraction studies are profiled analytically. E&L analysis uses a combination of gas chromatography-mass spectrometry (GC-MS) and liquid chromatography-mass spectrometry (LC-MS) for organic compounds, and inductively coupled plasma-mass spectrometry (ICP-MS) for elemental impurities (Source). Detected compounds are judged against the safety threshold. The Analytical Evaluation Threshold (AET) is the lower concentration below which there is no need to identify individual compounds, and the Safety Concern Threshold (SCT) is the concentration that requires toxicological evaluation if it exceeds it (Source).

In parallel with USP <665>, the BioPhorum (BPOG) standardized extraction protocol developed by the bio industry coexists as a practical practice. BPOG specifies a general reporting threshold of 0.1 µg/cm² at a defined surface area:volume ratio (Source). BPOG creates a larger dataset because the number of replicates and timepoints is greater than USP, but the BioPhorum guidance itself is not an official regulatory document (Source). The relationship between these two frameworks is the central tension addressed in ‘Issues’ below.

Key parameters and materials

The standard extraction scheme of USP <665> is defined by three factors — extraction solution, contact period, and temperature. Three extraction solutions are used, of which C1 is a 50% ethanol/purified water solution that simulates exposure to organic solvents, solubilizers, lipids, proteins, and blood-derived components (Source). Together with the remaining two solutions (low pH and high pH simulation), they cover different chemical environments. The contact period is divided into three sections: short-term 24 hours ± 1 hour, medium-term 168 hours (7 days) ± 4 hours, and long-term 504 hours (21 days) ± 8 hours ([Source] (https://ispe.org/pharmaceutical-engineering/september-october-2025/ensuring-drug-product-safety-compliance)). The temperature is limited to a maximum of 40°C, which is considered optimal to accelerate extraction without significantly changing the properties of the material (Source). The analysis consists of non-volatile residue (NVR), UV absorption, organic extract profiling, elemental impurities, and cytotoxicity testing.

In terms of materials, the most famous example of leachables is entangled in polyethylene films. The leachable bDtBPP (bis(2,4-di-tert-butylphenyl)phosphate), which inhibits cell growth, is derived from the decomposition of Irgafos 168 (tris(2,4-di-tert-butylphenyl)phosphite), an antioxidant additive for polyethylene (Source). Crucially, exposure of oxidized Irgafos 168 to ionizing radiation, such as gamma irradiation, is a critical condition for significant production of the leachable bDtBPP (Source). In other words, it is a representative example showing that materials, additives, and sterilization methods are intertwined in that the pre-sterilization process (irradiation) of SUS itself can be the cause of producing leachables. bDtBPP is harmful even at low concentrations, well below parts per million (ppm), and is reported to inhibit growth in several cell lines in the range of approximately 0.12 to 0.73 mg/L (Source). ⚠️

Quality/Regulation

There are two main risks managed by this section. One is the toxicological risk of leachables in terms of patient safety (evaluation compared to AET/SCT/acceptable daily exposure PDE), and the other is the risk in terms of process performance. USP <665> is of particular interest to process equipment-related leachables (PERLs), which are defined as molecules that can change critical quality characteristics (CQA) and affect the safety, quality, identity, potency, and purity (SQuIPP) of DP/DS (Source). This is typically the case when cytotoxic exudates, such as bDtBPP seen above, inhibit cell growth. The attributes actually tested and controlled in this tier are extract characterization, leachable risk assessment (and confirmatory leachable testing when necessary), particles, bioburden/sterility, endotoxin, and integrity.

The boundaries of the regulatory framework are also important. Rubber-based elastomers are covered separately under USP <381> and are therefore excluded from the scope of USP <665> (Source). Additionally, even if <665> is a mandatory standard, the responsibility for establishing and justifying how to apply it to a specific process stream lies with the component user, that is, the company that uses the component, not the manufacturer (Source). In terms of aseptic finished product manufacturing, EMA’s Annex 1 Part 8.131 appears to specifically address requirements for single-use systems (SUS) (Source). ⚠️

Differences by product type

The maturity and risk of SUS·E&L practices vary depending on the product type. In mAbs and recombinant proteins, SUS dominates from clinical to commercial use (within the upper limit of approximately 2,000 L for a single-use bioreactor), and E&L practices are also the most mature. SUS is widely used in mRNA-LNP, but the problem is that contact with organic solvents such as ethanol intervenes in the lipid nanoparticle (LNP) formation process. Ethanol is a strong extractant, so the risk of leachables from solvent-contacted parts increases and E&L of the assembly becomes especially important. In viral vector and cell gene therapy (CGT), SUS is the de facto standard due to the preference for small-scale, multi-variety scale-out and functionally-closed processes, but the risk is amplified because vulnerable cell products are very sensitive to cytotoxic leachables. In fact, the fact that leachables such as bDtBPP inhibit cell growth at low concentrations of approximately 0.12 to 0.73 mg/L (Source) ⚠️ supports why the risk of SUS leachables is particularly prominent in cell therapy that deals with vulnerable cells. Stainless steel is still common in processes that handle large-scale or multiple-dose bulk antigens, such as classical vaccines, but the adoption of SUS is increasing. In short, the SUS-versus-stainless steel decision depends on scale (stainless for larger quantities) and batch variety (SUS for higher variety), and E&L risk varies by product type depending on the aggressiveness of the contact solvent and the cellular/biosensitivity of the product. (This classification by product type is a comprehensive description of node research, and separate sources for individual sentences have not been secured.)

Practices and changes

The standard answer in current practice is a hybrid facility of ‘upstream SUS + (some) downstream stainless steel’, and SUS is the default in multi-product, clinical, and flexible production. The biggest change in this tier over the past three years has come in terms of regulation. With USP <665>/<1665> becoming mandatory on May 1, 2026 (Source), BPOG-based extract testing, which had previously been a voluntary practice, was formalized and harmonized into a mandatory compendial standard. As a result, companies that had been testing with BPOG faced the practical burden of having to recheck whether their data complied with <665> requirements (Source).Changes are also underway in sterilization methods. X-ray irradiation is considered a like-for-like modification to gamma irradiation, and the adoption of X-ray sterilization of disposable parts is being promoted in response to Co-60 supply constraints and gamma capacity shortages (Source). ⚠️ However, the remaining task is to quantitatively prove whether the X-ray and gamma extract profiles are actually equivalent. In addition, integration with the Annex 1 Contamination Control Strategy (CCS), expansion of point-of-use integrity testing, plastic waste and sustainability scrutiny, and supply chain resilience and multiple sourcing, which have emerged post-COVID, are all moving together. The last wave is a direct response to the structural weaknesses seen earlier — the fact that production capacity in single-use facilities relies on the availability of consumables from a small number of qualified suppliers (Source)—and the upper SUS size limit of approximately 2,000 L (Source) remains a fundamental limitation that still holds stainless steel in very large commercial processes.

Issue

There are three tensions in this verse. First, whether the extract will be tested using BPOG or USP <665>. On the one hand, the BPOG standardization protocol has many repetitions and viewpoints, giving a larger and more conservative dataset, but on the other hand, USP <665> is a compendial standard that is mandatory from 2026-05-01, but is less exhaustive than BPOG, and BPOG itself is not a regulatory document. As a result, companies face a double burden and reconciliation problem between data integrity and regulatory compliance. Second, does the standardized exaggerated extract data sufficiently represent the actual leachable risk? Because extractables are worst-case, when evaluated against a safety threshold, there is a conflict between the position that risks are conservatively covered, and the position that leachables are a ‘distinct’ concept that occurs only under actual process conditions, so exaggerated extraction alone can overestimate or underestimate the risk, so testing for leachables by product is necessary. This gap is pointed out as the so-called ‘compliance gap’. Third, is disposable a better choice than stainless steel? SUS reduces initial investment, cleaning validation, and downtime and provides flexibility, but at the cost of supply chain vulnerabilities, plastic waste, high consumable OpEx, and a cap of approximately 2,000 L. In high single-product operation, stainless steel has a lower TCO. Conclusions vary depending on product portfolio (multi-product small quantity vs. single large quantity) and scale.

Unresolved Questions

  • The exact scoring algorithm and weights of the USP <665> risk assessment matrix and the low, medium, and high grade boundary values were only briefly covered due to limitations in access to the original text (paid).
  • Only partial evidence was secured for supplier qualification verification practice parameters such as expiration date setting for disposable assemblies, transportation simulation, and gamma 25~40 kGy dose, due to failure to access the evidence page.
  • The details of the quantitative comparison of X-ray and gamma extract profile equivalence (specific compounds/differences) were only outlined.

5.4 Continuous/intensified processing, PAT & digitization

Purpose and role: Cross-cutting layer that “connects, measures, and orchestrates” unit processes

This section does not recreate the batch unit processes—cell culture, purification, and filling—that we have looked at one by one in Parts 2 to 4 so far, but is a cross-cutting layer that deals with “how” those unit processes are reorganized from batch to continuous, connected, and digital architecture. It is located in Part 5 (Facility, Pollution Control, Enabling Technology), and receives the traditional batch unit process and QC test requirements of Part 6 as input, and produces three products as output. The first is an integrated continuous process in which two or more unit processes are directly connected (ICH Q13), the second is a PAT/RTRT system that controls and releases quality through real-time measurement during the process, and the third is a digital plant orchestrated by MES, soft sensors, and digital twin (Bioprocessing 4.0).

These three axes may seem like different technologies, but they are tied together by a single philosophy. The FDA defines process analytical technology (PAT) as “a system for designing, analyzing, and controlling manufacturing by measuring key quality and performance attributes of raw materials, intermediates, and processes in a timely manner (during the process),” and its goal is to ensure the quality of the final product (Source). The underlying proposition here—quality is built into the design rather than tested in the finished product—is the QbD philosophy, and serialization, PAT, and digitization are the physical implementation of that philosophy. The common effects of the three axes are reduced cycle time, reduced facility footprint, and real-time emission potential. In terms of flow, this section leads to control strategy, process verification (continuous process verification), analytical method development/validation (ICH Q14/Q2(R2)), and regulatory submission (ICH Q13/Q12 life cycle). The upstream integration is interlocked with the culture mode/bioreactor node, and the downstream connection is interlocked with the chromatography/viral clearance node.

Operation method (Axis 1): Continuous/intensive/connected processes and ICH Q13

Let’s start with the definition of continuous manufacturing (CM). ICH Q13 defines CM as a method of “injecting input materials continuously, converting intermediate materials within the process, and discharging output simultaneously.” What is important is the location of focus. Although this definition may also apply to individual unit processes such as chromatography or perfusion culture, ICH Q13 focuses on the integrated aspects of an integrated system where “two or more unit processes are directly connected” (Source). In other words, what this section deals with is not perfusion itself, but the “connection” where perfusion and downstream purification are connected by a surge line to form a single flow. These guidelines apply to the continuous manufacturing of raw materials (DS) and finished products (DP) of chemicals and therapeutic proteins, and to batch-to-CM conversion of existing products as well as new products, and the principles can be extended to other biological and biotechnology entities (Source).Serialization forces us to redefine some of the concepts taken for granted in the batch format. First of all, the size of the “batch”. In CM, the batch definition of ICH Q7 is used as is, but the size can be defined as one of (a) the amount of output, (b) the amount of input, or (c) the operation time at a given mass flow rate, and can also be defined as the range of minimum to maximum operation time (Source). Time-based definitions such as “one batch after 3 weeks of operation” become possible. Next is the state of control. It is easy to think of a steady state in a batch method, but ICH Q13 specifies that a controlled state is established as long as the parameters (process parameters/quality attributes) vary within a specified range and does not necessarily have to be a steady state (Source). Third is process dynamics. This is characterized by the residence time distribution (RTD), which specifies the time given to the transfer and conversion of substances and is unique to the process, composition, physical properties, and equipment design. And understanding RTD and process dynamics is the basis for developing strategies for material tracking and sampling/diversion (separation and disposal of nonconforming materials in real time) (Source). This is because in continuous flow, you need to statistically know “when this material enters and when it comes out” so that you can accurately cut out the unsuitable section.

ICH Q13 Annex III provides an example of what this architecture looks like in a therapeutic protein. A bioreactor compatible with the perfusion culture system, continuous capture chromatography, virus inactivation, polishing purification chromatography, virus filtration, and buffer exchange/concentration through tangential flow filtration (TFF) are integrated into one, and a surge line/tank is placed between unit processes (Source). The role of the surge tank is key here. Because the upstream perfusion harvest flows steadily while the downstream multi-column collection operates cyclically, the surge buffers the mass flow mismatch between the two. Upstream, cells are captured with a cell retention device (ATF alternating tangential flow or TFF hollow fiber) and intensification is achieved through perfusion to reach a much higher cell density. Downstream, continuous capture is achieved through multi-column periodic countercurrent chromatography (PCC), which utilizes the resin binding capacity more than batch by capturing the breakthrough of one column to the next column.

Mode of Operation (Axis 2): PAT and Real-Time Emission Testing (RTRT)

PAT is the measurement and control layer that supports serialization. In addition to the FDA definition (cited earlier), ICH Q13 considers PAT to be particularly appropriate for CM. Examples include in-line UV flow cells that monitor the concentration of therapeutic proteins, in-line near-infrared spectroscopy (NIR) that evaluates blend homogeneity and water content, and on-line HPLC that tracks chemical reaction conversions. Because PAT allows real-time detection of disturbances, CM is well suited to active process control strategies such as feedforward and feedback (Source).

Depending on where the measurement is performed, PAT is divided into three types. At-line is a method in which the sample is removed and analyzed near the process stream, on-line is a method in which the sample is diverged from the process but can be returned to the process, and in-line is a method in which the sample is measured (invasively or non-invasively) in situ without being removed from the stream (Source). The reason why this distinction is important is because the more in-line, the more data can be entered into the control loop without delay. The power of Raman spectroscopy, a representative in-line tool, lies in reading multiple states simultaneously in one spectrum. In CHO culture, Raman spectrum can simultaneously measure glucose, glutamate, glutamine, lactic acid, ammonia, total cell density (TCD), and viable cell density (VCD) (Source). However, since the raw spectrum itself is not a quality attribute, Chemometrics translates it into a CQA value. Chemometric models commonly used in pharmaceutical and bioprocess PAT are partial least squares (PLS) and principal component analysis (PCA) (Source). As an example of actual feedback control working with the predicted value obtained in this way, when Raman-based glucose feedback control was applied in the fed-batch CHO process, the non-enzymatic glycosylation of the target protein was lowered from 9% to 4% (Source). ⚠️

The accumulation of these real-time measurements could change the emission paradigm itself. Real-time release (RTR) is the ability to evaluate and guarantee acceptable quality of intermediate/finished products based on process data, and its PAT component usually includes a verified combination of “evaluated material properties × process controls” (Source). This is an expanded concept of parametric emissions, which has existed since 1985, and requires regulatory approval before implementation. However, ICH Q13 itself clarifies that RTRT in biological products is partial rather than comprehensive. Quality attributes for which online or in-line measurement technology is not available—potency, for example—still require traditional offline release testing, even for tests with long turnaround times, such as microbiological testing (Source). In other words, the “real-time release” of a biological drug is not the parametric release of the entire product, but the partial real-time release at the level of some properties such as in-line pH, osmotic pressure, and protein concentration and online purity, charge heterogeneity, and aggregation.

Operation method (Axis 3): MES, soft sensor, digital twin (Bioprocessing 4.0)

The third axis is the digital layer that coordinates all these measurements and controls. The most ambitious concept here is the digital twin. Bioprocess digital twin is a virtual replication of a physical process that integrates real-time process data with mechanistic equations and statistical models. It goes beyond simple simulation and aims for predictive manufacturing and closed-loop control through defined logic based on production experience (Source). ⚠️ The model is three-pronged. Mechanistic models can deviate from actual processes if parameters are not well specified, and data-based models require representative datasets, and simulation accuracy is particularly poor outside the learning data domain. Therefore, a hybrid model that combines the two is presented as an approach to alleviate this limitation (Source). ⚠️ Soft sensors use this model to infer states that are not directly measured (e.g. product concentration) from other measurements.However, it must be honestly disclosed that the maturity of this axis lags behind the previous two axes, and the reliability of the evidence is low. As _uncertainty_notes explicitly points out in this section, the detailed implementation of the plant floor of MES (level of automation of electronic batch records, real-world examples of exception-based review) and the actual status of commercial GMP closed-loop deployment of digital twins are mainly based on trade and secondary sources, and the basis of primary regulatory documents is limited. From a practical perspective, the judgment of this section is that while MES and data historians have become standards for commercial plants, digital twins and AI/ML soft sensors largely remain in process development (PD), offline simulation, and soft sensing, and GMP closed-loop twins are still in their infancy.

Key parameters and materials

Continuous and integrated architecture brings to the fore parameters that were not present in the batch method. Upstream, cell retention devices (ATF/TFF, 0.2µm hollow fiber), viable cell density, perfusion rate (vessel volume per day, VVD), cell-specific perfusion rate (CSPR), and bioreactor operation time are subject to management. This uptime is particularly important. This is because CM bioreactors can operate for much longer periods than batch manufacturing (Source). Long runs create two new problems. One is control of foreign agents (viruses, bacteria, fungi, mycoplasma), and the other is cell age. Because aspects unique to CM include extended culture continuation and continuous processing of harvest material (Source), in vitro cell age limits established in batch mode may not be directly applicable to continuous mode and must be reset to pilot/commercial scale data per ICH Q5A/Q5B/Q5D.

In the downstream connection, the parameters are the number of columns (usually 3 to 4), residence time, and resin binding capacity utilization of the continuous capture PCC, low pH retention time, temperature, and residence time of the tubular reactor for in-line virus inactivation, concentration multiple of single-pass TFF (SPTFF), and RTD, homogeneity, microbial risk, and retention volume of the surge tank. The lifespan of the filter and resin must also be recalculated. In long continuous operations, filtration duration and throughput per unit area increase and replacement frequency increases. PAT measurement parameters include sample variables, measurement method, frequency, location, statistical method, and judgment criteria, which are determined depending on the intended use of the data and process dynamics. In addition, contingency must be prepared by evaluating the risk of data gaps caused by PAT recalibration, feeding recharge, and component failure. In the digital/IT layer, MES, data historian, DCS/SCADA, data integrity (ALCOA+), and GAMP5-based computerized system verification (CSV) are key management elements. In terms of input materials, lot-to-lot fluctuations in media and feed have a greater impact on long-term operation, and tube welds and connectors of single-use assemblies are assessed as being at risk of contamination the longer and more frequently they are replaced.

Quality and Regulatory: ICH Q13 and PAT/RTRT Framework

The regulatory framework of this section is supported by two primary documents. One is the FDA’s 2004 PAT guidance, which provides the definition of PAT, measurement location (at/on/in-line), and real-time release (RTR) concepts (Source). The other is ICH Q13 ‘Continuous Manufacturing of Drug Substances and Drug Products’ (Step 4 adopted 2022-11-16, FDA adopted 2023-03), which is the first harmonized regulatory framework for continuous manufacturing. Annex III covers examples of therapeutic protein CMs (perfusion + continuous capture chromatography + VI + polishing + VF + TFF), and Annex IV covers DS+DP integrated CMs (Source). On top of these two, ICH Q8 (design/PAT), Q9 (quality risk management), Q10 (control status/PQS), and Q11 (raw material development) are laid as the basis of the control strategy, ICH Q5A(R2)/Q5B/Q5D are for virus safety, genetic stability, and cell age in expanded culture, and ICH is for development and verification of PAT and multivariate analysis methods. Q2(R2)/Q14 is responsible for change management after approval, ICH Q12, and EU GMP Annex 1 for microbial control and single-use integrity for long-term continuous operation.

What is actually tested and controlled in this section is control status monitoring, RTD-based material divergence, PAT-derived in-process CQA (protein concentration, purity, charge variant, aggregation, pH, osmotic pressure), early detection of adventitious agents during extended culture, filter and resin life, and single-use integrity. The principle that is easy to miss here is that CQA itself does not change when the architecture changes, but the measurement point moves from finished testing to in-process. For RTRT to be established, a verified surrogate relationship is needed in which the in-process test is representative of the release test, and as seen previously, the efficacy and microbiological tests remain offline (Source). In process verification, the batch approach is applied to CM, but additional consideration is given to expanded operation, automation integration, and material flow. If justified, alternative verification approaches such as continuous process verification can be used. The suitability is judged based on risk assessment, applicability of development data, control strategy, prior knowledge, etc. (Source). Validation should cover from single cell bank thawing to the limit of in vitro cell age and the variability between multiple thawing.

Variants by product type

Continuous/concentrated architectures do not fit all product types equally. mAb/recombinant protein is the prototype and most mature area of ​​ICH Q13 Annex III. The combination of perfusion + PCC + in-line VI/VF + SPTFF is most naturally realized here, and upstream concentration (perfusion, N-1 perfusion seed concentration) is particularly advanced. The cell-free enzymatic reaction (IVT) itself of mRNA-LNP is friendly to flow and semi-continuous, and the LNP formulation based on microfluidic and collision jet mixing is inherently continuous mixing and is advantageous for continuous because of its small footprint. However, PAT for RNA integrity and encapsulation efficiency is still in its infancy, and TFF is used for buffer exchange.

The most interesting twist occurs in viral vector and cell gene therapy (CGT). Perfusion is used for lentivirus and some AAVs, but autologous CGT is a scale-out method rather than a scale-up method, so “continuous” here means multiple small, parallel, closed and automated processes. Because it is a very small amount, it is difficult to apply in-line PAT, and instead, a closed automation platform (digital/automation) becomes the key lever. Classical vaccines are dominated by egg- and large-scale fermentation-based batches, so complete serialization is rare, but PAT and digital monitoring are applied. In summary, while PAT/digitization applies regardless of product type, continuous-flow architecture is most natural for mAb and mRNA, and flips to “scale-out automation” for autologous CGT.

Current practices and changes: Upstream leads, downstream follows

The current industry standard is still batch/fed culture + offline QC release. Shifts are occurring most rapidly in upstream concentrations. In the Bio

Plan 2025 survey, a record-high 38.8% of facilities said they plan to evaluate new continuous upstream technologies in the next 12 months (Source). ⚠️ On the other hand, the downstream continuous chromatography adoption rate lags the upstream evaluation rate at 30.8% in 2024 and 33.9% in 2025 (Source). ⚠️ Research and field perspectives from the same series support that this gap between upstream and downstream is not a coincidence. 66.1% of respondents cited operational complexity as a concern for perfusion-based systems (Source) ⚠️, and more fundamentally, the unresolved interface mismatch between steady upstream harvest and cyclic downstream purification, rather than individual unit process technology, is identified as the dominant obstacle to continuous bioprocessing. This discrepancy requires a surge tank or hold step (Source). ⚠️

Regulations and policies were designed to encourage this movement. ICH Q13 (2022) institutionally enables batch size flexibility (uptime-based), continuous process verification, and RTRT, and the FDA Emerging Technology Program and advanced manufacturing path provide incentives for adoption. In PAT/RTRT, Raman has established itself as a representative in-line sensor and has proven feedback control such as glucose control → glycation reduction as seen earlier, but RTRT for all biologic drugs is limited by the absence of in-line efficacy and sterility tests. In digital, MES and historians have become the standard for commercial plants, while digital twins and AI/ML soft sensors are on the rise but largely remain in PD and offline, with propaganda outpacing proven deployment. The vendor landscape (Cytiva·Sartorius·Repligen·Pall·Merck for continuous/convergent equipment; WuXi Biologics·Enzene·Just-Evotec for integrated continuous CDMO; PAT sensor/MES suppliers) is summarized in this section, but as _uncertainty_notes reveals, some of the vendor-product mapping is based primarily on trade/secondary sources and can change over time, so caution is required. ⚠️ Remaining unresolved issues include interface inconsistency, difficulties with continuous scale-down models, genetic stability in long-term operation, data integrity of ML models, regulatory acceptance within CSV and GMP, reduced facility flexibility due to long-term operation, and high initial investment.

Issue: Between promise and proven reality

Issue:The biggest point of disagreement between sources in this section is the “maturity of end-to-end continuity.” Some vendors/CDMOs already offer commercial end-to-end continuity — for example, one vendor claims 5 to 15 times productivity compared to batch with intensive perfusion culture + continuous harvest + continuous capture (Source). ⚠️ However, this claim is based on vendor marketing materials (low reliability) and requires independent verification, and as we saw earlier, peer-reviewed and industry research shows that downstream lags upstream (approximately 34% continuous chromato adoption) and that interface inconsistency is the dominant obstacle — a tension between vendor marketing and survey reality. Second, there are tensions surrounding the scope of biologic RTRT. FDA/ICH suggest RTRT as a final test alternative, but ICH Q13 itself states that efficacy and microbiological testing are required offline, so “real-time release” here is a partial realization at the level of some attributes rather than the entire product. Third, although the digital twin trade source touts dramatic benefits (shortened development, solvent reduction, closed-loop control), the same and similar sources also acknowledge the limitation that the model deviates from the data domain, so propaganda is ahead of GMP verification deployment. Fourth, the economic feasibility argument of continuous operation (cost reduction, small footprint) and its limitations (decreased facility flexibility due to long-term operation, high initial investment, operational complexity 66.1%) are conflicting, and cost effectiveness is greatly dependent on comparative assumptions such as run length, equipment, and year-round operation.Unresolved Questions: - The detailed implementation of the plant floor of MES (automation of electronic batch records and actual cases of exception-based review) and the actual status of commercial GMP closed-loop deployment of Digital Twin depend on trade and secondary sources due to limited primary regulations and case evidence. - PCC/SMB/MCSGP’s quantitative cost savings (e.g. 20~30%) and vendor-specific productivity multiples (5~15X) are claims from vendors and secondary sources, and the independent verification basis is weak. - The specific requirements for GMP verification (CSV) and regulatory acceptance standards for AI/ML-based soft sensors were not secured as primary evidence in this investigation.

6. Quality Control — analytical testing

The analytical/laboratory arm: methods that identify, quantify, and qualify product quality and safety for release and stability.

6.1 Identity, purity & impurity testing

Role, scope and location

This step answers two different questions in quality control of finished product/dose (DP/DS). One is the identity check, “Is this substance really the target molecule we were trying to make?” and the other is the purity/impurity check, “How pure is it and how many impurities remain?” Upon receiving the raw solution purified by the downstream process (DSP), an identity test is performed to identify the target molecule, and a test is performed to quantify and characterize variants derived from the target substance (product-related, e.g. aggregates, fragments, charge mutations, glycan mutations) and impurities introduced in the process (e.g. HCP, residual DNA, residual Protein A).

The reason why this task is inevitable comes from the structure of recombinant production itself. During the production of these products, host cell-derived materials other than the target are inevitably introduced into the process stream, resulting in a mixture of target and host cell-derived impurities (including HCP), which are subject to removal through bioprocessing (Source). In other words, the key to 6.1 is to prove that it is “sufficiently pure” by measuring the amount remaining after removal through purification. The calculated results serve as the basis for batch release determination, stability testing, comparative equivalence/biosimilar verification, and process verification (verification of impurity removal ability).The design idea throughout this section comes from the higher-level regulatory framework, ICH Q6B. Q6B stipulates that since the absolute purity of biotechnology medicinal products varies depending on the method used, purity should usually be assessed by more than one method (Source). Since there is no one universal test, the basic principle in this field is to construct a panel using a combination of orthogonal methods targeting different physical properties such as size, charge, hydrophobicity, mass, sequence, and glycan.

How to test — Construction of an orthogonal panel

Identity.Whether the molecule is correct is confirmed by peptide mapping (digestion with trypsin, etc. and then confirmation of sequence and modification with RP-LC-MS/MS), intact/subunit mass spectrometry (confirmation of molecule with accurate mass), charge profile fingerprint such as icIEF/IEX, and target binding ELISA/SPR. By overlapping the independent characteristics of sequence, mass, charge, and bonding, the conclusion that “this molecule is correct” is supported from various angles.Size variation and aggregates. Monomer ratio and high molecular weight aggregates (HMW) and low molecular weight fragments (LMW) are quantified by size exclusion chromatography (SEC/SE-HPLC), and purity, fragments, and disulfide bond integrity are evaluated by reduced/non-reduced CE-SDS. Ultracentrifugation (AUC-SV) is used as an orthogonal aggregate measurement complementary to SEC.Charge mutation. Acidic and basic variants are separated and quantified using cation exchange (CEX) and imaged capillary IEF (icIEF). At this time, acidic mutations originate from deamidation, glycation, and sialylated glycans, and basic mutations arise from C-terminal lysine retention or aspartate isomerization (Source). The identity of the modification itself is identified using intact/peptide MS.Glycan. N-glycan characterization is performed by first enzymatically cutting the glycan from the denatured protein with PNGase F, and then separating the released glycan using a dedicated HILIC column (Source). The cleaved glycan is fluorescently labeled (2-AB, etc.), quantified by HILIC-FLR or CE-LIF, and the structure is confirmed by LC-MS.Process-derived impurities. It is customary to measure the total amount of HCPs using a polyclonal anti-HCP antibody-based sandwich ELISA, but this ELISA only gives total impurity levels and provides no information about the identity and amount of individual HCPs (Source). Therefore, regulatory authorities recommend the use of LC–MS as an orthogonal method to ELISA in HCP analysis (Source), and LC-MS/MS is used in parallel to identify and quantify individual HCPs. Residual host cell DNA is evaluated by quantitative PCR, including real-time PCR, and the results are expressed as ng/mL or ng per dose (Source). ⚠️ In the case of mAb, the eluate (residual Protein A) of Protein A used for purification is also measured by ELISA.Integrated approach—multi-attribute method (MAM). MAM is a method used to replace many traditional HPLC/CE-based purity/impurity tests for release and stability testing of protein biopharmaceuticals (particularly monoclonal antibodies) (Source). Structurally, MAM is an LC-MS-based peptide mapping approach that consists of two data analysis steps: (1) sample preparation (denaturation, reduction, alkylation, enzymatic digestion), (2) reversed-phase separation and high-resolution accurate mass detection, and (3) target quantitative monitoring and new peak detection (NPD) to capture unexpected new peaks (Source). In other words, the goal is to quantify several quality attributes at once through one-time peptide mapping and detect unexpected impurities and modifications through new peaks compared to the reference product.

Key parameters and materials

To actually operate this panel, the key is what to measure and to what extent. At its center is the extreme heterogeneity of HCPs. HCPs have isoelectric points ranging from approximately 3 to 11, have different hydrophobicities, and molecular weights range widely from about 5 kDa to at least 250 kDa, and hundreds to over a thousand types of proteins may be present in upstream samples (Source). This diversity is the root cause of difficulty in verifying antibody coverage, which will be discussed later.

HCP units also require attention. Conventionally, HCP is calculated as the ng value obtained by interpolating from the HCP standard curve (unit of ng/mL), but the ppm notation is not recommended in <1132> because it is a term used to indicate mass per unit volume in other types of tests (Source). In other words, the immuno-equivalent ng/mg notation meets the norm rather than the commonly seen “ppm”.

In terms of residual DNA, the widely cited standard is the content/size limit of 10 ng per dose and size of 200 bp recommended by WHO and FDA guidelines for final products derived from continuous cell lines (Source). However, as seen in the later issues, this is not an absolute limit but a risk-based recommendation. SEC is standardized as monomer%·HMW%·LMW%, CE-SDS as purity%, and charge variation as main/acidic/basic peak %. Parameters such as mobile phase pH, salt gradient, column temperature, and digestion conditions are carefully controlled as they can cause artefacts such as deamidation and oxidation during digestion. In terms of materials, the key elements are reference standards, polyclonal anti-HCP antibodies and HCP standards for HCP, host cell genomic DNA standards and primers/probes for residual DNA, digestive enzymes (trypsin, Lys-C) and PNGase F, and glycan fluorescent labeling reagents.

Quality and regulatory perspective

The reason why we care so much about HCPs comes from their dangerous nature. The primary concern about HCP in biopharmaceuticals is the possibility of inducing anti-HCP antibodies that can have clinical effects in patients, and in addition, HCP may act as an adjuvant and induce anti-drug antibodies (ADA) (Source). Even trace amounts can affect safety and efficacy, so quantitative and management are essential.However, HCP quantification is inherently difficult. The design and validation of HCP immunoassays poses unique and significant challenges: (1) the wide diversity of possible HCPs in the product, (2) the use of polyclonal antibody reagents for their detection, (3) the absence of an exact matching standard for quantitation, (4) sometimes significant sample dilution, and (5) the inherent limitations of measuring a single HCP species (Source). In particular, if a polyclonal antibody does not evenly recognize all HCPs, some HCPs will be missed, so antibody coverage must be proven separately.

The timing of measurement is also specified. HCP levels should be measured in (1) preclinical batches used for toxicity evaluation, (2) all batches during clinical development, and (3) process validation samples from the final manufacturing process (Source). On the other hand, there is no universal numerical limit of “how much should it be.” HCP guidance documents do not suggest numerical limits for HCP levels because the risks associated with HCP exposure depend on the clinical situation, including dose, route of administration, frequency of exposure, indications, patient population, and the nature of specific impurities (Source). In other words, the limit is something justified by risk assessment, not a number nailed down by the Pharmacopoeia.

In the coverage verification method, the Antibody Affinity Extraction (AAE) coverage evaluation method developed by Cygnus Technologies to overcome the deficiencies of the traditional orthogonal method is included in USP General Chapter <1132> (Source). ⚠️ Looking at the entire regulatory/pharmacopeia landscape, ICH Q6B (higher level framework), ICH Q2(R2) (analytical method validation), ICH Q5A(R2) (viral safety/adventitious agents), and USP <509> (residual DNA), <1132> (HCP), and <129> (mAb) form the basis for this section.

The most recent regulatory changes have come in the HCP space. USP published Chapter <1132.1> for LC-MS/MS-based HCP measurement on its website starting December 1, 2024, and officially took effect on May 1, 2025 (Source). As a result, the MS approach to identify and quantify individual HCPs was institutionalized as an option to supplement or replace the coverage limitations of ELISA.

Variations depending on product type

This panel is most mature for mAb/recombinant proteins (SEC, CE-SDS, CEX, icIEF, HILIC glycans, HCP ELISA, residual DNA qPCR, and residual Protein A are the standards, and MAM applications are also leading in this area), but for the remaining modalities, the very definition of “purity” changes.

mRNA-LNP.The protein purity paradigm is replaced by the nucleic acid/particle paradigm. Here, 5’ capping efficiency is a key quality attribute (CQA) of therapeutic mRNA vaccines (Source) ⚠️, RNA integrity (full length %), poly(A) tail, residual dsRNA (causing immunogenicity), residual template DNA, and LNP encapsulation efficiency and particle size are the centers of the identity/purity axis.Viral vector/CGT (AAV, etc.). Here, the definition of purity is “intact particle containing the correct genome” rather than “pure protein,” and the empty/full (and partial) capsid ratio is the key CQA. Empty and partial capsids are non-functional and immunogenic, which triggers an immune response, limiting re-administration and causing inflammation, and impurities such as host-cell DNA packaged together in these non-functional capsids can lead to off-target effects and genotoxicity (Source). ⚠️ Among the methods for measuring this ratio, ultracentrifugation (AUC) is widely considered the gold standard for empty/full ratio determination due to its fundamental physical separation principle and high accuracy (Source) ⚠️. In addition, AEX-HPLC, mass photometry, cryo-TEM, and ddPCR are also used.Classical vaccine. The focus is on antigen identification (immunochemistry/SDS-PAGE/Western), adjuvants/preservatives, residual cytosolic DNA, and medium-derived impurities tests, and the proportion of molecular-level charge variation/glycan profiling is relatively low.

Current practice and recent trends

The current standard is an orthogonal panel distributed by method (SEC+CE-SDS+CEX/icIEF+HILIC+HCP ELISA+DNA qPCR), and HCP ELISA and SEC/charge variation chromatography are the mainstays of release testing. There are three distinct movements over the past three years. First, LC-MS/MS-based HCP was standardized. With the entry into force of USP <1132.1> (May 2025), MS best practices for identifying and quantifying individual HCPs were formalized, opening the way to complement and replace the coverage bias and individual identification limitations of ELISA (Source). Second, as MAM spreads beyond the characterization stage and into QC/batch release, the trend to replace multiple HPLC/CE purity tests with a single LC-MS peptide mapping + NPD has become stronger (Source). Third, new product modality analysis (mass photometry·AUC of AAV empty/full, mRNA capping·integrity assay) has grown rapidly. Underlying all of this movement is the direction of regulatory authorities recommending LC–MS as an orthogonal method to ELISA for HCP analysis (Source).

Issue

There are three points where opinions differ among sources.
