First, the role of ELISA and LC-MS/MS in HCP release quantification.On one side, ELISA is believed to remain dominant in regulation and the market as it remains the release standard for measuring total HCP quickly and at low cost. On the other hand, it is emphasized that LC-MS/MS was institutionalized as a “complement or replacement” with USP <1132.1> (effective 2025), as ELISA does not identify individual HCPs and polyclonal antibodies suffer from coverage bias that misses weakly immunogenic and low molecular weight HCPs. Sources agree that MS is orthogonal/complementary, but are divided between “complementary” and “replacement” on whether MS can actually replace ELISA in its releases.Second, can MAM replace traditional purity panels? MAM is designed to replace multiple HPLC/CE purity tests with a single LC-MS peptide mapping + NPD and is spreading with QC release. However, MAM is a peptide-level method and cannot replace SEC because it cannot measure higher-order structural properties such as aggregates, and the false positives and regulatory acceptability of NPD are still in the mature stage. In the end, the difference in definition of “substitution range” is an issue, in that list-type properties (charge mutation and transformation) are replaceable, but orthogonal methods are still required for physical size and aggregation properties.Third, is 10 ng/dose·200 bp of residual DNA an absolute limit? Although WHO/FDA/EMA has adopted it as a widely cited limit for continuous cell line-derived products, there is a counterargument that this is only a recommendation and that risk assessment considering the activity/amount of product, cell substrate, process, and DNA is the key. The tension between standardized numbers and risk-based flexibility is evident across product types, especially in the case of CGT/human-derived cell lines, where reinterpretation of these values ​​is necessary.Unresolved questions: (1) Quantitative verification parameters (representative numerical ranges of accuracy/precision/LOD/LOQ) of each method were not obtained as concrete values ​​from the source. (2) Actual release specification values ​​for specific product groups (e.g., lower monomer % limit, company-specific practices for HCP ng/mg limit) are risk-based and confidential, so generalized values ​​were not obtained.

6.2 Potency & bioassays

Roles and Positions: Not “How many are there”, but “Do they work?”

Potency and bioassay are tests that quantify whether a biopharmaceutical actually has biological activity. This is a question on a different level from how many proteins or particles are contained (mass), and it is the core quality characteristic (CQA) that most directly reflects whether the product actually performs its intended therapeutic purpose. ICH Q6B defines potency as “a quantitative measure of activity (expressed in units) linked to the relevant biological properties of the product” and clearly distinguishes it from quantity, which is “a physicochemical measure of protein content (expressed in mass)” (Source). In other words, while measuring content is “how much drug is contained,” potency asks “does the drug actually work on cells, receptors, and pathways?”

In the overall process flow, this test is located in the analytical development and QC shipping testing stages. We receive raw drug substances (DS)/finished drug products (DP) that have completed purification and characterization, and usually calculate the relative potency value compared to the reference standard and provide it as the basis for lot shipment, stability testing, comparability after manufacturing changes, and product characterization. In particular, reference materials serve as a bridge between the materials administered to patients today and the batches used in clinical trials, linking manufacturing process consistency to clinical effectiveness. Therefore, potency tests should in principle reflect the intended biological effect of the product and ideally should correlate with clinical response (Source). Because every lot release ultimately depends on a passing titer result, titer is called the final analytical hurdle to be crossed just before product shipment.

Principles and Flow: Relative Potency and Parallelism Assumptions

Potency is almost always measured relatively. The dose-response curve of the test sample is compared with the curve of the reference material, and the horizontal shift (potency ratio) between the two curves is taken as the relative titer and reported as a percentage (e.g., 70-130%) of the reference material. This relative potency model rests on the similarity/parallelism assumption. Since the test and reference substances must behave like identical preparations differing only in concentration, the two dose-response curves must be parallel, and thus parallelism is both a scientific assumption of the relative potency model and a formal system suitability requirement. Tests that fail the parallelism test should be invalidated (Source). The curve is usually fit with a non-linear four-parameter logistic (4PL) model in the section that crosses the EC50.

ICH Q6B recognizes four formats of potency assays: Other procedures include (1) in vivo bioassay using animals, (2) cell-based bioassay (proliferation, cytotoxicity, reporter gene, ADCC/CDC, etc.), (3) biochemical/enzyme assay to measure the biological activity of the product, and (4) ligand/receptor binding assay (ELISA, SPR/BLI) (Source). Among these, the reporter gene assay connects target pathway activation to luminescence/fluorescence signals, providing a readout that reflects the mechanism of action (MOA) while having lower variability than the primary cell function assay. The assay design (USP <1032>) controls positional effects such as plate edge effects and controls suitability through serial dilution, repetition, randomization, and blocking throughout the reaction section, and the data analysis (USP <1034>) estimates titers with confidence intervals using a parallel line/parallel curve model and performs an equivalence-based parallelism test (Source). In complex products, a single assay cannot cover all mechanisms, and as biological activity is mediated by multiple factors, it is difficult to fully reflect the product function with a single label or assay. In this case, an assay matrix combining binding, functional, and cell-based assays is needed (Source).

Key parameters and materials: anchor called reference material

The anchor that anchors this entire test is the reference material, and it is usually operated in a two-tier system. The Primary Reference Standard (PRS) is the ‘gold standard’ from which all subsequent Working Reference Standards (WRS) are qualified and is not used for routine shipment. WRS is taken from the standard manufacturing stream, qualified against PRS, and then used for routine shipment testing (Source). In reality, most manufacturers do not have international reference materials and instead use engineering lots or pivotal clinical batches as PRS (Source).In terms of materials and reagents, key raw materials such as working cell bank (WCB) with a defined passage range, assay antibodies, detection substrates, and serum lots are key, and variation between reagent lots is a major cause of assay drift. Cell-based titer assays are highly dependent on the inherent variability of the cell line itself, and key method attributes (CMA) such as cell seeding density, culture time, and temperature significantly affect assay performance (Source). Equipment used include luminescence/fluorescence/absorbance plate readers, flow cytometers, SPR/BLI (Biacore, Octet) for binding measurement, and qPCR/ddPCR for transduction-based titer of viral vectors. The output values include relative titer (%), 95% confidence interval, system suitability index (reference material EC50, assay window/signal-to-background ratio, parallelism), and specification range (commonly 50~150% or 70~130%). Validation parameters follow USP <1033>, where accuracy is operationally defined only for designated reference materials, cannot be established absolutely, and is assessed by recovery studies of samples of known relative potency (Source). Precision is demonstrated at the level of repeatability, intermediate precision, and reproducibility, while specificity is proven by an orthogonal approach (neutralizing reagents, stress/mutant substances, etc.), and linearity and range are also addressed.

Quality and Regulatory: Essential CQA and Specification Framework

Potency is a CQA that must be included in the specifications. ICH Q6B requires that bioassay results be expressed in units of activity calibrated against international or national reference materials, where possible and appropriate, and that characterized in-house reference materials be established if such reference materials are not available (Source). ⚠️

The governing standards and guidance are layered. ICH Q6B in specifications, ICH Q2(R2) in method validation, and the USP bioassay trilogy form the axis. <1032> deals with design/development and experimental structure, <1033> deals with validation characteristics, plans, and criteria, and <1034> deals with data analysis, potency estimation, confidence intervals, and statistical tests (including parallelism approach) (Source). USP <1033> specifies a different logic from general analysis methods, such as tailoring the verification to the relative titer method and defining accuracy only against a designated standard since there is no absolute ‘true value’ (Source). Ph. Eur here. 5.3 (bioassay statistical analysis) and WHO/NIBSC international standards are added.

For cell and gene therapy (CGT), the FDA issued draft guidance ‘Potency Assurance for Cellular and Gene Therapy Products’ in December 2023, shifting the axis of control from single potency testing to potency assurance strategy. It is defined as “a multifaceted approach to reduce risk to product potency through manufacturing process design, manufacturing process control, raw material management, in-process testing, and potency lot release verification” (Source). This draft replaces FDA’s previous 2011 guidance (Source). As a regulatory nuance, the FDA generally expects a quantitative functional potency assay for release, whereas the EMA may allow a surrogate assay for release if the functional assay is reserved for characterization and a correlation between the two assays can be demonstrated (Source). ⚠️

Differences by product type

Monoclonal Antibodies/Recombinant Proteins is the most mature area, with cell-based (reporter gene, proliferation, ADCC/CDC) and binding assays, well-established two-step reference material systems, and standard relative titers compared to reference materials.

In mRNA-LNP vaccines, cell-based potency assays confirm that the functionally active antigen encoded by the mRNA sequence is actually expressed in mammalian cells such as HEK-293·HeLa. When LNPs are administered directly to cells, expressed and released antigens can be detected with antibodies (Source). Furthermore, loss of potency can be detected even before the observed loss of mRNA integrity becomes noticeable, making in vitro potency assays a very sensitive indicator of stability (Source). ⚠️

For viral vectors/CGT, functional/infectious titers are more appropriate than genomic titers. Functional titer measurement, which measures the cell transduction ability of AAV and transgene expression, is a more meaningful indicator than genomic titer in evaluating viral titer (Source). ⚠️ As MOA is often multifactorial or not fully characterized, an assay matrix is ​​virtually required, and potency assay defects remain a major cause of clinical hold in cell and gene therapy programs (Source). ⚠️

Traditional vaccines have historically relied on in vivo (animal) potency assays, but in vitro replacement methods for human vaccine potency assays are being widely developed due to the large demand for animals, long tests, and high variability (Source). Nevertheless, potency or potency-indicating assays for each lot remain a shipping requirement.

Practices and changes

Standard practice is to develop a validated relative potency bioassay performed against a two-step reference material using DoE/QbD to increase robustness and validate using USP <1033>/ICH Q2(R2). There are five trends over the past three years. First, the FDA’s December 2023 draft CGT potency assurance shifted the focus from single assay thinking to a risk-based assay matrix/strategy (Source). Second, with the revision of the USP bioassay chapter, the final versions of <1030> and <1033> are targeted for official entry into force in 2026, and <1032> and <1034> are scheduled to be updated in the 2025-2030 cycle (Source). ⚠️ Third, thanks to the 3

Rs drive, the movement to replace in vivo vaccine potency testing with in vitro consistency methods is accelerating (Source). Fourth, the adoption of reporter gene platforms and DoE/QbD that reflect low volatility and MOA is expanding, and fifth, digital/statistical automation of parallelism and relative titer analysis is progressing.

There are also clear unresolved challenges. The variability of cell-based assays remains persistently high (Source), and in practice, reliance on single titer assays has repeatedly proven insufficient and, in some cases, delayed regulatory approval (Source). ⚠️ Additionally, if a manufacturing change alters the dose-response curve, it may become impossible to value the new reference material from the old reference material, breaking direct bridging (Source). ⚠️ To manage this, a WRS qualification protocol, a reference material stability program, and a monitoring program that collects and evaluates official trend data are operated, usually on a semi-annual or annual basis (Source). ⚠️

Issue:The sources diverge at several points. First, regarding whether to allow non-functional surrogate tests in release tests, the FDA generally expects quantitative functional potency tests even for ATMPs, while the EMA may allow surrogate tests if there is a functional test for characterization and a correlation is proven — the difference is in regulatory risk tolerance whether to prioritize readouts directly linked to the MOA or to bypass slow or highly variable functional tests to enable timely shipment. Second, there is the issue of single test vs test matrix for complex products, where a well-designed single MOA reflective test is the traditional expectation and operationally simple, but single test has been repeatedly insufficient for CGT/complex products, and the FDA 2023 draft reorganizes controls into a multi-factor assurance strategy/matrix — multiplexing increases the development/validation burden, and sources are divided on ‘how much is enough’. Third, genomic titer vs. functional/infectious titer is an indicator of viral vector titer. Although genomic titer is precise, rapid, and widely used, titer can be overestimated by counting even empty capsids, and functional titer better captures the true biological activity and empty/partial capsid effects, but has much greater variability.Open Questions: (1) Failure to anchor quantitative benchmarks of achievable bioassay precision (e.g., typical %GCV/intermediate precision range) to primary sources. (2) The specific statistical mechanism (specific boundary value) of the equivalence-based parallelism acceptance criterion cannot be extracted as is from USP <1034>.

6.3 Adventitious agent & viral safety testing

Role and Scope — “Finding”, not “Creating”

In biopharmaceutical QC analysis, this section is responsible for detecting the safety of adventitious agents and viruses. Samples from various processing points, such as cell substrate, cell bank (MCB/WCB), production limit cells (LIVCA), virus seed lot, unprocessed bulk harvest, and purified bulk, are received and tested for the presence of contaminating viruses, other adventitious agents, and endogenous retroviruses harbored by the cell line itself. There is one conceptually important distinction here. This section is different from viral clearance (node ​​3.2) in downstream purification processes, which “create” safety by removing or inactivating the virus. The two activities represent different pillars of the virus safety strategy defined by ICH Q5A(R2). The three pillars are (1) testing that cell lines and raw materials are free from viruses, (2) testing to detect contamination at each production stage (the focus of this section), and (3) verifying the ability of the process to remove and inactivate viruses (Source). The output of this section, the “virus detection/absence” determination, flows to the batch release decision and CMC document (CTD 3.2.A.2 Adventitious Agents Safety Evaluation).

Pointing out the fundamental limitations of this section in advance is the key to understanding the discussion that follows. Detection of nucleic acids or particles does not immediately prove the presence of an infectious virus.Therefore, detection tests always work complementary to clearance verification, complementing each other.

How it works — a combination of broad and specific tests

The framework of the detection strategy follows the philosophy of ICH Q5A. In other words, it combines “broad/non-specific testing” and “specific virus (specific) testing.”(1) Extensive testing — A net that catches even unknown and unexpected viruses.The representative example is in vitro cell culture infectivity testing. After inoculating and co-culturing samples into various indicator/detector cell lines such as MRC-5, Vero, and HeLa, and the production cell line itself, infection is determined using cytopathic effect (CPE), hemadsorption (HAD), hemagglutination (HA), and immunofluorescence (IF). ICH Q5A(R2) stipulates that the indicator cell culture of the unprocessed bulk is usually observed for 28 days and passaged at least once every 2 weeks, which can be shortened to 14 days if justified by risk assessment (Source). Another extensive test, in vivo testing, involves inoculating test substances into suckling mice, adult mice, and embryonated hen’s eggs, observing the animal’s health, and investigating the cause if any abnormalities appear (Source).(2) Specific virus test — target narrowed by risk assessment. Targets specific viruses based on risk assessment of cell origin (human/bovine/porcine/rodent) and raw materials (bovine serum, porcine trypsin, etc.). In particular, antibody production tests have traditionally been used for rodent-derived cell lines. These are the mouse antibody production test (MAP), rat antibody production test (RAP), and hamster antibody production test (HAP). ICH Q5A(R2) specifies that this animal test can be replaced by molecular methods such as NAT such as PCR or targeted/untargeted NGS without head-to-head comparison (Source).(3) Retrovirus — a separate track that is not detected by the infectivity test. Endogenous retroviruses contained in cell lines are not detected by the above infectivity test, so a separate method is required. Retrovirus-like particles (RVLP; types A and C) in the cell substrate and cell-free culture supernatant are observed and quantified using a transmission electron microscope (TEM), and reverse transcriptase activity is detected using the PERT (product-enhanced reverse transcriptase)/RT test.(4) Molecular methods — New tools officially introduced by Q5A(R2). This includes NAT (single/multiple PCR) and NGS/HTS. PCR detects only known sequences, whereas NGS is fundamentally different in that it is a hypothesis-free approach that does not require prior sequence information (Source). NGS detects a wide range of known and unknown viruses using a non-targeted method, or targets specific viruses using bioinformatics. Different strategies are chosen depending on the nature of the sample. It is divided into genomics, which looks at whole genome nucleic acids extracted from cells, transcriptomics, which looks at viral mRNA, and viromics, which looks at encapsidated viral genomes in cell-free supernatants. When analyzing cell culture-derived materials, cell-derived nucleic acids are used in genomics and transcriptomics, and culture supernatants or cell-free virus preparations are used in viromics ([Source] (https://www.ema.europa.eu/en/documents/scientific-guideline/ich-q5ar2-guideline-viral-safety-evaluation-biotechnology-products-derived-cell-lines-human-or-animal-origin-step-5_en.pdf)). The NGS workflow consists of four key steps. These include sample preparation and virus concentration, nucleic acid extraction and library construction (both DNA and RNA), selection of an appropriate sequencing platform, and bioinformatics analysis against databases representing various virus families. At this time, if a positive signal is obtained, it must be investigated in a follow-up experiment whether the nucleic acid is related to the actual infectious virus (Source).

Key parameters and materials

Sample Matrix.Test subjects range from MCB/WCB, marginal production cells (LIVCA), viral seeds, raw bulk harvest (a mixture of cells and cell lysates or cell-free supernatant), and purified bulk.In vitro infectivity test. The standard indicator cell line is MRC-5·Vero·HeLa plus a production cell line. In fact, in a systematic comparative study, CPE was observed regularly for 14 days using MRC-5, HeLa, and Vero monolayers as indicator cells, and hemagglutination (HA) and hemagglutination (HAD) were tested (Source). The detection limits of these tests are often much more sensitive than in vivo.in vivo test. It uses suckling, adult mice and developing eggs, and is the preferred replacement subject of the 3Rs principle of replacing, reducing and improving animal testing.Retrovirus-related. TEM quantifies RVLP by calculating the number of particles in proportion to the reference bead, but since the particles are not uniformly distributed in cell sections, quantification is difficult, so it is reported as the ratio (%) of infected cells. According to data from one service provider, TEM detects and quantifies adventitious agents such as parvo, polyoma, picorna, reo, and adenovirus, as well as type A and C retrovirus particles (Source) ⚠️. According to Case A of Q5A, if RVLP is not detected and the PERT test is negative, there is no need to estimate the number of retroviral particles per dose (Source).NGS verification. When verifying NGS as a detection method, matrix-specific verification is expected using the WHO reference panel (or equivalent) representing different virus families, particle sizes, presence of envelopes, and genome topology, and the limit of detection (LOD) is determined by independently repeating the end-to-end method from sample preparation to analysis at least three times (Source). Since NGS is a limit test, the performance indicators (specificity, detection width, sensitivity) of method validation and qualification should consider the principles of ICH Q2 (Source).Replication-competent virus (RCV) test. While the gold standard cell culture method takes up to 45 days to produce results, ddPCR is presented as an attractive orthogonal method that produces results in approximately 8 hours (Source) ⚠️. The FDA recommends testing enough supernatant to detect replication-competent viruses with a 95% probability even if they are present at a level of 1 copy per dose (Source) ⚠️.Output and decision rules. The final output is a “virus detected/absent” decision. If adventitious viruses are detected at the raw bulk stage, the cell culture harvest should not be used in product manufacturing unless justified (Source).

Quality and regulatory framework

Key quality attributes (CQAs) are the absence of adventitious and endogenous viruses, and the absence of replication-competent and residual helper viruses. The highest level guideline governing this section is ICH Q5A(R2), formally EMA/CHMP/ICH/804363/2022 (Step 5). Revision 2 was announced on November 1, 2023, and adopted by the US FDA in January 2024 and the European EMA in June 2024 (Source). Compensatory documents that supplement this include USP <1050>/<1050.1>, which deals with virus safety evaluation of biotechnology products and design of clearance procedures, and Ph. Eur, which regulates adventitious agent testing for human viral vaccines. 2.6.16, and 5.2.3 for the cytosol.

From a detection perspective, three new things introduced by Q5A(R2) are particularly important. First, molecular methods such as NGS and PCR were introduced as formal virus screening tools. Second, non-targeted NGS was recognized as a means of replacing in vivo testing and supplementing or replacing in vitro cell culture testing without head-to-head comparison as long as suitability for purpose is proven (Source). In fact, Q5A(R2) “encourages” non-targeted NGS as an alternative to in vivo testing, citing the wide detection range and high sensitivity of NGS, the limitations of in vivo testing, and the international 3Rs initiative to replace, reduce, and improve animal testing (Source). Third, genetically engineered viral vectors, VLPs, and nanoparticle vaccines have been included in the regulatory scope, and related unprocessed bulk tests are covered in the annex.

Methodologically, it is also clearly explained why head-to-head comparison is not required. The results of in vitro/in vivo infectivity tests depend on virus replication and biological properties in a specific target system, so the detection range is fundamentally limited, and the end point itself is different from NGS, which detects a wide range of known and unknown viruses. Therefore, direct comparison of two tests of different nature is not recommended (Source). The structure of the verification burden is also determined. If NGS is used as an alternative method, matrix-specific validation is required in addition to method validation, and if NGS is used as a complementary method, matrix validation is required in addition to method qualification. And, as previously emphasized, positive nucleic acid results must be followed up to determine whether they are associated with an infectious virus. In terms of operating rules, unprocessed bulk is routinely tested for adventitious viruses in each batch, and if detected, use is prohibited in principle (Source).

Differences by product type

mAb·recombinant protein (CHO, etc.).Follows the standard system. MCB/WCB undergoes retroviral testing (TEM/PERT) in vitro and in vivo (or alternatively NGS), and unprocessed bulk undergoes extensive testing on a batch-by-batch basis. Endogenous RVLP in CHO cells is already well characterized, so retesting of purified bulk is usually not necessary (corresponding to Q5A Case B).Viral vector/gene therapy (AAV, lentivirus, adenovirus). This is where a fundamental difficulty arises. Because the product itself is a virus, it does not effectively neutralize the vector or the in vitro infectivity test suffers from assay interference due to the toxicity of the product and medium components. This is where Q5A(R2) states that NGS is particularly useful for cell line characterization or testing of cell banks, viral seeds, and raw bulk (Source). Replication-competent virus testing (RCA/RCL/rcAAV) and residual helper virus testing are additionally required for vector products, and since the virus clearance stage is limited in the vector process, this is supplemented by strengthening extensive detection testing and reducing raw material risks. Q5A(R2) included VLPs, protein subunits made from baculovirus, herpes-simplex virus, and adenovirus production systems, nanoparticle-based vaccines, and viral vectors such as AAV within the scope of the new annex (Source) ⚠️.mRNA-LNP. Because it is a cell-free IVT (in vitro transcription) reaction, the risk of viruses originating from the cell substrate is low, and the management focus shifts to plasmid banks, enzymes (E. coli/cell-derived), and adventitious agents in IVT raw materials. However, I would like to honestly state that this section is [uncertain] area that relies on some inferences due to the limited primary evidence to be cited directly within the scope of this section.Classical vaccine (egg/cell culture). The adventitious agent test of the virus seed lot system is the key (Ph.Eur. 2.6.16), and in cell-based vaccines, the cell substrate qualification test (Q5A / Ph.Eur. 5.2.3) is the key.

Practices and recent trends

Standard practice still is a combination of broad in vitro infectivity tests, specific PCR, retroviral tests (TEM/PERT), and historically used in vivo and rodent antibody production tests. The biggest change in the past three years is that non-targeted NGS/HTS was recognized as an official replacement for in vivo testing and multiple PCR through ICH Q5A(R2) (2023-2024). At the 4th NGS Conference held in December 2024, there was general agreement between industry and regulation that NGS is ready to replace in vivo adventitious virus detection tests and PCR tests (Source).

The evidence justifying this replacement is multifaceted. In one systematic comparative study, in vitro tests were more sensitive than in vivo for almost all viruses, while in vivo only detected 6 of 11 viruses, 4 of which were detected only at doses more than one order of magnitude higher than 1 TCID50 or PFU (Source). In addition, in a direct comparison in which several viruses were spiked into a complex matrix, in vitro and HTS detected both Reo1 and RSV, while in vivo detected only Reo1 (Source). MilliporeSigma states that in the 50 years of use of in vivo testing, there has never been a case where it has single-handedly identified viral contamination that was not detected by other simultaneous tests, and suggests NGS as an alternative (Source) ⚠️.

However, it is not all optimism. Several unresolved challenges exist side by side. First, gaps in standardization and verification of the bioinformatics pipeline are pointed out. In fact, interlaboratory detection sensitivity of HTS varied by up to 2 log due to different sample processing protocols and laboratory-specific bioinformatics pipelines (Source). To address this, challenging the pipeline by spiking standardized synthetic sequences into the dataset is discussed (Source) ⚠️. Second, since nucleic acid detection does not mean infectiousness, a positive result creates the burden of orthogonal confirmation. Third, in complex matrices, cell background interferes with detection. Fourth, statistics and references (WHO panel) to verify NGS as an alternative method are still in the process of being established. And there is one more interesting twist. Analysis of CAACB data found that even in vitro viral testing missed 5 out of 18 GMP contamination events as false negatives, suggesting that this test alone may not be sufficient to detect contamination (Source) ⚠️. That is, no single method is complete. Meanwhile, there is a parallel trend toward near-real-time decision-making through rapid methods such as RCV’s ddPCR (approximately 8 hours) and Mycoplasma NAT.

Issue:Sources differ on two points. The first is the annex number. BioProcess International (2nd article) refers to viral vector/VLP testing as ‘Annex 7’, but the official ICH Q5A(R2) article (EMA/CHMP/ICH/804363/2022, 1st article) refers to raw bulk testing of genetically engineered viral vectors as ‘Annex 6’. It is possible that the second article cited the Annex number from the draft stage, so ‘Annex 6’ of the first article is trusted here. Second, is NGS ready to replace in vitro? The regulatory and industrial side (Q5A(R2), 4th NGS Conference) believes that NGS is ready to replace in vivo and PCR and can complement or replace in vitro. On the other hand, the technical/skeptic side believes that in vitro replacement requires case-by-case verification, citing gaps in bioinformatics pipeline verification, sensitivity differences of up to 2 log between laboratories, and nucleic acid ≠ infectivity issues. In addition, the CAACB data show that in vitro testing itself is imperfect, and there is a cautious opinion that no single method can be a complete replacement. The difference in speed between the two sides stems from the tension between the drivers of the 3Rs, cost, and speed, and the need for verification maturity and confirmation of infectiousness.Unresolved questions: (1) The specific item of the extraneous agent test of the mRNA-LNP product modality relies on some inferences (notated [uncertain]) due to the limited primary citations within the scope of this section. (2) Ph.Eur. The detailed test items and acceptance criteria at the clause level of 2.6.16 and USP <1050> were confirmed only through secondary sources, and direct quotations from the clause were not secured. (3) Quantitative statistics (detection probability curve) of contamination detection probability compared to raw bulk sample amount are limited to qualitative description.

6.4 Sterility, endotoxin, mycoplasma & rapid micro methods

Purpose and role: The final checkpoint that does not create sterility

This section is the final microbiological safety/release testing step to ensure product safety. It verifies that the purified and filled stock solution (DS) and finished product (DP) satisfy three conditions: sterility (no live bacterial contamination), bacterial endotoxin being below the pyrogen limit, and no mycoplasma contamination. Once the output from upstream, downstream, and aseptic filling (6.x) is received and passes testing, QP/QA releases the batch (batch release, coupled with node 8.x regulatory testing).

There is a conceptual principle that must be held here. Sterility testing is only a “final confirmation of the contamination control strategy (CCS)” and does not create sterility itself. Sterility is ensured through process and environmental management, and testing only verifies it after the fact. In addition, sterility testing inherently has the limitation of not being able to statistically prove the sterility of the entire batch because the sample is destroyed and only a small amount of the entire batch is sampled for testing. Cell and gene therapy (CGT) is where this limitation becomes particularly sharp. For products with an expiration date shorter than 14 days, traditional 14-day sterility testing is physically impossible. Therefore, this section deals with “classical sterility/endotoxin/mycoplasma testing” and “rapid/alternative microbial methods (RMM)” together.

How it works: The three classic tests and their rapid replacements

Sterility test (USP <71> / Ph.Eur. 2.6.1 / JP 4.06, PDG harmonization).There are two mandatory methods. First, membrane filtration is a method of filtering the sample through a 0.45 µm membrane, rinsing the sample at least three times with a sterilizing washing solution to remove antibacterial and inhibitory substances, and then transferring the membrane to a medium. It is preferred for large-capacity and antibacterial products. Second, the direct inoculation method places the sample into the medium as is. Both methods inoculate two types of media: FTM (fluid thioglycollate medium) for anaerobes and TSB (tryptic soy broth, soybean-casein digest) for fungi and aerobes. Membrane filtration is preferred for large-volume and particulate products (Source). The inoculated medium is cultured for at least 14 days, and this 14-day period is to give slow-growing bacteria time to proliferate (Source). The culture temperature is 30–35°C for bacteria and 20–25°C for fungi, and growth is determined by visually observing the turbidity of the medium. The main test is preceded by negative/positive control, verification of method suitability (bacteriostasis/fungistasis, B&F), and growth promotion test (GPT).Endotoxin test (BET, USP <85> / Ph.Eur. 2.6.14). This is a test to detect lipopolysaccharide (LPS) of Gram-negative bacteria, using the coagulation cascade of horseshoe crab blood cell extract (LAL, Limulus Amebocyte Lysate). There are three methods: gel-clot (qualitative/semi-quantitative - coagulation is determined at label sensitivity λ), kinetic turbidimetric (quantitative - reaction onset time is inversely proportional to concentration), and chromogenic (quantitative - activated enzyme cleaves the chromogenic substrate to release p-nitroaniline), each with different sensitivity characteristics. has (Source). The endotoxin limit, which is a judgment standard, is calculated as K/M, where K is the threshold pyrogen dose, which is 5 EU/kg for most administration routes and 0.2 EU/kg for intrathecal administration, and M is the maximum dose per kg per hour (Source). An alternative reagent that is free from animal-derived reagents is recombinant Factor C (rFC), which is an endotoxin reporter protein of LAL. It has been introduced as an animal-derived alternative reagent to traditional LAL since 2004 and has been accepted by the FDA, EMA, and JP as an alternative (Source).Mycoplasma test (USP <63> / Ph.Eur. 2.6.7). Consists of two culture-based procedures. One is the agar and broth media method, which observes characteristic “fried-egg” colonies after subinoculation. The other is indicator cell culture, in which a sample is inoculated into indicator cells such as Vero, 3T6, and CV-1, and then fluorescence appearing in the cytoplasm outside the nucleus is detected with a DNA-binding fluorescent dye such as Hoechst 33258 (Source). The critical weakness of this traditional cultivation method is time. It takes at least 28 days to obtain results, making it unsuitable for cell therapy trials where treatment decisions must be made based on rapid results (Source). ⚠️ An alternative means of solving this bottleneck is Nucleic Acid Amplification Technique (NAT) such as PCR/qPCR, which reduces the time to several hours to several days.Rapid/Alternative Method (RMM). It is largely divided into growth-based and non-growth-based. Growth-based includes USP <72>, which uses respiration (CO2) detection, and USP <73> (linked to revised <1071>), which uses ATP bioluminescence, while non-growth-based includes USP <74>, which uses solid-phase cytometry and nucleic acid amplification. In particular, there is one pitfall in ATP bioluminescence: human cells also contain ATP, which creates a background signal, so preprocessing to selectively remove eukaryotic cells and leave only microorganisms for measurement is the core of this method.

Key parameters and materials

As previously seen, the control parameters for the sterility test are membrane pore size of 0.45 µm, washing three times, culture for at least 14 days, bacteria 30–35°C, fungi 20–25°C, medium FTM/TSB (SCDM), and growth promotion (GPT), negative, positive, and method compatibility (B&F) are required as essential controls. One thing to keep in mind again and again is the statistical limitation that small samples cannot prove the sterility of an entire batch.

The most frequently handled calculation by practitioners in endotoxin testing is the maximum effective dilution factor (MVD). It is calculated as MVD = (endotoxin limit For example, if the MVD is 100, the sample cannot be diluted more than 1:100. The lower λ (higher sensitivity), the greater the allowable dilution and the greater the margin for interference. Reagents used include LAL, rFC, recombinant cascade (rCR), and reference standard endotoxin (CSE/RSE).In the mycoplasma test, indicator cells (Vero/3T6/CV-1) and fluorescent dye Hoechst 33258 are standard materials. Culture takes a total of about 28 days, and NAT is shortened to several hours to several days. In order to use alternative NAT in place of traditional culture methods, the detection limit must be at least 10 CFU/mL and must be validated to meet this limit (Source).

The biggest physical constraint that CGT/ATMP encounters in RMM is the sample size. Because the final volume is so small that it is difficult to secure sufficient samples, revisions <1071>/<72> introduced a mathematical formula (calculating the probability of contamination and sample amount together) that determines the appropriate sample amount by estimating the detection probability instead of using a fixed detection limit (Source). ⚠️ The test products are not only DS/DP, but also master/working cell banks (MCB/WCB), virus seeds, unprocessed bulk harvest (UPB), and animal-derived raw materials such as serum and trypsin.

Quality and regulation: Harmonized classic tests and new rapid methods and recombinant reagents

The three core CQAs are sterility (absence of viable organisms), compliance with endotoxin/pyrogen limits, and absence of mycoplasma. Governing standards are well coordinated across exams. Sterility is USP <71>·Ph.Eur. 2.6.1·JP 4.06 (PDG harmonization) is the basis, and for sterility of cell-based products for CGT, Ph.Eur. There is 2.6.27. Endotoxin is USP <85>·Ph.Eur. 2.6.14 is the default, and the recombinant reagent chapter is included here. Mycoplasma is USP <63>·Ph.Eur. 2.6.7 (PDG harmonization, JP G3 equivalent) covers this.

The institutionalization of recombinant endotoxin reagents is a key pillar of recent regulatory changes. On July 26, 2024, the USP approved a new chapter <86> for endotoxin testing using recombinant reagents (rFC and rCR), and this new chapter contains both methods, recombinant cascade (rCR) and recombinant Factor C (rFC) (Source). The important thing is that <86> does not replace <85>. Manufacturers who already use the LAL test can continue to use it and <86> will have no effect on them (Source). In Europe, the recombinant Factor C endotoxin test chapter, Ph.Eur. 2.6.32 came into effect in January 2021, and the amendment allowing the use of rFC in the water monograph was reflected in Supplement 11.4, effective April 1, 2024 (Source). ⚠️

New establishments and revisions in the rapid aseptic series are also active. Growth-based USP <72> (respiration/CO2) and <73> (ATP bioluminescence) and non-growth-based solid-phase cell counting/nucleic acid amplification chapters were developed to support CGT short-term products (Source). ⚠️ <1071>, which contains a risk-based framework, states that current growth-based sterility testing (minimum 14-day incubation) is inappropriate for short-shelf life products or products formulated for immediate use (Source).

In order for the alternative/expedited method to be recognized as an official test, it must pass verification requirements. USP <1223> (“Validation of Alternative Microbiological Methods”)·Ph.Eur. 5.1.6·PDA TR33 defines this, and the core logic is statistical non-inferiority. The alternative method does not need to be numerically identical to the Compendial (Pharmacopoeia) method, but only needs to show that detection ability, quantification, and pass/fail are not inferior to the traditional method (Source). Parameters included in verification are robustness, robustness, repeatability, specificity, detection/quantification limit, accuracy, precision, linearity, range, and equivalence in routine operation (Source).

In the overall regulatory environment, EU GMP Annex 1 carries great weight. The final revision, effective August 25, 2023, requires a documented contamination control strategy (CCS) as an essential element of a pharmaceutical quality system (PQS) and specifies consideration of the adoption of appropriate technologies, such as rapid/alternative methods and continuous monitoring systems, to aid rapid contamination detection (Source). Adjacent regulations include ICH Q5A(R2), which deals with adventitious agents (mycoplasma and viruses), and the FDA/EMA aseptic manufacturing guidance, which work together.

Lastly, there is a dual structure that must be remembered. Even if the expedited method is adopted, the standard sterility test USP <71> will remain as the referee method for resolving disputes. In other words, the expedited law does not replace <71> in all contexts, but exists as a scientifically justified alternative (Source). ⚠️

Variants by product type

Depending on the product type, the center of gravity for these three tests varies greatly.

mAb/recombinant protein.Due to the long shelf life, a 14-day sterility test, standard BET, and MCB/WCB (from CHO) and mycoplasma testing at the raw bulk harvest stage are sufficient. Sterility is ensured through aseptic filtration and aseptic filling, not terminal sterilization.mRNA-LNP. Since IVT (in vitro transcription) is a cell-free reaction, the mycoplasma risk of DS itself is low. However, management of plasmid and E. coli origins and sterility and endotoxin of the finished product are key, and it is completed with aseptic filling after aseptic filtration.Viral vector/CGT (ATMP). Due to the characteristics of autologous origin, small quantity, and ultra-short shelf life, a 14-day sterility test is physically impossible. Therefore, RMM (USP <72>/<73>·<1071>) and rapid mycoplasma NAT become virtually essential, combining risk-based early release and look-back confirmation. The biggest limitation is the lack of sample size. Endotoxin testing is still required, and vector-specific safety testing, such as replication-competent virus (RCV/RCL), is performed adjacently.Classical vaccine (egg/cell culture). Mycoplasma testing of seed lot and bulk is very important, and sterility and endotoxin of the finished lot are also managed. It is closely intertwined with the control of cytosol-derived adventitious agents (ICH Q5A(R2)).

Practices and recent changes

Standard practice remains the growth-based 14-day sterility test, approximately 28-day mycoplasma culture, and the LAL endotoxin test. However, movements in three directions are evident over the past three years.

First, there is a trend away from animal-derived reagents. USP <86> containing rFC/rCR was formalized and Ph.Eur. permitted the use of rFC in water monographs. The drivers are horseshoe crab resource conservation and supply chain sustainability. Second, the institutionalization of the expedited law. The USP allows for the release of short-term CGT products through <72> (respiration-based), <73> (ATP), <74> (solid cell counting) and revision <1071>. In particular, <72> allows breath-based methods to be used for designated product releases without extensive “alternative method verification,” and is evaluated as lowering the verification requirements to virtually equivalent to the level of the long-standing standard USP <71> (Source). ⚠️ Third, CCS in Annex 1 (2023) explicitly encourages the adoption of fast/alternative methods and continuous monitoring, providing institutional support. In the mycoplasma field, there is a trend in which NAT replaces 28-day culture in a few hours (detection limit ≤10 CFU/mL).

Looking at the tool/player landscape, the endotoxin sector is represented by Charles River (Endosafe), Lonza/FUJIFILM Wako (PyroGene rFC, PyroSmart NextGen rCR), and bioMérieux (ENDONEXT rCR). Rapid sterilization/bioburden includes bioMérieux (BacT/ALERT, growth-based), Charles River (Celsis, ATP bioluminescence), Rapid Micro Biosystems (Growth Direct), and Merck/Millipore (Milliflex Rapid), while mycoplasma NAT includes Roche (MycoTOOL) and Thermo Fisher (MycoSEQ). Michael J. Miller is considered a representative author in the field of RMM.

Issue:Sources differ on two points. The first is the time required for mycoplasma culture. Many sources state a total of about 28 days (the bottleneck for sterility testing), but some (such as DSDP) write “at least 21 days” for direct culture. This appears to be a difference in which sub-procedures/passages are actually included, and it is understood that the sum of liquid/agar passage and indicator cell culture usually takes about 28 days, and 21 days refers only to the direct culture portion. Second is the regulatory status of rFC. The vendor and paper view rFC as a “completely equivalent replacement,” and USP created a separate chapter <86> instead of revising <85>, and Ph.Eur. The fact that it has not yet been harmonized with 2.6.32 coexists. The former emphasizes immediate adoption of supply chain and animal welfare drivers, while the latter points to a cautious approach to regulatory harmonization and data accumulation, with product-specific verification still required.Unresolved Questions: - The detailed acceptance criteria (specific judgment threshold, culture replacement time) of USP <72>/<73>/<74> at the article level were confirmed only at the vendor blog level, so the first article citation could not be secured. - The numerical basis for the quantitative statistical detection power of the sterility test (contamination detection probability curve compared to the number of samples) was limited to qualitative description.

6.5 Stability, extended characterization & comparability

Purpose and Role: “Evidence synthesis” layer between release specifications and regulatory submissions.

This section serves as the “evidence synthesis” layer between release specifications and regulatory submission, where each batch is judged based on established methods in QC emission testing (e.g. 6.1). Three interlocking activities are tied together. The first is the ICH stability program, which tests stock solutions (DS) and finished products (DP) over time under real-time, accelerated, and stress conditions to establish and justify shelf-life and storage conditions. The second is extended characterization, which goes beyond routine batch release testing and provides in-depth characterization of molecules using orthogonal and high-resolution techniques such as higher-order structure (HOS), mass spectrometry, and forced decomposition to support structure-function relationships, reference standard qualification, and regulatory submissions. The third is comparative equivalence/biosimilarity evaluation (ICH Q5E), which proves that the product (or biosimilar vs. originator) before and after changes in the manufacturing process does not have a negative impact on quality, safety, and efficacy.

The reason why these three activities must be understood in one frame is because the tools and logic used overlap. The stability indicator method is soon verified for specificity through forced decomposition, the method panel is mobilized again for side-by-side comparison of comparability, and HOS data simultaneously supports reference standard qualification and comparability judgment. This section receives the purified crude solution and verified analysis method from the previous step (DSP·6.1 QC), and passes the output (expiration date, characterization data package, comparative equivalence conclusion) to CMC document (8.1), post-marketing change management (ICH Q12), cold chain/distribution (4.4), and technology transfer (7.2).

There is a first conceptual principle to hold on to here. The stability of biological drugs is not just a matter of chemical purity like small molecules, but is tied to the maintenance of molecular structure and biological activity (Source). Proteins can lose potency due to aggregation, fragmentation, and conformational changes even if their chemical composition is almost the same. This is the fundamental reason why stability programs always include potency assays and extended characterization studies delve into higher-order structures.

How it operates: Four branches of activity

Stability Program (ICH Q5C → new ICH Q1).

At least three batches representative of manufacturing scale are tested in real-time at the proposed long-term storage conditions, and this real-time long-term data serves as the basis for the shelf life. ICH Q5C requires a minimum of three batches and potency assays representative of the intended manufacturing process and scale (Source), and shelf life is supported by real-time studies under proposed long-term storage conditions, which are typically refrigerated or frozen but are product specific (Source). Another key principle emerges here. Accelerated (e.g. 25°C) and stress (e.g. 40°C) conditions are used to understand degradation pathways and accumulate product knowledge, but do not justify shelf life in biological drugs (Source) ⚠️. The same logic can be seen again in the context of stress testing, which supports stability directive compliance but does not replace real-time stability data for shelf life claims (Source). What these two statements overlap is the fact that dependence on real-time data is unavoidable in biologics. The test panel consists of stability-indicating methods and includes SEC (aggregation/fragmentation), CE-SDS, charge variation (CEX/icIEF), content, and necessarily titer assays. It also evaluates chemical modifications such as deamidation and oxidation and appearance, pH, and container-closure stability. Photostability (equivalent to ICH Q1B) and in-use, transport, and temperature stability are also covered, and bracketing/matrixing and statistical stability modeling/extrapolation are permitted with scientific justification.

Forced decomposition/stress test. The product is intentionally exposed to harsh conditions such as heat, high/low pH, oxidation (e.g. H2O2·AAPH), photolysis, mechanical stress (stirring), and freeze-thaw to identify decomposition products and decomposition pathways, and prove the specificity of the stability indicator method to detect aggregation, fragmentation, PTM, disulfide bond, glycan, and higher-order structure changes (linked to ICH Q2(R2)). In fact, literature recommends that stress selection categories should include heat, pH, oxidation, photolysis, mechanical stress, and freeze/thaw (Source). This research is not limited to a specific stage and plays an important role in the overall selection of candidate/dosage, comparative equivalence study, identification of degradation path, and development of stability indicator method (Source). However, there is a limitation, which we will return to later, in that there is no well-established standard protocol for selecting stress conditions, timing of stress, and degree of degradation required (Source).

Extended characterization (higher-order structure HOS). Beyond primary structure (peptide mapping/accurate mass MS), circular dichroism (CD; secondary/tertiary structure), differential scanning calorimetry (DSC; thermal stability/unfolding), multidimensional NMR (atomic-level HOS fingerprint), hydrogen-deuterium exchange mass spectrometry (HDX-MS; local shape/flexibility), sedimentation rate. Combines ultracentrifugal analysis (AUC-SV; agglutination), mass photometry, etc. In fact, DSC·CD·NMR·AUC are standard biophysical tools used to identify higher-order protein structures, and HDX-MS is an emerging MS-based technique for studying protein dynamics and flexibility (Source). The principle here is that a single technique is not enough. It is difficult to comprehensively evaluate HOS using a single biophysical technique, so the synergy of multiple complementary approaches increases the accuracy and resolution of characterization (Source). Regulatory authorities also reflect this, and the FDA and EMA expect to demonstrate that biologics/biosimilars maintain the correct form and biological activity through a combination of physicochemical HOS techniques and functional bioassays (Source) ⚠️. This in-depth analysis is distinct from routine release testing in that it is performed during the development, reference standard qualification, comparability, and biosimilarity phases rather than on each batch.

Comparability (ICH Q5E). The starting point for this activity is a side-by-side comparison of the methods panel. Start with a side-by-side comparison of the material before and after the change using a panel of sufficiently sensitive and appropriately validated (or phased) methods (Source). The approach is risk-based and stepwise, first establishing quality (analytical) comparability and resolving residual uncertainty with targeted nonclinical (PK/PD/toxicity) studies only when analysis results reveal differences with uncertain clinical impact (Source). Stability data is also used in this comparison, where the products before and after the change are put together under accelerated stability conditions and degradation profiles such as increased aggregation and loss of potency are compared side by side (Source) ⚠️. In biosimilars, this logic is extended to the analytical similarity/totality of evidence frame that stratifies CQAs according to clinical relevance, and the FDA proposes to obtain totality-of-the-evidence by applying equivalence testing (TOST) in Tier 1, quality range approach in Tier 2, and raw data or graph presentation in Tier 3 (Source).

Key parameters and materials

In terms of equipment, a stability chamber that controls temperature, humidity, and light is the basis of the program, which includes UHPLC/HPLC (SEC·CEX·RP), CE system (CE-SDS·icIEF), high-resolution accurate mass MS (Orbitrap·Q-TOF), DSC microcalorimeter, circular dichroism (CD), multidimensional NMR, HDX-MS platform, analytical ultracentrifuge (AUC), and mass photometry. It adds up. DLS/particle size analyzer and subvisible particle counter (light blocking/MFI) are added to mRNA-LNP·particle products. Core reagents and standards include reference standards and pre-change standards, oxidizing agents for forced digestion (H2O2·AAPH) and pH buffers, columns and mobile phases for stability indicators, PNGase F·digestive enzymes, and deuterium buffer for HDX.

In terms of the range of critical parameters, the number of batches is at least three at a representative scale, and in practice, stability data from three primary batches corresponding to the proposed shelf life are generally required unless otherwise justified (Source) ⚠️. Long-term storage conditions are product-specific, so they are 2-8°C or frozen, and mRNA-LNP is -20~-70°C, and the test time is usually 0/3/6/9/12/18/24 months. Forced decomposition induces “significant but not excessive” decomposition of the target (usually aimed at 5-20% level), but as previously stated, there is no standard protocol. Stability indicator properties track monomer %, HMW%, LMW%, charge variation main/acidic/basic %, relative titer %, content, oxidation/deamidation %, appearance, pH, and osmotic pressure. In comparability statistics, real-time ± accelerated side-by-side comparison and pre-specified comparability range (based on past fluctuations·n·SD) are used, and in biosimilar Tier 1, equivalence margin (e.g. ±1.5×σ_ref) and TOST are applied. However, we must be honest that it is difficult to obtain generalized values ​​because the actual tolerance standards for each attribute and the representative values ​​of the equivalent margin formula are confidential and product-specific.

The typical output of the mouth stage of this section is as follows. As input, it receives purified crude/finished product, verification/development stage analysis method, and reference material before change, and as output, it provides expiration date, storage conditions, label claim, degradation path/structure-function data package, and comparative equivalence/similarity conclusion (pass or not and required bridging range).

Quality and regulation: ICH Q1 integrated into the triangle of Q5C·Q5E·Q6B

The core CQAs are three bundles. Stability-indicating properties (aggregate HMW, fragment LMW, charge mutation, oxidation/deamidation, glycan change, potency loss, content, appearance, color, pH, osmotic pressure, subvisible particles, container closure integrity), extended characterization properties (primary sequence, disulfide bond arrangement, 2nd, 3rd, and 4th order higher order structures, thermal stability, conformational flexibility), and comparative equivalence properties (before and after changes to the overall CQA above). consistency).

Understanding the triangular structure of governance regulations will organize this entire section. ICH Q5C specifies biological drug stability testing (three batches, real-time, titer assay required, container closure), ICH Q5E specifies comparative equivalence after manufacturing changes, and ICH Q6B specifies specifications. Here, it is important to retain the core principles of Q5E in their original form. Proving comparative equivalence does not mean that the quality attributes of the product before and after the change are the same, but that they are highly similar and that existing knowledge sufficiently predicts that the difference will not have a negative impact on safety and effectiveness (Source). In other words, “comparable” is not “the same.” Another regulatory basis for this principle is ICH Q6B, which allows the stability indication and comparability panel to be composed of multiple orthogonal methods. Since the absolute purity of biotechnology medicinal products is method dependent, it usually must be evaluated by more than one method (Source). The logic is that purity cannot be determined with a single indicator because another method catches decomposed species that one method misses.

In the biosimilar field, the weight of this analytical similarity evaluation is particularly high. Analytical similarity assessment is considered a critical first step in biosimilar development by both the FDA and EMA, and the scope of clinical trials required for approval is determined in part by the degree of analytical similarity (Source). Since the stronger the similarity is demonstrated in the analysis, the subsequent clinical burden is reduced, so the quality data in this section directly determines the clinical development strategy.

The latest pillar of the regulatory landscape is the integrated ICH Q1. The new integrated ICH Q1 draft released in April 2025 completely integrates the existing Q1A-F and Q5C into a single document (Source) ⚠️, and covers small molecules, biologics, vaccines, gene therapy, and even drug-device combinations in a single harmonized document (Source) ⚠️. According to confirmation from secondary media, this revision provides stability guidance for complex biological products such as advanced therapy medicinal products (ATMPs), vaccines, and combination drugs that were not covered in the previous stability guidance (Source). Structurally, it consists of 18 major sections and 3 annexes. Annex 2 deals with stability modeling and extrapolation, and Annex 3 deals with ATMP stability (Source) ⚠️. Entry into force is expected in 2026-2027 and will soon become the parent frame of this section. The stability and comparability output accumulated in this way becomes the core basis for justification of expiration date and change in CMC submission (8.1), and leads to post-marketing change management (ICH Q12).

Variants by product type

Depending on the product type, the difficulty and center of gravity of stability and characteristic analysis vary dramatically.

mAb/recombinant protein.This is the most mature region. Acceleration/stress and forced decomposition are added to real-time (2-8°C), HOS panel consisting of DSC/CD/AUC/HDX, Q5E comparative equivalence, and biosimilar tiered-CQA analytical similarity have become the standard, and the shelf life is usually several years.mRNA-LNP. Stability is the defining challenge of this product modality. Storage at ultra-low temperatures (-20 to -70°C) is virtually essential, but if kept at 2-8°C, the shelf life is drastically shortened to about 30 days for the Moderna vaccine and about 5 days for the Pfizer-BioNTech vaccine (Source). The center of stability CQA is RNA integrity, and the integrity of the mRNA molecule includes the 5′ cap, coding region, untranslated region (UTR), and poly-A tail, and CQAs affect the stability and effectiveness of the vaccine (Source). The main factor in degradation is hydrolysis, and hydrolysis is still considered the main factor in mRNA degradation (Source), and the carboxyl ester bond of lipids is also susceptible to temperature- and pH-dependent hydrolysis during storage. Therefore, lyophilization is emerging as an alternative to extending the shelf life of refrigerated products, and comparative equivalence focuses on tracking the consistency of particle size, RNA integrity, and encapsulation efficiency.Viral vector/CGT (AAV, lentivirus, cell therapy). Autologous cell therapies have an extremely short shelf life (several hours to days for fresh products, extended by cryopreservation), so the stability program is compressed and reversed. It is difficult to indicate stability based on titer (transduction/survival rate), and proving comparative equivalence is particularly difficult due to lot-to-lot variation and material scarcity. AAV is usually stored frozen, and its stability properties include empty/full ratio, capsid integrity, and genome integrity. A step forward is that the new ICH Q1 annex (ATMP Stability) has begun to explicitly address this area. However, only general information on the specific safety program design (test timing, potency stability indication method) for AAV and autologous cell therapy is still available.Classic vaccine. Real-time + accelerated thermostability and antigen content/titer are key, and adjuvant/preservative stability and decision on liquid vs. freeze-drying are important. Historically, the proportion of animal potency tests for comparative equivalence was large and the proportion of molecular-level HOS was relatively low.

Practices and recent changes

Current standard practice is clear. The expiration date is set by three-batch, real-time long-term testing based on ICH Q5C, acceleration/stress is used to establish the decomposition path and stability indication method, and structure-function/equivalence is supported by extended characterization (HOS multiple techniques) and forced decomposition. Movement in four directions has been evident over the past three years.

First, regulatory integration. The new ICH Q1 draft combines Q1A-F and Q5C into a single document (18 sections and 3 appendices), covers small molecules, biologics, vaccines, gene therapy, oligos, and combinations in one frame, and strengthens statistical stability modeling and extrapolation (Annex 2) and reduction design (Annex 1). Second, the maturity of extended characteristic analysis techniques. HDX-MS·2D-NMR·mass photometry is spreading to prove comparative equivalence/biosimilar HOS. Third, with the rapid growth in the stability of new modalities, ultralow-temperature/freeze-drying/RNA integrity of mRNA-LNPs and short-term stability of AAV/ATMP have become the subject of active research. Fourth, with the rise of prediction and modeling, ASAPprime-type accelerated stability models and AI/ML-based shelf life prediction are being attempted.

Looking at the tool/player landscape, representative HOS equipment companies include Malvern Panalytical·TA Instruments in DSC, Applied Photophysics·Jasco in CD, Bruker in NMR, Waters·Trajan/MOBILion in HDX-MS·SEC, Beckman Coulter in AUC, and Refeyn in mass photometry. Stability chamber/modeling includes Q1 Scientific·Weiss (chamber), FreeThink ASAPprime (accelerated stability modeling), and TOST series statistical tools, and characterization CRO includes BioPharmaSpec·Alphalyse·SGS·Charles River. In terms of regulations and standards, the background is ICH (Q5C, Q5E, new Q1, Q1A-F, Q2(R2), Q6B, Q12), FDA and EMA (biosimilar analytical similarity guidance), WHO (vaccine and mRNA stability recommendations), and USP/Ph.Eur.

Issue:There are four points where the sources diverge. First, is it possible to extrapolate the expiration date of biological products from accelerated stability data? The new ICH Q1 Annex 2 strengthens statistical stability modeling and extrapolation, and the industry is attempting to predict expiration date using ASAPprime, and the principle that accelerated data is used only for product knowledge and expiration date is justified only by real-time data is tense because biologic degradation is not simply extrapolated using the Arrhenius method. Although the value of predictive models is recognized, opinions are divided on the extent to which they can be used as the basis for regulatory expiration date claims. Second, is analysis alone sufficient for comparative equivalence of manufacturing changes? While there is an observation that in practice, a significant number (up to approximately 90%) of comparability studies are completed without new human trials and clinical data are rarely required (Source) ⚠️, there is a cautious view that PK/PD/toxicity/clinical bridging is necessary when analysis results reveal differences with uncertain clinical impact. This is a difference in judgment about “how much analytic similarity predicts clinical no difference,” and depends on the clinical relevance of the attribute and the level of existing knowledge. Third, there is the possibility of standardizing the conditions, extent, and timing of forced decomposition stress. While the stress categories (thermal/pH/oxidation/light/mechanical/freeze-thaw) are virtually agreed upon, there is no standard protocol for selection of conditions, required degree of decomposition, and timing, resulting in large deviations in practice. Fourth, the regulatory weight and acceptance criteria of the HOS technique. FDA/EMA expects a combination of physical and chemical HOS + functional bioassay, but the HOS technique is relatively qualitative in nature, so it is difficult to establish quantitative pass/fail acceptance criteria, and inter-method reproducibility and standardization are immature. This is why HOS is primarily used as a basis for characterization/equivalence rather than a release specification.Unresolved Questions: - Representative values of each company’s actual acceptance criteria for each stability attribute (e.g., monomer % lower limit, potency % range) and biosimilar equivalent margin formula are confidential and product-specific, so generalized values could not be obtained. - The detailed methodology of the new ICH Q1 specific temperature table for biological products (long-term/intermediate/accelerated condition values) and statistical modeling (Annex 2) were not secured in concrete values ​​in the original draft. - The specific safety program design (test timing, potency stability indication method) for AAV/autologous cell therapy has only been secured at an overview level.

6.6 Analytical procedure development & validation

Purpose and Role — The basis for QC testing to produce ‘reliable numbers’

The preceding 6.1 to 6.5 dealt with individual QC tests such as identity, purity, potency, sterility/foreign agents, and stability. This section is a cross-cutting layer that ensures the suitability of the method itself so that the tests actually produce ‘reliable numbers’. In other words, 6.6 is not one specific test, but a life cycle infrastructure layer that underlies all tests above and supports their measurement reliability. The starting point is CQA/QTPP, which is carried over from the previous product and process understanding (ICH Q8/Q11) and management strategy (7.4), and the measurement requirement of ‘what should be measured and how accurately?’ Here, (1) a fit-for-purpose analysis procedure is developed, (2) the procedure is validated for its purpose, and (3) performance is maintained and changed during the product lifespan. The output, verified analytical procedures and verification report, are included in CTD Module 3 (3.2.S.4 / 3.2.P.5) and serve as the basis for batch release, stability, and comparability determination.

The regulatory framework is divided into two axes. One is ICH Q14, which covers development and life cycle change management of procedures, and the other is ICH Q2(R2), which covers validation. ICH Q14 is defined as guidance that describes a science- and risk-based approach to developing and maintaining analytical procedures appropriate for quality assessment of drug substances and finished products (Source). These two guidelines were adopted together as Step 4 by regulatory members at the ICH Assembly meeting on 31 October–1 November 2023 (Source), institutionalizing method QbD (AQbD) into a formal framework for the first time. Here, the United States Pharmacopeia USP <1220> (Analytical Procedure Life Cycle) complements this with a three-stage life cycle.

How it operates — ‘Analytical procedures have a life cycle like products’There are two key ideas. First, like a product, the analysis process also has a life cycle of design → verification → continuous verification. Second, development can be performed in a minimal (traditional) or enhanced (QbD) manner.

Q14 — Development (minimal vs enhanced).The minimum elements of the minimal (traditional) approach are ① identification of product properties to be tested, ② selection of appropriate technology and equipment, ③ evaluation of performance characteristics such as specificity, accuracy, precision (reportable range, calibration model, including upper and lower limits) and robustness, and ④ documentation of procedures, including management strategies, and are often performed in an OFAT (one factor at a time) method. Importantly, this traditional approach is still recognized as an ‘acceptable’ way to create a sound, fit-for-purpose process. The guidelines state that the minimal approach is still valid and that some or all elements of the enhanced approach can be used to support development and lifecycle management (Source). The enhanced approach adds (a) ATP documentation, (b) ICH Q9-based risk assessment (e.g., Ishikawa diagram) to identify performance-impacting parameters, (c) univariate and multivariate experiments/DoE to explore scope and interactions, and (d) management strategy definition including set-point/PAR/MODR. In the enhanced approach, expected performance criteria for relevant performance characteristics must be documented in ATP (Source).Analytical Target Profile (ATP). The ATP consists of the intended purpose of the analytical procedure, the product attributes to be measured, the relevant performance characteristics and their criteria, which drives which analytical technique to use (Source). Just as QTPP leads development like a product, ATP serves as a reference point that leads method development. Although formal documentation and submission of ATP is optional, it can facilitate regulatory communication regardless of the development approach chosen (Source).PAR vs MODR. A management strategy defines set-points and/or ranges of parameters, which may include proven acceptable ranges (PAR) and method operable design regions (MODR) (Source). If the allowable range of a single parameter is PAR (univariate), MODR is the combined range of two or more parameters, and is the area within which the procedure has been proven to be suitable for its purpose, that is, the design space of the analytical method version (Source). The reason this distinction is important in practice is because of flexibility. Shifting operating conditions within an approved PAR or MODR does not require regulatory notification (Source). However, it should be noted that there is no need or possibility to verify the entire MODR. Verification of PAR/MODR is only required for performance characteristics not covered by development data, and according to the risk-based approach, in reality, only the daily use part needs to be covered with verification data (Source).Management strategy and SST. The management strategy consists of SST (system suitability test), sample suitability (common in biological products), and parameters that need to be controlled, and is defined ‘before’ verification and confirmed after verification.Q2(R2) — Verification. The purpose of verification is to demonstrate that the analytical procedure is suitable for its intended purpose (Source). Verification characteristics are specificity/selectivity, accuracy, precision (repeatability·intermediate precision·reproducibility), range/reportable range, detection limit, quantitation limit, linearity/calibration model, and robustness. The new concept of Q2(R2) is a combined approach that evaluates the total impact without looking at accuracy and precision separately. This is a method of evaluating the two against the combined performance criteria, and can be evaluated in terms of prediction interval, tolerance interval, and confidence interval (i.e., total analytical error perspective) (Source). Additionally, appropriate data obtained during development (such as DoE robustness in ICH Q14) can be reused as part of validation data to reduce test duplication, and when established platform analysis procedures are used for new purposes, validation can be abbreviated under scientific justification (Source). Therefore, robustness is usually assessed as part of the development phase prior to executing validation studies (Source).Lifecycle/PostChange (Q12). This is where ICH Q12 tools (Established Conditions, Reporting Categories, PACMP, PLCM, PQS) are connected. Changes to parameters that are not Established Conditions (EC) do not require regulatory communication (Source), and ATP can be the basis for PACMP, allowing even changes such as switching between technologies to be reported in a lower reporting category if predefined requirements are met (Source). This is the core mechanism of ‘post change without full resubmission’ promised by AQbD.Step 3 of USP <1220>. On the pharmacopeia side, USP <1220> defines the analytical task as a continuous life cycle consisting of three interconnected steps. Stage 1 (procedure design/development) → Stage 2 (performance qualification, i.e., confirming whether the reportable value produced by the procedure meets ATP standards; including traditional verification/verification/transfer) → Stage 3 (continuous performance verification/trending during daily use) (Source). ⚠️

Key parameters and materials

Verification characteristics and acceptance criteria.A linearity/calibration model is added to the six major axes of Q2 (R2) (specificity, accuracy, precision, range, DL/QL, robustness), and verification items vary depending on the test type (specificity-centered for identity testing, accuracy·precision·range·DL/QL for quantitative impurity, accuracy·precision·range for assay/potency, etc.), as exemplified in the table in the Annex. However, it is important to note that the guidelines leave the specific numerical acceptance criteria for each characteristic to case-by-case justification. Examples of acceptance criteria for biological assays commonly used in practice include values such as ELISA recovery rate of 80–120%, repeatability CV ≤15%, intermediate precision CV ≤20%, and cell-based titer assay recovery rate of 70–130%, repeatability CV ≤20%, intermediate ≤30%. However, these values are referenced, but these are not from the guideline text but from secondary practice sources. It’s a shame (Source). ⚠️ On the other hand, the example of anti-TNF-alpha mAb titer in Q14 Annex presented as 80–125% of the relative titer standard is a work example in the guideline text. Reportable range is derived from the standard and is checked by accuracy·precision·response, including upper and lower limits.Analysis procedure parameters (inputs). Procedure parameters are divided into continuous (flow rate, column temperature, incubation time) and categorical (instrument, reagent lot) types, and those that affect performance are selected through risk assessment and explored through DoE. In particular, guidelines for representative robustness parameters of cell-based titer assays include cell density, effector/target cell ratio, and cell generation number (Source). Representative examples of binding assays include coating protein·capture/detection antibody, plate type, buffer composition, and reagent lot.Core materials/standards. Reference materials with documented identity, purity, and properties are core materials.
