However, in biological drugs, the ‘true value’ of the reference standard is assigned by the consensus of multiple orthogonal methods, so there is no absolute true value, which makes accuracy assessment difficult (Source). ⚠️ In addition, standard/positive/negative controls for SST, forced degradation samples for stability-indicating specificity, and positive template/no-template/no-RT controls for qPCR are used.Instruments, tools, input and output. The instruments used are HPLC/UPLC, CE, mass spectrometry, spectroscopy (NIR/Raman/NMR), plate reader, and qPCR, and the tools used are DoE and statistical software for prediction/allowance/confidence interval calculation and MODR modeling. The starting point is ATP/CQA/measurement requirements and management strategy, and the output of this step is verification protocol → verification report, confirmed management strategy (including SST), Established Conditions and reporting categories, and verification data of CTD 3.2.S.4.3/3.2.P.5.3.

Quality and Regulation

The governing guidelines are the core pair ICH Q14 and Q2(R2). As seen earlier, both guidelines were adopted as Step 4 at the end of 2023, and local regulatory agencies then began implementation. In March 2024, the FDA published two guidelines, Q2(R2) (validation) and Q14 (development), as industrial guidance (Federal Register, 2024-03-07) (Source). Q14 expands the QbD/QRM principles of ICH Q8 (pharmaceutical development), Q9 (quality risk management), Q10 (PQS), and Q11 (drug substance development) into analytical methods, and leads to Q12 (life cycle management) and M4Q (CTD). In terms of pharmacopoeia, USP <1220> (life cycle), <1225> (validation of complementary procedures), <1210> (statistics for verification), <1033> (biological potency assay) and Ph.Eur. The corresponding general provisions correspond.

It should be made clear that what is controlled here is not the product CQA, but the ‘quality of the measured value (reportable value)’, that is, the accuracy/precision and the suitability of the method itself. ATP defines the acceptance frame, SST/sample suitability defines the routine validity gateway, and Established Conditions defines the regulatory binding elements.

There is one most important regulatory proviso. Q14 specifies that using an enhanced approach should not provide a less detailed description of the analytical procedures in regulatory submissions (Source). Additionally, PAR/MODR/EC and its reporting categories are proposed by the applicant and approved by the regulatory body on the basis of scientific justification, and the reporting categories are still classified according to the regional framework. As a result, the actual amount of flexibility will depend on the extent to which each region has adopted ICH Q12.

Differences by product type

mAb/recombinant protein is the most mature area. Physicochemical methods (HPLC/CE/MS) are relatively clearly verified, and potency is verified by combined/cell-based bioassay. A representative work example in the Q14 Annex is the previously mentioned anti-TNF-alpha mAb relative titer (80–125% of the standard), which shows the mapping of ATP → technology selection (combined vs. cell-based) → verification characteristics.Common difficulties with biologic drugs can be summarized in three points. First, the true value of the reference standard is assigned by orthogonal agreement, so there is no absolute true value, so the definition of accuracy is ambiguous (Source). ⚠️ Second, interference in complex matrices must be evaluated in terms of specificity and accuracy. Third, bioassays have large inherent fluctuations. Therefore, Q14 acknowledges that some procedures with parameters with large inherent variation, such as biological reagents, may require wider parameter ranges (Source). This method focuses on relative titer/parallelism (USP <1033>) rather than absolute titer, and evaluation of sample suitability is often necessary.mRNA-LNP·viral vector·cell gene therapy (CGT).Q2(R2) covers quantitative nucleic acid verification by specifying qPCR/RT-qPCR verification examples (Table 8: gel/melting/sequence/BLAST for specificity, spiking for accuracy, standard curve slope for efficiency). Potency verification is particularly difficult in this product modality. Cell-based transduction/expression assays have overlapping requirements for variability, reagent lot, throughput, lack of reference materials, and MoA reflection, and CGT is an order of magnitude more complex than small molecules and proteins, so potency assurance has become a regulatory focus (FDA 2023 CGT potency guidance).Classical vaccines have historically been based on in-vivo/immunogenicity, but are moving to in-vitro, and the verification target is shifting to focus on antigen content and immunochemical assays.Multivariate·PAT·RTRT. This is the main axis of Q2(R2) revision. Q2(R2) expands the scope of the previous edition (Q2(R1)) to include, for the first time, verification principles for advanced analytical procedures such as mass spectrometry and spectroscopic techniques such as NIR, Raman, and NMR (Source). These multivariate procedures are validated by calibration/internal and external test sets and metrics such as RMSEP, supporting real-time emission testing (RTRT) (→ 5.4 Overlap with PAT/RTRT).

Practices and changes

The majority of current standards still remain in the traditional (minimal) method and Q2(R1) legacy verification (fixed parameters, OFAT robustness), and ATP/MODR/AQbD are in the stage of partial application by leading companies. The movement over the past three years has been in three directions. First, Q14 + Q2(R2) was confirmed as 2023-11 Step 4, and analytical method development and AQbD were institutionalized as an official ICH frame for the first time (Source). Second, Q2(R2) expanded its scope to biopharmaceuticals, multivariate/spectrometry, and life cycle. In particular, new concepts used throughout the life of the procedure, such as revalidation, co-validation, and platform analysis procedures, were introduced (Source). Third, general life cycle and statistical rules such as USP <1220>/<1210>/<1033> have been established to support verification of PAT/RTRT methods.

The limits are also clear. Industry adoption rates are low, with one 2024 survey reporting that only about 19% of respondent organizations said they were ready to implement Q14 immediately (Source). ⚠️ The regulatory flexibility promised by MODR/EC is uncertain to be realized due to lack of harmonization between regions (particularly restrictions on the application of ICH Q12 Established Conditions in the EU), and Q14 itself restricts the return on investment (ROI) by adding a proviso that ‘do not lower the level of detail’. In addition, statistical/DoE capabilities and costs, practical constraints such as not being able to verify the entire MODR, and establishing life cycle verification of ML/soft-sensor based methods still remain open issues.

Issue

Issue 1 — Does enhanced/AQbD actually reduce the burden of post-change changes?One side believes that Q14 reduces the burden of repetitive submissions by exempting regulatory communication for movements and non-EC changes within an approved MODR/PAR and even processing technology replacements with ATP-based PACMP in a low reporting category. On the other side, the promised flexibility is not yet realized as the reporting categories are dependent on regional frameworks (particularly the EU’s Q12 EC application restrictions), Q14 itself puts a caveat ‘do not reduce the level of detail’, and only ~19% of organizations are ready for adoption. The source of the gap is that flexibility ultimately depends on whether local regulatory agencies actually approve lower reporting categories, but the harmonization is incomplete. In other words, it is the distance between the potential of the instructional text and its actualization on the ground.Issue 2 — Is the additional investment in the enhanced approach worth it compared to minimal? There is a conflict between the position that enhanced has long-term benefits by providing a wider operating range, life cycle flexibility, and OOS reduction with ATP, DoE, and MODR, and the position that many companies remain with the minimal approach because Q14 recognizes the minimal approach as still completely valid, the costs of statistics, DoE capabilities, and documentation are high, and regulatory compensation is uncertain. Since the guidelines allow for both paths, the choice will depend on the organization’s capabilities, risk appetite, and jurisdictional expectations, and the cost-benefit breakeven will vary by product and market.

Unresolved Questions

  • Since the guidelines leave the specific numerical acceptance criteria for each verification characteristic (e.g., recovery rate/CV of small molecule assay, DL/QL equation constant) to case-by-case justification, generalized standard values have not been secured other than secondary sources such as biological assay CV/recovery rate.
  • Actual acceptance cases and approval statistics by regional regulatory agencies (FDA vs EMA vs PMDA) for MODR/Established Conditions were not confirmed with public primary data (EU’s Q12 EC reservation is only qualitatively reflected).

7. Development, validation & quality systems

Cross-cutting layer that develops, transfers, validates, and governs the process and its release.

7.1 Process development & characterization

Role and scope and place in the life cycle

Process Development & Characterization (PD/PC) is the stage of “designing, understanding, and defining the process” before GMP commercial production begins. When placed on the regulatory map, it corresponds to Stage 1 (Process Design), the first stage of the three-stage FDA process verification life cycle, and ICH Q11’s ‘Manufacturing Process Development’. If the task of this section can be condensed into one sentence, it is to identify “which process parameters affect which product quality.” In fact, the purpose of process characterization (PC) is defined as identifying variables that affect product quality and yield by identifying the interaction between process variables and critical quality characteristics (CQA) (Source). The key point is that although batches are not released, it is a ‘knowledge generation’ stage that lays the scientific foundation on which quality, management, and standards for the entire subsequent life cycle can be based.

The compass at this stage is CQA. ICH Q11 defines CQAs as “physical, chemical, biological, or microbiological properties or properties that must be within appropriate limits, ranges, or distributions to ensure the desired product quality,” and states that the list of potential CQAs guides the entire process development (Source). CQA is derived from the target product quality characteristics (QTPP), and the cell line/cell bank (1.1/1.2), medium/feed/raw material (1.4), and initial laboratory process are received as input, and the confirmed commercial process definition, key process parameters (CPP), tolerance range, and management strategy for each unit process, and a qualified scale-down model (SDM) and characterization data package are calculated. These outputs will soon be transferred to technology transfer (7.2) and process verification PPQ (7.3).

ICH Q11 specifies two paths for this development. While the traditional approach determines the set point and operating range of process variables and then manages them by proving process reproducibility and standard testing, the enhanced approach mobilizes risk management and scientific knowledge more broadly to identify process variables and unit processes that affect CQA and establishes a flexible management strategy including design space (Source). The important principle here is that regulatory flexibility, such as design space, is not given for free. In other words, the deeper the scientific understanding submitted, the greater the flexibility of the management strategy that can be achieved. From the FDA’s perspective, the purpose of Stage 1 is to secure process knowledge by revealing multivariate interactions between variable inputs and result outputs through design of experiments (DOE), and this accumulated knowledge becomes the basis for establishing a management approach for each unit process and the overall process (Source). ⚠️

How It Works — The 7 Steps of ISPE

The flow of practice is well summarized in the 7 steps organized by ISPE (Source). The major order is (1) defining governance, scope, and output through a process verification master plan (PVMP) → (2) linking and prioritizing potential CPPs to CQAs through risk assessment → (3) mapping which unit processes influence which CQAs through impurity clearance analysis → (4) qualifying SDM → (5) performing DoE experimental design → (6) deriving a management strategy (PAR) for each unit process → (7) The integrated process model calculates the management strategy and holistic criticality of the entire process. This framework aligns precisely with the logical development of the enhanced approach presented by ICH Q11. Q11 specifies the order of “identifying sources of variation → identifying maximum impact parameters through prior knowledge and risk assessment → conducting DoE/mechanism/modeling research → setting the scope (and optional design space) through data analysis” (Source).

Criticality screening through risk assessment.The tool commonly used in the second step is FMEA. Severity (S), Occurrence (O), and Detection (D) are assigned to calculate the risk priority RPN (=S ⚠️ In practice, RPNs exceeding 100-200 typically trigger mandatory corrective action and those below 30 are generally tolerated without further mitigation, but failure modes of severity 8 or higher are operationalized by requiring documented justification regardless of RPN (Source). ⚠️ This type of risk assessment is conducted early in development and updated repeatedly as knowledge is accumulated, which is consistent with the spirit of ICH Q9 quality risk management (Source).SDM Qualification. Most characterization experiments are conducted in small bioreactors (typically 2 to 15 L) designed to represent commercial scale (2,000 to 25,000 L) (Source). ⚠️ The key here is to match engineering parameters to commercial scale — matching power per unit volume (P/V) and oxygen transfer coefficient (kLa), maintaining set points such as pH, DO, temperature, and cell age, and linearly scaling down volume-dependent factors such as medium, feed, and working volume. However, small containers have a large liquid surface area/volume ratio, which strips excess dissolved CO2 that accumulates on a commercial scale, so the standard correction is to supplement CO2 (usually 5 to 15%) in the sparge/overlay gas (Source). ⚠️ The point of qualification is that, as ICH Q11 specifies, the small model must reflect scale effects and be representative of the proposed commercial process, and only a scientifically justified model can support quality prediction and extrapolation of operating conditions between multiple scales and equipment (Source). Statistically, demonstrating equivalence to manufacturing scale with at least five independent set point runs is a commonly accepted requirement to support a BLA (Source) ⚠️, where an equivalence test such as a two-tailed test (TOST) identifies and characterizes potential offsets between manufacturing scale and small scale (Source). The idea to keep in mind here is that the goal is not to prove that the two scales are ‘equal’ (they never are), but to show that the difference is sufficiently small. Each attribute is classified into a predictability level from Case A (predictive), which is statistically indistinguishable from manufacturing scale and can be used directly for characterization, to Case D, which is non-predictive (Source). ⚠️ As an actual example, in a study where SDM for a 12,000 L fed-batch mAb commercial process was built in a 2 L bench bioreactor, CO2 stripping due to the large liquid surface area/volume ratio on a small scale was corrected with a 6% CO2 headspace overlay to obtain performance comparable to commercial scale (Source).DoE characterization. For parameters filtered out by risk assessment, main effects and interaction effects on CQA are identified with the minimum number of experiments through experimental design, which is superior to the univariate (OFAT) approach that changes only one variable at a time (Source). The typical animal cell culture QbD characterization is a step-by-step DoE strategy that filters parameters by FMEA risk ranking, then designs the first fractional factorial screening, and then defines the unit process design space with central composite design (CCD) (Source). Which parameter is a CPP is determined by evaluating statistical significance using t-ratio and p-value, and determining relevance by comparing the effect size with the change in the center point (Source).Range/design space setting. Lastly, by cross-applying the CQA acceptance criteria to experimental data (model predictions), the verified acceptance range (PAR) of each CPP can be derived, and the multidimensional combination of several PARs forms the design space (Source). Even by the definition of ICH Q11, design space is “a multidimensional combination and interaction of input variables and process variables for which quality assurance has been proven,” and work within the design space is not considered a change, and movement outside it usually triggers post-change management (Source). In the latest practice, the holistic criticality and operational flexibility of the entire process are evaluated using an integrated process model that concatenates individual unit process models so that the output of one model becomes the input of the next model (Source). ⚠️

Key parameters and materials

The conceptual assets covered in this section are QTPP, (potential)CQA, (potential)CPP, material properties (MA), PAR/NOR/design space, and control strategy. Based on the regulatory definition that design space is “a multidimensional combination of input and process variables with proven quality assurance,” these concepts are inherited (Source).

In terms of equipment and tools, 2~15 L bench and automatic parallel minibioreactor (e.g. Sartorius Ambr 15/250), scale-down rig for downstream unit processes such as chromatography and filtration, DoE/statistical software (JMP, Sartorius MODDE, Stat-Ease Design-Expert), multivariate analysis (SIMCA/PCA/OPLS), and integrated process model/mechanistic/hybrid. Models and digital twins are used. However, this list of specific tools is a comprehensive summary of this section, and citations of primary sources defining individual tools were not secured.

Example values frequently cited in engineering matching of SDM are that the P/V of mammalian culture is approximately in the range of 10 to 100 W/m³ with a tolerance of ±20%, kLa is also adjusted to within ±20% of commercial, and dissolved CO2, which reaches about 100 to 200 mmHg in commercial, is corrected by raising CO2 supplementation from about 30 to 60 mmHg on the bench. (Source). ⚠️ The statistical margin for equivalence determination is also set differently for each attribute, for example, 10% for VCD, survival rate, and perfusion rate, 15% for glucose consumption, lactate, specific productivity, and titer, 25% for specific growth rate, and an absolute limit (e.g. ±2%) for product quality (glycan, etc.), which is an example of the actual operation of the TOST-based equivalence test. Applicable (Source). FMEA’s RPN threshold also has a practical practice of triggering action if it exceeds 100 to 200, and generally allowing it if it is less than 30 (Source)。 ⚠️Organized into the flow of input → output, the starting point is QTPP, potential CQA, cell line (MCB/WCB), medium, feed, raw materials, and initial process knowledge, and the output of this stage is the CPP list, PAR/NOR, management strategy, (optional) design space, qualified SDM, and process characterization report (Source). This output is input into CTD Module 3.2.S.2.6 (Manufacturing Process Development History), a regulatory submission document, along with technology transfer (7.2) and PPQ (7.3).

Quality and regulatory perspective

What this step actually controls is CQA. Identify which process parameters affect purity/impurities (process-related: HCP, residual DNA, column leachable, product-related: aggregates, charge, size variants, glycosylation), potency/bioactivity, and accidental contamination safety, and set acceptable ranges. Q11 points out that most CQAs are a product of the raw drug product (process) due to the special nature of biological drugs, so maintaining CQAs within appropriate limits, range, and distribution is a matter of controlling the process (Source).

The governing guidance is ICH Q11 (development/manufacturing of raw drug products), Q8(R2) (pharmaceutical development/design space/QbD), Q9(R1) (quality risk management/FMEA), Q10 (knowledge management), Q14/Q2(R2) (analysis method development/verification), Q6B (biological specifications/impurity classification), and FDA’s 2011 Process Validation. This is guidance Stage 1. Among these, QRM is a development tool used to evaluate process design, quality attributes, and process variables, the principle that risk assessment should be performed early and repeated, and the requirement that a small model reflect the scale effect and represent the commercial process form the axis of regulatory expectations (Source). The practical implications are clear. Characterization data obtained from unqualified SDM is difficult to trust in BLA (Source)。 ⚠️

The regulatory rules surrounding design space are also clear. The design space is proposed by the applicant and subject to regulatory review and approval, and work within it is not considered a change, but movement out of it triggers post-change management (Source). However, this flexibility is especially difficult to achieve in biologics. Q11 states that design space development and approval of some biological products is challenging due to process variability and drug substance complexities such as post-translational modifications, and that residual risks resulting from uncertainty about scale sensitivity may remain after approval (Source). In summary, the regulatory value of characterization lies in de-risking — establishing a scientific basis for management strategies, specifications, and robustness in advance by identifying CPP, interactions, and edge-of-failure before PPQ.

Differences by product type

How this principle is applied varies greatly depending on the product type. However, it should be noted that the description below is a framework synthesized in this section, and securing primary evidence is limited to the specific CPP list and numerical range of the new product modality (see ‘Unresolved Questions’ below).

mAb/recombinant protein is the most mature reference standard. With abundant platform knowledge, the CPP set, SDM qualification, and DoE design are standardized (CHO upstream, Protein A, ion exchange downstream), and the description in this section also stands on this spine. When going to mRNA-LNP, cell culture disappears and cell-free enzymatic reaction (IVT) and LNP micromix formation become the core unit processes. The characterization target is changed to CPP of IVT (nucleotide/enzyme/DNA template ratio, temperature, time) and LNP (flow rate ratio FRR, total flow rate, lipid:RNA ratio, buffer pH), and scale-down is redefined as a ‘mixing/reaction’ scale problem rather than a ‘culture’ problem. Viral vector/cell/gene therapy (CGT) is not scale-up but scale-out (multiple small batches in parallel, batch = 1 patient in the case of autologous), so the materials and repetitions required for statistical SDM/DoE are fundamentally limited, the materials are very expensive, and donor variability is large, increasing dependence on high-efficiency mini-bioreactors and modeling. Classical vaccines have large biological variability such as egg/cell-based antigens and adjuvants, so traditional approaches based on experience are still common, and more emphasis is placed on demonstrating established scope and robustness rather than on a complete design space. In either case, the principle of “CPP → CQA connection and SDM representativeness” is maintained, but the feasibility and statistical robustness of SDM/DoE change significantly.

Practices and recent changes

The current de facto standard is the QbD enhanced approach. The pipeline of QTPP → CQA → FMEA risk assessment → SDM qualification → DoE (partial factor/CCD) → PAR/design space operates as a regulatory expectation, which is consistent with the logic defined by Q11 as an enhanced approach (Source).

The movement over the past three years is clear. First, with the proliferation of automatic parallel minibioreactors (Ambr 250, etc.), high-throughput DoE/process mapping is accelerating. Second, in-silico characterization is emerging with mechanistic/hybrid models and digital twins. In fact, it is claimed that hundreds of experiments can be performed within a few days within a bioprocess digital twin (BPDT), reducing unnecessary engineering runs by virtualizing the process and correcting scale-up standards and confirming set points (Source). ⚠️ At this time, the mechanistic model (disparity from reality when parameters are not specified) and the data-based model (inaccuracy outside the data domain) have their own limitations, and the hybrid model is explained to alleviate these by combining mechanistic simulation and ML statistical algorithms (Source). ⚠️ Third, a flow that evaluates holistic criticality with an integrated process model connecting unit processes (Source) ⚠️, fourth, a flow that supports characterization with an improved approach to analysis methods with ICH Q14, and fifth, a flow that complements the subjectivity of FMEA with data-based criticality.To be honest, limitations still remain. FMEA has a subjectivity in which risk assessment is “mainly influenced by personal opinion,” and even the ISPE practice guide recommends introducing data-based prior information and supplementing it with evidence-based decision-making (Source). SDM cannot fully reproduce commercial scale due to differences in mixing, material transfer, and geometry, so some CQAs remain as non-predictions (Case C/D) (Source) ⚠️, and for digital twins, regulatory acceptance and verification frameworks are still being established.

Issue

Should criticality be viewed as binary or continuous?Traditional definitions classify CPPs as binary (core/non-critical), but some in the industry argue for ‘continuous criticality’ that classifies CPPs according to the size of their impact on CQA. Viewing it as a continuum has the advantage of being able to focus management and monitoring resources on the point that has the greatest impact on product quality (Source). ⚠️ Conversely, binary classification is advantageous for regulatory clarity and simplification of management strategies, but continuum leaves the burden of procedurally defining risk levels and tools for each company and applying them consistently.FMEA subjectivity vs. data-based criticality. ISPE acknowledges that FMEA is dependent on individual opinions and recommends the introduction of prior data (Source), and quantitative and integrated process model-based criticality emerges as an alternative. Despite criticism that the threshold (100–200) for the RPN score is arbitrary and the statistical flaws of the multiplicative scale, FMEA remains the standard screening tool.Fundamental limitations of SDM representativeness. SDM cannot fully reproduce commercial scale due to differences in mixing, mass transfer (kLa·pCO2), and geometry, and the goal is not ‘same’ but ‘sufficiently similar’. As a result, some CQAs remain non-predictive, and there is a gap between developers and regulators regarding the reliability of extrapolating small-scale characterization data to commercial scale. Q11 itself acknowledges that residual risks due to scale sensitivity uncertainty may remain even after approval (Source).Promise vs. maturity of digital twin/in-silico characterization. Medicine Maker (2025) claims that digital twins can perform hundreds of experiments within days and eliminate engineering runs, but the same article admits that the model deviates from reality when parameters are not specified and is inaccurate outside the data domain (Source). ⚠️ The fact that the regulatory acceptability and verification framework is still being established is a reason to take this promise with caution.

Unresolved Questions

  • Detailed characterization by product type (especially CGT scale-out, mRNA IVT/LNP) — The existence of the BioPhorum workstream is confirmed, but primary evidence for the specific CPP list and numerical range is limited.
  • The binary vs. continuous criticality debate and the statistical methodology of data-based criticality — There are limitations in definitive description as it cannot directly render the original source (BioPharm Part I) and partially relies on search snippets (medium confidence).

7.2 Technology transfer & scale-up

Role and location

Technology Transfer & Scale-up is the stage where the already defined process developed and characterized in 7.1 (key process variables, management strategy, qualified scale-down model, and process characterization data) is transferred to actual GMP commercial manufacturing. WHO defines technology transfer as a systematic process of transferring documented knowledge and experience accumulated during the development and commercialization process to an appropriate, responsible, and authorized party. This includes not only the simple transfer of documents, but also the verification that the recipient can effectively perform key elements of the transferred technology (WHO TRS Annex 7). ⚠️ ICH Q10 defines technology transfer as an independent step in the pharmaceutical life cycle, with the goal of ensuring that product knowledge and process understanding are successfully transferred from development to manufacturing or from one site to another, preserving product integrity and performance (ICH Q10 Commentary). ⚠️

There are two main directions before. PD → GMP transfer, which is the first transition from development scale to commercial scale, and site-to-site transfer (company ↔ company, company ↔ CDMO, CDMO ↔ CDMO), which moves an already commercialized process to another manufacturing facility. TRS 1044 Annex 4, the latest edition of the WHO Technology Transfer Guide, provides principle-level guidance and general recommendations on the activities required for successful technology transfer, whether intra-site or inter-site, and also covers method transfer (gmp-compliance). In terms of organizational structure, there is a sending unit (SU) that transfers knowledge and documents and a receiving unit (RU) that reproduces and performs them, and in some cases, there is a third unit that manages the process, which may or may not be a separate entity (WHO TRS Annex 7). ⚠️

It is clear why this section is called ‘the most dangerous inflection point’ in the entire program. This is because process variability, which was manageable at pilot scale, becomes a problem that determines supply at commercial scale, and when equipment size, mixing, heat transfer, and management strategies deviate from the development scale, process behavior changes substantially (cdmoworld). ⚠️ In short, this step is about ‘translating knowledge into physical manufacturing capabilities,’ and the costs of lost batches, supply interruptions, and regulatory delays in case of failure are the highest of the entire program.

How it works — 6-step practical flow

In practice, biopharmaceutical technology transfer proceeds in approximately six stages. The order is knowledge/document transfer → equipment suitability/gap analysis → analytical method transfer (AMT) → raw material sorting → engineering/pilot run → process verification/cGMP batch, and the final process verification (PPQ) usually requires three consecutive successful batches at full scale (cdmoworld). ⚠️

Step 1, knowledge and document transfer.SU transfers the Technology Transfer Protocol/Plan (TTP) and Technology Transfer Dossier (TTD; process description, master batch record, CMC, and development history) to RU. At this time, the absence of deep knowledge about the manufacturing process or the product itself is considered a fatal pitfall in technology transfer, and is alleviated by a detailed risk assessment of the knowledge gap through consultation with the original process developer, review of deviation logs, and trend data (bioprocessonline). ⚠️

Step 2, facility fit/gap analysis.Compare whether the equipment, utilities, and scale of the RU match the process requirements and list any differences as risks. Variability in equipment type, capacity, and scale between the original and new sites can cause delays, which must be managed through thorough facility evaluation and confirmation testing at scale (bioprocessonline). ⚠️

Step 3, Analytical Method Transfer (AMT).Transfer and verify the release/characterization test method to the RU laboratory so that it can be performed equally. The practical advice is clear — AMT should be initiated at least 3 to 6 months ahead of the first engineering run rather than waiting for the manufacturing process to be ready, and failure of the AMT can bring the entire production schedule to a halt (cdmoworld). ⚠️

Step 4, Sorting raw materials and consumables.RU secures and sorts the sources and specifications of media, buffers, resins, and single-use assemblies.Step 5, Engineering/Demo Run. As a non-GMP full-scale dry run without regulatory pressure, the Engineering Run is a ‘safe zone where failure is allowed’ to check facility compliance and automation without regulatory consequences prior to cGMP manufacturing. Biological drugs grown in living systems are extremely sensitive to environmental and equipment variables, and a single mistake can lead to a batch failure and millions of dollars in lost sales. At this stage, leaks, software defects, and operation errors are caught in advance, personnel are trained, and the accuracy of the scale-up model is verified (cdmoworld). ⚠️

Step 6, Process Verification/cGMP Batch.Typically, PPQ (7.3) is performed with three successive batches, and in case of failure, there is a risk of investigation and restart.

Governing documents supporting this flow include the TTP, TTD, gap analysis/equipment conformance report, Quality/Technical Agreement dividing SU↔RU responsibilities, method transfer protocol, comparability protocol (ICH Q5E), risk assessment (FMEA), master/electronic batch record (MBR/EBR), and PACMP for regulatory change management (ICH Q12). The WHO guide structures this transfer along the following axes: organization/management → production transfer (processing, packaging, cleaning) → QC analysis method transfer → facilities/equipment → documentation → qualification/verification (gmp-compliance).

Scale-up engineering and key parameters

As bioreactors scale up, they scale up while maintaining certain engineering parameters — power per unit volume (P/V), oxygen transfer coefficient (kLa), impeller tip speed/mixing time, and ventilation rate (vvm). The key challenge is that it is impossible to keep these values ​​constant at the same time (non-linear scale-up), so compromises are inevitable. In fact, even slight differences in bioreactor geometry or agitation rate can change the glycosylation pattern of proteins, making scale-up inherently non-linear and risky (cdmoworld). ⚠️ In the downstream, scale rules such as chromatography column volume/linear velocity, membrane load (LMH), and flux are matched. The scale-down model (SDM) qualified in 7.1 is reused as a basis for predicting and correcting this scale behavior.

The scale spectrum spans from development/pilot (several L to hundreds L) to commercial (thousands to tens of thousands L, up to approximately 25,000 L for mAb fed-batch), and PPQ is typically performed in three commercial-scale batches in succession. However, the difficulty of not affecting product quality while scaling up from pilot to full-scale commercial production is itself a representative pitfall, and this can be alleviated through effective risk management for changes in facilities, equipment, materials, methods, and personnel (bioprocessonline). ⚠️ Please note that ‘3 consecutive batches’ is not a number fixed by regulations but a customary heuristic, and the basis for the number of batches is covered again in the CPV approach in 7.3 (Process Verification).

Comparability — Criteria for judging previous success

Ultimately, whether the transfer is successful is determined by whether the products before and after the transfer can be considered the ‘same product.’ What is required here is not ‘identical’ but ‘highly similar’. Demonstration of comparability according to ICH Q5E does not necessarily mean that the quality attributes of the pre-change and post-change products are the same, but rather means ‘highly similar,’ and must ensure that differences in attributes do not negatively affect safety and effectiveness (ICH Q5E). In practice, multiple pre-transfer historical lots and post-transfer lots are compared head-to-head with extensive analytical characterization, biological testing, and stability, and are aligned to predefined acceptance criteria (usually pre-transfer historical ranges). And this loss of comparability is pointed out as the most fundamental risk in the transfer of biopharmaceutical technology to CDMOs (cdmoworld). ⚠️ Clinical/non-clinical bridging is only rarely necessary, analytical data is usually sufficient, and it is recommended that comparability protocols be established ‘before’ implementation to allow regulators to pre-approve the strategy.

Quality and regulatory expectations

Although technology transfer itself is not an emission test, it must be ensured that the product after the transfer is highly similar in quality, safety, and effectiveness to the product before the transfer. There are three key controls — comparability (whether CQAs, including purity/impurities, charge, size variants, and glycans, are within historical ranges and meet specifications), method equivalence (whether RU laboratories produce equivalent precision, accuracy, and detection power as SU, especially titer/bioassay sensitivity), and process reproducibility (whether CPPs remain within control ranges during engineering runs and PPQs).

Governing guidance is layered. ICH Q10 defines technology transfer as a life cycle stage (ICH Q10 Commentary) ⚠️, and WHO TRS 1044 Annex 4 (2022, replacing the old TRS 961 Annex 7 (2011)) sets out principles and recommendations for in-house and inter-site transfer and analytical method transfer. (gmp-compliance), ICH Q5E treats site transfers as ‘manufacturing changes’ and requires comparability (ICH Q5E), and ICH Q12 (PACMP, established conditions) and ICH Q9 (risk management) support post-change management and risk assessment. The key point from a regulatory perspective is that a site move is a manufacturing change, not a new product development. The FDA guidance states that comparability has also been used successfully “when a production facility is transferred from one legal entity to another,” meaning that site transfers can be handled with pre-established comparability protocols (intuitionlabs). ⚠️ The strength of the comparability package varies depending on the severity of the change (major/moderate/minor) (new cell line/significant scale-up is major, same equipment/same scale is possible only with analysis data), and on top of that, the RU must not only hand over documents but demonstrate the ability to effectively perform key elements of the transferred technology (WHO TRS Annex 7). ⚠️ After transfer, the first commercial lot must be verified with PPQ (7.3) before being released.

Differences by product type

Note — The details by product type in this subsection are areas that are explicitly stated to be covered ‘at a conceptual level only’ due to limited primary evidence in the source files themselves (see ‘Open Questions’ below). Therefore, here, we only present the previous ‘how the frame moves’ conceptually rather than specific numbers and protocols, and make it clear that there is no dedicated citation basis in the file for individual statements. ⚠️

In this framework, mAb/recombinant proteins are the most mature reference standards. Thanks to platform knowledge, standard SDM, and established comparability practices, the SU↔RU transfer is relatively predictable, and the scale-up rules and comparability protocols upstream of CHO and downstream of Protein A are standardized. Since mRNA-LNP does not require cell culture, the key is not ‘culture scale-up’ but expansion of IVT (enzyme reaction) and scale-up of LNP micromixing (microfluidic/T-mixer). Scale-up variables include flow rate ratio (FRR), total flow rate, and mixer shape, and the key is to reproduce the mixer, pump, and mixing scale during transfer. The IVT batch is short, so the transfer cycle is fast, but the arrangement of raw material supply such as enzymes, nucleotides, and pDNA templates becomes a bottleneck. Virus vector/CGT is not scale-up but scale-out (multiple small batches in parallel, autologous is batch = 1 patient), so the previous goal changes from ‘reproduction of one large batch’ to ‘consistency of multiple small batches’. Materials are expensive and repetition is limited, making engineering runs and statistical comparability difficult, and donor variability, short shelf life, and point-of-care distributed manufacturing are the most difficult product modality, shaking up the previous concept itself. Classical vaccines have large biological variability such as egg/cell-based antigens, inactivation, glycoconjugates, and adjuvants, so experience-based transfer is common, and emphasis is placed on reproducing established scope and robustness rather than complete comparability. In particular, egg-based vaccines have low flexibility in site transfer due to special facilities and supply chains (fertilized eggs). In summary, Protein Spine’s ‘culture scale-up + comparability’ framework has moved to ‘mixing/reaction scale-up’ in mRNA and ‘scale-out/small-batch consistency’ in CGT, but the principles of SU→RU knowledge transfer and comparability verification are maintained.

Practice status and recent trends

The current standard is the step-by-step transfer described above — document/knowledge transfer → gap analysis/equipment suitability → AMT → raw material alignment → engineering/demo run → PPQ — and the SU↔RU quality agreement and ICH Q5E comparability form the regulatory framework. The majority of commercial technology transfers go to CDMOs, and the CDMO market is estimated to grow from approximately $135 billion in 2022 to more than $250 billion in the 2030s, making transfer execution a core competency of the industry (however, this market size figure is only indicated as an ‘advisor’s estimate’ in the source file, and there is no basis for exclusive citation). ⚠️Over the past three years, there have been five major movements. First, digital technology transfer. While paper-based (‘paper on glass’) methods make it difficult to maintain a ‘single version of truth’, increasing the risk of failure due to misunderstanding, digital platforms such as product life cycle management (PLM) and digital twin provide connectivity and collaborative information sources to prevent information silos and aim for ‘design one, build many’/digital cloning (ValGenesis). ⚠️ Second, strengthen knowledge management. However, there is ample evidence that knowledge management is not yet a mature field in the biopharmaceutical sector even though it was introduced in ICH Q10 more than 10 years ago, and it is pointed out that improved knowledge transfer could contribute to bridging the gap between ICH Q10 and Q12 (PubMed). ⚠️ Third, there is a trend to agree in advance on pre- and post-change changes based on the PACMP/established conditions of ICH Q12. Fourth, the previous guide was modernized with WHO TRS 1044 Annex 4 (2022) (gmp-compliance). Fifth, the FDA Platform Technology Designation/AMT Program (detailed in Section 8.3) promotes iterative transfer and distributed manufacturing. In terms of standards and best practices, the ISPE Good Practice Guide: Technology Transfer (3rd edition) emphasizes alignment with the Quality by Design principle and recognizes that for successful technology transfer, both sending and receiving units must have a solid quality culture (ISPE). ⚠️

The limits are also clear. These are the practice of sponsors incurring greater losses due to GMP deployment failure by omitting non-GMP engineering runs amid cost pressure, unexpected CQA changes resulting from scale-up nonlinearity that cannot simultaneously maintain P/V·kLa, AMT bottlenecks that stop the entire schedule due to failure to reproduce titer/bioassay sensitivity, lack of regulatory acceptance and maturity of knowledge management and digital twins, and differences in judgment between developers and regulators surrounding the judgment criteria of ‘highly similar’. Standardization prior to the new product modality (CGT/mRNA) is still in progress.

Issue:There are four points where opinions differ among sources. (1) Omitting engineering runs vs. performing them — The working consensus is to verify scale-up models and facilities with non-GMP engineering runs, and it is believed that one failed GMP deployment incurs much greater costs than two engineering runs (cdmoworld) ⚠️ On the other hand, sponsors under budget and schedule pressure have a strong incentive to omit them, which directly conflicts with short-term cost savings and long-term deployment failure risk. (2) Comparability Judgment boundaries of ‘highly similar’ — ICH Q5E states that analytical data alone is usually sufficient, but there are differences of opinion between developers and regulators in determining what is ‘highly similar’ and which CQA differences are ‘clinically meaningless’ (e.g., whether statistically significant differences can be allowed if there is mechanistic justification). (3) Promise vs. maturity of digital technology transfer — Vendors such as ValGenesis claim that PLM and digital twins eliminate silos (ValGenesis) ⚠️ However, peer-reviewed literature points out that knowledge management is still an immature field (PubMed) ⚠️ Tool maturity and regulatory acceptance fall short of the claims. (4) PPQ ‘3 batches in a row’ practice vs. regulatory flexibility — The practice description presents PPQ as 3 successive full-scale successful batches (cdmoworld) ⚠️, but the FDA 2011 Process Verification Guide (detailed in 7.3) does not fix the number of batches but determines it based on risk and data, so the ‘Rule of 3’ is closer to a customary heuristic rather than a regulatory requirement.Unresolved Questions: (1) Previous details by product type, especially CGT scale-out batch consistency and mRNA mixer scale-up specific numbers and protocols, were covered only at the concept level due to limited primary evidence. (2) WHO TRS 1044 Annex 4·ISPE GPG 3rd edition·ICH Q10 detailed provisions of the original text verbatim relied on secondary/summary evidence due to PDF binary rendering failure (core definitions were secured, but detailed procedural provisions were partial).

7.3 Process & cleaning validation lifecycle

Role and location

In short, process validation (PV) is an activity that proves through documentation that ‘when the process is operated within the set parameter range, pharmaceuticals that meet predefined standards and quality characteristics can be produced reproducibly.’ EU GMP nails this as a GMP requirement, stating that manufacturers must control key aspects of their operations through qualification and validation throughout the life cycle of their products and processes, and that retrospective verification, which was common in the past, is no longer an acceptable approach (EU Annex 15). In other words, the main premise of modern regulations is that verification is not an ‘event’ that collects data after the batch is made, but a ‘life cycle’ that continues from development to disposal.

The FDA’s 2011 process verification guidance summarizes this life cycle in the most widely used language. Here, process validation is redefined as three stages — Stage 1 Process Design, Stage 2 Process Qualification, and Stage 3 Continued Process Verification (FDA Process Validation Guidance). Understanding will become faster if you overlap this frame with the structure of this research map. Since Stage 1 (Process Design/Characterization) is virtually the same activity as 7.1 (Process Development/Characterization) of this map, this section (7.3) is the place to execute Stage 2 and Stage 3 by taking as input the outputs—confirmed process definition, CPP (Critical Process Parameters)/PAR (Provable Acceptable Range), management strategy, and qualified scale-down model—and the technology transfer completed in 7.2.This section ties together the three different axes of verification. These are (a) product process verification (PPQ/CPV), (b) cleaning validation, and (c) computer system verification (CSV/CSA). All three axes do not directly release batches, but serve as regulatory gateways to commercial production and distribution and the basis for ‘state of control’ throughout the entire commercial life. Europe does not have separate three-stage names like the United States, but rather regulates qualification, process verification, cleaning verification, and computer system verification together under Annex 15. The output of this section becomes the core review subject of 8.1 CMC submission (process verification data is reflected in 3.2.P/3.2.S module) and 8.2 GMP inspection.

Stage 2 — Process qualification confirmation is two-fold

Stage 2 consists of two interlocking elements. First, check the eligibility of facilities, equipment, and utilities. Annex 15 defines this as a step-by-step chain — URS (User Requirement Specification) → DQ (Design Qualification) → FAT/SAT (factory and field acceptance test) → IQ (Installation Qualification) → OQ (Operation Qualification) → PQ (Performance Qualification). At this time, OQ must include upper and lower limits and worst-case condition testing, and after successful completion of IQ and OQ, PQ requires confirming reproducibility under normal operating conditions and worst-case batch size using production materials or qualified substitutes or simulated products with proven equivalent behavior (EU Annex 15).Second, PPQ (Process Performance Qualification) — This is the step to demonstrate that the process operates reproducibly by actually manufacturing a commercial-scale batch according to an approved protocol. PPQ batches involve higher levels of sampling, additional testing, and more rigorous scrutiny of process performance than typical commercial production, and this enhanced scrutiny continues throughout the process validation phase and serves as the basis for establishing subsequent routine sampling and monitoring levels and frequencies (FDA Process Validation Guidance). The critical point in regulatory logic is that PPQ must be successfully completed before commercial distribution begins, and the length of this enhanced sampling and monitoring period is determined by considering production volume, process complexity, level of process understanding, and experience with similar products (FDA Process Validation Guidance). ⚠️ In short, if IQ/OQ/PQ asks ‘Does the facility stand properly and rotates properly?’, PPQ is a gate that asks ‘Does the facility adhere to the standards when real products are made repeatedly?’

Number of PPQ batches — ‘3 batches’ or statistics?

The most frequently encountered question in the field is “How many batches do I need to make for verification?” The old practice was ‘three batches in a row’ and Annex 15 also recognizes that it is generally accepted that at least three consecutive batches manufactured under normal conditions can constitute process validation. However, the same document immediately provides a clue - a different number of batches may be justified considering whether a standard manufacturing method is used or whether a similar product or process is already used at the site, and that initial verification starting with three batches may need to be supplemented with subsequent batch data obtained during the continuous process verification phase (EU Annex 15). More fundamentally, Annex 15 stipulates that each manufacturer must determine and justify for itself the number of batches required to demonstrate ‘a high level of assurance that the process will consistently yield a quality product’ (EU Annex 15). In other words, ‘3’ is only a minimum convention and is not automatically a sufficient number.

As to how to do this justification, the ISPE white paper recommends a statistical approach. In determining the number of batches, a science- and risk-based approach is preferred over ‘unconditionally defaulting to 3 batches’, and specifically, tolerance intervals can be used as a tool (ISPE White Paper). The γ/p tolerance interval refers to the limit at which the minimum specific proportion (p, coverage) of future individual observations in the population is predicted to be received with a specific confidence level (γ) (ISPE White Paper). The practical logic goes like this — setting the confidence level γ, coverage p, and acceptance coefficient k uniquely determines the sample number N (k and N are a function of each other through γ·p), so the greater the risk, the higher (non-decreasing) γ·p is set and the more batches are required. The acceptance criteria can be set, for example, as ‘the PPQ tolerance range will fall within the normal emission standards (CQA standards).’ This way, you can defend ‘Why are there 3 batches or why are there 5 batches?’ in statistical language.

Stage 3 — Continuous Process Verification (CPV)

Verification does not end once you pass the PPQ. Stage 3 (Continuous Process Verification) monitors whether the process remains under state control throughout the entire period of commercial production. Annex 15 calls this concept ‘ongoing/continued process verification’, applies to all three verification approaches - traditional, continuous and hybrid - and stipulates that manufacturers must monitor product quality and evaluate relevant process trends to ensure that condition control is maintained throughout the product life cycle (EU Annex 15). As practical tools, statistical process control (SPC) control charts and Cpk (process capability index) trending are most commonly used in current CPV programs, and CPV is included as part of the annual product quality evaluation (PQR/APR) to trigger investigation and correction when abnormal trends are seen (ISPE CPV Commentary). ⚠️

Three branches of verification approach and terminological pitfalls

Annex 15 recognizes three approaches to process verification: It is a traditional approach, continuous process verification, and a hybrid of the two. Among these, continuous process verification has been developed as QbD (Quality by Design) and can be used as an alternative to traditional process verification only for products for which the management strategy has been scientifically established to provide a high level of quality assurance during the development process (EU Annex 15). Here, what learners must catch is the trap of terminology. Two concepts with almost the same names that can be confusing actually refer to completely different things. The Annex 15 glossary makes the distinction clearly — ‘continuous process verification’ is an alternative approach to process verification (Stage 2) (ICH Q8) where manufacturing process performance is continuously monitored and evaluated, while ‘ongoing/continued process verification’ is documented evidence (Stage 3) that the process remains in state control during commercial manufacturing (EU Annex 15). The former replaces PPQ with real-time monitoring during the development stage, and the latter is continuous monitoring after commercialization.

Cleaning Verification — From HBEL to MACO

Cleaning verification proves that ‘when equipment is cleaned after making one product, the previous product or cleaning agent does not transfer to a dangerous level for the next product.’ The Annex 15 glossary defines this as ‘documented evidence showing that an approved cleaning procedure reproducibly removes old product or used cleaning agents below the scientifically established maximum acceptable carryover level’ (EU Annex 15).

The starting point for setting limits is toxicity. Annex 15 requires carryover limits for product residues in shared facilities to be set based on toxicological evaluation, and this footnote directly quotes the EMA’s Health-Based Exposure Limits (HBEL) guidelines (EU Annex 15). HBEL is the same concept as PDE (Permissible Daily Exposure)/ADE (Acceptable Daily Exposure), and is defined as ‘a dose that will not cause harmful effects even if exposed to less than that every day of a lifetime through any route’, and replaces the past arbitrary legacy standard — 0.001 times the dose (1/1000), 10 ppm ([ASTM E3106 Introduction] (https://www.pharmaceuticalonline.com/doc/introduction-to-the-astm-e-standard-guide-to-science-based-and-risk-based-cleaning-process-development-and-validation-0001)). The PDE obtained in this way is converted to the actual residual limit and is MACO (Maximum Allowable Carry Over). One source gives the calculation as MACO = (PDE × minimum batch size of next product) / (maximum daily dose of next product) (intuitionlabs). ⚠️

Once you have set a limit, you need to measure whether it is actually below that limit. Residues are recovered by swab and rinse sampling, and Annex 15 requires solubility, cleanability, toxicity, and potency to be used as criteria when selecting worst-case products and equipment, proving recovery from all materials contacted in swabs and rinses, and defining dirty hold time and clean hold time (EU Annex 15). The recovered residue is quantified using methods such as HPLC/UV or total organic carbon (TOC, a surrogate indicator for organic residue). Regarding one pitfall, the regulations are particularly resolute — visual cleanliness is an important part of the acceptance criteria, but using this criterion alone is generally not acceptable, and repeated cleaning and retesting until acceptable residual results are not an acceptable approach (EU Annex 15). The standard that operates this entire life cycle (design → qualification → continuous verification) is ASTM E3106, where risk is defined as a function of the toxicity, residual level, and detectability of the residue, and the level of effort, formality, and documentation put into verification is proportional to the risk level (ASTM E3106 Introduction).

Computer System Verification — From CSV to CSA

Software used to determine GMP (manufacturing execution system, data historian, automated control, etc.) must also be proven to operate as intended and that electronic records are defect-free. Annex 15 stipulates that computer systems used for the manufacture of pharmaceuticals must be validated in accordance with the requirements of Annex 11 (EU Annex 15). The standard industry framework is GAMP 5 (2nd edition, 2022), which proportions the depth of verification to the complexity and risk of the system through the V-model and software categories (Cat 1 infrastructure / Cat 3 unchanged COTS / Cat 4 configurable / Cat 5 custom development). The second edition maintains the core life cycle model and risk-based spirit of the first edition, emphasizing that each company classifies systems by complexity and intended use and then applies a ‘fit-for-purpose’ verification approach (intuitionlabs GAMP 5). ⚠️A recent big trend is the transition from traditional CSV to CSA. While traditional CSV focuses on extensive documentation and predefined testing to demonstrate compliance, FDA’s Computer Software Assurance (CSA) is a risk-based approach that prioritizes critical thinking and targeted testing based on potential impact on patient safety and product quality (Scilife CSV vs CSA). CSA divides testing methods into two classes and distributes risk — unscripted testing is ad-hoc, error-guessing, and exploratory testing, which requires minimal documentation, and scripted testing ranges from ‘robust script testing’ for high-risk components to hybrid ‘limited script testing’ (Scilife CSV vs CSA). ⚠️ In other words, robust script testing is allocated to direct impact software that controls key process variables, and non-script testing with minimal documentation is allocated to low-risk functions such as simple data recording. Electronic records and electronic signatures themselves are governed by 21 CFR Part 11 (USA) and EU Annex 11.

Differences by product type

Although the principles so far are common regardless of product modality, the form of implementation varies greatly depending on the product type. Monoclonal antibodies (mAb) and recombinant proteins are the most mature reference standards. Because it is a platform process, PPQ is usually performed in commercial-scale batches of three or more, and cleaning verification covering chromatography campaigns such as Protein A, resin life, and CIP/SIP are standardized, and CSV is also mature.mRNA-LNP has a short process (cell-free IVT transcription + lipid nanoparticle micromixing) and quick batch rotation, so it is relatively easy to secure PPQ batches, but since it is a new process, the accumulation of statistical evidence is still in progress. The focus of cleaning verification shifts to lipid and RNase control and organic solvent (ethanol) residue, and there is great interest in continuous process and real-time delivery testing (RTRT).Viral vector/cell gene therapy (CGT) shakes the traditional PPQ logic itself. Because it is scale-out (parallel small batch, in the case of autologous, batch = 1 patient), securing the materials and repetitions required for commercial-scale PPQ is limited and the materials are very expensive, making it difficult to establish the logic of ‘3 batch statistics’. Therefore, in ultra-rare indications, reliance on concurrent validation increases. On the other hand, as single-use systems dominate, the burden of equipment cleaning verification is reduced, and the verification focus shifts to extractables and leachables (E&L) and assembly integrity. Above all, the structure of autologous ‘placement = patient’ redefines the very concept of ‘reproducibility’ of traditional PPQ.Classical vaccines have large biological variability and campaign manufacturing and dedicated facilities are common, so traditional verification and established scope and robustness verification are emphasized, and cleaning and cross-contamination management are special for each egg/cell-based antigen, inactivation, glycoconjugation, and adjuvant. In short, based on the protein spine, mRNA is divided into the direction of ‘PPQ becomes easier and the cleaning focus moves’, and CGT is divided into the direction of ‘PPQ statistics collapse due to scale-out and simultaneous verification/disposable → minimization of washing verification and movement to E&L’. (This product modality contrast is a summary of this section; primary evidence at the individual numerical level is limited — see ‘Open Questions’ below.)

Practices and recent changes

The current de facto global expectation is ‘FDA 3-stage life cycle + EU Annex 15’. Stage 1 (=7.1) Characterization → Stage 2 IQ/OQ/PQ + PPQ → Stage 3 CPV flow, HBEL-based risk approach for cleaning verification, and CSV’s GAMP 5 frame have become the standard. The movement of the past three years can be summarized in six directions. First, the practice of justifying the number of PPQ batches based on statistics and risk (tolerance interval, Bayesian) is spreading, moving away from the ‘3-batch default’. Second, the verification paradigm is moving from batch-centered to continuous monitoring due to the expansion of continuous process verification, RTRT, and PAT and alignment with ICH Q13 (continuous manufacturing) and Q8. Third, with the finalization of the FDA CSA, computer system verification is shifting from document-centered to risk-based critical thinking and minimal burden testing. Fourth, AI/ML and data governance regulations are being strengthened with the draft revision of EU Annex 11 (Computing Systems) and the proposed revision of Annex 22 (AI) and Chapter 4. Fifth, the EU GMP Annex 15 revision discussion (scheduled for 2026) is expected to reflect the management strategy, harmonization with ICH Q12, and verification maturity. Sixth, digital and paperless verification platforms (ValGenesis, Kneat, etc.) and CPV automatic data pipelines are spreading.

At the same time, there are clear limitations. These are the gap between statistics and practice surrounding ‘how many batches are enough’, the immaturity of data infrastructure and governance to support CPV, the absence of a frame to verify and re-verify learning systems such as AI/ML, the applicability of traditional PPQ and cleaning verification in the new product modality of CGT/mRNA, confusion over the terms continuous vs. continued/ongoing process verification, and limitations in visual inspection and detection of trace high-potency substances in cleaning.

Issue

PPQ number of batches — ‘3 batches’ or statistical justification.Even within Annex 15, 5.20 says that at least 3 consecutive batches are ‘generally accepted’, but 5.19 and the ISPE white paper require the number of batches to be justified with QRM and tolerance interval statistics. Although both are recognized in regulations, there is a practical friction between whether 3 batches can be used as is in simple and well-understood processes and how much statistical evidence should be required in new product modality and complex processes.Conflict between continuous and continued/ongoing verification terms. ‘Continuous process verification’ is an alternative to traditional verification of ICH Q8 (replacing Stage 2 with real-time monitoring of the development stage), and ‘continued/ongoing process verification’ is monitoring during commercial production of FDA Stage 3 / Annex 15. Data often use these interchangeably, causing confusion, but only the Annex 15 glossary clearly distinguishes between the two.CSV vs CSA — Documentation Burden. The extensive documentation of traditional CSV competes with the risk-based, minimal burden testing of CSA. Although the FDA CSA specifies document reduction for low-risk systems, some in the industry are concerned about the risk of document reduction being misunderstood as a reduction in rigor and the subjectivity of the decision to determine what is a direct/indirect impact.Inconsistency in timing of FDA CSA finalization. The Federal Register notice indicates final availability as of 2025-09-24 (Federal Register CSA), but some secondary sources cite ‘2026-02-03 finalization’ (draft date 2022). The date of the first government notification (2025-09) is more reliable.Washing Limits — Legacy vs HBEL. Regulations now strongly favor toxicity-based HBEL/PDE (especially for high potency/cytotoxic substances), but the field is still in a transitional phase with legacy standards such as 10 ppm/1/1000 dose remaining in practice.Unresolved questions: (1) The exact original text of the FDA PV guidance Stage 3 CPV relies on the canonical citation of the search snippet due to failure to directly secure the FDA PDF (verbatim for the enhanced sampling text, but confidence medium for the PPQ completion text before distribution), and was supplemented with the first citation from the ongoing process verification in EU Annex 15. (2) Specific figures for PPQ batch number and cleaning verification by product type (especially CGT scale-out·mRNA) were covered only at the principle level, and the basis for primary figures was limited. (3) The detailed provisions of the EU Annex 15 2026 amendments and Annex 11/Annex 22 (AI) amendments are in the draft stage and were only confirmed at the search level.

7.4 Quality by Design & control strategy

Role and scope and place in the life cycle

7.4 is an ‘integrating design layer’ that combines the outputs created separately by previous nodes—product and process understanding (7.1 process development), CQA and analysis methods (6.x QC), and raw material understanding (1.4)—into one. Quality by Design (QbD), which is covered here, is defined as “a systematic approach that starts from pre-defined goals and emphasizes product and process understanding based on sound science and quality risk management” (Review). This is in direct contrast to traditional quality assurance. While the traditional approach does not provide any prior insight into process variations because it only checks compliance with pre-determined standards through finished product testing (Review), QbD shifts the paradigm to ‘creating quality through design.’

The final product of this section is the control strategy. A QbD-based control strategy is defined as “a set of planned controls that ensure process performance and product quality, derived from current product and process understanding” (ICH Q10) (BPI mAb). In other words, the task of this section is to tie together raw material management, process variable management, in-process testing (IPC), finished product specifications, and monitoring to create a guarantee that “if the process is operated this way and tested this way, the specifications will always be met.” This output is passed on to the higher-level design of process verification (7.3 PPQ/CPV), CMC submission (8.1 Module 3), and batch release (7.6), and is the point where the ‘enhanced approach’ of ICH Q8/Q9/Q10/Q11 is actually implemented.

How it works — causal chain from QTPP to control

The key is a logical chain that starts from the goal, narrows down the risk, and ends up in control.

1) QTPP → CQA.The starting point is the Target Product Quality Profile (QTPP). QTPP describes the design criteria of the product, so it should be the basis for developing CQA·CPP·control strategy (ICH Q8/Q9/Q10 Q&A). There is one rule that must be adhered to here. While the criticality of quality attributes (CQA) is mainly based on the ‘severity of harm to patients’ and does not change to risk management, the criticality of process variables (CPP) can be changed to risk management because the impact of the variable on CQA is viewed from the perspective of occurrence probability and detectability (ICH Q8/Q9/Q10 Q&A). This asymmetry is a subtle point of QbD—no matter how tightly you tighten the controls, the fact that an attribute is ‘important’ doesn’t go away.2) Derive CPP through risk assessment. After listing potential variables in Ishikawa, select potential CPP by scoring severity × occurrence × detection (RPN=S×O×D) using FMEA, etc. The A-Mab case study, which is the de facto standard template for bio QbD, goes one step further and presents a risk ranking method that calculates the criticality of quality attributes as the product of Impact (2–20) and Uncertainty (1–7), that is, a risk score of 2–140 (A-Mab Case Study). This approach places risk on a criticality continuum, which has the effect of raising the threshold to require active evidence to declare an attribute ‘non-CQA’.3) DoE → design space. Selected variables are characterized by design of experiments (DoE) and multivariate experiments to define the design space, which is a multidimensional combination of input variables and process variables that guarantee quality (the definition and regulatory implications of the design space are discussed in detail in 7.1). What is important is the connection rule between the design space and the control strategy. CQA and CPP included in the design space must also be included in the control strategy connected to it, and the purpose of the control strategy is to ensure that the process maintains a state of control within the design space to satisfy quality goals (BPI mAb).4) Control strategy composition. The overall control strategy is based on the design space of unit processes, and an individual control strategy is designed for each quality attribute, and the sum of the individual control strategies forms the overall process control strategy (A-Mab case study). Control elements consist of input material management, process/performance parameter management, IPC, finished product/solution specifications, and process monitoring. A-Mab classifies process variables into CPP, well-controlled CPP (WC-CPP), key performance parameters (KPP), and general process parameters (GPP), and defines control methods according to these classifications (A-Mab Case Study). The strength of control is proportional to the risk, and the risk is determined by three factors: the criticality level of the CQA, process capability (probability of CQA deviating from the relevant stage), and detection probability (A-Mab case study).5) Spectrum of control levels. As control moves from relying on finished product testing (traditional type) → to process variable control/IPC → to real-time shipment testing (RTRT), the ‘design-based’ becomes stronger. Based on enhanced product and process understanding, specification testing is significantly reduced compared to traditional approaches, with some specification testing being transferred to IPC (including PAT) and others being eliminated altogether because operation within the design space already provides high assurance (A-Mab Case Study). RTRT falls on the most ‘design-based’ end of this spectrum, an approach that uses in-process/real-time data to demonstrate that batches meet predefined quality criteria for release rather than relying primarily on finished product laboratory testing. In practice, it combines PAT, a well-understood process, a validated model, and a robust control strategy (RTRT Glossary).6) Life cycle. The control strategy is not a static document. A well-developed control strategy reduces risk but does not change the criticality of the attribute, and this strategy plays a key role in satisfying CQA and further realizing QTPP (ICH Q8/Q9/Q10 Q&A). Here comes another principle that pairs with the above rule. A control strategy or analysis method cannot change the critical/noncritical designation of an individual CQA; it only demonstrates that the parameters affecting that attribute are under control (BPI mAb). Afterwards, this strategy is verified for performance through CPV under the ICH Q10 quality system and is continuously updated with new knowledge.

Key deliverables and tools

The characteristic feature of this section is that the ‘materials’ are not reagents but design outputs (documents/models). Key artefacts include QTPP (design criteria summary), CQA list and its criticality basis, risk assessment document (Ishikawa, FMEA/RPN=S The outputs are Established Conditions and PACMP. On the analysis side, analysis target profile (ATP), critical analysis parameter (CMP), and method usable design region (MODR) are added here.

The principle value that gives a quantitative sense is A-Mab’s criticality risk score of 2–140 (Impact 2–20 Case study](https://ispewebassets.org/files/attachments/public/a-mab-case-study-version.pdf)). In terms of tools, statistical/DoE software (JMP, Design-Expert, MODDE), multivariate prediction model (MSPC·soft sensor), scale-down model as a premise for process characterization, and PAT (NIR·Raman), which is the measurement basis for RTRT control strategy, are used. However, this list of specific tools is a comprehensive summary of this section, and does not include primary source citations defining individual tools.

Quality and regulatory perspective

This section itself defines ‘what to control’—i.e., it is the ‘adequacy’ of the control strategy and residual risk that is assessed, not individual tests. The parent frameworks are arranged in the ICH Quality Series. ICH Q8(R2) defines pharmaceutical development (definition of QTPP·design space·control strategy and minimal vs enhanced approach), ICH Q9 (quality risk management) provides tools for risk-based decision-making, ICH Q10 (pharmaceutical quality system) defines control strategy as a PQS element and is responsible for life cycle management, and ICH Q11 (raw drug substance development/manufacturing) specifies 6 elements of criticality and control strategy. The document that connects these in practice is ICH Q8/Q9/Q10 Points to Consider, and the asymmetric rule of criticality and the status of QTPP cited earlier are stipulated in this document (ICH Q8/Q9/Q10 Q&A). Ultimately, all of these outputs are documented in section 3.2.S/3.2.P ‘Manufacturing process development·control strategy·specification justification’ of CTD Module 3 and linked to 8.1.

The framework has recently been expanded in two directions. First, as a post-life cycle axis, ICH Q12 introduced the concepts of established conditions (ECs), post-change management protocols (PACMP), and harmonized reporting categories, enabling specific post-CMC changes to be implemented with reduced regulatory burden when supported by product and process knowledge (ValGenesis). Second, as an analysis axis, ICH Q14 expanded QbD into an analysis method, and the central concept, Analysis Target Profile (ATP), defines the standards that analysis results must meet to meet patient-quality requirements, and the strengthened approach includes ATP, risk assessment, analysis control strategy, MODR, etc. (Premier). Meanwhile, the risk management framework itself has also been updated—ICH Q9, first adopted in November 2005, was revised to Q9(R1) in January 2023, adding new sections on risk-based decision making, formality of QRM, subjectivity management, and PQS integration (RegDev). ⚠️

To summarize the core regulatory principles, control strategy only reduces risk without changing criticality, movement within the design space is not considered change, providing regulatory flexibility, and RTRT is explicitly recognized as a control strategy option that replaces finished testing with regular data (RTRT Glossary).

Differences by product typem

Ab·recombinant protein.

QbD and control strategy are the most mature areas. The A-Mab case with accumulated platform knowledge serves as a de facto industry standard template, suggesting control strategies for individual properties such as aggregation, glycosylation, HCP, and deamidation, and transferring a significant portion of the specification testing to IPC/PAT. The reason this move is possible is the enhanced understanding, and A-Mab has actually significantly reduced its specification testing, moving some to IPC or eliminating testing of items where operation within the design space is warranted (A-Mab Case Study).mRNA-LNP. The QbD framework itself is applied as is, but CQAs such as capping efficiency, RNA integrity, encapsulation efficiency, and residual dsRNA and their clinical relevance are relatively new, so the design space and control strategy are less established. However, parametric control is advantageous as the number of process steps is small and platformization is fast. (This mRNA-LNP-related description is a qualitative synthesis of this section, and individual cited sources directly supporting it were not secured.)

Viral vector·CGT (AAV·cell therapy).QbD is the most difficult area.
