The most difficult step in most CGTs is defining product quality (CQA) in terms of biological activity (BPI Gene Therapy), and unlike recombinant proteins, CGT is complex and variable and highly dependent on biological starting materials and process controls (BPI Gene Therapy). As a result, most manufacturers set acceptance criteria broadly based on historical ranges rather than the true design space—for example, potency tests are initially measured as cell-based/biological assays with large inherent variations, so acceptance criteria are initially set at a broad range (BPI Gene Therapy). ⚠️ In other words, it has the format of QbD, but the actual design space is shallow, so it is easy to become ‘QbD only in appearance’.Classical vaccine. Because antigen production is empirical (egg/cell-based) and the granularity of CQA at the molecular level is low, the control strategy leans toward the traditional type focusing on process control, potency, and safety (residual DNA, etc.), and the use of the enhanced design space is limited. (This vaccine-related description is also a qualitative synthesis of this section, and direct citation sources were not secured.)

Practices and recent changes

The current standard is a dual reality, nominally adopting a strengthened approach but rarely utilizing full design space and regulatory flexibility tools. The adoption of QbD elements themselves has spread—in Japan, new drug approvals using QbD elements increased from 4% in 2009 to 71% in 2021, and reached 76% (34 out of 45) in 2023 (EC Survey). ⚠️ However, cases of actually requesting regulatory flexibility as established conditions (ECs) are still rare, with only 5 out of 85 applications including ECs in the US, 3 out of 107 EU cases, and 64 out of 191 cases in Japan (EC Survey). ⚠️

The movements of the past three years can be organized into five categories. (1) With the revision of ICH Q9(R1) (2023), there has been a trend to alleviate FMEA/RPN bias by directly addressing the ‘subjectivity’ and ‘formality’ of risk assessment and risk-based decision-making (RegDev) ⚠️, (2) With ICH Q12, EC·PACMP-based life cycle change management has become a post-QbD extension. (ValGenesis), (3) with ICH Q14, QbD was expanded as an analysis method through ATP·MODR·analysis control strategy (Premier), (4) with RTRT/PAT, the control strategy moved to ‘constant data/model-based’ (RTRT Glossary), (5) Onlineization of control strategy is underway with digital twin and MSPC. There are also clear barriers to this expansion, and in practice, the perception that ‘adoption is unnecessary because regulations do not require it’ and the inappropriate use of basic QbD terms such as design space are pointed out as signs of a fundamental gap in understanding (BPI Practice). ⚠️

Issue:There are three main points where sources diverge. First, regarding whether the “enhanced (QbD) approach is actually adopted and utilized,” one side believes that the adoption of QbD elements is widespread (76% of new drug approvals in Japan) and that regulations encourage an approach with strengthened regulations, but the other side believes that cases of actually requesting regulatory flexibility tools (EC design space) are very rare (US 5/85, EU 3/107 in applications including EC) and that it is limited to ‘QbD-lite’ due to misuse of the term and lack of training. The root of this gap is the difference in the definition of ‘adoption’—QbD language and documentation have spread, but the regulatory flexibility that should be gained in return has not been realized due to concerns about review delays and regional inconsistencies, leaving a gap between formal adoption and practical use. Second, regarding “Can criticality be reliably determined by risk assessment such as FMEA/RPN?”, the ubiquity as a standard tool (established in A-Mab, etc.) and criticism of its reliability (mathematical flaws in the RPN multiplication scale, subjectivity of the evaluator) coexist, and the regulation officially acknowledged this tension by creating a ‘subjectivity’ section in Q9(R1). Third, regarding “Is the QbD/design space paradigm valid for new modalities such as CGT?”, the applicability of the framework regardless of product modality is confronted with the reality of ‘Appearance Only QbD’, which broadly sets the acceptance criteria based on the history range instead of the true design space due to limited knowledge of CGT and weak CQA-clinical correlation. All three issues have a common root in that the premise of QbD (sufficient process understanding and measurable CQA) is fully established only in a mature product modality.Unresolved Questions: (1) The quantitative performance of each control element—for example, the actual design space coordinates or PAR/NOR values ​​of a specific product—was a product-specific and confidential matter, so generalized values ​​could not be obtained. (2) Accurate verbatim citation of the original text for the subjectivity/officiality section of ICH Q9(R1) is secured through a secondary summary instead of the primary text, so key facts are confirmed, but the citation intensity is relatively low.

7.5 Pharmaceutical Quality System (PQS)

Purpose and Role: Quality governance umbrella across all process sections

If the previous nodes deal with upstream processes, downstream processes, QC, and discharge respectively, PQS is a governance umbrella that governs quality across the individual stages vertically. The document that defines the conceptual framework is ICH Q10, which defines itself as “one comprehensive pharmaceutical quality system model” that is based on ISO quality concepts, includes applicable GMPs, and complements ICH Q8 (pharmaceutical development) and ICH Q9 (quality risk management), and is designed to be implemented across multiple stages of the product life cycle (ICH Q10). In other words, it does not replace regional GMP, but rather builds on top of it and augments each country’s GMP into a life cycle perspective model.

PQS has three goals: The first is ‘product realisation’, which provides products with quality characteristics that meet the needs of patients, medical staff, and regulatory agencies, the second is ‘state of control’, which ensures the continuous suitability and ability of the process through a system that effectively monitors and controls process performance and product quality, and the third is ‘continual improvement’ through reduction of variation, innovation, and system strengthening. Among these, the state of control is achieved through the development and operation of an effective monitoring and control system for process performance and product quality, and the literature states that quality risk management is useful in identifying what monitoring and control is necessary (ICH Q10). The scope of application is the entire life cycle from pharmaceutical development → technology transfer → commercial production → product discontinuation, and is appropriately applied in proportion to the goals of each stage.

From a flow perspective, PQS is a circulatory device that receives deviations, monitoring data, complaints, change requests, and audit notes that occur in all other sections as inputs, and passes batch release decisions, CAPA, approved changes, management review decisions, and product quality review (PQR) as outputs. Regulatoryally, an independent quality unit with authority to perform specific PQS responsibilities is required by local regulations (ICH Q10).

There is a delicate point here that must be grasped. ICH Q10 is not intended to create new expectations beyond current regulatory requirements, and therefore ICH Q10’s content beyond local GMPs states itself as ‘optional’ (ICH Q10). However, the framework elements and responsibilities are already effectively embedded in each country’s GMP (EU GMP Chapter 1, 21 CFR 211), so that the nominally optional framework operates as a de facto expectation in practice (a tension we return to in the issue below).

Operating method: Closed loop of 2 enablers + management responsibility + 4 elements

PQS operates as a closed loop involving two ‘enablers’, management responsibility, and four ‘elements’.

Two enablers — Knowledge Management and Quality Risk Management.Knowledge Management (KM) and Quality Risk Management (QRM) are designated as two enablers for effective implementation of ICH Q10. Knowledge management is a systematic approach to acquiring, analyzing, storing, and disseminating information about products, manufacturing processes, and components, and its knowledge sources include prior knowledge (public domain or internal documents), pharmaceutical development research, technology transfer activities, process verification over the life cycle, manufacturing experience, innovation, continuous improvement, and change management activities (ICH Q10). The other axis, QRM (ICH Q9), is essential for effective PQS and provides an approach to proactively identify, scientifically evaluate, and control potential risks to quality and promotes continuous improvement throughout the life cycle (ICH Q10). The reason why QRM is important is because it serves as a common engine that adjusts the ‘effort/formality level’ of all four elements below to be proportional to risk.Four elements. A PQS must include four elements—a process performance and product quality monitoring system, a corrective and preventive action (CAPA) system, a change management system, and management review of process performance and product quality (ICH Q10).

The first element, the monitoring system, establishes a control strategy using QRM, verifies the control status by measuring and analyzing CPP/CQA, facility operating conditions, in-process management, and finished product specifications, and triggers timely feedback/feedforward and corrective and preventive actions. This integrates internal and external feedback such as complaints, nonconformities, recalls, departures, audits, and regulatory points.The second element, CAPA, is the practical heart of this section. The company must have a system for implementing corrective and preventive actions resulting from complaints, product rejections, nonconformities, recalls, departures, audits, regulatory audits, and ‘investigations’ of trends in process performance and product quality monitoring, and must use a structured investigation approach aimed at identifying the root cause, and the level of effort, formality, and documentation of the investigation must be proportional to the risk to comply with ICH Q9. (ICH Q10). In practice, 5-Why, Ishikawa (Fishbone) diagram, and 8D are used as root cause analysis tools. There is a conceptually important distinction here: corrective action is taken to prevent a recurrence, and preventive action is taken to prevent an occurrence (which has not yet occurred) (ICH Q10). Measures are completed by the QA/Quality department after implementation and effectiveness check.

The most common event that actually triggers this CAPA loop is deviation. Deviation operation follows the flow of discovery → documentation → risk classification (minor/major/critical) → investigation → RCA → CAPA derivation → effectiveness confirmation → closure. Significant deviations from EU GMP must be fully recorded and investigated to determine the root cause (Part I Chapter 1), and a documented CAPA system is required to address deviations, complaints, audit notes, and quality defects (Pharmaceutical Microbiology). ⚠️ QA also identifies deviations in the process of reviewing completed batch records, and repeated incidents may not be revealed until across multiple batches. Release is only determined after an independent quality department reviews the batch records and confirms control status and closure of the deviation (in the EU, QP certifies according to Annex 16).

The third element, change management, manages the points where innovation, continuous improvement, monitoring, and CAPA calculations cause changes. The change management system must ensure that continuous improvement occurs in a timely and effective manner, while providing a high level of assurance that changes do not have unintended consequences (ICH Q10). There is an interesting boundary between the regulatory and quality system perspectives. As ICH Q8 states, working within the design space is not considered a change (from a regulatory filing perspective), but from a pharmaceutical quality system perspective, all changes must be evaluated by the company’s change management system (ICH Q10). In other words, the change is evaluated using QRM (effort and formality are proportional to risk), the filing impact is judged based on permission and design space, and goal achievement and harmlessness are confirmed after implementation.

The fourth element, management review, ensures that process performance and product quality are managed throughout the life cycle. Depending on the size and complexity of the company, review of multiple management layers is continuous and should include a process for timely conveying and escalating appropriate quality issues to upper management (ICH Q10). Inputs include regulatory audit results, periodic quality review (PQR), CAPA and effectiveness of changes, and follow-up actions from previous reviews. Outputs include process and product improvement, resource reallocation and training, and knowledge capture and dissemination.

Data Integrity Layer.All records of the above four elements must be trustworthy, and the data integrity layer supports this reliability. The MHRA guidance views data as a record that allows complete reconstruction and evaluation of GxP activities, and stipulates that it must meet ALCOA — Attributable, Legible, Contemporaneous, Original, and Accurate (MHRA GxP DI). Here, the audit trail is metadata that allows the history of recorded events to be reconstructed regardless of the medium, and must contain the ‘who, what, when and why’ of the action (MHRA GxP DI). Crucially, when a data integrity weakness is discovered, the company must implement appropriate corrective and preventive actions across all related activities and systems, not just one issue (MHRA GxP DI). This statement nails the point that data integrity is not a separate area but is integrated into the CAPA loop of PQS.

Key parameters and assets: systems, documents, and indicators, not physical quantities

The core assets of PQS are not physical parameters but ‘systems, documents, and indicators.’ Key documents and systems include the quality manual (describing quality policy, PQS scope, process map, and management responsibility), quality policy, quality goals, SOP system, batch manufacturing record (BMR), departure system, CAPA system, change management system, product quality review (PQR/APR), and electronic QMS (eQMS) that unites them.Whether this system is working well is monitored using performance indicators (KPI). In practice, management should have a formal process to periodically review the PQS, and that review should include the evaluation of performance indicators that monitor the effectiveness of the PQS internal processes—typically complaints, attrition, CAPA, and change management processes (ICH Q10). In practice, the number of processed cases and on-time closure of each process, repetition rate, recall, self-evaluation (risk assessment, trending, internal audit), regulatory audit indications, and outsourcing activity feedback are added to this and serve as input for management review.

QRM formality spectrum.It is the formality spectrum of QRM that determines the depth of deviation investigation, formality of change evaluation, and intensity of data review. It is a continuous spectrum in which the higher the risk, uncertainty, importance, and complexity, the more formal the approach, and the lower the risk, the less formal the approach. This is a concept formalized by ICH Q9(R1) (see Practice Trends below).ALCOA+ required attributes. In addition to the previous ALCOA five attributes, the specific requirements of data integrity are expanded to the so-called ‘+’ attribute, which requires that data governance must ensure that data is Complete, Consistent, Enduring, and Available throughout its life cycle (MHRA GxP DI). The effort and resources invested in this control must be proportional to the risk and impact of data integrity failure on patients or the environment, and the sum of these measures constitutes the concept of ‘data governance’ (MHRA GxP DI). So which data is more important? The criticality of data is determined by considering what decision the data is used for, that is, how it affects the decision (MHRA GxP DI). The standards for originality are also clear. Raw data is defined as the original record that is the first-capture of information, whether paper or electronic. Data created in a dynamic state must remain in that state, so a copy printed on paper cannot be considered raw data (MHRA GxP DI).

If you organize all of this from input → output, PQS receives deviations, complaints, nonconformities, OOS, monitoring / process data, change requests, audit notes, and regulatory findings, and calculates batch release / hold decisions, CAPA (and effectiveness results), approved / rejected changes, management review results / follow-up actions, PQR reports, and regulatory commitments. Although the scope of operations is a mix of company-wide and site-specific, PQS effectiveness is typically proven through regulatory audits at the site level.

Quality and regulation: Control the ‘system’, not the ‘product’

The key to understanding the quality perspective of PQS is that the CQA of the output is not product-specific CQA, but maintenance of a ‘state of control’ and batch release decisions based thereon. Review of batch records, closure of deviations, CAPA effectiveness, and verification of harmlessness of changes are prerequisites for release. In other words, PQS controls the system, not the product. The substance of control is whether deviations are investigated and closed in a timely manner, CAPA eliminates the root cause, changes do not affect filing without approval, records meet ALCOA+, and audit trails are turned on and reviewed on a risk-based basis.

The high-level literature governing this overlaps in layers. At the ICH level, there are Q10 (PQS model), Q9 (R1) (QRM), Q8 (pharmaceutical development/design space), Q7 (GMP for raw drug substances), and Q12 (life cycle management/post-approval change). At the EU level, EudraLex Vol.4 is subdivided into Part I Chapter 1 (PQS and PQR are defined in §1.10), Chapter 4 (documents), Chapter 7 (outsourcing), Chapter 8 (complaints/quality defects/recalls), Annex 11 (computerized system), Annex 15 (verification/qualification), and Annex 16 (QP certification/release). At the FDA level, there are 21 CFR Part 210/211 (cGMP), 21 CFR Part 11 (electronic records and electronic signatures), Q10 industry guidance, 2018 Data Integrity Q&A, and CDER’s Quality Management Maturity (QMM) program. For data integrity, there are MHRA’s GxP Data Integrity Guidance (2018, Rev.1), PIC/S PI 041, and WHO Annex 4/5, and the ISO 9000 series is the source of definitions for terms such as continual improvement and corrective action.

In practice, data reviews are designed proportionally to risk. Routine data reviews should include a review of the documented audit trail if so determined by the risk assessment — conversely, it is not necessary to exhaustively review all system activity (MHRA GxP DI). Where to draw the appropriate line for this risk-based approach is the source of differences in interpretation that will be discussed in later issues. For reference, data integrity violations are considered one of the most common causes of FDA warning letters and EU GMP nonconformities.

Variations by product type: Same structure, different emphasis and pressure

PQS is essentially a product type-agnostic governance framework, so the ‘structure’ of the four major elements and two enablers is the same for any product type. The difference comes not from the collapse or reversal of elements, but from ‘emphasis and pressure.’ However, it should be noted that the description by product type in this section is based on practical observations rather than primary regulatory documents, so the strength of evidence is relatively low (Node itself marks this as an unresolved area). ⚠️

mAb/recombinant protein.This is the most mature region. Platform knowledge (KM) is abundant, CPP/CQA is well established, change management is predictable, and statistical trending and PQR are robust thanks to large-scale deployment. It is the de facto reference standard for PQS operations. ⚠️

mRNA-LNP.As a new and high-speed platform, frequent changes such as mutant strain updates place a burden on change control. Because the deployment history is short, trending data is shallow, and it relies on platform knowledge-based justification (KM). Exit definitions for new CQAs such as capping and RNA integrity are still evolving. ⚠️Viral vectors/CGT (especially autologous). The elements themselves are maintained, but operation is the most pressing. Since the batch is an individual patient, ‘chain of identity (COI)/chain of custody (COC)’ tracking is expanded to the scope of data integrity, and statistical trending is difficult due to the small volume and single batch. Since deviation directly leads to the delay or inability of patient administration, deviation management, CAPA, and release decisions are made under extreme time pressure (sometimes in real time), and the frequency of deviation is high due to the volatility of donors and raw materials. The time window between release determination and administration is fundamentally different for mAbs. ⚠️

Classic vaccine.Although the characteristics of deviation are different due to biological variability and egg/cell matrix-based processes, PQS itself is mature thanks to its long history. Taken together, elements do not ‘disappear’ in any product type, but in CGT, small batches undermine statistical-based monitoring (element 1) and time pressures force the departure/release process itself to be redesigned. ⚠️

Practices and recent changes

The current standard integrates deviation, CAPA, change, documentation, training, and complaints based on the documented eQMS, and tops it with ALCOA+ data governance. Movements in four directions are noticeable over the past three years.

First, the enforcement of ICH Q9(R1). This revision was adopted on January 18, 2023 and revised to address product availability (supply) risks due to manufacturing quality issues, lack of understanding of what ‘formality’ means in QRM work, uncertainty in risk-based decision-making, and high subjectivity in risk assessment/QRM calculations (PharmTech). ⚠️ In particular, it states that everyone participating in QRM must recognize, anticipate, and address the potential for subjectivity (ICH Q9(R1). ⚠️ This revision directly affects the ‘effort proportionality’ judgment across PQS.

Second, FDA CDER’s Quality Management Maturity (QMM) program. This is a voluntary program that goes beyond pass/fail compliance to evaluate and grade ‘maturity’ such as management commitment, data governance, and continuous improvement. In 2024, 9 business sites were evaluated using a prototype protocol and a detailed report containing strengths and areas for improvement was provided to participants (FDA CDER). ⚠️ It is a catalyst that moves PQS from ‘minimum compliance’ to ‘maturity competition’.

Third, the digital modernization of EU GMP. On July 7, 2025, the European Commission simultaneously released the revised draft Annex 11 (Computerized Systems), the newly added Annex 22 (Artificial Intelligence), and the revised Chapter 4 (Documents), and comments on the three drafts were received until October 7, 2025 (GMP Compliance). Annex 11 introduced cybersecurity as a core GMP requirement for the first time with a significant increase in volume, and the accompanying Annex 22 limits AI to static and deterministic models in GMP critical applications and excludes dynamic models, generative AI, and large-scale language models (LLM) from critical uses (GMP Compliance). ⚠️ Data integrity principles are being extended to the AI ​​and cloud era.

Fourth, the continued regulatory focus on data integrity. The ALCOA++ notation emphasizing ‘Traceable’ is spreading, and risk-based standardization of PIC/S PI 041 and audit trail review is taking root.

Limitations and unresolved issues.Although knowledge management (KM) is an enabler of ICH Q10, it remains the area with the lowest level of maturity due to unclear implementation methods, and it is still difficult to prove CAPA ‘effectiveness’ and eradicate repeated deviations. The burden of audit trail reviews, data integrity gaps in hybrid/legacy systems, real-time release pressures in low-volume/personalized product types (CGT), and QRM subjectivity that is acknowledged but impossible to eliminate remain as unresolved challenges.

Looking at the tools and players landscape, on the standard/regulatory body side there are ICH (Q10·Q9(R1)·Q8·Q7·Q12), FDA (21 CFR 210/211/Part 11·CDER QMM·2018 DI Q&A), EMA/European Commission’s EU GMP, MHRA, PIC/S (PI 041), and WHO, and as industry reference groups There are ISPE, PDA, and ASQ. Representative eQMS/QMS software include Veeva Vault QMS, MasterControl, Honeywell Sparta TrackWise, ETQ Reliance, Dot Compliance, Scilife, and AmpleLogic.

Issue:There are four points where the sources diverge. The first is the ‘optional versus de facto mandatory’ aspect of ICH Q10. Q10 specifies that exceeding local GMPs is optional (ICH Q10), but the FDA QMM program and each country’s GMPs (EU Chapter 1, 21 CFR 211) effectively require those elements and responsibilities, so the voluntary framework operates as a de facto expectation. The second is ALCOA vs. ALCOA+ vs. ALCOA++. MHRA states that there is no difference in expectations regardless of which abbreviation is used (MHRA GxP DI), but the industry is broadening the notation to ALCOA++ with ‘Traceable’ added, and there is disagreement as to whether this is semantic inflation without substantive change or a new requirement. Third is the scope of audit trail review. MHRA allows risk-based and exception reporting, saying it is not necessary to review all system activities (MHRA GxP DI), but in practice, due to the subjectivity of risk assessment, there is a large difference in interpretation between regulators and companies regarding the appropriate range and frequency of review. Fourth is QRM subjectivity. ICH Q9(R1) requires that subjectivity be recognized, predicted, and resolved (ICH Q9(R1)) ⚠️ but acknowledges that it cannot be eliminated, so there remains a difference of perspective on the extent to which limitations can be alleviated through formality and quantification.Unresolved Questions: - As for the specific implementation methodology of knowledge management (KM), ICH Q10 only presents principles and the practical standards are immature, so detailed identification is limited. - Differences in PQS operation by product type (CGT real-time emissions, etc.) rely on practical observations rather than primary regulatory documents, so the strength of evidence is relatively low.

7.6 Batch disposition & QP certification/release

What this gate does — the final gate that targets quality on the ‘release decision’ rather than the product

Even after all previous processes and testing are completed, the batch cannot yet be released to the market. Batch disposition and QP certification/release are ‘terminal quality acts’ that must be passed before a manufactured/tested batch can be released for sale or use, and are crucially different from other nodes in this report. What is controlled here is not the physical or chemical test values ​​of the product, but the ‘qualification of the release decision itself.’ In other words, the execution batch record (BMR/BPR), test report (CoA) and QC data, in-process control (IPC) results, environmental monitoring, and departure/CAPA/change management/OOS investigation results concluded at the PQS node (7.5) are received as input, and an independent quality department (US quality unit) or Qualified Person (EU) reviews them. It is the gateway to making a go/no-go decision on whether release is possible.

The key to understanding this gateway is to distinguish between two ‘releases’. One is ‘quality release’ where QP certifies that the batch complies with GMP and Marketing Authorization (MA), and the other is ‘physical release’ where the batch is transferred to actual sales inventory. EU GMP Annex 16 defines batch release as three steps: (i) checking manufacturing and testing according to defined release procedures, (ii) finished product batch certification by QP indicating that the batch complies with GMP and MA (this is quality release), and (iii) transferring and exporting to sales inventory reflecting the certification (Source). The responsibility structure is also clear. The ultimate responsibility for the safety, quality and effectiveness of the product throughout its life lies with the Marketing Authorization Holder (MAH), but the QP is responsible for ensuring that ‘individual batches’ have been manufactured and tested in accordance with the laws, MA and GMP of the member state where certification takes place (Source). In response, the United States requires quality departments to review and approve all production and control records in accordance with 21 CFR 211.192 before batches can be released or distributed (Source).

How it works

(1) Review of batch records—Starting point for disposition.Disposal begins with a systematic review of completed batch records. US regulations stipulate this. 21 CFR 211.192 requires that all pharmaceutical production and control records, including packaging and labeling, be reviewed and approved by the quality department prior to batch release or distribution, and that unexplained deviations (including maximum or minimum exceedance of theoretical yield) or non-compliance with batch or component specifications must be thoroughly investigated even if they have already been distributed (Source). In addition, 21 CFR 211.165 requires appropriate laboratory determination of conformance to final specifications, including the identity and content of each active ingredient, for each batch prior to release (Source). As a result of this review, the disposition is divided into approved, quarantined (quarantine/hold), rework/reclaim, and rejected. Importantly, the initial OOS result does not immediately mean that the batch is rejected (Source). ⚠️

(2) Stage 2 release in the EU and personally guaranteed by the QP.In the EU, prior to certification, a QP must ‘personally’ fulfill three operational responsibilities — ensuring that the certification is acceptable under the Manufacturing/Import Authorization (MIA), complying with additional obligations under national law, and recording the certification in a register or equivalent document (Source). In addition, the QP is responsible for ensuring that items 1.7.1 to 1.7.21 of Annex 16 are secured. However, these tasks can be delegated to appropriately trained personnel or third parties, and the QP must rely on the Pharmaceutical Quality System (PQS), but must continuously check whether the reliance is ‘sufficiently based’ (Source). 1.7 Two items from the list are particularly important as they substantially determine the release determination. Finished QC test data must conform to finished product specifications listed in the MA or, if approved, a real-time release testing (RTRT) program (1.7.13) — Annex 16 accepts RTRT as an explicit alternative to specification testing (Source) — All OOS/OOT investigations associated with the batch to be certified must be completed to a sufficient level to support certification. Do (1.7.16) (Source). Until certification is completed, the batch cannot be released into the market and must either remain at the manufacturing site or be transported under quarantine to another site approved for that purpose by the competent authority (Source). The certification must be recorded in a register or equivalent document to show that each production batch complies with Directive 2001/83/EC Article 51, and the records must be kept for at least 5 years (1.10.1) (Source).(3) Certification vs. confirmation — Dividing responsibilities across multiple sites. When a supply chain spans multiple sites, terminology becomes important in practice. ‘Certification’, as defined in Article 51, refers to the quality release of a finished batch that a QP leaves in the register, and ‘confirmation’ is a signed statement by the QP that certain processes and tests have been performed in accordance with GMP and the relevant MA, product specification file, and technical agreement, and the QP providing the confirmation is responsible for its activities (Source). The sharing of batch conformance responsibility among multiple QPs should be defined in a document formally agreed upon by all parties, which should specify responsibility for assessing the impact of deviations on the GMP/MA conformance of the batch (1.4.3) (Source).(4) OOS/OOT Resolution — Prerequisite for Release. Since release is only accomplished when all deviations/out-of-specification investigations for a batch have been closed, the Out-of-Spec (OOS) investigation methodology is a key subprocess of this gateway. The FDA defines OOS as any test result that falls outside the specifications and standards set by the new drug approval, DMF, compendium, or manufacturer (Source). ⚠️ FDA OOS guidance specifies a two-stage investigation. In Phase I (laboratory investigation), the laboratory supervisor reviews the raw data, calculations, instruments, and standards to invalidate the initial OOS results only when an ‘obvious laboratory error’ is identified. If a laboratory error is ruled out, the investigation is expanded to include manufacturing, processes, and documents led by the quality department in Phase II (full investigation). At this time, it is inappropriate to ‘average’ the original OOS results with the subsequent retest/resampling passing results because it conceals the variation between individual results (Source). ⚠️ Furthermore, if the entire Phase II investigation determines that there were no laboratory or calculation errors in the initial test, there is no scientific basis to invalidate the initial OOS with a passing retest result, and all passing and questionable results must be reported and reflected in the batch release decision (Source). ⚠️ Retesting is permitted only when justified, on the same homogeneous sample, and this documented investigation must be completed to conclusion and follow-up prior to batch disposition in accordance with 21 CFR 211.192 (Source).(5) Certification of batches with unexpected deviations (Annex 16 §3). Release does not always require perfect compliance. As long as the registration specifications for raw materials, excipients, packaging materials, and finished products are met, QP can consider certification even for batches that have experienced ‘unexpected deviations’ in the manufacturing process or analysis method that differ from the details of MA/GMP. However, the deviation must be thoroughly investigated and the root cause corrected, and submission of a MA variation may be required for continued manufacturing (Source). For this certification to be established, a quality risk management (QRM) impact assessment must conclude that the impact on the quality, safety, and effectiveness of the batch is ‘negligible’ (Source).

Key parameters·materials·governance

The ‘inputs’ reviewed for release determination include running batch records (BMR/BPR), CoA/finished product QC data, IPC results, environmental/microbiological monitoring, departure/CAPA/OOS/OOT investigation records and closure status, change management status, ongoing stability data, label/packaging adjustments, technology/quality agreements, and third-party audit reports. The ‘output’ is the disposition decision, QP certification (quality release), certification register record (Article 51, kept for at least 5 years) ([Source] (https://health.ec.europa.eu/document/download/20c41532-33d5-4635-ae80-8735d3d09fe0_en?filename=v4_an16_201510_en.pdf)), and batch certificate (Annex 16 Appendix II: product name, strength, dosage form, packaging, batch number, country of destination, certification statement, QP name, signature, date). Disposal status is customarily categorized as approved/quarantine/hold/rework or recyclable/rejected (Source)。 ⚠️

The two axes of governance are the EU’s Qualified Person (a person who meets the qualifications and experience requirements of Directive 2001/83/EC Art.48·49 and is specified in the MIA) and the US’ Independent Quality Department (the authority to approve/reject placements granted by 21 CFR 211.22). Internationally, the PIC/S system has an ‘Authorised Person’ corresponding to the QP. PIC/S ported Annex 16 to the ‘Authorised Person’ term for international harmonization of batch release requirements, and specifies that the two are ‘strictly equivalent’ (Source). In terms of tools, disposal is supported by certification registers, eQMS and electronic batch records (EBR)/MES, and these computerized systems must comply with EU GMP Annex 11.

Quality and regulatory framework

The ‘quality attribute’ (CQA) of this gateway is not a product-specific test value, but rather a qualification of emission determination. In other words, release is established only when the QP/quality department confirms questions such as whether the batch was manufactured and inspected according to MA/GMP, whether all OOS/OOT investigations have been sufficiently completed (1.7.16), and whether the QC data meets the finished product specifications or approved RTRT (1.7.13) (Source).Governing regulations are divided by region. In the EU, EU GMP Annex 16 (‘Certification by a Qualified Person and Batch Release’) and Directive 2001/83/EC Article 51 (Batch Certification) and Article 48/49 (QP Qualification) are key, and are supported by several chapters of EU GMP Part I dealing with PQS, documentation, QC, complaints/recalls, and outsourcing (Source). In the United States, 21 CFR 211.192 (Production record review and deviation investigation) (Source), 211.165 (Laboratory determination of compliance with specifications before release) (Source), 211.22 (Quality department authority), and FDA’s ‘Investigating Out-of-Specification (OOS) Test Results’ The guidance governs the release and the investigation upon which it is based (Source). ⚠️ The key point here is that rather than conducting new tests at this gateway, it controls whether ‘all emission tests were performed and were within specifications’ and ‘whether the investigation was closed’, and data integrity (ALCOA+, see 7.5) underpins the reliability of this decision.

Variations by product type

The ‘structure’ of the emission gateway (review → certification/disposal → transfer) remains regardless of product type, but ‘time window, statistical basis, and nature of investigation’ vary greatly. mAb/recombinant protein corresponds to the reference standard. It is a large batch and there is time to complete all QC panels before release, and thanks to mature standards and platform knowledge, QP certification follows a predictable sequential flow of ‘quarantine → test → certification’. Although release test items for mRNA-LNP are evolving with new CQAs such as capping, RNA integrity, and encapsulation efficiency, they still undergo batch certification before release.

The biggest departure is from viral vector and cell gene therapy (CGT) products, especially autologous products. Due to the short shelf life and patient specificity, release may be permitted in certain circumstances even before full QC results are obtained if robust risk management is in place, and in autologous treatments, etc., even ‘use/release of OOS batches under strictly defined conditions’ may be considered due to the absence of alternative batches and imminent patient benefit (Source). ⚠️ In this case, because the batch is an individual patient, chain of identity/custody verification becomes part of the release, and because it is a small quantity and a single batch, the statistical basis for disposition is weak, so insufficient release control is compensated for through strengthened process verification. Variations in the opposite direction appear in classical vaccines/immunological/blood products. In addition to QP certification, there can be a double layer of ‘National Control Agency Batch Release (OCABR)’ where an independent national testing laboratory (OMCL) releases batches separately. Although Annex 16 itself does not cover OCABR, Annex 16 still applies to QP certification and release of the batch.

Practices and recent changes

The current standard is review of batch records centered on eQMS and electronic batch records, and QP certification (EU)/quality department disposition (US), and is premised on quarantine, termination of withdrawal, and completion of OOS investigation before certification. The movements of the past three years can be organized into five categories. First, the spread of real-time emission testing (RTRT) and parametric emission. As Annex 16 1.7.13 explicitly accepts the approved RTRT program as an alternative to finished product specification testing, the paradigm of releasing in-process data without testing or withholding finished products in combination with PAT and continuous manufacturing is spreading (Source). Second, it is a digital batch release based on ‘review by exception’. In EBR software, a proven process of reviewing only the deviation data that occurred in a batch and releasing it when all exceptions are handled has become a popular trend, and when combined with electronic signatures, it shortens the disposal cycle time (Source). ⚠️ Third, remote QP authentication. As the COVID-19 temporary permit moves into permanent discussion, remote authentication will only be permitted if accepted by national regulators, authentication actions must in all cases be substantiated by technical means within the EU/EEA (or Northern Ireland), and the IT systems used must comply with Annex 11 (Source). ⚠️ Fourth, international harmonization through Annex 16 porting of PIC/S (Source). Fifth, there is a trend of regulatory elaboration of conditional and OOS release frameworks in short validity period and self-derived CGT (Source). ⚠️ Meanwhile, the limitations are also clear. The quality and timeliness of OOS investigations still remain one of the most criticized areas in FDA 483s and warning letters, and the practice of attempting to offset or unfairly invalidate OOS through passing retests has been repeatedly pointed out (Source). ⚠️

Issue:The sources reveal tensions between norms and practices, or principles and exceptions, at several points. (1) Averaging/invalidation of OOS: FDA guidance prohibits averaging of OOS with passing retests and strictly limits invalidation to ‘obvious laboratory errors,’ but in the field, pressure to offset/invalidate OOS with retests is repeated, resulting in ongoing conflict between norms and practices. (2) Certification of unexpected deviations (Annex 16 §3): If the registration specifications are met and the impact is ‘negligible’, the QP is allowed to certify even if there are deviations from the MA/GMP details. This is in tension with the basic premise that ‘the batch must fully comply with the MA’, and the judgment of ‘negligible’ is subjective. (3) Conditional/OOS release of ATMP/CGT: In a short shelf life/autologous single patient batch, release or OOS release before securing full QC results may be permitted on the grounds of ‘urgent patient benefit and non-substitutability’, but this fundamentally conflicts with the strict first test and later release principle of mAb. (4) Allowable scope of remote QP certification: Although the conditions of performance within the EU/EEA, compliance with Annex 11, and PQS control are shared, the acceptance and additional requirements for full-time remote certification differ by country, resulting in significant differences in interpretation. (5) QP personal responsibility vs. delegation/reliance on PQS: Annex 16 allows the QP to ‘personally’ guarantee certification, but also allows for delegation of 1.7 tasks and reliance on PQS/third-party audits, making the boundaries between the scope of QP’s actual control and legal responsibility ambiguous in a multi-item, multi-site environment.Unresolved questions: (1) Detailed operation by product type (especially conditional/OOS release of CGT, double layer of vaccine OCABR) relies on secondary summaries and industry literature rather than primary regulatory text, so the strength of evidence is relatively weak. (2) The legal scope and punishment of QP individuals and the different acceptance conditions for remote authentication by country were confirmed only at the normative text level, and precedents and case data were not in-depth in this scope.

8. Regulatory affairs & inspection

How the manufacturing and quality story is documented, submitted, inspected, and maintained across the product lifecycle.

8.1 CMC documentation & regulatory submissions

Purpose and Role — Translating “made” into “regulatory commitments”

8.1 is the first gateway to regulatory affairs and inspection (Part 8), and is an activity that assembles the output of the entire manufacturing and quality process into one CMC package that the regulatory agency can review, approve, and maintain. The release specifications for purified bulk drug substances (3.3), container closure system·CCIT (4.3), stability·shelf life·comparability (6.5), process verification PPQ data (7.3), and CQA·CPP·design space·control strategy (7.4) are reorganized here into the standard table of contents of Module 3 (Quality) of the Common Technical Document (CTD). The key role of this clause is to translate “technically made (manufacturing, testing, verification)” into “regulatory promise (approved conditions, specifications)” to give it legal binding force, and the completed dossier is soon subject to review against the field during the GMP inspection/pre-approval inspection (PAI) in 8.2.

In particular, biopharmaceuticals have different regulatory principles from synthetic drugs. Unlike synthetic drugs that prove safety and effectiveness under the U.S. FD&C Act, biopharmaceuticals must prove safety, purity, and potency in accordance with §351 of the U.S. Public Health Service Act (PHS Act) (Source). This difference explains why items such as cell bank characterization, viral clearance, and titer assays take center stage in the dossier.

How it works — Structure of CTD Module 3

Submission documents are organized into five modules of the CTD, with Quality/CMC contained in Module 3 and Module 2.3 (Quality Overall Summary, QOS) as its summary. The main text of Module 3 (3.2) is divided into four axes. First, 3.2.S, which deals with drug substances, is S.1 General information (name, structure, characteristics) → S.2 Manufacturing (manufacturer, process, material management, verification) → S.3 Characterization (impurities, structure identification) → S.4 Drug substance management (specifications, analysis methods, verification) → S.5 Standard products → S.6 Container closure system → S.7 Stability. It continues (Source). Second, 3.2.P, which deals with the finished drug product, leads to P.1 base material/composition → P.2 formulation development → P.3 manufacturing → P.4 excipient management → P.5 finished product management → P.6 standard product → P.7 container closure system → P.8 stability (Source). Third and fourth, there are 3.2.A Appendix and 3.2.R Regional Information, and the appendix contains facility information, foreign agent safety evaluation, new excipients, etc. depending on the product (Source). The appendix is ​​further subdivided into cross-sectional information on facilities and equipment (3.2.A.1), foreign agent safety (3.2.A.2), and new excipients (3.2.A.3) (Source) ⚠️.

This table of contents is not just a format, but a traceability structure designed to allow the examiner to trace “what and how it is made, how it is managed, and how stability supports shelf life” as a control strategy in the manufacturing process. Upstream nodes result in specific sections of this table of contents — Process Verification (7.3) in 3.2.S.2.5 and 3.2.P.3.5, Control Strategy and Design Space (7.4) in 3.2.S.2.6 and 3.2.P.2, and Release Specification (6.1 and 3.3) in 3.2.S.4 and 3.2.P.5. Container closure (4.3) is incorporated into 3.2.S.6·3.2.P.7, stability (6.5) is incorporated into 3.2.S.7·3.2.P.8, and safety against foreign agents (6.3·3.2) is incorporated into 3.2.A.2.

Step-by-step CMC — from IND to BLA/MAA

The detail of the submitted CMC is proportional to the clinical stage. The principle of the FDA guidance is that “the level of CMC information submitted must be appropriate for the clinical trial stage,” so early-stage submissions do not have to be complete, but subject safety must be guaranteed at any stage (Source). In a Phase 1 IND, you only need to provide enough detail to ensure that the product can be safely administered to humans, even if some verification data is preliminary (Source) ⚠️. This early-stage biopharmaceutical CMC focuses on the core of safety, including cell bank characterization, adventitious agent testing, titer assays, and viral clearance studies (Source) ⚠️.

When development progresses and reaches Phase 3, the commercial process is locked, process verification begins, and the shelf life is supported with a stability program (Source) ⚠️. This complete version is contained in a BLA, which follows the eCTD structure and contains raw drug product characterization/specification, manufacturing process technology/verification, master/working cell bank (MCB/WCB) characterization, viral clearance verification, standard product, container closure qualification, and real-time/accelerated stability data in Module 3 (Quality/CMC) (Source). In the EU, the same CTD is submitted as MAA. To proactively align CMC expectations during this process, a Type B pre-BLA meeting is strongly recommended 6-12 months prior to the planned submission (Source) ⚠️.

Post-approval change management — Tools for ICH Q12

The dossier does not end with approval. The internationally harmonized framework that manages CMC changes after approval is ICH Q12, and its core concept is Established Conditions (EC). EC is defined as “legally binding information necessary to ensure product quality” (Source). Changes to established conditions trigger regulatory submissions, while changes to non-EC elements are managed within the manufacturer’s internal quality system (PQS, ICH Q10) without regulatory submissions (Source) ⚠️. Therefore, how narrowly the EC set is defined determines the burden of future changes — applicants with enhanced product and process knowledge, such as the ICH Q8·Q11 design space, can justify a smaller EC set by scientifically demonstrating that a wider parameter range does not affect quality (Source) ⚠️

Q12 increases predictability with two auxiliary documents. The Post Change Management Protocol (PACMP) is a regulatory tool that transparently presents regulatory reporting categories and expectations for specific post-change changes in advance, and must be approved by regulatory authorities (Source). The Product Life Cycle Management (PLCM) document is a central repository of established conditions and their reporting categories, together with control strategy summary, PACMP, and post-CMC commitments (Source). The scale of the problem that Q12 is trying to solve can be guessed from the current situation; even a simple post-CMC change can take more than 5 years for global regulatory approval (Source). Meanwhile, post-approval manufacturing changes in biopharmaceuticals themselves require a comparability assessment according to ICH Q5E (Source), and the results of this assessment serve as the basis for regulatory justification of the change (connected to node 6.5).

Key deliverables and regulatory framework

The “materials” in this section are not reagents but document/data packages — the eCTD submission, Module 2.3 QOS, Module 3.2.S/3.2.P/3.2.A/3.2.R, EC lists and reporting categories, PACMP, PLCM documents, and the Q5E comparability protocol/report. Rather than performing testing directly, this section documents the CQA, specifications, verification, stability, and control strategies confirmed upstream in the dossier and makes regulatory commitments. Therefore, the subject of judgment is not the individual batch, but the completeness, consistency, and traceability of the submitted document, and after approval, whether or not EC changes are appropriately reported. Due to the characteristic of biopharmaceuticals that “the process defines the product,” characterization must be comprehensive, and the importance of foreign agent safety assessment (3.2.A.2) and equipment appendix (3.2.A.1), which deal with the introduction of viruses and mycoplasma from human-, bovine-, and porcine-derived materials and animal-derived reagents, is particularly large (Source) ⚠️.

Differences by product type

The frame of CTD/CMC itself is common regardless of product modality, but it differs greatly in the definition of the boundary between drug substance (DS) and finished drug product (DP), document incorporation of starting materials, and proportion of extraneous agent appendices. Monoclonal antibodies and recombinant proteins are the standard framework to which CTD Module 3 is most directly applied. The boundary between DS (purified bulk protein) and DP (preparation/filling) is clear, so 3.2.S and 3.2.P correspond well. For mRNA-LNP, DS is defined as the mRNA stock solution and DP as the LNP preparation, but the number of process steps is small and the dossier is relatively concise. However, the focus is on justifying the specifications of new CQAs such as capping efficiency, RNA integrity, and encapsulation efficiency.

The most difficult ones are viral vector and cell gene therapy. Typically, when CAR-T cells are manufactured in a continuous process without a clear distinction between DS and DP, the applicant must separately provide an explanation supporting the DS/DP distinction in the summary information of CTD Module 2 (Source) ⚠️. Reflecting this trend, the revised guideline, ICH M4Q(R2), aims to provide guidance on the location of information for multi-ingredient and complex products such as antibody-drug conjugates (ADCs), vaccines, and ATMPs (cell, gene therapy, and tissue engineering products) (Source).

Practice status and recent trends

The current standard practice is to organize the CMC with CTD Module 3 in the eCTD format, submit a stage-appropriate IND/CTA at clinical entry, a completed BLA/MAA at the time of marketing approval, and add the ICH Q12 tool on top of regional change management after approval. However, there are large regional differences in the actual implementation of Q12. The current ICH Q12 guidelines are not completely consistent with existing legal systems in some regions, such as the EU variation guidance and the FDA’s CMC change guidance, so regulation revision may be necessary for implementation (Source) ⚠️. As a result, as of December 2024, only 3 countries have fully implemented Q12, 9 countries are preparing, and 4 countries have not started (Source) ⚠️. Actual utilization is also low, with established conditions included in new drug applications in only 5 out of 85 cases in the US, 3 out of 107 cases in the EU, and 64 out of 191 cases in Japan (Source) ⚠️.Another big trend is the digitization of CTD quality modules. ICH M4Q(R2) supports digitization by converting quality information from text-centric descriptions into consistent, reusable modular components and is designed to be compatible with eCTD v4.0 (Source). This is a direction that clearly separates core quality information (Module 2.3) and auxiliary content (Module 3) and minimizes duplication by introducing a flexible modular structure in Module 3 (Source). However, M4Q(R2) is in the draft stage, scheduled to be signed in May 2025 and final adopted in June 2027 (Source), and its effective date is still ahead.

Issue

The evidence is conflicting on three points. First, does Q12 actually reduce the burden of change?One side believes that reporting categories harmonized with EC, PACMP, and PLCM reduce the regulatory burden when supported by knowledge, but the other side believes that the current Q12 is not fully consistent with existing regional legal systems, so there are only three countries that have fully implemented it, and cases of actually including EC in applications are extremely rare (US 5/85, EU 3/107), so it is divided into “formal adoption” and “substantial adoption.” It points out the gap between “utilization” (Source). Second, does the structured transition in M4Q(R2) mean an improvement? The design advantages (reusable structured data, reduction of duplication) are clear, but the adoption of eCTD v4.0 itself, which is the premise of it, is delayed, so it is unclear when the improvement effect will be realized. Third, how much CMC should be paid for the initial IND. According to the phase-appropriate principle, only the minimum safety key information should be submitted, but in practice, there is a tendency to avoid risk by submitting more than necessary to avoid clinical hold, and the optimal point between oversubmission (waste of resources and burden of early confirmation) and undersubmission (risk of hold) varies depending on the product, product modality, and institutional practices.

Unresolved Questions

  • ICH Q12·M4Q(R2)·M4Q original text (FDA/ICH PDF) was binary and failed to secure direct citation. Phrases related to EC definition, PACMP, PLCM, and structured data were replaced with citations from ISPE (authoritative secondary data) and consulting blogs (cloudtheapp, assyro, Boyds), ​​and verbatim comparison of the original text was not performed.
  • The precise mapping of the EU MAA’s unique procedure details (centralized/mutual recognition procedure, Type IA/IB/II classification of Variation Regulation EC 1234/2008) and the US supplement (PAS/CBE-30/CBE-0/AR) was secured only at an overview level.
  • CTD incorporation details by product type (mRNA’s IVT template, CGT’s vector/cell starting material where in 3.2.S, ATMP’s DS/DP definition) were secured only at the snippet/secondary level, except for the DS/DP continuous process issue of CAR-T (overlapping with node 9.x).

8.2 GMP inspections & inspection readiness

Role and Scope: The final regulatory gateway between documents and the field

If the preceding 8.1 submits a regulatory promise in writing to the CMC dossier, saying, “This is how we make and test it,” and the Pharmaceutical Quality System (PQS) of 7.5 operates that promise on a daily basis, then 8.2 is an activity in which the regulatory authority sends a person to physically check and determine whether the statement in the document is actually happening on the factory floor. In other words, 8.2 is not a section that tests products, but an axis that externally verifies “whether this site is in a state of control, whether the statement in the application matches the site, and whether the submitted data is true and complete.” The inspection inputs the application being reviewed for approval, its CMC data, the site’s actual GMP operation status (equipment, quality system, batch record, deviation/CAPA, data), and past inspection history and risk profile. The results include inspection result documents (FDA’s Form 483 observations/inspection report/classification, EU’s GMP certificate or nonconformity statement), approval recommendation, and correction request in case of nonconformity (CAPA) and follow-up. Coercive measures are taken. In particular, pre-approval inspection operates as a gateway to approval, and remediation in case of nonconformity is absorbed back into the CAPA/change management loop of 7.5 PQS, forming a closed loop.

How It Works — Types of inspections

According to FDA standards, there are four main inspections. The first is a Pre-Approval Inspection (PAI), which is conducted to help FDA ensure that the manufacturing facility first named in the application is actually capable of making the drug product and that the data submitted is accurate and complete (FDA CPGM 7346.832) (Source). Biopharmaceuticals are a little different. Vaccines, blood products, therapeutic proteins, cell therapies, etc. subject to PHS Act 351(a) are conducted through Pre-License Inspection (PLI) led by CBER, and the legal hurdle is that companies cannot distribute biological drugs until they receive an FDA license and pass a facility inspection (on the other hand, traditional drugs are subject to PAI under the FD&C Act). ⚠️(Source). The second is surveillance/routine inspection, which checks the GMP compliance status of commercial sites on a periodic and risk-based basis and follows the six-system approach of FDA CPGM 7356.002 (quality, facility/equipment, material, production, packaging labeling, and laboratory). The third is a for-cause inspection triggered by specific concerns (complaints, recalls, data integrity suspicions), and is usually unnoticed and focused. Fourth is a follow-up inspection that confirms the correction of previous nonconformities.

PAI’s 3 major goals and inspection progress

What the inspection looks at before approval is structured around three goals. Objective 1 is commercial manufacturing readiness, which determines whether the site has a quality system designed to achieve sufficient control over the facility and commercial manufacturing operations. Goal 2 is application conformity, which verifies whether the dosage form, manufacturing (processing) method, and analysis method are consistent with the application CMC description. Goal 3 is a data integrity audit, which verifies the authenticity of CMC submitted data by auditing raw data, whether paper or electronic (Source). After reviewing these three objectives, the lead investigator makes a conclusion. If there are no significant problems, ‘Recommend Approval’ is recommended, and if the site does not comply with GMP, CMC information is inconsistent with field records, or submitted information is not accurate or complete, ‘Recommend Withholding of Approval’ is recommended (Source), and this recommendation directly affects whether or not the application is approved.The inspection itself generally follows a standardized trend. It begins with an opening meeting (presentation of Form FDA 482, an inspection notice), facility walk-through, system/document review (SOP, batch record, deviation, CAPA, verification, stability, data/audit trail), SME (subject matter expert) interview, and daily briefing, and ends with a closing meeting. During this process, if the inspector observes conditions that may violate FDA regulations, they are recorded on Form FDA 483 (Inspectional Observations), and each inspection is followed by an Establishment Inspection Report (EIR), which the inspector writes and sends a copy to the company. An EIR is a detailed summary document containing the investigator’s findings, discussions, and collected evidence (Source).

Classification of results and aftermath

The inspection results are classified into three categories. NAI (No Action Indicated) is when no problematic conditions or practices are found, VAI (Voluntary Action Indicated) is when there is a matter pointed out but voluntary correction is sufficient and the authorities are not ready to take or recommend administrative or regulatory action, and OAI (Official Action Indicated) is when regulatory or administrative action is recommended (Source). The final classification is usually notified in writing within 45 to 90 days after completion, depending on the type of inspection, and classification as OAI may lead to additional regulatory actions such as regulatory meetings, untitled letters, warning letters, import alerts, injunctions, and seizures (Source).

483 Response and remediation

After receiving the observations, the real battle begins. When you receive Form FDA 483, you are advised to submit a written response within 15 business days (Monday through Friday, excluding federal holidays) of issuance, and the warning letter also requires a written response within 15 business days containing a plan to correct the problem and prevent recurrence (Source). For observations that cannot be fully resolved within 15 days, the initial response is recommended to include a CAPA plan and actual remediation completion schedule. If remediation cannot be completed within that period, provisional progress reports, milestones, and a communication plan for all ongoing activities are recommended (Source). This response practice has recently entered a codified phase. On March 6, 2026, the FDA issued draft guidance on its response to Drug CGMP 483 (comments accepted until May 8, 2026), codifying a 15 business day response, the design and implementation of a CAPA that is proportional to the risk of the observation and addresses the root cause, effectiveness measures, and a communication plan, and, crucially, “FDA will not delay regulatory action, including issuing a warning letter, to review an untimely initial response.” “No,” he nailed down (Source). The same guideline interestingly presents criteria for poor response. It defines not only missing relevant data but also excessive data as poor, and requires that the response be responsive to each observation, but also well-organized and comprehensive (Source).

EU inspection system

Europe has a different jurisdictional structure. Manufacturing sites within the EU are inspected by the national competent authority of the member state where the EU importer is located, and sites outside the EU are inspected by the authorities of the member state where the EU importer is located. EMA does not directly carry out inspections of medicines approved through a centralized procedure, but is responsible for coordinating them (Source). EU competent authorities plan regular inspections using a risk-based approach or inspect when noncompliance is suspected, and issue a GMP certificate or non-compliance statement after the inspection (Source). These documents are listed in the public database EudraGMDP, which is a public EU database containing manufacturing/import permits, active substance manufacturer registrations, GMP certificates and non-conformity statements, and now non-conformity documents as well as positive GMP certificates are publicly available (Source). EU inspectors apply EU GMP (EudraLex Vol.4) and PIC/S standards.

The United States and Europe have been approaching each other’s inspections with respect. The EU-US Mutual Recognition Agreement (MRA) became fully operational on July 11, 2019, when the FDA completed an assessment of the capabilities of all 28 EU GMP inspection bodies for human pharmaceuticals in July 2019, allowing both sides to recognize each other’s manufacturing facility inspections, avoiding duplicate inspections and focusing resources on high-risk sites (Source).

Key parameters/materials — The ‘materials’ of inspection are not reagents but documents and preparation infrastructure.

The materials covered in this section are not media or reagents, but inspection documents, output documents, and preparation infrastructure. The FDA side’s outputs are Form FDA 482 (inspection notice), Form FDA 483 (observations), EIR, warning letter, and NAI/VAI/OAI classification, and the EU side is GMP certificate and nonconformity statement listed in EudraGMDP. Governing programs and standards include FDA CPGM 7346.832 (pre-approval/pre-approval inspection) and CPGM 7356.002 (manufacturing inspection 6-system), 21 CFR 210/211 (cGMP) and Part 11 (electronic records), FD&C Act §704 (inspection authority), §501 (adulteration), and EU There are GMP (EudraLex Vol.4)·PIC/S PE 009·ICH Q7/Q9(R1)/Q10.Timing is also a practical parameter. PAI is usually done 2-6 months before the target approval date and can influence the product launch schedule ⚠️(Source). As seen previously, the response deadline is 15 business days for both Form 483 and warning letters, and 45 to 90 days for final classification notification. Conditions for triggering PAI/PLI include that PAI is mandatory for all manufacturing sites first named in an NDA, ANDA, BLA, or PMA, and priority criteria include applicant’s first application, new entity (NME), narrow threshold medicinal product, high-risk API, multiple changes in site/process/product, OAI status, or lack of recent inspection (Source).

Readiness is the practical highlight of this section. The inspection-response space is divided into a front room and a back room. The front room is the formal space where inspectors are responded to, documents are presented, and interviews are conducted, while the back room (or war room) is the nerve center of strategy during the inspection and is where the actual orchestration of predicting requests, preparing responses, and managing the flow of information takes place. In practice, equipment such as monitors, laptops, and printers are kept only in the back room (Source). To this, a document request log (record the request time, description, and deadline, and submit only the latest approved version), SME interview preparation, storyboard, and key messages are added. As an example of team size, Bio PLI is so multidisciplinary and large that in one case, nine investigators conducted the inspection over 15 business days.

What is judged from a quality and regulatory perspective?

To reiterate, 8.2 does not conduct product testing. The judgment targets are whether the site is under control, whether the site matches the application statement, and whether the data is authentic and complete. PAI’s three major goals (readiness, compliance, and data integrity) structure this judgment. In particular, data integrity (ALCOA+ principles, grounding in Section 7.5) is the core of PAI Goal 3 and one of the areas most frequently pointed out in 483/warning letters. Governing regulations and standards include the FDA’s CPGM 7346.832·7356.002, 21 CFR 210/211·Part 11, FD&C Act §704·§501, and PHS Act §351, from which several important principles are derived. Inspections are assigned based on risk, and inspection refusal or non-compliance can result in the product being considered adulterated under §501(j) of the FD&C Act, the warning letter itself is not a final administrative action but a preliminary step to enforcement action, and above all, nonconformity in inspection prior to approval will result in withholding approval no matter how good the dossier is — meaning that consistency between document and site is a requirement for approval. In Europe, EU GMP (EudraLex Vol.4) and PIC/S PE 009 are applied, Directive 2001/83/EC and Regulation (EU) 2019/6 are the background legislation, and internationally, EU-US MRA·PIC/S·WHO·ICH Q9(R1)/Q10 are the axis of harmonization.

Differences by product type

The inspection framework (PAI/PLI · 483 · NAI/VAI/OAI · remediation) is the same regardless of product modality, but the jurisdiction, emphasis, and physical requirements are different. mAb and recombinant proteins are mature reference standards, and thanks to PAI linked to CDER/OPQ review, well-established 6-system inspection, and platform knowledge, preparation is relatively predictable, and sterility, virus safety, cell banking, and data integrity are the key review axes. In the overall biopharmaceutical industry, the aforementioned CBER-led PLI operates as a separate concept, passing licensing and facility inspections becomes the legal gateway to distribution, and the team is multidisciplinary and large-scale ⚠️(Source).

The most challenging area is the viral vector/CGT (AAV, lenti, CAR-T) area. Gene therapy vector facilities raise new challenges such as virus safety, gene sequence verification, and long-term stability, requesting adjustments to the inspection approach ⚠️(Source). In autologous, the batch is the patient, so the chain of identity/custody and small single batch records are expanded to the scope of inspection, and starting materials (plasmids, vector seeds, patient-derived cells) and sterility/contamination control (Annex 1) is intensively reviewed. As a new/low-volume product modality, both inspectors and companies are accumulating experience. Since mRNA-LNP has few processing steps and is cell-free, some of the traditional cell culture inspection items do not apply, but process control of new CQAs such as capping, RNA integrity, and LNP encapsulation becomes an issue, and CBER/CDER jurisdiction may be divided depending on regulatory classification (gene therapy product or not). Classical vaccines have been inspected for a long time, but the focus of the review is biological variability, multiple components (antigens, adjuvants), and potency/sterility. However, the EU-US MRA is known to have historically excluded some biological products and vaccines from the scope of mutual recognition operations, and it appears that bio and vaccines are still subject to a large portion of internal inspections by both authorities. The exact and up-to-date scope of exclusion was not confirmed in this investigation, so it remains [uncertain].

Preparation practices and recent trends

The core practice of inspection readiness is mock inspection (mock/pre-PAI). A mock inspection is a realistic rehearsal in which a senior auditor takes on the role of an inspector before the actual inspection, requests documents, tours the facility, and interviews SMEs. It concludes with a detailed exit briefing and a written report containing findings by risk level, serving as a roadmap for remediation ⚠️(Source). In interview coaching, SMEs are trained to listen to questions, pause, and answer directly and honestly, with “I don’t know, but I’ll check” emphasized as a valid answer preferred over guessing ⚠️ (Source).The movements of the past three years can be organized into five categories. First, the institutionalization of remote regulatory assessment (RRA). RRA is an activity that assesses compliance by remotely reviewing FDA regulated facilities and records, and is not an inspection under §704(a)(1)/(a)(5) of the FD&C Act. 704(a)(4) records request is a mandatory RRA for drug and medical device facilities, so failure to comply may be a violation of the FDCA (Source). On the other hand, refusal to participate in voluntary RRA is not a violation of the FDCA, but refusal may delay regulatory decisions such as pending applications (Source). Second, the expansion of unannounced inspections of overseas facilities. On May 6, 2025, the FDA announced plans to expand unannounced inspections of overseas manufacturing facilities that produce food, essential drugs, and medical products for U.S. consumers and patients, explaining this as a measure to ensure that overseas manufacturers receive the same level of supervisory and regulatory scrutiny as domestic ones (Source). The background to this is that historically, approximately 90% of FDA’s overseas inspections have been notified in advance, leading to a ‘double standard’ being pointed out with domestic (generally unnotified) companies, and the supply reality that approximately 40% of US prescription drug APIs are manufactured in China and India, and approximately 20% in the EU ⚠️(Source).

Third, with the standardization of the 483 response, the aforementioned March 2026 draft guidance standardized response practices (Source). Fourth, it is an inspection reflection of quality management maturity (QMM). With the revision of CPGM 7346.832 in 2022, the FDA provided direction for inspectors to evaluate and document the maturity of quality management that exceeds CGMP requirements in pre-approval and surveillance inspections, and stated that pre-approval inspections should be an opportunity to evaluate the maturity of the quality management system ⚠️(Source). This is linked to the CDER QMM program in 7.5. Fifth, with the expansion of the international trust base, the full operation of the EU-US MRA and the PIC/S frame mentioned earlier are expanding multilateral trust (Source).

Issue:The data reveal tensions between policy will and operational reality at several points. (1) The expansion of unnotified overseas inspections has a clear justification for eliminating double standards, but due to practical limitations such as visas, logistics, interpretation, and on-site access, policy and implementation conflict as to whether it can achieve the same effect as domestic unnotified inspections, and most of the related materials remain in the perspective of preparation, saying, “It will expand, so be prepared at all times.” (2) RRA has become established as a permanent tool during the pandemic, and 704(a)(4) records requests are mandatory, but RRA is not an “inspection” under the law and cannot fully replace on-site GMP inspections — the tension between efficiency and reach and depth of verification. (3) QMM is directed to evaluate and document maturity that exceeds CGMP, but the definition and measurement of ‘what is maturity’ is subjective, so there are differences of opinion on consistency among inspectors and the boundary between maturity compared to minimum compliance. (4) There remains a problem of completeness of harmonization between the EU-US MRA’s principle of recognizing each other’s inspections to reduce duplication and the practice of each authority still conducting its own inspections as some product groups (biotechnology, vaccines, etc.) are excluded from the operational scope, and the exact scope of exclusion was not confirmed in this investigation.Unresolved Questions: (1) Specifics of inspection methodology by product type (CGT/mRNA) — for example, actual inspection practices for chain-of-identity verification of CAR-T, etc., rely on lower-tier secondary evidence and general principles level and overlap with node 9.x. (2) The latest operational scope of EU-US MRA (exclusion of vaccines, blood products, etc. and status by 2025) and details of EU inspection procedures (opening/closing mechanics, PIC/S PE 009 provisions) are only at an overview level, and some parts remain [uncertain]. (3) Verbatim comparison of the three major goals of PAI and the original text of CPGM 7346.832 — FDA media PDF direct fetch failed, so the text, which is widely reproduced, was secured through secondary verbatim citation, and the original text comparison is only partial.

8.3 Expedited & advanced-manufacturing pathways

Role and scope: Promotion and advanced manufacturing track on top of the standard path

If Part 8 so far has dealt with the standard regulatory path of “IND → BLA/MAA submission (8.1) → GMP inspection (8.2)”, 8.3 deals with a new regulatory mechanism that is optionally added on top of that path. The core idea is simple — to provide regulatory support for modern manufacturing transitions such as continuous manufacturing, cell and gene therapy (CGT), mRNA, and proximity manufacturing by designing regulatory incentives to avoid having to re-examine something once well understood (mature platform, proven advanced manufacturing technology) from scratch for each subsequent product.
