· side_a: The positively charged depth filter is advantageous for clarification and impurity removal due to DNA/HCP adsorption and high colloid holding capacity, and adenovirus recovery is good. · side_b: Negatively charged viruses (lentivirus, influenza) bind to positively charged filters and greatly reduce recovery, so product modality-specific responses such as filters without inorganic filter aids, open TFF, and high-salt washing are necessary. · Why: Although the charge of the filter is advantageous for adsorption of mAb impurities, it causes loss for charged virus products, so the optimal selection is reversed depending on the charge and properties of the product. | | 3.1 Capture & purification chromatography | claim: Is Protein A capture the dominant bottleneck in process costs? · side_a: Protein A resin is the ‘most expensive single step’, media $8,000–15,000/L, large column >$1M, 50% more expensive than traditional media, and accounting for 30–50% of downstream costs and 15–25% of total COGS. · side_b: If the per-batch cost is amortized by reusing high-density resin over 150 cycles, Protein A may only represent about 3% of the total manufacturing cost—that is, upfront capital should not be confused with per-batch amortization. · why: The conclusion is opposite depending on whether the ‘cost’ is viewed as the initial capital/column cost or the cost per batch amortized over the resin lifetime, and resin lifetime (number of cycles) is a key variable in this calculation and is strongly dependent on the reuse/cleaning verification strategy. | | 3.1 Capture & purification chromatography | claim: Should mAb capture remain with Protein A or move to a non-affinity/aggregate platform? · side_a: Protein A is a ‘platform anchor’ that gives purity of 95%+ and HCP 2–3 log in a single step, and is a de facto standard in terms of verification, robustness, and regulatory familiarity. · side_b: Synthetic mimetic, mixed-mode, activated carbon + flow-through integrated platform is emerging as an alternative due to cost, capacity, and low pH aggregation, but the alternatives have low selectivity and affinity and the selectivity of each antibody is different, so the burden of redevelopment of the process for each product is high. · why: The economic and capacity advantages of alternatives conflict with the selectivity and platform reproducibility advantages of Protein A, and the optimal choice is dependent on product properties (Fc presence, potency, stability) and facility strategy, so there is no universal solution. | |3.1 Capture & purification chromatography | claim: Should polishing/AEX be done with a batch resin column or a convection type membrane/monolith/continuous multi-column? · side_a: The packed-bed resin column is a mature standard in terms of verification, robustness, and capacity predictability, and is advantageous for bind-elute high-selectivity separation. · side_b: Due to the nature of AEX’s flow-through operation, membrane/monolith convection media encroach on the packed-bed with high flow, single use, and large particle processing, and PCC/SMB multi-column doubles ligand utilization and productivity, but process control and verification maturity are still key. · why: It is a trade-off between productivity, single-use, facility flexibility (alternatives) and verification maturity and robustness (batch resin), and adoption is dependent on scale, product modality, and continuity strategy (ICH Q13). | | 3.2 Viral clearance (inactivation & filtration) | claim: Is using a single worst-case model virus (MMV) sufficient for virus filter verification? · side_a: Since the Parvo filter operates by size exclusion, the smallest MMV is the worst-case that is easiest to pass, and there is a strong scientific basis for evaluating the filter removal ability as a single worst-case, which simplifies research and reduces costs (Q5A(R2) also accepts Parvo as the worst-case for filter verification. · side_b: Traditional verification requires a multi-virus panel (X-MuLV, MMV, PRV, Reo-3) of related viruses + specific model + non-specific model—Viruses with different chemical and surface properties in addition to size behave differently in different steps (inactivation, chromatography), so there are mechanisms that are missed if a single virus represents the entire process. · why: When looking only at ‘size exclusion of a single unit operation called a filter’, a single minimal virus is sufficient, but when looking at ‘orthogonal clearance of the entire process’, different viruses are needed for each mechanism—a trade-off between simplification and cost (side_a) and mechanism coverage and conservatism (side_b). | | 3.2 Viral clearance (inactivation & filtration) | claim: Is it possible to actually verify ‘viral clearance’ for viral vector (AAV/lenty) products? · side_a: Because the product is a virus, the classical orthogonal strategy collapses—low pH, detergents, and heat are powerless against AAV, the 20nm filter filters out the product and reduces yield, and the 35–70nm filter does not remove small foreign viruses. Therefore, safety moves to prevention and detection (selection and testing of raw materials and cell substrates), and Q5A (R2) is also limited to the ‘range where clearance is possible.’ · side_b: Nevertheless, ‘possible clearance’ is demonstrated—there are cases where detergent lysis removed enveloped contaminating viruses (XMuLV >4.67 ​​LRV) and affinity/AEX chromatography removed non-enveloped MVMs (4.6·4.38 LRV, cumulative ~9.07), and a compromise is possible to obtain model virus reduction in >90% yield with 35/50nm nanofilters. · why: Due to the fundamental constraint of removing only foreign viruses without destroying the product itself, ‘orthogonal clearance is not possible’ (side_a) and ‘partial clearance is demonstrated limited to contaminating viruses’ (side_b) coexist—depending on whether the contaminant is enveloped or non-enveloped and how different the size and chemistry are from the product. | | 3.2 Viral clearance (inactivation & filtration) | claim: Does risk-based mitigation (NGS in-vivo replacement, PrK, CHO RVLP <10^-4/dose) in Q5A(R2) reduce the burden while maintaining viral safety? · side_a: Replacing animal testing with accumulated knowledge/platform data and highly sensitive NGS for well-characterized CHO, etc., and reducing duplicate verification for each product is scientifically justified and meets the 3Rs (<10^-4/dose allowed, batch reduction model continuous manufacturing applied). · side_b: Relaxation is largely dependent on regulatory discretion and prior consultation, and NGS has a complex workflow, so it is recommended to discuss method verification and data submission with regulators. The safety implications of RVLP threshold relaxation (<10^-6→<10^-4) and the boundaries of platform data reuse still require case-by-case judgment. · why: There is a tension between ‘refining conservatism with accumulated knowledge’ (side_a) and ‘maturity of evidence and verification of relaxation is still case-specific’ (side_b), and it is dependent on the speed of regulatory acceptance of new technology (NGS) and platform data. | | 3.3 Polishing, concentration & buffer exchange (UF/DF) | claim: Are hollow fibers in TFF for shear-sensitive large particles (AAV, etc.) inherently softer than flat cassettes? · side_a: Hollow fiber has an open channel and has low shear, so it is recommended for shear-sensitive products such as AAV, lentivirus, and living cells (practical practice). · side_b: The assumption that hollow fibers are inherently softer is an error, and choosing a type based solely on shear sensitivity can harm process efficiency and manufacturing economics—type selection should be judged by fluid dynamics, yield, and productivity. · Why: There is a conflict between the customary shear avoidance logic and the actual performance and economic feasibility logic, and there is no general solution because the optimal form is dependent on flow path design, flux, and scale as well as product vulnerability. | | 3.3 Polishing, concentration & buffer exchange (UF/DF) | claim: Should concentration/buffer exchange be batch recirculating TFF or single pass TFF (SPTFF)/continuous? · side_a: Batch recirculating TFF cassettes are the mature standard for validation, robustness and dose predictability and ensure target concentrations are reached. · side_b: SPTFF has no recirculation, so shear and cohesion are low, tank and time are reduced, and it is directly connected to chromatography, enabling continuous and connected processes (ICH Q13), but process control and verification maturity are still key. · why: Due to the trade-off between recycling front end and processing time (weakness of deployment) and verification maturity and robustness (weakness of SPTFF), adoption is dependent on scale, product modality, and serialization strategy. | | 3.3 Polishing, concentration & buffer exchange (UF/DF) | claim: What is the optimal protein concentration for high-concentration diafiltration? · side_a: Considering only hydraulics (flux and viscosity), diafiltration at high concentration (e.g. 120 g/L) is advantageous in terms of volume and buffer consumption. · side_b: Because the charged excipients are retained by Gibbs-Donnan/volume exclusion, the diavolume increases rapidly (43 vs 17) at high concentrations, so the optimal concentration reflecting this is calculated to be much lower at 57 g/L. · why: Conclusions vary more than twice depending on whether ‘optimum’ is viewed as hydraulic volumetric efficiency or excipient removal efficiency (Donnan correction), and is strongly dependent on the charge density of charged excipients and proteins. | | 4.1 Formulation development | claim: What is the basic choice between liquid and freeze-drying? · side_a: Freeze-drying significantly increases the shelf life by removing moisture and is resistant to temperature fluctuations and light, which is advantageous from a stability perspective (preferred for unstable molecules). · side_b: Freeze-drying takes a lot of time and cost, and there is a burden of reconstitution and risk of loss of activity, so once stability is ensured, liquid is preferred in terms of patient convenience and manufacturing cost. · why: It is a compromise between stability benefits and manufacturing/administration convenience/cost, and the conclusion varies depending on the intrinsic stability of the molecule and TPP (administration route/distribution environment). | | 4.1 Formulation development | claim: Standard storage form of mRNA-LNP · side_a: Ultra-low temperature storage of −60 to −90°C is the de facto standard for commercial mRNA vaccines. · side_b: Freeze-dried mRNA-LNP maintains encapsulation efficiency and transduction efficiency for more than 12 weeks at 4℃, showing that it can be replaced by cold chain. · Why: Due to the difference in timing between commercial performance (ultra-low temperature) and the latest research (freeze-drying), freeze-drying is an emerging approach that has not yet been established as a commercial standard. | | 4.1 Formulation development | claim: Solution path to high concentration mAb viscosity problem · side_a:Viscosity is a result of electrostatic and hydrophobic self-association resulting from sequence/structure, so it must be fundamentally solved through protein engineering (sequence optimization). · side_b: A practical solution is to lower the viscosity with formulation additives such as arginine and multi-functional excipients. · Why: The intervention is different between the molecular design stage and the formulation stage, and when the candidate material is already fixed, there is no choice but to rely on the formulation approach. | | 4.2 Aseptic fill-finish, sterilization & lyophilization | claim: Should PUPSIT (filter integrity testing before use after sterilization) be mandatory · side_a: EU/Annex 1 basically requires pre-use testing to detect damage during sterilization, transportation, and installation or flaw masking during filtration. · side_b: US industry/regulation believes that the complexity of sterilizing filtrate manipulation and setup actually adds to the risk of contamination, and concealment of defects is extremely rare, so post-use testing is sufficient if justified by risk assessment. · Why: With the same goal of ‘sterility assurance’, the net benefit judgment between the theoretical benefit of detecting defects and the risk of contamination caused by actual manipulation is different depending on the region and regulatory philosophy. | | 4.2 Aseptic fill-finish, sterilization & lyophilization | claim: Which to choose between an isolator and RABS as an aseptic filling barrier? · side_a: Annex 1 pushes isolators that provide higher sterility assurance through automatic biological contamination removal and complete separation of workers into the ‘new normal’, and requires a minimum RABS for new products. · side_b: RABS has low introduction and conversion costs and is flexible for multiple products and frequent changeovers, and the isolator has a long preparation time for operation due to the VHP biological decontamination cycle. · Why: It is a compromise between the level of sterility assurance and capital cost, flexibility, and operation rate, and the conclusion varies depending on the product portfolio (single large quantity vs. small quantity of various types) and regulatory expectations. | | 4.2 Aseptic fill-finish, sterilization & lyophilization | claim: Whether to push biological products toward terminal sterilization or aseptic processing · side_a: Annex 1 applies regulatory pressure to prioritize terminal sterilization, which has superior SAL and reproducibility, ‘as much as possible.’ · side_b: Most proteins, mRNA-LNP, and cell products cannot withstand moist heat/radiation, so sterile filtration + aseptic processing is virtually the only route. · Why: Because the highest assurance method (terminal sterilization) preferred by regulations conflicts with the physical heat/radiation resistance limits of the molecule, and product stability data determines the choice. | | 4.3 Container closure system & CCIT | claim: Does deterministic CCIT, which measures physical leaks, actually guarantee microbial integrity · side_a: USP <1207> · FDA · Annex 1 prefers deterministic methods (vacuum attenuation, HVLD, helium leakage) that are quantitative, reproducible, and objective, and it is believed that physical tests with MALL detection capabilities have replaced and improved stochastic microbial and dye penetration tests. · side_b: Only the microbial penetration challenge directly measures the result directly related to safety (sterility), and the deterministic physical leakage value is translated into sterility only through ‘correlation’ with the probability of bacterial penetration (e.g. Kirsch limit), so MALL correlation justification for each product is necessary. · Why: Because the translation step intervenes between the surrogate indicator of ‘completeness’ (physical leakage rate) and the actual safety result (exclusion of microorganisms), there are differences in judgment as to how much of the burden of reliability and justification is required. | | 4.3 Container closure system & CCIT | claim: Should in-line 100% CCIT be required for all sterile products? · side_a: Annex 1 mandates 100% integrity testing for each batch for fusion sealing (BFS, ampoules), and with the commercialization of non-destructive determinism techniques (vacuum attenuation, laser headspace, microcurrent HVLD), 100% inspection has become technically possible. · side_b: Mechanical sealing (vials, PFS) does not require 100% release if there is a solid management strategy, and statistical CCIT within the stability program is sufficient, and 100% inspection has a large burden of capacity, cost, and product exposure. · Why: Because of the difference in defect risk profile of sealing method (fusion vs. mechanical) and the position that risks can be managed with management strategies and statistics versus the position that 100% inspection is the best guarantee. | | 4.3 Container closure system & CCIT | claim: Whether to maintain or remove the silicone oil of PFS · side_a: Silicone oil is essential for transmission functions such as plunger glide force and injection force as barrel and stopper lubrication, and has a long commercial history. · side_b: Silicone oil dissolves to create subvisible particles and increases the risk of protein aggregation and immunogenicity, so siliconeless/PTFE coated stopper/crosslink coating is preferred for sensitive biopharmaceuticals. · Why: Because the same member causes both ‘function (lubrication)’ and ‘risk (agglomeration/particles)’, resulting in a different compromise between product sensitivity and delivery device requirements. | | 4.4 Cold chain, storage & distribution (GDP) | claim: MKT’s role and reliability in temperature deviation disposal · side_a: MKT is an Arrhenius-based, defensible data-based indicator that broadly supports temperature deviation evaluation of refrigerated and room temperature products and GDP compliance. · side_b: MKT cannot handle frozen products, freeze-thaw, or instantaneous spikes. It should not be misused to compensate for poor temperature management and should only be applied to occasional deviations when management is good. · Why: Because MKT is an index that assumes chemical thermal decomposition (reversible/accumulation), it is unsuitable for some biological drugs where physical/irreversible damage (freezing/agglomeration) dominates. Misunderstanding the scope of application creates the risk of releasing damaged product. | | 4.4 Cold chain, storage & distribution (GDP) | claim: Standard temperature conditions for mRNA-LNP distribution · side_a: For commercial mRNA vaccines, freezing/ultra-low temperatures of −80℃ (Pfizer) or −20℃ (Moderna) are the de facto standard and rely on dry ice logistics. · side_b: Freeze-dried LNP showed stability for 21 months at 4℃, proving that it can be distributed without an ultra-low temperature cold chain. · Why: Due to the difference in timing between commercial performance (ultra-low temperature) and the latest heat stabilization research (refrigeration/room temperature), freeze-drying and heat stabilization are emerging approaches that have not yet become established as commercial standards. | | 4.4 Cold chain, storage & distribution (GDP) | claim: Choice of passive vs. active temperature management packaging · side_a: Passive packaging (PCM/VIP) can be maintained for 96 to 120 hours without external power, making it simple and low-cost and sufficient for most regular routes. · side_b: Active containers precisely control long distances, large capacities, and long periods of time by cooling the compressor, but they are expensive and have a large burden on power, charging, and operation. · Why: It is a compromise between route length, cargo volume, required precision, cost/carbon footprint, and the conclusion varies depending on maintenance time and risk tolerance. | | 5.1 Facilities, utilities & cleanrooms | High temperature WFI vs. ambient WFI: Annex 1 cites ‘continuous circulation above 70°C’ as an example of microbial inhibition (high temperature thermal disinfection is a proven practice). On the other hand, ISPE and others have been pushing cold WFI (RO+EDI+UF+ozone) as a means of decarbonization and cost reduction since 2017, suggesting up to 50% power savings - practices are divided regarding the reliability of microorganism/biofilm control in room temperature storage and distribution (cold WFI still meets less than 1/5 of new demand). | | 5.1 Facilities, utilities & cleanrooms | Normative ACH vs. risk-based ACH: FDA aseptic guidance exemplifies normative values ​​such as a minimum of 20 ACH for an ISO 8 support area, but Annex 1 (2022) does not have a universal ACH table and instead requires ventilation rates to be determined by CCS/risk assessment for each space—there is a tension between the practice of wanting quantitative standards and risk-based flexibility. | | 5.1Facilities, utilities & cleanrooms | Traditional IQ/OQ/PQ vs. risk-based verification (verification): Annex 15 stipulates the discrete steps and definitions of IQ, OQ, and PQ, but ASTM E2500-25·ISPE BG5 (2nd edition) seeks to replace and lighten the blanket qualification (using FAT/SAT) with a ‘verification’ approach centered on SMEs and critical elements (CA/CDE) — with regulatory acceptance and terminology harmonization of the two frames A difference in interpretation remains. | | 5.1 Facilities, utilities & cleanrooms | Inconsistencies in cold WFI energy savings figures: Savings vary widely between sources, ranging from 30% (some vendors), 50% (ISPE, compared to VC), and 60-90% (compared to distillation) - with large variations depending on the basis of comparison (including VC/ME/storage loop or not). | | 5.2 Contamination control strategy & environmental monitoring | Fixed CFU action limits (EU) vs. contamination recovery rates (US): Annex 1 Table 6 specifies absolute CFU action limits for each grade (e.g. 10 CFU/m³ for Grade B air). On the other hand, USP <1116> believes that individual CFU counts are statistically unreliable in low-contamination sterile environments, and recommends the contamination recovery rate (CRR), which is the ‘percentage (%) of plates with recovery regardless of the number of CFU’, as the main trending indicator. Both systems require Grade A/ISO 5 to be virtually zero/extremely rare, but their EM data interpretation philosophies (absolute limits vs. frequencies) differ. | | 5.2 Contamination control strategy & environmental monitoring | Culture-based EM vs. real-time continuous viable monitoring: Annex 1 §9.24 requires Grade A continuous viable monitoring and §9.28 recommends rapid methods, but live fluorescence particle counting (BFPC) does not provide species identification and CFU correlation/validation (Table 6 Note 2) is still maturing. Vendors are pushing real-time adoption, but the current practice standard is to parallel culture methods rather than completely replace them. | | 5.2 Contamination control strategy & environmental monitoring | Status of settling plates: Although settling plates are criticized for their lower recovery rates and irregularity (passive/sedimentation-based) compared to active (volumetric displacement) air sampling, Annex 1 Table 6 still defines settling plates as an accepted method of live bacteria monitoring and also cites them as examples of Grade A and B continuous monitoring — they remain in the regulatory text despite their known limitations. | | 5.2 Contamination control strategy & environmental monitoring | ‘Living system’ vs. ‘one-time document’: CCS was intended to be an actively reviewed and updated system (§2.3), but post-implementation inspections have pointed out that many manufacturers have made CCS a deliverable for submission (e.g. MHRA FQS Pharma) — and a substantive gap is observed between regulatory intent and field implementation. | | 5.3 Single-use systems & extractables/leachables | claim: Whether to test the SUS extract using the BPOG protocol or USP <665> · side_a: The BPOG (BioPhorum) standardized extraction protocol is a practice developed by the bio industry and has many repetitions and time points, giving a more extensive and conservative dataset. · side_b: USP <665> is a mandatory compendial standard on 2026-05-01, but is less exhaustive than BPOG. BPOG is not a formal regulatory document, so <665> compliance must be confirmed separately. · Why: Because, with the same ‘extract characterization’ goal, companies face a double burden and harmonization problem between the data completeness of autonomous industry best practices and the regulatory compliance of mandatory compendial standards. | | 5.3 Single-use systems & extractables/leachables | claim: Does the standardized exaggerated extract data sufficiently represent the actual leachable risk? side_a: Extractable is the worst-case under aggressive/exaggerated conditions, so evaluating it against the safety threshold (AET/SCT) usually conservatively covers the leachable risk. · side_b: Leachables occur only under actual process conditions (normal temperature, contact time, solvent) and are a ‘related but distinct’ concept from extracts. Exaggerated extraction alone can overestimate or underestimate the actual risk, so product-specific leachate testing is necessary. · Why: Because the regulatory convenience and cost-effectiveness of worst-case proxy testing clash with the demand for accurate safety verification in the actual process, and the gap is pointed out as a ‘compliance gap’. | | 5.3 Single-use systems & extractables/leachables | claim: Is single-use (SUS) a better choice than stainless steel? · side_a: SUS is superior in multi-product, clinical, and flexible production by reducing initial investment, cleaning validation, and downtime, and providing flexibility and quick changeover. · side_b: SUS has a weak supply chain dependent on a few suppliers, plastic waste, high consumable OpEx, and an upper scale limit of ~2000 L, and the TCO of stainless steel is lower in the case of high operation of a single product. · why: It is a compromise between initial capital cost/flexibility and long-term total cost of ownership/supply stability/sustainability, and the conclusion varies depending on the product portfolio (multi-product small quantity vs. single large quantity) and scale. | | 5.4 Continuous/intensified processing, PAT & digitization | Maturity of end-to-end continuity: Some vendors/CDMOs (WuXiUP 5-15X, Enzene fully connected continuity) offer commercial end-to-end continuity, but academic and industry research shows that downstream lags upstream (~34% adoption of continuous chromatography) and that the normal upstream-periodic downstream interface mismatch is the ‘dominant obstacle.’ Cause: Vendor Marketing vs. Peer Review/Survey Reality. | | 5.4 Continuous/intensified processing, PAT & digitization | Scope of RTRT for biological products: FDA/ICH suggests RTRT as an alternative to definitive testing, but ICH Q13 itself states that potency and microbiological testing are required offline — ‘real-time release’ for biological products is partial real-time release at the level of some attribute rather than full product parametric release. Tension between promises and feasible subsets. | | 5.4 Continuous/intensified processing, PAT & digitization | Digital Twin Value vs. Proven Maturity: Trade sources cite dramatic gains (90% faster development, 70% solvent reduction, closed-loop control), but same/different sources point out that the model falls outside the data domain and ‘lack of an overall goal/operational model for Bioprocessing 4.0’ — Shenzhen is ahead of GMP verification deployment. | | 5.4 Continuous/intensified processing, PAT & digitization | Continuous economic feasibility claims vs. limitations: PCC/SMB’s claims of 20-30% cost reduction and small footprint are in conflict with reduced facility flexibility due to long-term operation, high initial investment, and operational complexity (66.1% of responses). Cost effectiveness largely depends on the comparison assumptions (run length, equipment, year-round operation). | | 6.1 Identity, purity & impurity testing | claim: The role of ELISA vs LC-MS/MS in HCP release quantification · side_a: ELISA remains the standard for rapid, low-cost release quantification of total HCP and dominates the regulatory and market (36–40% market share in 2024) · side_b: ELISA does not identify individual HCPs and polyclonal antibodies have coverage bias that misses weakly immunogenic/low molecular weight HCPs, making USP <1132.1> (effective 2025) LC-MS/MS is potency and captures empty/partial-capsid effects. · why: Genomic titre counts particles regardless of activity, so it can overstate potency when empty or non-infectious capsids are present; functional assays are more meaningful but far more variable. | | 6.3 Adventitious agent & viral safety testing | New annex number: BioProcess International (2nd) refers to viral vector/VLP testing as ‘Annex 7’, while the official ICH Q5A(R2) article (EMA/CHMP/ICH/804363/2022, 1st) refers to raw bulk testing of genetically engineered viral vectors as ‘Annex 6’. (Both sides: Annex 7 (second article) vs Annex 6 (official article) / Reason: the second article may have cited annex numbers from the draft stage — relying on the first article) | | 6.3 Adventitious agent & viral safety testing | Readiness for NGS to replace in vitro: Regulation and industry (Q5A(R2), 4th NGS Conference) reported that NGS is ‘ready to replace’ in vivo and PCR and said that in vitro can also ‘complement or replace’. On the other hand, the technical/skeptic side believes that in vitro replacement requires case-by-case verification, citing gaps in bioinformatics pipeline verification, up to 2 log sensitivity deviation between laboratories, and nucleic acid ≠ infectivity issues. In addition, the CAACB data shows that ‘existing methods are also incomplete’ with 5 out of 18 in vitro cases being false negative, and there is a cautious opinion that no single method is a complete replacement. (Both sides: Ready for immediate replacement vs. need for verification and orthogonal confirmation on a case-by-case basis / Reason: 3Rs, cost, and speed vs. speed difference between verification maturity and infectiousness confirmation requirements) | | 6.4 Sterility, endotoxin, mycoplasma & rapid micro methods | Mycoplasma culture time required: Many sources describe a total of ~28 days (bottleneck compared to sterility test), but some, such as DSDP, describe direct culture as ‘at least 21 days’ — in reality, when liquid/agar passage and indicator cell culture are combined, ~28 days is typical, and 21 days seems to refer only to the direct culture part. (Both sides: 21 days vs 28 days / Reason: Which sub-procedures and passages are included) | | 6.4 Sterility, endotoxin, mycoplasma & rapid micro methods | Regulatory status of rFC: Vendor/paper view that rFC is a ‘completely equivalent replacement’ vs. the fact that USP created a separate chapter <86> instead of revising <85> and is not yet harmonized with Ph.Eur.2.6.32 — the cautious view that product-specific verification is still required coexists. accumulation speed) | | 6.5 Stability, extended characterization & comparability | claim: Can the shelf life of biological products be extrapolated using accelerated stability data? · side_a: The new the EMA also believes that clinical data is only necessary for manufacturing changes · side_b: If analysis results reveal differences with uncertain clinical impact, PK/PD·toxic·clinical bridging is necessary, and regulators are cautious in large-scale and late-stage changes · why: ‘Analytical similarity is not Differences in judgment about ‘how much it predicts clinical no difference’ — depend comparative equivalence · side_b: There is no well-established standard protocol for condition selection and required decomposition degree/time, so there is a large deviation in practice · why: It is difficult to establish a universal protocol due to the diversity of molecules and formulations of biological drugs, so it relies on scientific justification for each case. | | 6.5 Stability, extended validation | claim: Does the enhanced approach/AQbD actually provide regulatory flexibility that reduces the burden of post-change changes? side_a: Q14 states that it exempts regulatory communication text and field realization. | | 6.6 Analytical procedure development & validation | claim: Is the additional investment in the enhanced (AQbD) approach worth it compared to minimal · side_a: Enhanced provides long-term benefits by providing a wider operating range, life cycle flexibility, and OOS reduction with ATP·DoE·MODR · side_b: Q14 recognizes the minimal (traditional) approach as still fully valid, and the statistical/DoE capabilities and documentation costs are high, and regulatory compensation is uncertain, so many companies remain with the minimal approach · why: Since the guidelines allow both paths, the choice is up to the organization. It varies depending on capabilities, risk acceptance, and regulatory expectations by jurisdiction, and the cost-benefit break-even differs by product and market. | | 7.1 Process development & characterization | Criticality Binary vs. Continuous: The traditional definition classifies CPP as binary (core/non-core), but industries such as BioPharm insist on ‘continuous criticality’ based on the size of CQA impact. Binary is advantageous for regulatory clarity and simplification of management strategies, while continuous focuses management resources on the point of maximum impact, but there is the burden of having to procedurally define risk levels and tools for each company and apply them consistently. | | 7.1 Process development & characterization | FMEA subjectivity vs. data-based criticality: ISPE recognizes that FMEA is ‘led by individual opinions’ and recommends the introduction of prior data, and quantitative/integrated process model-based criticality emerges as an alternative. Although criticized for the randomness of the RPN score (threshold 100–200) and statistical flaws in the multiplicative scale, FMEA remains the standard screening tool. | | 7.1 Process development & characterization | Limitations of scale-down model representativeness: SDM cannot fully reproduce commercial scale due to differences in mixing, mass transfer (kLa·pCO2), and geometry, and the goal is not ‘same’ but ‘sufficiently similar’. Some CQAs remain non-predictive (Case C/D), and there is a difference in judgment between developers and regulators regarding the reliability of extrapolating small-scale characterization data to commercial scale. | | 7.1 Process development & characterization | Promise vs. maturity of digital twin/in-silico characterization: Medicine Maker (2025) claims that digital twins can perform hundreds of experiments within days and eliminate engineering runs, but the same article admits that the model deviates from reality when parameters are not specified and is inaccurate outside the data domain. The regulatory acceptance and verification framework is still being established. | | 7.2 Technology transfer & scale-up | Omitting engineering runs vs. performing them: The working consensus is to verify scale-up models and facilities with non-GMP engineering runs (one failed GMP batch is more expensive than two engineering runs), but sponsors under budget and schedule pressure have a strong incentive to omit them — a conflict between short-term cost savings and long-term deployment failure risk. | | 7.2 Technology transfer & scale-up | Comparability ‘Highly similar’ judgment boundary: ICH Q5E states that analytical data alone is usually sufficient and clinical bridging is rare, but there are differences of opinion between developers and regulators in determining what is ‘highly similar’ and which CQA differences are ‘clinically meaningless’ (e.g., whether statistically significant differences can be accepted for mechanistic justification). | | 7.2 Technology transfer & scale-up | Promise vs. maturity of digital technology transfer: Vendors such as ValGenesis claim that PLM and digital twins eliminate silos and enable ‘design one, build many,’ but peer-reviewed literature points out that knowledge management is an ‘immature field’ even 10 years after the introduction of ICH Q10 — tool maturity and regulatory acceptance fall short of the claims. | | 7.2 Technology transfer & scale-up | PPQ ‘three batches in a row’ practice vs. regulatory flexibility: The practice description presents PPQ as ‘three successful batches in a row at full scale’, but the FDA 2011 Process Verification Guide (detailed in 7.3) does not fix the number of batches but sets it based on risk and data, so the ‘Rule of Three’ is a conventional heuristic and not a regulatory requirement. | | 7.3 Process & cleaning validation lifecycle | Number of PPQ batches ‘3 batches’ vs. statistical justification: Annex 15 5.20 states that a minimum of 3 consecutive batches is generally accepted, but the same document 5.19 and the ISPE white paper require the number of batches to be justified with QRM/acceptance interval statistics. Both are recognized in regulations, but there is practical friction regarding whether 3 batches can be used as is (simple/well-understood process) vs. how much statistical basis is required (new product modality/complex process). | | 7.3 Process & cleaning validation lifecycle | continuous vs continued/ongoingConflict in process verification terms: ‘continuous process verification’ is an alternative to traditional verification in ICH Q8 (replacing Stage 2 with real-time monitoring in the development phase), while ‘continued/ongoing process verification’ is monitoring during commercial production in FDA Stage 3 / Annex 15. Materials often use them interchangeably, resulting in confusion for learners and practitioners. The Annex 15 glossary clearly distinguishes between the two. | | 7.3 Process & cleaning validation lifecycle | CSV vs CSA / Documentation Burden: Extensive documentation of traditional CSV vs risk-based/minimally burdensome testing of CSA. The FDA CSA specifies the reduction of low-risk system documentation, but some in the industry are concerned about the risk of document reduction being misunderstood as rigor reduction and the subjectivity of determining what is a direct/indirect impact. | | 7.3 Process & cleaning validation lifecycle | Inconsistency in timing of FDA CSA final guidance: The Federal Register notice shows a date of 2025-09-24 (last available), but some secondary sources (Scilife·IntuitionLabs) cite ‘2026-02-03 finalization.’ The draft date is 2022. The date of the first government notification (2025-09) is more reliable. | | 7.3 Process & cleaning validation lifecycle | Legacy washout limit vs. HBEL: Regulations now strongly favor toxicity-based HBEL/PDE (especially high potency/cytotoxicity), but the 10 ppm/1/1000 dose legacy standard remains the practice in the field, making it a transitional period. | | 7.4 Quality by Design & control strategy | claim: Is the enhanced (QbD) approach actually adopted and utilized? side_a: Adoption of QbD elements is widespread (76% of new drug approvals in Japan use QbD) and regulations encourage an approach with enhanced regulations side_b: Cases of actual claims for regulatory flexibility tools (Established Conditions/design space) are very rare (US 5/85, EU 3/107, applications including EC), and terminology misuse and lack of training lead to ‘QbD-lite’ Stop · why: Differences in the definition of ‘adoption’ — QbD language and documentation have spread, but the regulatory flexibility gained in return (free and relaxed change reporting within the design space) has not been realized due to concerns about review delays and regional inconsistencies, leaving a gap between formal adoption and actual use. | | 7.4 Quality by Design & control strategy | claim: Can criticality be reliably determined through risk assessment such as FMEA/RPN? · side_a: FMEA (RPN=S Emphasis on data utilization · Why: Although criticality judgments appear quantitative, they actually rely on subjective scoring — the ubiquity of standard tools and criticism of their reliability coexist, and the regulation has officially acknowledged this tension through revisions. | | 7.4 Quality by Design & control strategy | claim: Is the QbD/design space paradigm valid for new modalities such as CGT? · side_a: The QbD framework is applicable regardless of product modality, and a solid control strategy can be established with a scale-down model and DoE · side_b: CGT has limited product and process knowledge, small deployment, and weak CQA-clinical correlation, resulting in ‘appearance-only QbD’ that broadly sets the acceptance criteria based on the history range instead of the true design space · why: QbD The premise (sufficient process understanding and measurable CQA) holds true for maturity product modality (mAb) but falls short for CGT, making the same framework qualitatively different in actual control intensity. | | 7.5 Pharmaceutical Quality System (PQS) | ICH Q10’s ‘optional’ vs. de facto mandatory: ICH Q10 specifies that exceeding regional GMP is ‘optional’, but the FDA QMM program and each country’s GMP (EU Chapter 1, 21 CFR 211) de facto require elements and responsibilities, so the voluntary framework operates as a de facto expectation. | | 7.5 Pharmaceutical Quality System (PQS) | ALCOA vs ALCOA+ vs ALCOA++: MHRA states that ‘there is no difference in expectations regardless of which abbreviation is used’, but the industry has expanded the notation to ALCOA++ with the addition of ‘Traceable’, and the EU Annex 22(AI) draft discussion also refers to ALCOA++ — meaning without substantive change Disagreement on whether it is inflation or a new requirement. | | 7.5 Pharmaceutical Quality System (PQS) | Scope of audit trail review: MHRA allows risk-based and exception reporting, saying ‘there is no need to review all system activities’, but in practice, due to the subjectivity of risk assessment, there is a large difference in interpretation between regulators and companies regarding the appropriate scope and frequency of review. | | 7.5 Pharmaceutical Quality System (PQS) | QRM Subjectivity: ICH Q9(R1) calls for ‘recognizing, predicting and resolving’ the subjectivity of risk assessment, but acknowledges that subjectivity cannot be eliminated – differences of opinion remain as to whether it is a limitation that can be alleviated through formality and quantification. | | 7.6 Batch disposition & QP certification/release | Averaging/Invalidation of OOS: FDA guidance prohibits averaging of OOS with passing retests and strictly limits invalidations to ‘obvious laboratory errors,’ but in the field, there is repeated pressure to offset/invalidate OOS with retests, which remains one of the most pointed areas of FDA 483/Warning Letters — an ongoing conflict between norms and practical practices. | | 7.6 Batch disposition & QP certification/release | Certification of unexpected deviations (Annex 16 §3) vs. the principle that ‘batch must fully comply with MA’: Annex 16 allows QPs to certify even if there are deviations from the MA/GMP details if the registration specifications are met and the impact is ‘negligible’. This is in tension with the basic premise of release (complete compliance with MA) and the judgment of ‘negligible’ is subjective. | | 7.6 Batch disposition & QP certification/release | Conditional/OOS release of ATMP/CGT: Due to the short shelf life and autologous single patient batch, release before obtaining full QC results or OOS batch release may be permitted on the grounds of ‘imminent patient benefit and non-substitutability’, but this fundamentally conflicts with the strict first test and later release principle commonly used in mAbs, and the penalty for patient safety varies from case to case. | | 7.6 Batch disposition & QP certification/release | Permissible scope of remote QP certification: EMA Q&A · The industry allows remote certification on the condition of performance within the EU/EEA, compliance with Annex 11, and PQS control, but the acceptance and additional requirements for regular remote certification are different depending on the national regulatory authority, so there is a large difference in interpretation in practical application. | | 7.6 Batch disposition & QP certification/release | QP personal responsibility vs. delegation/reliance on PQS: Annex 16 requires the QP to ‘personally’ guarantee certification, while explicitly allowing delegation of 1.7 tasks and reliance on PQS/third-party audits — in a multi-item, multi-site environment, the boundaries between the QP’s actual scope of personal control and legal responsibility are blurred. | | 8.1 CMC documentation & regulatory submissions | claim: Does ICH Q12 actually reduce the burden of post-marketing changes and achieve harmonization? · side_a: Q12 is a reporting category harmonized with EC, PACMP, and PLCM.Provides an internationally harmonized framework that reduces regulatory burden and enables changes to be implemented when supported by product and process knowledge. · side_b: The current Q12 is not completely consistent with existing regional legal systems such as EU variation and FDA CMC change guidance, so only 3 countries have fully implemented it, and cases of actually including EC in new drug applications are extremely rare (US 5/85, EU 3/107), and companies still prefer the existing regional system when making changes. · why: There is a conflict between the principles of international harmonization and the existing legally binding procedures in each region, and the regulatory flexibility of Q12 is not realized without regulation revision, so there is a gap between ‘formal adoption’ and ‘practical use’. | | 8.1 CMC documentation & regulatory submissions | claim: Will M4Q(R2)’s transition to a structured/modular CTD soon bring about effective improvements? · side_a: M4Q(R2) converts text-centric descriptions into reusable modular structured data, aligns with eCTD v4.0 and cloud screening, and reduces duplication by separating core (2.3)/auxiliary (3). · side_b: M4Q(R2) is a draft scheduled for adoption in 2027-06 and will take effect after that, and the adoption of eCTD v4.0 itself, which is its premise, is being delayed by regions and companies, making the timing and realization of the improvement effect uncertain. · Why: Because of the lag and uncertainty between the design benefits of the guidelines (structuring and reducing duplication) and the implementation conditions of infrastructure and industry readiness (adoption of eCTD v4.0), the promise of improvement and the timing of its realization are divergent. | | 8.1 CMC documentation & regulatory submissions | claim: How much CMC should be submitted during the initial IND stage. · side_a: Submitted CMC details must be appropriate for the clinical stage, and early stages may be less complete, so only the minimum safety key information is submitted and the rest is postponed to later stages. · side_b: Some sponsors submit more CMC information than necessary for the first human administration Phase 1 IND (FDA clarifies the minimum requirements), and oversubmission creates a waste of resources and burden of early confirmation, and undersubmission creates the risk of clinical hold. · why: The judgment of the optimal submission amount is divided between the lower limit of ‘phase-appropriate’ (minimum safety guarantee) and practical risk aversion (more is safe), and is dependent on product, product modality, and institutional practice. | | 8.2 GMP inspections & inspection readiness | Policy vs. feasibility of expanding unnotified overseas inspections: The FDA announced that it will expand overseas unnotified inspections to resolve the double standard in 2025-05, but due to practical limitations such as visas, logistics, interpretation, and on-site access, policy will and operational reality are in conflict as to whether it can achieve the same effect as domestic unnotified inspections (most of the data is a ‘prepare’ contrast perspective). | | 8.2 GMP inspections & inspection readiness | Remote Regulatory Assessment (RRA) vs. Physical Inspection: RRA has gone from a pandemic tool to a permanent tool, and 704(a)(4) records requests are mandatory, but RRA is not an “inspection” under the law and is not a complete replacement for on-site GMP inspections — there is a tension between increased efficiency and reach and the limitations of depth of verification and on-site observation. | | 8.2 GMP inspections & inspection readiness | Inspection reflection of quality management maturity (QMM): The FDA has provided direction to evaluate and document maturity exceeding CGMP (2022 CPGM), but the definition and measurement of ‘what is maturity’ is subjective, so there are differences of opinion on consistency among inspectors and the boundary between maturity compared to minimum compliance (linked to QMM discussion in node 7.5). | | 8.2 GMP inspections & inspection readiness | EU-US MRA trust vs. self-inspection: There remains an issue of completeness of coordination between the principle that MRA recognizes each other’s inspections to reduce duplication and the practice of each authority still conducting its own inspections as some product groups (bio/vaccines, etc.) are excluded from the scope of operation (the exact scope of exclusion is [uncertain] in this survey). | | 8.3 Expedited & advanced-manufacturing pathways | claim: Does platform technology/AMT designation actually provide significant added value by accelerating development and review? · side_a: Designation accelerates development and review and increases regulatory predictability by referring to and utilizing prior data as prior knowledge rather than re-examining the platform/technology for each subsequent product (90-day decision, AMT independent of application, data reference). · side_b: Adoption is very low (5 cases received and 0 cases designated as of CDER AMT 2025-10) and effectiveness is uncertain, and some regulators believe that platform designation ‘may be close to promotional purposes’ as the practical benefits are not clear compared to the existing §314.54 data utilization route. · Why: Due to the gap between the design advantages of the new statutory program, overlap with existing data utilization pathways, initial low adoption, and ambiguous graduation standards, ‘formal introduction’ and ‘practical use’ are divided. | | 8.3 Expedited & advanced-manufacturing pathways | claim: Is reusing a platform based on the same delivery method (capsid/LNP) safe in gene therapy? · side_a: The same vector/delivery platform is a well-understood, reproducible technology, so efficient development and review can be justified with the minimum difference argument (design intent of §506K). · side_b: Safety risks arise from the inserted gene sequence and payload, not the delivery method, and even if the same capsid has different promoters and payloads, expression varies greatly, so ‘same delivery method → ​​mitigation screening’ may overlook the risk. · why: Platform logic assumes that the delivery method dominates the risk, but in CGT biology, the sequence/payload dominates the risk and efficacy, causing a conflict between the premise of the regulatory frame and the actual risk source. | | 8.3 Expedited & advanced-manufacturing pathways | claim: Can the existing CGMP (21 CFR 211.110) be appropriately applied to distributed/on-site (DM/POC) manufacturing? · side_a: FDA believes that 211.110 can be applied to distributed units through in-process management and process models (parallel testing) with a science/risk-based frame, and proposes a model that governs multiple sites with a central oversite and single manufacturing license. · side_b: 211.110 is used on the premise of a centralized batch plant, so batch uniformity and physical separation of samples during the process are difficult in distributed and continuous units, and there is a large gap and uncertainty in regulatory harmonization between countries in DM technology. · why: Due to the structural gap between the ‘centralized manufacturing’ assumed by CGMP and the actual ‘distributed, automated, continuous and proximate manufacturing’, the judgments of regulators and industry are divided on the applicability of the same regulations. | | 9.1 mRNA/LNP manufacturing | claim: Which is the standard for large-scale GMP manufacturing: cotransfer capping or enzymatic capping? · side_a: NEB specifies enzymatic capping as the recommended approach for large-scale/mRNA manufacturing, citing ~100% conversion. · side_b: CleanCap achieves >95% efficiency in a single reaction, reducing the burden of steps, enzymes, and QC, and has been adopted by commercial products such as BioNTech/Pfizer. · why: Due to the trade-off between additional enzyme steps, cost, and QC (enzymatic) versus slightly lower capping efficiency and template initiation sequence constraints (cotransfer), it is difficult to call it a single standard as the actual industry is divided into two paths. | | 9.1 mRNA/LNP manufacturing | claim: Which mixer solves the scale-up of mRNA-LNP mixing/encapsulation. · side_a: Microfluidics (SHM, etc.) provides the highest reproducibility and control of physical properties with millisecond uniform mixing, but is a bottleneck in GMP scale due to low throughput (<100 mL/h). · side_b: Impinging jet mixer (IJM) has been chosen for COVID commercial production as it is suitable for large-scale production, but the minimum working volume is large, so it is unsuitable for initial screening, discovery~clinical use.There is no single technology that connects them all. · Why: Because mixed physics does not scale linearly, there is a conflict between small-scale controllability and large-scale throughput, and alternatives such as parallelism and toroidal are still in the established stage. | | 9.1 mRNA/LNP manufacturing | claim: Whether mRNA-LNP components (especially lipids) will be considered starting materials or excipients for regulatory purposes. · side_a: The EMA draft seeks to define nucleotides, cap reagents, and linear DNA templates as starting materials, mRNA as raw materials, and the entire composition, including lipids, as finished products. · side_b: There is still no international agreement on whether to view components as starting materials or excipients, so standards and screening approaches vary by region. · Why: Because mRNA-LNP is a new product modality that does not fit neatly into the traditional drug substance/excipient dichotomy, it is in a transitional period where the regulatory framework is being reorganized. | | 9.2 Viral vector & cell/gene therapy manufacturing | claim: Will the future standard for vector production be transient transfection or stable producer cell lines? · side_a: Transient triple/four-plasmid transfection is the overwhelming current standard and is advantageous for early clinical trials because the envelope, accessory genes, and ratios can be flexibly changed (‘most programs still rely on transient production’). · side_b: Stable producer cell line (Asimov AAV Edge, ELEVECTA) advocates transition to mAb level manufacturing standards with GMP plasmid removal, batch consistency, and scale-up (up to 6E15 vg/L). · why: Stable stocks still have a shallow regulatory history and commercial scale performance and are strongly vendor-led, so it is too early to treat them as a mature fact — a trade-off between flexibility (temporary) and consistency and cost (stability). | | 9.2 Viral vector & cell/gene therapy manufacturing | claim: Autologous vs. allogeneic, which is the dominant model for CGT manufacturing? · side_a: Autologous is the patient’s own cells, so there are no issues with immune rejection or persistence, and the approval record has been accumulated, but scale-out, extremely expensive COGS, and long vein-to-vein are limitations. · side_b: Allogeneic products are available off-the-shelf for large batches and standard QC, improving accessibility and cost, but the issues of immune rejection and decreased cell persistence remain unresolved. · why: There is a conflict between manufacturing economics (homologous superiority) and biological sustainability/safety (autologous superiority) — a single winner has not yet been determined due to differences in clinical maturity. | | 9.2 Viral vector & cell/gene therapy manufacturing | claim: Gap between regulatory expectations and practical capabilities regarding empty/full capsid control. · side_a: Empty capsids increase the immune load, so they should be removed as much as possible, and AUC is the gold standard quantification method. · side_b: In fact, the only way to separate empty/full in large-scale actual processes is serotype-dependent ion exchange, and since there is no harmonized quantitative regulatory limit for full:empty, it is temporarily operated according to product-specific standards. · Why: The mismatch between high-resolution analysis (AUC) and scalable refinement (IEX), and the regulatory standards gap, perpetuate this tension. | | 9.2 Viral vector & cell/gene therapy manufacturing | claim: Should the RCL test be a 6-week cell-based assay, or can it be replaced by rapid qPCR? · side_a: FDA recommendations are based on a minimum of 5 passages (approximately 3 weeks) and a complete cell-based assay to catch slow-growing viruses. · side_b: Fresh infusion products cannot wait 6 weeks, so rapid tests such as VSV-G qPCR are used as a practical alternative. · Why: A real trade-off between assay sensitivity/regulatory acceptability (cell-based) and short shelf life/patient urgency (qPCR) — product shelf-life forces method selection. | | 9.3 Vaccine-specific operations | claim: Are cell-based/recombinant influenza vaccines clinically more effective than embryonic egg vaccines? · side_a: There is real-world evidence that cell-based (MDCK) and recombinant methods have excellent antigen matching, avoiding egg-adaptation, and are more effective in reducing influenza-like diseases and hospitalizations in certain seasons (particularly H3N2). · side_b: Some randomized controlled immunogenicity trials report that the differences between the three platforms are not significant or season-dependent — the advantage appears primarily in years with high circulating strain-vaccine strain mismatch. · why: Because the size of the benefit depends on seasonal antigen drift and the degree of egg-adaptation, a single conclusion is difficult, and the difference in endpoints between observational studies (effectiveness) and RCTs (immunogenicity) creates a gap in interpretation. | | 9.3 Vaccine-specific operations | claim: Which is superior to reductive amination or CDAP cyanylation as a conjugate vaccine activation chemistry? · side_a: Report that cyanylation (CDAP) preserves the sugar ring and induces higher opsonophagocytic antibody and 19A cross-reactivity in pneumococcal 19F compared to reductive amination. · side_b: Reductive amination has been widely verified and used in major commercial conjugate vaccines such as Prevnar, and has a strong robustness and regulatory history. · why: The optimal chemistry is different for each serotype and polysaccharide structure, and the effect of ring opening on new epitope creation/immunogenicity is serotype-specific, making generalization difficult. | | 9.3 Vaccine-specific operations | claim: Should we keep or replace SRID in influenza titer testing? · side_a: Regulatory agencies maintain SRID as the only internationally recognized potency/stability test with 40 years of validation — changing the method is a significant validation burden. · side_b: SRID delays shipment due to bottlenecks, low sensitivity, and inability to automate reference reagents for each strain, so there is great pressure to introduce alternative methods such as mAb-based ELISA, BLI, and structure-selective biophysical analysis. · why: Due to the trade-off between the need for speed (especially the pandemic) and automation and regulatory continuity and intercomparability of standard products, the alternative method has not yet fully proven its correlation and versatility with SRID. |
